23𓀴     KIDNAPPED
24𓀴     BEING
25𓀴     MEMOIRS OF THE ADVENTURES OF
26𓀴     DAVID BALFOUR
27𓀴     IN THE YEAR 1751
28𓀴
29𓀴
30𓀴
31𓀴     HOW HE WAS KIDNAPPED AND CAST AWAY; HIS SUFFERINGS IN
32𓀴     A DESERT ISLE; HIS JOURNEY IN THE WILD HIGHLANDS;
33𓀴     HIS ACQUAINTANCE WITH ALAN BRECK STEWART
34𓀴     AND OTHER NOTORIOUS HIGHLAND JACOBITES;
35𓀴     WITH ALL THAT HE SUFFERED AT THE
36𓀴     HANDS OF HIS UNCLE, EBENEZER
37𓀴     BALFOUR OF SHAWS, FALSELY
38𓀴     SO CALLED
39𓀴

41𓀴     ROBERT LOUIS STEVENSON

46𓀴
47𓀴PREFACE TO THE BIOGRAPHICAL EDITION
48𓀴
49𓀴
50𓀴While my husband and Mr. Henley were engaged in writing plays in
51𓀴Bournemouth they made a number of titles, hoping to use them in the
52𓀴future. Dramatic composition was not what my husband preferred, but
53𓀴the torrent of Mr. Henley’s enthusiasm swept him off his feet. However,
54𓀴after several plays had been finished, and his health seriously impaired
55𓀴by his endeavours to keep up with Mr. Henley, play writing was abandoned
56𓀴forever, and my husband returned to his legitimate vocation. Having
57𓀴added one of the titles, The Hanging Judge, to the list of projected
58𓀴plays, now thrown aside, and emboldened by my husband’s offer to give me
59𓀴any help needed, I concluded to try and write it myself.
60𓀴
61𓀴As I wanted a trial scene in the Old Bailey, I chose the period of 1700
62𓀴for my purpose; but being shamefully ignorant of my subject, and my
63𓀴husband confessing to little more knowledge than I possessed, a London
64𓀴bookseller was commissioned to send us everything he could procure
65𓀴bearing on Old Bailey trials. A great package came in response to our
66𓀴order, and very soon we were both absorbed, not so much in the trials
67𓀴as in following the brilliant career of a Mr. Garrow, who appeared as
68𓀴counsel in many of the cases. We sent for more books, and yet more,
69𓀴still intent on Mr. Garrow, whose subtle cross-examination of witnesses
70𓀴and masterly, if sometimes startling, methods of arriving at the truth
71𓀴seemed more thrilling to us than any novel.
72𓀴
73𓀴Occasionally other trials than those of the Old Bailey would be included
74𓀴in the package of books we received from London; among these my husband
75𓀴found and read with avidity:--
76𓀴
77𓀴     THE
78𓀴     TRIAL
79𓀴     OF
80𓀴     JAMES STEWART
81𓀴     in Aucharn in Duror of Appin
82𓀴     FOR THE
83𓀴     Murder of COLIN CAMPBELL of Glenure, Efq;
84𓀴     Factor for His Majefty on the forfeited
85𓀴     Estate of Ardfhiel.
86𓀴
87𓀴My husband was always interested in this period of his country’s
88𓀴history, and had already the intention of writing a story that should
89𓀴turn on the Appin murder. The tale was to be of a boy, David Balfour,
90𓀴supposed to belong to my husband’s own family, who should travel in
91𓀴Scotland as though it were a foreign country, meeting with various
92𓀴adventures and misadventures by the way. From the trial of James Stewart
93𓀴my husband gleaned much valuable material for his novel, the most
94𓀴important being the character of Alan Breck. Aside from having described
95𓀴him as “smallish in stature,” my husband seems to have taken Alan
96𓀴Breck’s personal appearance, even to his clothing, from the book.
97𓀴
98𓀴A letter from James Stewart to Mr. John Macfarlane, introduced as
99𓀴evidence in the trial, says: “There is one Alan Stewart, a distant
100𓀴friend of the late Ardshiel’s, who is in the French service, and came
101𓀴over in March last, as he said to some, in order to settle at home; to
102𓀴others, that he was to go soon back; and was, as I hear, the day that
103𓀴the murder was committed, seen not far from the place where it happened,
104𓀴and is not now to be seen; by which it is believed he was the actor. He
105𓀴is a desperate foolish fellow; and if he is guilty, came to the country
106𓀴for that very purpose. He is a tall, pock-pitted lad, very black hair,
107𓀴and wore a blue coat and metal buttons, an old red vest, and breeches of
108𓀴the same colour.” A second witness testified to having seen him wearing
109𓀴“a blue coat with silver buttons, a red waistcoat, black shag breeches,
110𓀴tartan hose, and a feathered hat, with a big coat, dun coloured,” a
111𓀴costume referred to by one of the counsel as “French cloathes which were
112𓀴remarkable.”
113𓀴
114𓀴There are many incidents given in the trial that point to Alan’s fiery
115𓀴spirit and Highland quickness to take offence. One witness “declared
116𓀴also That the said Alan Breck threatened that he would challenge
117𓀴Ballieveolan and his sons to fight because of his removing the
118𓀴declarant last year from Glenduror.” On another page: “Duncan Campbell,
119𓀴change-keeper at Annat, aged thirty-five years, married, witness cited,
120𓀴sworn, purged and examined ut supra, depones, That, in the month of
121𓀴April last, the deponent met with Alan Breck Stewart, with whom he was
122𓀴not acquainted, and John Stewart, in Auchnacoan, in the house of the
123𓀴walk miller of Auchofragan, and went on with them to the house: Alan
124𓀴Breck Stewart said, that he hated all the name of Campbell; and the
125𓀴deponent said, he had no reason for doing so: But Alan said, he had very
126𓀴good reason for it: that thereafter they left that house; and, after
127𓀴drinking a dram at another house, came to the deponent’s house, where
128𓀴they went in, and drunk some drams, and Alan Breck renewed the former
129𓀴Conversation; and the deponent, making the same answer, Alan said, that,
130𓀴if the deponent had any respect for his friends, he would tell them,
131𓀴that if they offered to turn out the possessors of Ardshiel’s estate, he
132𓀴would make black cocks of them, before they entered into possession by
133𓀴which the deponent understood shooting them, it being a common phrase in
134𓀴the country.”
135𓀴
136𓀴Some time after the publication of Kidnapped we stopped for a short
137𓀴while in the Appin country, where we were surprised and interested to
138𓀴discover that the feeling concerning the murder of Glenure (the “Red
139𓀴Fox,” also called “Colin Roy”) was almost as keen as though the tragedy
140𓀴had taken place the day before. For several years my husband received
141𓀴letters of expostulation or commendation from members of the Campbell
142𓀴and Stewart clans. I have in my possession a paper, yellow with age,
143𓀴that was sent soon after the novel appeared, containing “The Pedigree of
144𓀴the Family of Appine,” wherein it is said that “Alan 3rd Baron of Appine
145𓀴was not killed at Flowdoun, tho there, but lived to a great old age. He
146𓀴married Cameron Daughter to Ewen Cameron of Lochiel.” Following this
147𓀴is a paragraph stating that “John Stewart 1st of Ardsheall of his
148𓀴descendants Alan Breck had better be omitted. Duncan Baan Stewart in
149𓀴Achindarroch his father was a Bastard.”
150𓀴
151𓀴One day, while my husband was busily at work, I sat beside him reading
152𓀴an old cookery book called The Compleat Housewife: or Accomplish’d
153𓀴Gentlewoman’s Companion. In the midst of receipts for “Rabbits, and
154𓀴Chickens mumbled, Pickled Samphire, Skirret Pye, Baked Tansy,” and
155𓀴other forgotten delicacies, there were directions for the preparation
156𓀴of several lotions for the preservation of beauty. One of these was so
157𓀴charming that I interrupted my husband to read it aloud. “Just what
158𓀴I wanted!” he exclaimed; and the receipt for the “Lily of the Valley
159𓀴Water” was instantly incorporated into Kidnapped.
160𓀴
161𓀴F. V. DE G. S.
162𓀴
163𓀴
164𓀴
165𓀴
166𓀴DEDICATION
167𓀴
168𓀴MY DEAR CHARLES BAXTER:
169𓀴
170𓀴
171𓀴If you ever read this tale, you will likely ask yourself more questions
172𓀴than I should care to answer: as for instance how the Appin murder has
173𓀴come to fall in the year 1751, how the Torran rocks have crept so near
174𓀴to Earraid, or why the printed trial is silent as to all that touches
175𓀴David Balfour. These are nuts beyond my ability to crack. But if you
176𓀴tried me on the point of Alan’s guilt or innocence, I think I could
177𓀴defend the reading of the text. To this day you will find the tradition
178𓀴of Appin clear in Alan’s favour. If you inquire, you may even hear that
179𓀴the descendants of “the other man” who fired the shot are in the country
180𓀴to this day. But that other man’s name, inquire as you please, you shall
181𓀴not hear; for the Highlander values a secret for itself and for the
182𓀴congenial exercise of keeping it. I might go on for long to justify one
183𓀴point and own another indefensible; it is more honest to confess at once
184𓀴how little I am touched by the desire of accuracy. This is no furniture
185𓀴for the scholar’s library, but a book for the winter evening school-room
186𓀴when the tasks are over and the hour for bed draws near; and honest
187𓀴Alan, who was a grim old fire-eater in his day has in this new avatar
188𓀴no more desperate purpose than to steal some young gentleman’s attention
189𓀴from his Ovid, carry him awhile into the Highlands and the last century,
190𓀴and pack him to bed with some engaging images to mingle with his dreams.
191𓀴
192𓀴As for you, my dear Charles, I do not even ask you to like this tale.
193𓀴But perhaps when he is older, your son will; he may then be pleased to
194𓀴find his father’s name on the fly-leaf; and in the meanwhile it pleases
195𓀴me to set it there, in memory of many days that were happy and some (now
196𓀴perhaps as pleasant to remember) that were sad. If it is strange for
197𓀴me to look back from a distance both in time and space on these bygone
198𓀴adventures of our youth, it must be stranger for you who tread the same
199𓀴streets--who may to-morrow open the door of the old Speculative,
200𓀴where we begin to rank with Scott and Robert Emmet and the beloved and
201𓀴inglorious Macbean--or may pass the corner of the close where that great
202𓀴society, the L. J. R., held its meetings and drank its beer, sitting in
203𓀴the seats of Burns and his companions. I think I see you, moving there
204𓀴by plain daylight, beholding with your natural eyes those places that
205𓀴have now become for your companion a part of the scenery of dreams. How,
206𓀴in the intervals of present business, the past must echo in your memory!
207𓀴Let it not echo often without some kind thoughts of your friend,
208𓀴
209𓀴R.L.S. SKERRYVORE, BOURNEMOUTH.
210𓀴

252𓀴
253𓀴CHAPTER I
254𓀴
255𓀴I SET OFF UPON MY JOURNEY TO THE HOUSE OF SHAWS
256𓀴
257𓀴
258𓀴I will begin the story of my adventures with a certain morning early in
259𓀴the month of June, the year of grace 1751, when I took the key for the
260𓀴last time out of the door of my father’s house. The sun began to shine
261𓀴upon the summit of the hills as I went down the road; and by the time
262𓀴I had come as far as the manse, the blackbirds were whistling in the
263𓀴garden lilacs, and the mist that hung around the valley in the time of
264𓀴the dawn was beginning to arise and die away.
265𓀴
266𓀴Mr. Campbell, the minister of Essendean, was waiting for me by the
267𓀴garden gate, good man! He asked me if I had breakfasted; and hearing
268𓀴that I lacked for nothing, he took my hand in both of his and clapped it
269𓀴kindly under his arm.
270𓀴
271𓀴“Well, Davie, lad,” said he, “I will go with you as far as the ford, to
272𓀴set you on the way.” And we began to walk forward in silence.
273𓀴
274𓀴“Are ye sorry to leave Essendean?” said he, after awhile.
275𓀴
276𓀴“Why, sir,” said I, “if I knew where I was going, or what was likely
277𓀴to become of me, I would tell you candidly. Essendean is a good place
278𓀴indeed, and I have been very happy there; but then I have never been
279𓀴anywhere else. My father and mother, since they are both dead, I shall
280𓀴be no nearer to in Essendean than in the Kingdom of Hungary, and, to
281𓀴speak truth, if I thought I had a chance to better myself where I was
282𓀴going I would go with a good will.”
283𓀴
284𓀴“Ay?” said Mr. Campbell. “Very well, Davie. Then it behoves me to tell
285𓀴your fortune; or so far as I may. When your mother was gone, and your
286𓀴father (the worthy, Christian man) began to sicken for his end, he gave
287𓀴me in charge a certain letter, which he said was your inheritance. ‘So
288𓀴soon,’ says he, ‘as I am gone, and the house is redd up and the gear
289𓀴disposed of’ (all which, Davie, hath been done), ‘give my boy this
290𓀴letter into his hand, and start him off to the house of Shaws, not far
291𓀴from Cramond. That is the place I came from,’ he said, ‘and it’s where
292𓀴it befits that my boy should return. He is a steady lad,’ your father
293𓀴said, ‘and a canny goer; and I doubt not he will come safe, and be well
294𓀴liked where he goes.’”
295𓀴
296𓀴“The house of Shaws!” I cried. “What had my poor father to do with the
297𓀴house of Shaws?”
298𓀴
299𓀴“Nay,” said Mr. Campbell, “who can tell that for a surety? But the name
300𓀴of that family, Davie, boy, is the name you bear--Balfours of Shaws:
301𓀴an ancient, honest, reputable house, peradventure in these latter
302𓀴days decayed. Your father, too, was a man of learning as befitted his
303𓀴position; no man more plausibly conducted school; nor had he the manner
304𓀴or the speech of a common dominie; but (as ye will yourself remember)
305𓀴I took aye a pleasure to have him to the manse to meet the gentry; and
306𓀴those of my own house, Campbell of Kilrennet, Campbell of Dunswire,
307𓀴Campbell of Minch, and others, all well-kenned gentlemen, had pleasure
308𓀴in his society. Lastly, to put all the elements of this affair before
309𓀴you, here is the testamentary letter itself, superscrived by the own
310𓀴hand of our departed brother.”
311𓀴
312𓀴He gave me the letter, which was addressed in these words: “To the hands
313𓀴of Ebenezer Balfour, Esquire, of Shaws, in his house of Shaws, these
314𓀴will be delivered by my son, David Balfour.” My heart was beating hard
315𓀴at this great prospect now suddenly opening before a lad of seventeen
316𓀴years of age, the son of a poor country dominie in the Forest of
317𓀴Ettrick.
318𓀴
319𓀴“Mr. Campbell,” I stammered, “and if you were in my shoes, would you
320𓀴go?”
321𓀴
322𓀴“Of a surety,” said the minister, “that would I, and without pause.
323𓀴A pretty lad like you should get to Cramond (which is near in by
324𓀴Edinburgh) in two days of walk. If the worst came to the worst, and
325𓀴your high relations (as I cannot but suppose them to be somewhat of your
326𓀴blood) should put you to the door, ye can but walk the two days back
327𓀴again and risp at the manse door. But I would rather hope that ye shall
328𓀴be well received, as your poor father forecast for you, and for anything
329𓀴that I ken come to be a great man in time. And here, Davie, laddie,” he
330𓀴resumed, “it lies near upon my conscience to improve this parting, and
331𓀴set you on the right guard against the dangers of the world.”
332𓀴
333𓀴Here he cast about for a comfortable seat, lighted on a big boulder
334𓀴under a birch by the trackside, sate down upon it with a very long,
335𓀴serious upper lip, and the sun now shining in upon us between two peaks,
336𓀴put his pocket-handkerchief over his cocked hat to shelter him. There,
337𓀴then, with uplifted forefinger, he first put me on my guard against a
338𓀴considerable number of heresies, to which I had no temptation, and urged
339𓀴upon me to be instant in my prayers and reading of the Bible. That done,
340𓀴he drew a picture of the great house that I was bound to, and how I
341𓀴should conduct myself with its inhabitants.
342𓀴
343𓀴“Be soople, Davie, in things immaterial,” said he. “Bear ye this in
344𓀴mind, that, though gentle born, ye have had a country rearing. Dinnae
345𓀴shame us, Davie, dinnae shame us! In yon great, muckle house, with all
346𓀴these domestics, upper and under, show yourself as nice, as circumspect,
347𓀴as quick at the conception, and as slow of speech as any. As for the
348𓀴laird--remember he’s the laird; I say no more: honour to whom honour.
349𓀴It’s a pleasure to obey a laird; or should be, to the young.”
350𓀴
351𓀴“Well, sir,” said I, “it may be; and I’ll promise you I’ll try to make
352𓀴it so.”
353𓀴
354𓀴“Why, very well said,” replied Mr. Campbell, heartily. “And now to come
355𓀴to the material, or (to make a quibble) to the immaterial. I have here
356𓀴a little packet which contains four things.” He tugged it, as he spoke,
357𓀴and with some great difficulty, from the skirt pocket of his coat. “Of
358𓀴these four things, the first is your legal due: the little pickle money
359𓀴for your father’s books and plenishing, which I have bought (as I have
360𓀴explained from the first) in the design of re-selling at a profit to
361𓀴the incoming dominie. The other three are gifties that Mrs. Campbell and
362𓀴myself would be blithe of your acceptance. The first, which is round,
363𓀴will likely please ye best at the first off-go; but, O Davie, laddie,
364𓀴it’s but a drop of water in the sea; it’ll help you but a step, and
365𓀴vanish like the morning. The second, which is flat and square and
366𓀴written upon, will stand by you through life, like a good staff for the
367𓀴road, and a good pillow to your head in sickness. And as for the last,
368𓀴which is cubical, that’ll see you, it’s my prayerful wish, into a better
369𓀴land.”
370𓀴
371𓀴With that he got upon his feet, took off his hat, and prayed a little
372𓀴while aloud, and in affecting terms, for a young man setting out into
373𓀴the world; then suddenly took me in his arms and embraced me very hard;
374𓀴then held me at arm’s length, looking at me with his face all working
375𓀴with sorrow; and then whipped about, and crying good-bye to me, set off
376𓀴backward by the way that we had come at a sort of jogging run. It might
377𓀴have been laughable to another; but I was in no mind to laugh. I watched
378𓀴him as long as he was in sight; and he never stopped hurrying, nor once
379𓀴looked back. Then it came in upon my mind that this was all his sorrow
380𓀴at my departure; and my conscience smote me hard and fast, because I,
381𓀴for my part, was overjoyed to get away out of that quiet country-side,
382𓀴and go to a great, busy house, among rich and respected gentlefolk of my
383𓀴own name and blood.
384𓀴
385𓀴“Davie, Davie,” I thought, “was ever seen such black ingratitude? Can
386𓀴you forget old favours and old friends at the mere whistle of a name?
387𓀴Fie, fie; think shame.”
388𓀴
389𓀴And I sat down on the boulder the good man had just left, and opened the
390𓀴parcel to see the nature of my gifts. That which he had called cubical,
391𓀴I had never had much doubt of; sure enough it was a little Bible, to
392𓀴carry in a plaid-neuk. That which he had called round, I found to be a
393𓀴shilling piece; and the third, which was to help me so wonderfully both
394𓀴in health and sickness all the days of my life, was a little piece of
395𓀴coarse yellow paper, written upon thus in red ink:
396𓀴
397𓀴
398𓀴“TO MAKE LILLY OF THE VALLEY WATER.--Take the flowers of lilly of the
399𓀴valley and distil them in sack, and drink a spooneful or two as there is
400𓀴occasion. It restores speech to those that have the dumb palsey. It is
401𓀴good against the Gout; it comforts the heart and strengthens the memory;
402𓀴and the flowers, put into a Glasse, close stopt, and set into ane hill
403𓀴of ants for a month, then take it out, and you will find a liquor which
404𓀴comes from the flowers, which keep in a vial; it is good, ill or well,
405𓀴and whether man or woman.”
406𓀴
407𓀴
408𓀴
409𓀴And then, in the minister’s own hand, was added:
410𓀴
411𓀴“Likewise for sprains, rub it in; and for the cholic, a great spooneful
412𓀴in the hour.”
413𓀴
414𓀴
415𓀴To be sure, I laughed over this; but it was rather tremulous laughter;
416𓀴and I was glad to get my bundle on my staff’s end and set out over the
417𓀴ford and up the hill upon the farther side; till, just as I came on the
418𓀴green drove-road running wide through the heather, I took my last look
419𓀴of Kirk Essendean, the trees about the manse, and the big rowans in the
420𓀴kirkyard where my father and my mother lay.
421𓀴
422𓀴
423𓀴
424𓀴
425𓀴CHAPTER II
426𓀴
427𓀴I COME TO MY JOURNEY’S END
428𓀴
429𓀴
430𓀴On the forenoon of the second day, coming to the top of a hill, I saw
431𓀴all the country fall away before me down to the sea; and in the midst
432𓀴of this descent, on a long ridge, the city of Edinburgh smoking like
433𓀴a kiln. There was a flag upon the castle, and ships moving or lying
434𓀴anchored in the firth; both of which, for as far away as they were, I
435𓀴could distinguish clearly; and both brought my country heart into my
436𓀴mouth.
437𓀴
438𓀴Presently after, I came by a house where a shepherd lived, and got a
439𓀴rough direction for the neighbourhood of Cramond; and so, from one to
440𓀴another, worked my way to the westward of the capital by Colinton, till
441𓀴I came out upon the Glasgow road. And there, to my great pleasure and
442𓀴wonder, I beheld a regiment marching to the fifes, every foot in time;
443𓀴an old red-faced general on a grey horse at the one end, and at the
444𓀴other the company of Grenadiers, with their Pope’s-hats. The pride of
445𓀴life seemed to mount into my brain at the sight of the red coats and the
446𓀴hearing of that merry music.
447𓀴
448𓀴A little farther on, and I was told I was in Cramond parish, and began
449𓀴to substitute in my inquiries the name of the house of Shaws. It was a
450𓀴word that seemed to surprise those of whom I sought my way. At first I
451𓀴thought the plainness of my appearance, in my country habit, and that
452𓀴all dusty from the road, consorted ill with the greatness of the place
453𓀴to which I was bound. But after two, or maybe three, had given me the
454𓀴same look and the same answer, I began to take it in my head there was
455𓀴something strange about the Shaws itself.
456𓀴
457𓀴The better to set this fear at rest, I changed the form of my inquiries;
458𓀴and spying an honest fellow coming along a lane on the shaft of his
459𓀴cart, I asked him if he had ever heard tell of a house they called the
460𓀴house of Shaws.
461𓀴
462𓀴He stopped his cart and looked at me, like the others.
463𓀴
464𓀴“Ay” said he. “What for?”
465𓀴
466𓀴“It’s a great house?” I asked.
467𓀴
468𓀴“Doubtless,” says he. “The house is a big, muckle house.”
469𓀴
470𓀴“Ay,” said I, “but the folk that are in it?”
471𓀴
472𓀴“Folk?” cried he. “Are ye daft? There’s nae folk there--to call folk.”
473𓀴
474𓀴“What?” say I; “not Mr. Ebenezer?”
475𓀴
476𓀴“Ou, ay” says the man; “there’s the laird, to be sure, if it’s him
477𓀴you’re wanting. What’ll like be your business, mannie?”
478𓀴
479𓀴“I was led to think that I would get a situation,” I said, looking as
480𓀴modest as I could.
481𓀴
482𓀴“What?” cries the carter, in so sharp a note that his very horse
483𓀴started; and then, “Well, mannie,” he added, “it’s nane of my affairs;
484𓀴but ye seem a decent-spoken lad; and if ye’ll take a word from me, ye’ll
485𓀴keep clear of the Shaws.”
486𓀴
487𓀴The next person I came across was a dapper little man in a beautiful
488𓀴white wig, whom I saw to be a barber on his rounds; and knowing well
489𓀴that barbers were great gossips, I asked him plainly what sort of a man
490𓀴was Mr. Balfour of the Shaws.
491𓀴
492𓀴“Hoot, hoot, hoot,” said the barber, “nae kind of a man, nae kind of a
493𓀴man at all;” and began to ask me very shrewdly what my business was;
494𓀴but I was more than a match for him at that, and he went on to his next
495𓀴customer no wiser than he came.
496𓀴
497𓀴I cannot well describe the blow this dealt to my illusions. The more
498𓀴indistinct the accusations were, the less I liked them, for they left
499𓀴the wider field to fancy. What kind of a great house was this, that all
500𓀴the parish should start and stare to be asked the way to it? or what
501𓀴sort of a gentleman, that his ill-fame should be thus current on the
502𓀴wayside? If an hour’s walking would have brought me back to Essendean, I
503𓀴had left my adventure then and there, and returned to Mr. Campbell’s.
504𓀴But when I had come so far a way already, mere shame would not suffer me
505𓀴to desist till I had put the matter to the touch of proof; I was bound,
506𓀴out of mere self-respect, to carry it through; and little as I liked
507𓀴the sound of what I heard, and slow as I began to travel, I still kept
508𓀴asking my way and still kept advancing.
509𓀴
510𓀴It was drawing on to sundown when I met a stout, dark, sour-looking
511𓀴woman coming trudging down a hill; and she, when I had put my usual
512𓀴question, turned sharp about, accompanied me back to the summit she had
513𓀴just left, and pointed to a great bulk of building standing very bare
514𓀴upon a green in the bottom of the next valley. The country was pleasant
515𓀴round about, running in low hills, pleasantly watered and wooded, and
516𓀴the crops, to my eyes, wonderfully good; but the house itself appeared
517𓀴to be a kind of ruin; no road led up to it; no smoke arose from any of
518𓀴the chimneys; nor was there any semblance of a garden. My heart sank.
519𓀴“That!” I cried.
520𓀴
521𓀴The woman’s face lit up with a malignant anger. “That is the house of
522𓀴Shaws!” she cried. “Blood built it; blood stopped the building of it;
523𓀴blood shall bring it down. See here!” she cried again--“I spit upon
524𓀴the ground, and crack my thumb at it! Black be its fall! If ye see the
525𓀴laird, tell him what ye hear; tell him this makes the twelve hunner and
526𓀴nineteen time that Jennet Clouston has called down the curse on him
527𓀴and his house, byre and stable, man, guest, and master, wife, miss, or
528𓀴bairn--black, black be their fall!”
529𓀴
530𓀴And the woman, whose voice had risen to a kind of eldritch sing-song,
531𓀴turned with a skip, and was gone. I stood where she left me, with my
532𓀴hair on end. In those days folk still believed in witches and trembled
533𓀴at a curse; and this one, falling so pat, like a wayside omen, to arrest
534𓀴me ere I carried out my purpose, took the pith out of my legs.
535𓀴
536𓀴I sat me down and stared at the house of Shaws. The more I looked,
537𓀴the pleasanter that country-side appeared; being all set with hawthorn
538𓀴bushes full of flowers; the fields dotted with sheep; a fine flight of
539𓀴rooks in the sky; and every sign of a kind soil and climate; and yet the
540𓀴barrack in the midst of it went sore against my fancy.
541𓀴
542𓀴Country folk went by from the fields as I sat there on the side of the
543𓀴ditch, but I lacked the spirit to give them a good-e’en. At last the sun
544𓀴went down, and then, right up against the yellow sky, I saw a scroll of
545𓀴smoke go mounting, not much thicker, as it seemed to me, than the smoke
546𓀴of a candle; but still there it was, and meant a fire, and warmth, and
547𓀴cookery, and some living inhabitant that must have lit it; and this
548𓀴comforted my heart.
549𓀴
550𓀴So I set forward by a little faint track in the grass that led in my
551𓀴direction. It was very faint indeed to be the only way to a place
552𓀴of habitation; yet I saw no other. Presently it brought me to stone
553𓀴uprights, with an unroofed lodge beside them, and coats of arms upon
554𓀴the top. A main entrance it was plainly meant to be, but never finished;
555𓀴instead of gates of wrought iron, a pair of hurdles were tied across
556𓀴with a straw rope; and as there were no park walls, nor any sign of
557𓀴avenue, the track that I was following passed on the right hand of the
558𓀴pillars, and went wandering on toward the house.
559𓀴
560𓀴The nearer I got to that, the drearier it appeared. It seemed like the
561𓀴one wing of a house that had never been finished. What should have been
562𓀴the inner end stood open on the upper floors, and showed against the sky
563𓀴with steps and stairs of uncompleted masonry. Many of the windows were
564𓀴unglazed, and bats flew in and out like doves out of a dove-cote.
565𓀴
566𓀴The night had begun to fall as I got close; and in three of the lower
567𓀴windows, which were very high up and narrow, and well barred, the
568𓀴changing light of a little fire began to glimmer. Was this the palace
569𓀴I had been coming to? Was it within these walls that I was to seek
570𓀴new friends and begin great fortunes? Why, in my father’s house on
571𓀴Essen-Waterside, the fire and the bright lights would show a mile away,
572𓀴and the door open to a beggar’s knock!
573𓀴
574𓀴I came forward cautiously, and giving ear as I came, heard some one
575𓀴rattling with dishes, and a little dry, eager cough that came in fits;
576𓀴but there was no sound of speech, and not a dog barked.
577𓀴
578𓀴The door, as well as I could see it in the dim light, was a great piece
579𓀴of wood all studded with nails; and I lifted my hand with a faint heart
580𓀴under my jacket, and knocked once. Then I stood and waited. The house
581𓀴had fallen into a dead silence; a whole minute passed away, and nothing
582𓀴stirred but the bats overhead. I knocked again, and hearkened again.
583𓀴By this time my ears had grown so accustomed to the quiet, that I
584𓀴could hear the ticking of the clock inside as it slowly counted out the
585𓀴seconds; but whoever was in that house kept deadly still, and must have
586𓀴held his breath.
587𓀴
588𓀴I was in two minds whether to run away; but anger got the upper hand,
589𓀴and I began instead to rain kicks and buffets on the door, and to shout
590𓀴out aloud for Mr. Balfour. I was in full career, when I heard the cough
591𓀴right overhead, and jumping back and looking up, beheld a man’s head
592𓀴in a tall nightcap, and the bell mouth of a blunderbuss, at one of the
593𓀴first-storey windows.
594𓀴
595𓀴“It’s loaded,” said a voice.
596𓀴
597𓀴“I have come here with a letter,” I said, “to Mr. Ebenezer Balfour of
598𓀴Shaws. Is he here?”
599𓀴
600𓀴“From whom is it?” asked the man with the blunderbuss.
601𓀴
602𓀴“That is neither here nor there,” said I, for I was growing very wroth.
603𓀴
604𓀴“Well,” was the reply, “ye can put it down upon the doorstep, and be off
605𓀴with ye.”
606𓀴
607𓀴“I will do no such thing,” I cried. “I will deliver it into Mr.
608𓀴Balfour’s hands, as it was meant I should. It is a letter of
609𓀴introduction.”
610𓀴
611𓀴“A what?” cried the voice, sharply.
612𓀴
613𓀴I repeated what I had said.
614𓀴
615𓀴“Who are ye, yourself?” was the next question, after a considerable
616𓀴pause.
617𓀴
618𓀴“I am not ashamed of my name,” said I. “They call me David Balfour.”
619𓀴
620𓀴At that, I made sure the man started, for I heard the blunderbuss rattle
621𓀴on the window-sill; and it was after quite a long pause, and with a
622𓀴curious change of voice, that the next question followed:
623𓀴
624𓀴“Is your father dead?”
625𓀴
626𓀴I was so much surprised at this, that I could find no voice to answer,
627𓀴but stood staring.
628𓀴
629𓀴“Ay,” the man resumed, “he’ll be dead, no doubt; and that’ll be what
630𓀴brings ye chapping to my door.” Another pause, and then defiantly,
631𓀴“Well, man,” he said, “I’ll let ye in;” and he disappeared from the
632𓀴window.
633𓀴
634𓀴
635𓀴
636𓀴
637𓀴CHAPTER III
638𓀴
639𓀴I MAKE ACQUAINTANCE OF MY UNCLE
640𓀴
641𓀴
642𓀴Presently there came a great rattling of chains and bolts, and the
643𓀴door was cautiously opened and shut to again behind me as soon as I had
644𓀴passed.
645𓀴
646𓀴“Go into the kitchen and touch naething,” said the voice; and while the
647𓀴person of the house set himself to replacing the defences of the door, I
648𓀴groped my way forward and entered the kitchen.
649𓀴
650𓀴The fire had burned up fairly bright, and showed me the barest room I
651𓀴think I ever put my eyes on. Half-a-dozen dishes stood upon the shelves;
652𓀴the table was laid for supper with a bowl of porridge, a horn spoon, and
653𓀴a cup of small beer. Besides what I have named, there was not another
654𓀴thing in that great, stone-vaulted, empty chamber but lockfast chests
655𓀴arranged along the wall and a corner cupboard with a padlock.
656𓀴
657𓀴As soon as the last chain was up, the man rejoined me. He was a mean,
658𓀴stooping, narrow-shouldered, clay-faced creature; and his age might have
659𓀴been anything between fifty and seventy. His nightcap was of flannel,
660𓀴and so was the nightgown that he wore, instead of coat and waistcoat,
661𓀴over his ragged shirt. He was long unshaved; but what most distressed
662𓀴and even daunted me, he would neither take his eyes away from me nor
663𓀴look me fairly in the face. What he was, whether by trade or birth, was
664𓀴more than I could fathom; but he seemed most like an old, unprofitable
665𓀴serving-man, who should have been left in charge of that big house upon
666𓀴board wages.
667𓀴
668𓀴“Are ye sharp-set?” he asked, glancing at about the level of my knee.
669𓀴“Ye can eat that drop parritch?”
670𓀴
671𓀴I said I feared it was his own supper.
672𓀴
673𓀴“O,” said he, “I can do fine wanting it. I’ll take the ale, though, for
674𓀴it slockens[1] my cough.” He drank the cup about half out, still
675𓀴keeping an eye upon me as he drank; and then suddenly held out his
676𓀴hand. “Let’s see the letter,” said he.
677𓀴
678𓀴    [1] moistens
679𓀴
680𓀴I told him the letter was for Mr. Balfour; not for him.
681𓀴
682𓀴“And who do ye think I am?” says he. “Give me Alexander’s letter.”
683𓀴
684𓀴“You know my father’s name?”
685𓀴
686𓀴“It would be strange if I didnae,” he returned, “for he was my born
687𓀴brother; and little as ye seem to like either me or my house, or my good
688𓀴parritch, I’m your born uncle, Davie, my man, and you my born nephew. So
689𓀴give us the letter, and sit down and fill your kyte.”
690𓀴
691𓀴If I had been some years younger, what with shame, weariness, and
692𓀴disappointment, I believe I had burst into tears. As it was, I could
693𓀴find no words, neither black nor white, but handed him the letter, and
694𓀴sat down to the porridge with as little appetite for meat as ever a
695𓀴young man had.
696𓀴
697𓀴Meanwhile, my uncle, stooping over the fire, turned the letter over and
698𓀴over in his hands.
699𓀴
700𓀴“Do ye ken what’s in it?” he asked, suddenly.
701𓀴
702𓀴“You see for yourself, sir,” said I, “that the seal has not been
703𓀴broken.”
704𓀴
705𓀴“Ay,” said he, “but what brought you here?”
706𓀴
707𓀴“To give the letter,” said I.
708𓀴
709𓀴“No,” says he, cunningly, “but ye’ll have had some hopes, nae doubt?”
710𓀴
711𓀴“I confess, sir,” said I, “when I was told that I had kinsfolk
712𓀴well-to-do, I did indeed indulge the hope that they might help me in
713𓀴my life. But I am no beggar; I look for no favours at your hands, and
714𓀴I want none that are not freely given. For as poor as I appear, I have
715𓀴friends of my own that will be blithe to help me.”
716𓀴
717𓀴“Hoot-toot!” said Uncle Ebenezer, “dinnae fly up in the snuff at me.
718𓀴We’ll agree fine yet. And, Davie, my man, if you’re done with that bit
719𓀴parritch, I could just take a sup of it myself. Ay,” he continued,
720𓀴as soon as he had ousted me from the stool and spoon, “they’re fine,
721𓀴halesome food--they’re grand food, parritch.” He murmured a little grace
722𓀴to himself and fell to. “Your father was very fond of his meat, I mind;
723𓀴he was a hearty, if not a great eater; but as for me, I could never
724𓀴do mair than pyke at food.” He took a pull at the small beer, which
725𓀴probably reminded him of hospitable duties, for his next speech ran
726𓀴thus: “If ye’re dry ye’ll find water behind the door.”
727𓀴
728𓀴To this I returned no answer, standing stiffly on my two feet, and
729𓀴looking down upon my uncle with a mighty angry heart. He, on his part,
730𓀴continued to eat like a man under some pressure of time, and to throw
731𓀴out little darting glances now at my shoes and now at my home-spun
732𓀴stockings. Once only, when he had ventured to look a little higher, our
733𓀴eyes met; and no thief taken with a hand in a man’s pocket could have
734𓀴shown more lively signals of distress. This set me in a muse, whether
735𓀴his timidity arose from too long a disuse of any human company; and
736𓀴whether perhaps, upon a little trial, it might pass off, and my uncle
737𓀴change into an altogether different man. From this I was awakened by his
738𓀴sharp voice.
739𓀴
740𓀴“Your father’s been long dead?” he asked.
741𓀴
742𓀴“Three weeks, sir,” said I.
743𓀴
744𓀴“He was a secret man, Alexander--a secret, silent man,” he continued.
745𓀴“He never said muckle when he was young. He’ll never have spoken muckle
746𓀴of me?”
747𓀴
748𓀴“I never knew, sir, till you told it me yourself, that he had any
749𓀴brother.”
750𓀴
751𓀴“Dear me, dear me!” said Ebenezer. “Nor yet of Shaws, I dare say?”
752𓀴
753𓀴“Not so much as the name, sir,” said I.
754𓀴
755𓀴“To think o’ that!” said he. “A strange nature of a man!” For all that,
756𓀴he seemed singularly satisfied, but whether with himself, or me, or
757𓀴with this conduct of my father’s, was more than I could read. Certainly,
758𓀴however, he seemed to be outgrowing that distaste, or ill-will, that he
759𓀴had conceived at first against my person; for presently he jumped up,
760𓀴came across the room behind me, and hit me a smack upon the shoulder.
761𓀴“We’ll agree fine yet!” he cried. “I’m just as glad I let you in. And
762𓀴now come awa’ to your bed.”
763𓀴
764𓀴To my surprise, he lit no lamp or candle, but set forth into the dark
765𓀴passage, groped his way, breathing deeply, up a flight of steps, and
766𓀴paused before a door, which he unlocked. I was close upon his heels,
767𓀴having stumbled after him as best I might; and then he bade me go in,
768𓀴for that was my chamber. I did as he bid, but paused after a few steps,
769𓀴and begged a light to go to bed with.
770𓀴
771𓀴“Hoot-toot!” said Uncle Ebenezer, “there’s a fine moon.”
772𓀴
773𓀴“Neither moon nor star, sir, and pit-mirk,”[2] said I. “I cannae see
774𓀴the bed.”
775𓀴
776𓀴     [2] Dark as the pit.
777𓀴
778𓀴“Hoot-toot, hoot-toot!” said he. “Lights in a house is a thing I dinnae
779𓀴agree with. I’m unco feared of fires. Good-night to ye, Davie, my man.”
780𓀴 And before I had time to add a further protest, he pulled the door to,
781𓀴and I heard him lock me in from the outside.
782𓀴
783𓀴I did not know whether to laugh or cry. The room was as cold as a well,
784𓀴and the bed, when I had found my way to it, as damp as a peat-hag; but
785𓀴by good fortune I had caught up my bundle and my plaid, and rolling
786𓀴myself in the latter, I lay down upon the floor under lee of the big
787𓀴bedstead, and fell speedily asleep.
788𓀴
789𓀴With the first peep of day I opened my eyes, to find myself in a great
790𓀴chamber, hung with stamped leather, furnished with fine embroidered
791𓀴furniture, and lit by three fair windows. Ten years ago, or perhaps
792𓀴twenty, it must have been as pleasant a room to lie down or to awake in
793𓀴as a man could wish; but damp, dirt, disuse, and the mice and spiders
794𓀴had done their worst since then. Many of the window-panes, besides, were
795𓀴broken; and indeed this was so common a feature in that house, that I
796𓀴believe my uncle must at some time have stood a siege from his indignant
797𓀴neighbours--perhaps with Jennet Clouston at their head.
798𓀴
799𓀴Meanwhile the sun was shining outside; and being very cold in that
800𓀴miserable room, I knocked and shouted till my gaoler came and let me
801𓀴out. He carried me to the back of the house, where was a draw-well, and
802𓀴told me to “wash my face there, if I wanted;” and when that was done,
803𓀴I made the best of my own way back to the kitchen, where he had lit the
804𓀴fire and was making the porridge. The table was laid with two bowls and
805𓀴two horn spoons, but the same single measure of small beer. Perhaps my
806𓀴eye rested on this particular with some surprise, and perhaps my uncle
807𓀴observed it; for he spoke up as if in answer to my thought, asking me if
808𓀴I would like to drink ale--for so he called it.
809𓀴
810𓀴I told him such was my habit, but not to put himself about.
811𓀴
812𓀴“Na, na,” said he; “I’ll deny you nothing in reason.”
813𓀴
814𓀴He fetched another cup from the shelf; and then, to my great surprise,
815𓀴instead of drawing more beer, he poured an accurate half from one cup
816𓀴to the other. There was a kind of nobleness in this that took my breath
817𓀴away; if my uncle was certainly a miser, he was one of that thorough
818𓀴breed that goes near to make the vice respectable.
819𓀴
820𓀴When we had made an end of our meal, my uncle Ebenezer unlocked a
821𓀴drawer, and drew out of it a clay pipe and a lump of tobacco, from which
822𓀴he cut one fill before he locked it up again. Then he sat down in the
823𓀴sun at one of the windows and silently smoked. From time to time his
824𓀴eyes came coasting round to me, and he shot out one of his questions.
825𓀴Once it was, “And your mother?” and when I had told him that she, too,
826𓀴was dead, “Ay, she was a bonnie lassie!” Then, after another long pause,
827𓀴“Whae were these friends o’ yours?”
828𓀴
829𓀴I told him they were different gentlemen of the name of Campbell;
830𓀴though, indeed, there was only one, and that the minister, that had ever
831𓀴taken the least note of me; but I began to think my uncle made too light
832𓀴of my position, and finding myself all alone with him, I did not wish
833𓀴him to suppose me helpless.
834𓀴
835𓀴He seemed to turn this over in his mind; and then, “Davie, my man,” said
836𓀴he, “ye’ve come to the right bit when ye came to your uncle Ebenezer.
837𓀴I’ve a great notion of the family, and I mean to do the right by you;
838𓀴but while I’m taking a bit think to mysel’ of what’s the best thing to
839𓀴put you to--whether the law, or the meenistry, or maybe the army, whilk
840𓀴is what boys are fondest of--I wouldnae like the Balfours to be humbled
841𓀴before a wheen Hieland Campbells, and I’ll ask you to keep your tongue
842𓀴within your teeth. Nae letters; nae messages; no kind of word to
843𓀴onybody; or else--there’s my door.”
844𓀴
845𓀴“Uncle Ebenezer,” said I, “I’ve no manner of reason to suppose you mean
846𓀴anything but well by me. For all that, I would have you to know that I
847𓀴have a pride of my own. It was by no will of mine that I came seeking
848𓀴you; and if you show me your door again, I’ll take you at the word.”
849𓀴
850𓀴He seemed grievously put out. “Hoots-toots,” said he, “ca’ cannie,
851𓀴man--ca’ cannie! Bide a day or two. I’m nae warlock, to find a fortune
852𓀴for you in the bottom of a parritch bowl; but just you give me a day or
853𓀴two, and say naething to naebody, and as sure as sure, I’ll do the right
854𓀴by you.”
855𓀴
856𓀴“Very well,” said I, “enough said. If you want to help me, there’s no
857𓀴doubt but I’ll be glad of it, and none but I’ll be grateful.”
858𓀴
859𓀴It seemed to me (too soon, I dare say) that I was getting the upper
860𓀴hand of my uncle; and I began next to say that I must have the bed and
861𓀴bedclothes aired and put to sun-dry; for nothing would make me sleep in
862𓀴such a pickle.
863𓀴
864𓀴“Is this my house or yours?” said he, in his keen voice, and then all of
865𓀴a sudden broke off. “Na, na,” said he, “I didnae mean that. What’s mine
866𓀴is yours, Davie, my man, and what’s yours is mine. Blood’s thicker than
867𓀴water; and there’s naebody but you and me that ought the name.” And
868𓀴then on he rambled about the family, and its ancient greatness, and his
869𓀴father that began to enlarge the house, and himself that stopped the
870𓀴building as a sinful waste; and this put it in my head to give him
871𓀴Jennet Clouston’s message.
872𓀴
873𓀴“The limmer!” he cried. “Twelve hunner and fifteen--that’s every day
874𓀴since I had the limmer rowpit![3] Dod, David, I’ll have her roasted on red
875𓀴peats before I’m by with it! A witch--a proclaimed witch! I’ll aff and
876𓀴see the session clerk.”
877𓀴
878𓀴     [3] Sold up.
879𓀴
880𓀴And with that he opened a chest, and got out a very old and
881𓀴well-preserved blue coat and waistcoat, and a good enough beaver hat,
882𓀴both without lace. These he threw on any way, and taking a staff from
883𓀴the cupboard, locked all up again, and was for setting out, when a
884𓀴thought arrested him.
885𓀴
886𓀴“I cannae leave you by yoursel’ in the house,” said he. “I’ll have to
887𓀴lock you out.”
888𓀴
889𓀴The blood came to my face. “If you lock me out,” I said, “it’ll be the
890𓀴last you’ll see of me in friendship.”
891𓀴
892𓀴He turned very pale, and sucked his mouth in.
893𓀴
894𓀴“This is no the way,” he said, looking wickedly at a corner of the
895𓀴floor--“this is no the way to win my favour, David.”
896𓀴
897𓀴“Sir,” says I, “with a proper reverence for your age and our common
898𓀴blood, I do not value your favour at a boddle’s purchase. I was brought
899𓀴up to have a good conceit of myself; and if you were all the uncle, and
900𓀴all the family, I had in the world ten times over, I wouldn’t buy your
901𓀴liking at such prices.”
902𓀴
903𓀴Uncle Ebenezer went and looked out of the window for awhile. I could
904𓀴see him all trembling and twitching, like a man with palsy. But when he
905𓀴turned round, he had a smile upon his face.
906𓀴
907𓀴“Well, well,” said he, “we must bear and forbear. I’ll no go; that’s all
908𓀴that’s to be said of it.”
909𓀴
910𓀴“Uncle Ebenezer,” I said, “I can make nothing out of this. You use me
911𓀴like a thief; you hate to have me in this house; you let me see it,
912𓀴every word and every minute: it’s not possible that you can like me; and
913𓀴as for me, I’ve spoken to you as I never thought to speak to any man.
914𓀴Why do you seek to keep me, then? Let me gang back--let me gang back to
915𓀴the friends I have, and that like me!”
916𓀴
917𓀴“Na, na; na, na,” he said, very earnestly. “I like you fine; we’ll agree
918𓀴fine yet; and for the honour of the house I couldnae let you leave the
919𓀴way ye came. Bide here quiet, there’s a good lad; just you bide here
920𓀴quiet a bittie, and ye’ll find that we agree.”
921𓀴
922𓀴“Well, sir,” said I, after I had thought the matter out in silence,
923𓀴“I’ll stay awhile. It’s more just I should be helped by my own blood
924𓀴than strangers; and if we don’t agree, I’ll do my best it shall be
925𓀴through no fault of mine.”
926𓀴
927𓀴
928𓀴
929𓀴
930𓀴CHAPTER IV
931𓀴
932𓀴I RUN A GREAT DANGER IN THE HOUSE OF SHAWS
933𓀴
934𓀴
935𓀴For a day that was begun so ill, the day passed fairly well. We had the
936𓀴porridge cold again at noon, and hot porridge at night; porridge and
937𓀴small beer was my uncle’s diet. He spoke but little, and that in the
938𓀴same way as before, shooting a question at me after a long silence; and
939𓀴when I sought to lead him to talk about my future, slipped out of it
940𓀴again. In a room next door to the kitchen, where he suffered me to go,
941𓀴I found a great number of books, both Latin and English, in which I took
942𓀴great pleasure all the afternoon. Indeed, the time passed so lightly in
943𓀴this good company, that I began to be almost reconciled to my residence
944𓀴at Shaws; and nothing but the sight of my uncle, and his eyes playing
945𓀴hide and seek with mine, revived the force of my distrust.
946𓀴
947𓀴One thing I discovered, which put me in some doubt. This was an entry on
948𓀴the fly-leaf of a chap-book (one of Patrick Walker’s) plainly written
949𓀴by my father’s hand and thus conceived: “To my brother Ebenezer on his
950𓀴fifth birthday.” Now, what puzzled me was this: That, as my father was of
951𓀴course the younger brother, he must either have made some strange error,
952𓀴or he must have written, before he was yet five, an excellent, clear
953𓀴manly hand of writing.
954𓀴
955𓀴I tried to get this out of my head; but though I took down many
956𓀴interesting authors, old and new, history, poetry, and story-book, this
957𓀴notion of my father’s hand of writing stuck to me; and when at length I
958𓀴went back into the kitchen, and sat down once more to porridge and small
959𓀴beer, the first thing I said to Uncle Ebenezer was to ask him if my
960𓀴father had not been very quick at his book.
961𓀴
962𓀴“Alexander? No him!” was the reply. “I was far quicker mysel’; I was a
963𓀴clever chappie when I was young. Why, I could read as soon as he could.”
964𓀴
965𓀴This puzzled me yet more; and a thought coming into my head, I asked if
966𓀴he and my father had been twins.
967𓀴
968𓀴He jumped upon his stool, and the horn spoon fell out of his hand upon
969𓀴the floor. “What gars ye ask that?” he said, and he caught me by the
970𓀴breast of the jacket, and looked this time straight into my eyes:
971𓀴his own were little and light, and bright like a bird’s, blinking and
972𓀴winking strangely.
973𓀴
974𓀴“What do you mean?” I asked, very calmly, for I was far stronger than
975𓀴he, and not easily frightened. “Take your hand from my jacket. This is
976𓀴no way to behave.”
977𓀴
978𓀴My uncle seemed to make a great effort upon himself. “Dod man, David,”
979𓀴 he said, “ye should-nae speak to me about your father. That’s where the
980𓀴mistake is.” He sat awhile and shook, blinking in his plate: “He was all
981𓀴the brother that ever I had,” he added, but with no heart in his voice;
982𓀴and then he caught up his spoon and fell to supper again, but still
983𓀴shaking.
984𓀴
985𓀴Now this last passage, this laying of hands upon my person and
986𓀴sudden profession of love for my dead father, went so clean beyond my
987𓀴comprehension that it put me into both fear and hope. On the one hand,
988𓀴I began to think my uncle was perhaps insane and might be dangerous;
989𓀴on the other, there came up into my mind (quite unbidden by me and even
990𓀴discouraged) a story like some ballad I had heard folk singing, of a
991𓀴poor lad that was a rightful heir and a wicked kinsman that tried
992𓀴to keep him from his own. For why should my uncle play a part with a
993𓀴relative that came, almost a beggar, to his door, unless in his heart he
994𓀴had some cause to fear him?
995𓀴
996𓀴With this notion, all unacknowledged, but nevertheless getting firmly
997𓀴settled in my head, I now began to imitate his covert looks; so that
998𓀴we sat at table like a cat and a mouse, each stealthily observing the
999𓀴other. Not another word had he to say to me, black or white, but was
1000𓀴busy turning something secretly over in his mind; and the longer we
1001𓀴sat and the more I looked at him, the more certain I became that the
1002𓀴something was unfriendly to myself.
1003𓀴
1004𓀴When he had cleared the platter, he got out a single pipeful of tobacco,
1005𓀴just as in the morning, turned round a stool into the chimney corner,
1006𓀴and sat awhile smoking, with his back to me.
1007𓀴
1008𓀴“Davie,” he said, at length, “I’ve been thinking;” then he paused, and
1009𓀴said it again. “There’s a wee bit siller that I half promised ye before
1010𓀴ye were born,” he continued; “promised it to your father. O, naething
1011𓀴legal, ye understand; just gentlemen daffing at their wine. Well, I
1012𓀴keepit that bit money separate--it was a great expense, but a promise
1013𓀴is a promise--and it has grown by now to be a matter of just
1014𓀴precisely--just exactly”--and here he paused and stumbled--“of just
1015𓀴exactly forty pounds!” This last he rapped out with a sidelong glance
1016𓀴over his shoulder; and the next moment added, almost with a scream,
1017𓀴“Scots!”
1018𓀴
1019𓀴The pound Scots being the same thing as an English shilling, the
1020𓀴difference made by this second thought was considerable; I could see,
1021𓀴besides, that the whole story was a lie, invented with some end which
1022𓀴it puzzled me to guess; and I made no attempt to conceal the tone of
1023𓀴raillery in which I answered--
1024𓀴
1025𓀴“O, think again, sir! Pounds sterling, I believe!”
1026𓀴
1027𓀴“That’s what I said,” returned my uncle: “pounds sterling! And if you’ll
1028𓀴step out-by to the door a minute, just to see what kind of a night it
1029𓀴is, I’ll get it out to ye and call ye in again.”
1030𓀴
1031𓀴I did his will, smiling to myself in my contempt that he should think I
1032𓀴was so easily to be deceived. It was a dark night, with a few stars low
1033𓀴down; and as I stood just outside the door, I heard a hollow moaning
1034𓀴of wind far off among the hills. I said to myself there was something
1035𓀴thundery and changeful in the weather, and little knew of what a vast
1036𓀴importance that should prove to me before the evening passed.
1037𓀴
1038𓀴When I was called in again, my uncle counted out into my hand seven and
1039𓀴thirty golden guinea pieces; the rest was in his hand, in small gold and
1040𓀴silver; but his heart failed him there, and he crammed the change into
1041𓀴his pocket.
1042𓀴
1043𓀴“There,” said he, “that’ll show you! I’m a queer man, and strange wi’
1044𓀴strangers; but my word is my bond, and there’s the proof of it.”
1045𓀴
1046𓀴Now, my uncle seemed so miserly that I was struck dumb by this sudden
1047𓀴generosity, and could find no words in which to thank him.
1048𓀴
1049𓀴“No a word!” said he. “Nae thanks; I want nae thanks. I do my duty. I’m
1050𓀴no saying that everybody would have done it; but for my part (though
1051𓀴I’m a careful body, too) it’s a pleasure to me to do the right by my
1052𓀴brother’s son; and it’s a pleasure to me to think that now we’ll agree
1053𓀴as such near friends should.”
1054𓀴
1055𓀴I spoke him in return as handsomely as I was able; but all the while
1056𓀴I was wondering what would come next, and why he had parted with his
1057𓀴precious guineas; for as to the reason he had given, a baby would have
1058𓀴refused it.
1059𓀴
1060𓀴Presently he looked towards me sideways.
1061𓀴
1062𓀴“And see here,” says he, “tit for tat.”
1063𓀴
1064𓀴I told him I was ready to prove my gratitude in any reasonable degree,
1065𓀴and then waited, looking for some monstrous demand. And yet, when
1066𓀴at last he plucked up courage to speak, it was only to tell me (very
1067𓀴properly, as I thought) that he was growing old and a little broken, and
1068𓀴that he would expect me to help him with the house and the bit garden.
1069𓀴
1070𓀴I answered, and expressed my readiness to serve.
1071𓀴
1072𓀴“Well,” he said, “let’s begin.” He pulled out of his pocket a rusty key.
1073𓀴“There,” says he, “there’s the key of the stair-tower at the far end of
1074𓀴the house. Ye can only win into it from the outside, for that part of
1075𓀴the house is no finished. Gang ye in there, and up the stairs, and bring
1076𓀴me down the chest that’s at the top. There’s papers in’t,” he added.
1077𓀴
1078𓀴“Can I have a light, sir?” said I.
1079𓀴
1080𓀴“Na,” said he, very cunningly. “Nae lights in my house.”
1081𓀴
1082𓀴“Very well, sir,” said I. “Are the stairs good?”
1083𓀴
1084𓀴“They’re grand,” said he; and then, as I was going, “Keep to the wall,”
1085𓀴 he added; “there’s nae bannisters. But the stairs are grand underfoot.”
1086𓀴
1087𓀴Out I went into the night. The wind was still moaning in the distance,
1088𓀴though never a breath of it came near the house of Shaws. It had fallen
1089𓀴blacker than ever; and I was glad to feel along the wall, till I came
1090𓀴the length of the stairtower door at the far end of the unfinished wing.
1091𓀴I had got the key into the keyhole and had just turned it, when all upon
1092𓀴a sudden, without sound of wind or thunder, the whole sky lighted up
1093𓀴with wild fire and went black again. I had to put my hand over my eyes
1094𓀴to get back to the colour of the darkness; and indeed I was already half
1095𓀴blinded when I stepped into the tower.
1096𓀴
1097𓀴It was so dark inside, it seemed a body could scarce breathe; but I
1098𓀴pushed out with foot and hand, and presently struck the wall with the
1099𓀴one, and the lowermost round of the stair with the other. The wall, by
1100𓀴the touch, was of fine hewn stone; the steps too, though somewhat steep
1101𓀴and narrow, were of polished masonwork, and regular and solid underfoot.
1102𓀴Minding my uncle’s word about the bannisters, I kept close to the tower
1103𓀴side, and felt my way in the pitch darkness with a beating heart.
1104𓀴
1105𓀴The house of Shaws stood some five full storeys high, not counting
1106𓀴lofts. Well, as I advanced, it seemed to me the stair grew airier and a
1107𓀴thought more lightsome; and I was wondering what might be the cause of
1108𓀴this change, when a second blink of the summer lightning came and went.
1109𓀴If I did not cry out, it was because fear had me by the throat; and if I
1110𓀴did not fall, it was more by Heaven’s mercy than my own strength. It was
1111𓀴not only that the flash shone in on every side through breaches in the
1112𓀴wall, so that I seemed to be clambering aloft upon an open scaffold, but
1113𓀴the same passing brightness showed me the steps were of unequal length,
1114𓀴and that one of my feet rested that moment within two inches of the
1115𓀴well.
1116𓀴
1117𓀴This was the grand stair! I thought; and with the thought, a gust of
1118𓀴a kind of angry courage came into my heart. My uncle had sent me here,
1119𓀴certainly to run great risks, perhaps to die. I swore I would settle
1120𓀴that “perhaps,” if I should break my neck for it; got me down upon my
1121𓀴hands and knees; and as slowly as a snail, feeling before me every
1122𓀴inch, and testing the solidity of every stone, I continued to ascend
1123𓀴the stair. The darkness, by contrast with the flash, appeared to have
1124𓀴redoubled; nor was that all, for my ears were now troubled and my mind
1125𓀴confounded by a great stir of bats in the top part of the tower, and the
1126𓀴foul beasts, flying downwards, sometimes beat about my face and body.
1127𓀴
1128𓀴The tower, I should have said, was square; and in every corner the step
1129𓀴was made of a great stone of a different shape to join the flights.
1130𓀴Well, I had come close to one of these turns, when, feeling forward
1131𓀴as usual, my hand slipped upon an edge and found nothing but emptiness
1132𓀴beyond it. The stair had been carried no higher; to set a stranger
1133𓀴mounting it in the darkness was to send him straight to his death; and
1134𓀴(although, thanks to the lightning and my own precautions, I was safe
1135𓀴enough) the mere thought of the peril in which I might have stood, and
1136𓀴the dreadful height I might have fallen from, brought out the sweat upon
1137𓀴my body and relaxed my joints.
1138𓀴
1139𓀴But I knew what I wanted now, and turned and groped my way down again,
1140𓀴with a wonderful anger in my heart. About half-way down, the wind sprang
1141𓀴up in a clap and shook the tower, and died again; the rain followed; and
1142𓀴before I had reached the ground level it fell in buckets. I put out my
1143𓀴head into the storm, and looked along towards the kitchen. The door,
1144𓀴which I had shut behind me when I left, now stood open, and shed a
1145𓀴little glimmer of light; and I thought I could see a figure standing
1146𓀴in the rain, quite still, like a man hearkening. And then there came
1147𓀴a blinding flash, which showed me my uncle plainly, just where I had
1148𓀴fancied him to stand; and hard upon the heels of it, a great tow-row of
1149𓀴thunder.
1150𓀴
1151𓀴Now, whether my uncle thought the crash to be the sound of my fall, or
1152𓀴whether he heard in it God’s voice denouncing murder, I will leave you
1153𓀴to guess. Certain it is, at least, that he was seized on by a kind of
1154𓀴panic fear, and that he ran into the house and left the door open behind
1155𓀴him. I followed as softly as I could, and, coming unheard into the
1156𓀴kitchen, stood and watched him.
1157𓀴
1158𓀴He had found time to open the corner cupboard and bring out a great case
1159𓀴bottle of aqua vitae, and now sat with his back towards me at the table.
1160𓀴Ever and again he would be seized with a fit of deadly shuddering and
1161𓀴groan aloud, and carrying the bottle to his lips, drink down the raw
1162𓀴spirits by the mouthful.
1163𓀴
1164𓀴I stepped forward, came close behind him where he sat, and suddenly
1165𓀴clapping my two hands down upon his shoulders--“Ah!” cried I.
1166𓀴
1167𓀴My uncle gave a kind of broken cry like a sheep’s bleat, flung up his
1168𓀴arms, and tumbled to the floor like a dead man. I was somewhat shocked
1169𓀴at this; but I had myself to look to first of all, and did not hesitate
1170𓀴to let him lie as he had fallen. The keys were hanging in the cupboard;
1171𓀴and it was my design to furnish myself with arms before my uncle should
1172𓀴come again to his senses and the power of devising evil. In the cupboard
1173𓀴were a few bottles, some apparently of medicine; a great many bills and
1174𓀴other papers, which I should willingly enough have rummaged, had I had
1175𓀴the time; and a few necessaries that were nothing to my purpose. Thence
1176𓀴I turned to the chests. The first was full of meal; the second of
1177𓀴moneybags and papers tied into sheaves; in the third, with many
1178𓀴other things (and these for the most part clothes) I found a rusty,
1179𓀴ugly-looking Highland dirk without the scabbard. This, then, I concealed
1180𓀴inside my waistcoat, and turned to my uncle.
1181𓀴
1182𓀴He lay as he had fallen, all huddled, with one knee up and one arm
1183𓀴sprawling abroad; his face had a strange colour of blue, and he seemed
1184𓀴to have ceased breathing. Fear came on me that he was dead; then I
1185𓀴got water and dashed it in his face; and with that he seemed to come a
1186𓀴little to himself, working his mouth and fluttering his eyelids. At last
1187𓀴he looked up and saw me, and there came into his eyes a terror that was
1188𓀴not of this world.
1189𓀴
1190𓀴“Come, come,” said I; “sit up.”
1191𓀴
1192𓀴“Are ye alive?” he sobbed. “O man, are ye alive?”
1193𓀴
1194𓀴“That am I,” said I. “Small thanks to you!”
1195𓀴
1196𓀴He had begun to seek for his breath with deep sighs. “The blue phial,”
1197𓀴 said he--“in the aumry--the blue phial.” His breath came slower still.
1198𓀴
1199𓀴I ran to the cupboard, and, sure enough, found there a blue phial
1200𓀴of medicine, with the dose written on it on a paper, and this I
1201𓀴administered to him with what speed I might.
1202𓀴
1203𓀴“It’s the trouble,” said he, reviving a little; “I have a trouble,
1204𓀴Davie. It’s the heart.”
1205𓀴
1206𓀴I set him on a chair and looked at him. It is true I felt some pity for
1207𓀴a man that looked so sick, but I was full besides of righteous anger;
1208𓀴and I numbered over before him the points on which I wanted explanation:
1209𓀴why he lied to me at every word; why he feared that I should leave him;
1210𓀴why he disliked it to be hinted that he and my father were twins--“Is
1211𓀴that because it is true?” I asked; why he had given me money to which I
1212𓀴was convinced I had no claim; and, last of all, why he had tried to kill
1213𓀴me. He heard me all through in silence; and then, in a broken voice,
1214𓀴begged me to let him go to bed.
1215𓀴
1216𓀴“I’ll tell ye the morn,” he said; “as sure as death I will.”
1217𓀴
1218𓀴And so weak was he that I could do nothing but consent. I locked him
1219𓀴into his room, however, and pocketed the key, and then returning to
1220𓀴the kitchen, made up such a blaze as had not shone there for many a long
1221𓀴year, and wrapping myself in my plaid, lay down upon the chests and fell
1222𓀴asleep.
1223𓀴
1224𓀴
1225𓀴
1226𓀴
1227𓀴CHAPTER V
1228𓀴
1229𓀴I GO TO THE QUEEN’S FERRY
1230𓀴
1231𓀴
1232𓀴Much rain fell in the night; and the next morning there blew a bitter
1233𓀴wintry wind out of the north-west, driving scattered clouds. For all
1234𓀴that, and before the sun began to peep or the last of the stars had
1235𓀴vanished, I made my way to the side of the burn, and had a plunge in a
1236𓀴deep whirling pool. All aglow from my bath, I sat down once more
1237𓀴beside the fire, which I replenished, and began gravely to consider my
1238𓀴position.
1239𓀴
1240𓀴There was now no doubt about my uncle’s enmity; there was no doubt I
1241𓀴carried my life in my hand, and he would leave no stone unturned that
1242𓀴he might compass my destruction. But I was young and spirited, and
1243𓀴like most lads that have been country-bred, I had a great opinion of my
1244𓀴shrewdness. I had come to his door no better than a beggar and little
1245𓀴more than a child; he had met me with treachery and violence; it would
1246𓀴be a fine consummation to take the upper hand, and drive him like a herd
1247𓀴of sheep.
1248𓀴
1249𓀴I sat there nursing my knee and smiling at the fire; and I saw myself in
1250𓀴fancy smell out his secrets one after another, and grow to be that man’s
1251𓀴king and ruler. The warlock of Essendean, they say, had made a mirror in
1252𓀴which men could read the future; it must have been of other stuff than
1253𓀴burning coal; for in all the shapes and pictures that I sat and gazed
1254𓀴at, there was never a ship, never a seaman with a hairy cap, never a big
1255𓀴bludgeon for my silly head, or the least sign of all those tribulations
1256𓀴that were ripe to fall on me.
1257𓀴
1258𓀴Presently, all swollen with conceit, I went up-stairs and gave my
1259𓀴prisoner his liberty. He gave me good-morning civilly; and I gave the
1260𓀴same to him, smiling down upon him, from the heights of my sufficiency.
1261𓀴Soon we were set to breakfast, as it might have been the day before.
1262𓀴
1263𓀴“Well, sir,” said I, with a jeering tone, “have you nothing more to say
1264𓀴to me?” And then, as he made no articulate reply, “It will be time,
1265𓀴I think, to understand each other,” I continued. “You took me for
1266𓀴a country Johnnie Raw, with no more mother-wit or courage than a
1267𓀴porridge-stick. I took you for a good man, or no worse than others at
1268𓀴the least. It seems we were both wrong. What cause you have to fear me,
1269𓀴to cheat me, and to attempt my life--”
1270𓀴
1271𓀴He murmured something about a jest, and that he liked a bit of fun; and
1272𓀴then, seeing me smile, changed his tone, and assured me he would make
1273𓀴all clear as soon as we had breakfasted. I saw by his face that he had
1274𓀴no lie ready for me, though he was hard at work preparing one; and I
1275𓀴think I was about to tell him so, when we were interrupted by a knocking
1276𓀴at the door.
1277𓀴
1278𓀴Bidding my uncle sit where he was, I went to open it, and found on the
1279𓀴doorstep a half-grown boy in sea-clothes. He had no sooner seen me than
1280𓀴he began to dance some steps of the sea-hornpipe (which I had never
1281𓀴before heard of far less seen), snapping his fingers in the air and
1282𓀴footing it right cleverly. For all that, he was blue with the cold; and
1283𓀴there was something in his face, a look between tears and laughter, that
1284𓀴was highly pathetic and consisted ill with this gaiety of manner.
1285𓀴
1286𓀴“What cheer, mate?” says he, with a cracked voice.
1287𓀴
1288𓀴I asked him soberly to name his pleasure.
1289𓀴
1290𓀴“O, pleasure!” says he; and then began to sing:
1291𓀴
1292𓀴     “For it’s my delight, of a shiny night,
1293𓀴     In the season of the year.”
1294𓀴
1295𓀴“Well,” said I, “if you have no business at all, I will even be so
1296𓀴unmannerly as to shut you out.”
1297𓀴
1298𓀴“Stay, brother!” he cried. “Have you no fun about you? or do you want
1299𓀴to get me thrashed? I’ve brought a letter from old Heasyoasy to Mr.
1300𓀴Belflower.” He showed me a letter as he spoke. “And I say, mate,” he
1301𓀴added, “I’m mortal hungry.”
1302𓀴
1303𓀴“Well,” said I, “come into the house, and you shall have a bite if I go
1304𓀴empty for it.”
1305𓀴
1306𓀴With that I brought him in and set him down to my own place, where he
1307𓀴fell-to greedily on the remains of breakfast, winking to me between
1308𓀴whiles, and making many faces, which I think the poor soul considered
1309𓀴manly. Meanwhile, my uncle had read the letter and sat thinking; then,
1310𓀴suddenly, he got to his feet with a great air of liveliness, and pulled
1311𓀴me apart into the farthest corner of the room.
1312𓀴
1313𓀴“Read that,” said he, and put the letter in my hand.
1314𓀴
1315𓀴Here it is, lying before me as I write:
1316𓀴
1317𓀴“The Hawes Inn, at the Queen’s Ferry.
1318𓀴
1319𓀴“Sir,--I lie here with my hawser up and down, and send my cabin-boy to
1320𓀴informe. If you have any further commands for over-seas, to-day will be
1321𓀴the last occasion, as the wind will serve us well out of the firth.
1322𓀴I will not seek to deny that I have had crosses with your doer,[4] Mr.
1323𓀴Rankeillor; of which, if not speedily redd up, you may looke to see some
1324𓀴losses follow. I have drawn a bill upon you, as per margin, and am, sir,
1325𓀴     your most obedt., humble servant,
1326𓀴
1327𓀴                                       “ELIAS HOSEASON.”
1328𓀴
1329𓀴[4] Agent.
1330𓀴
1331𓀴“You see, Davie,” resumed my uncle, as soon as he saw that I had done,
1332𓀴“I have a venture with this man Hoseason, the captain of a trading brig,
1333𓀴the _Covenant_, of Dysart. Now, if you and me was to walk over with
1334𓀴yon lad, I could see the captain at the Hawes, or maybe on board the
1335𓀴_Covenant_ if there was papers to be signed; and so far from a loss of
1336𓀴time, we can jog on to the lawyer, Mr. Rankeillor’s. After a’ that’s
1337𓀴come and gone, ye would be swier[5] to believe me upon my naked word; but
1338𓀴ye’ll believe Rankeillor. He’s factor to half the gentry in these parts;
1339𓀴an auld man, forby: highly respeckit, and he kenned your father.”
1340𓀴
1341𓀴     [5] Unwilling.
1342𓀴
1343𓀴I stood awhile and thought. I was going to some place of shipping, which
1344𓀴was doubtless populous, and where my uncle durst attempt no violence,
1345𓀴and, indeed, even the society of the cabin-boy so far protected me. Once
1346𓀴there, I believed I could force on the visit to the lawyer, even if my
1347𓀴uncle were now insincere in proposing it; and, perhaps, in the bottom
1348𓀴of my heart, I wished a nearer view of the sea and ships. You are to
1349𓀴remember I had lived all my life in the inland hills, and just two days
1350𓀴before had my first sight of the firth lying like a blue floor, and the
1351𓀴sailed ships moving on the face of it, no bigger than toys. One thing
1352𓀴with another, I made up my mind.
1353𓀴
1354𓀴“Very well,” says I, “let us go to the Ferry.”
1355𓀴
1356𓀴My uncle got into his hat and coat, and buckled an old rusty cutlass on;
1357𓀴and then we trod the fire out, locked the door, and set forth upon our
1358𓀴walk.
1359𓀴
1360𓀴The wind, being in that cold quarter the north-west, blew nearly in our
1361𓀴faces as we went. It was the month of June; the grass was all white with
1362𓀴daisies, and the trees with blossom; but, to judge by our blue nails
1363𓀴and aching wrists, the time might have been winter and the whiteness a
1364𓀴December frost.
1365𓀴
1366𓀴Uncle Ebenezer trudged in the ditch, jogging from side to side like an
1367𓀴old ploughman coming home from work. He never said a word the whole
1368𓀴way; and I was thrown for talk on the cabin-boy. He told me his name was
1369𓀴Ransome, and that he had followed the sea since he was nine, but could
1370𓀴not say how old he was, as he had lost his reckoning. He showed me
1371𓀴tattoo marks, baring his breast in the teeth of the wind and in spite
1372𓀴of my remonstrances, for I thought it was enough to kill him; he swore
1373𓀴horribly whenever he remembered, but more like a silly schoolboy than a
1374𓀴man; and boasted of many wild and bad things that he had done: stealthy
1375𓀴thefts, false accusations, ay, and even murder; but all with such a
1376𓀴dearth of likelihood in the details, and such a weak and crazy swagger
1377𓀴in the delivery, as disposed me rather to pity than to believe him.
1378𓀴
1379𓀴I asked him of the brig (which he declared was the finest ship that
1380𓀴sailed) and of Captain Hoseason, in whose praises he was equally loud.
1381𓀴Heasyoasy (for so he still named the skipper) was a man, by his account,
1382𓀴that minded for nothing either in heaven or earth; one that, as people
1383𓀴said, would “crack on all sail into the day of judgment;” rough, fierce,
1384𓀴unscrupulous, and brutal; and all this my poor cabin-boy had taught
1385𓀴himself to admire as something seamanlike and manly. He would only admit
1386𓀴one flaw in his idol. “He ain’t no seaman,” he admitted. “That’s Mr.
1387𓀴Shuan that navigates the brig; he’s the finest seaman in the trade, only
1388𓀴for drink; and I tell you I believe it! Why, look’ere;” and turning down
1389𓀴his stocking he showed me a great, raw, red wound that made my blood run
1390𓀴cold. “He done that--Mr. Shuan done it,” he said, with an air of pride.
1391𓀴
1392𓀴“What!” I cried, “do you take such savage usage at his hands? Why, you
1393𓀴are no slave, to be so handled!”
1394𓀴
1395𓀴“No,” said the poor moon-calf, changing his tune at once, “and so he’ll
1396𓀴find. See’ere;” and he showed me a great case-knife, which he told me
1397𓀴was stolen. “O,” says he, “let me see him try; I dare him to; I’ll do
1398𓀴for him! O, he ain’t the first!” And he confirmed it with a poor, silly,
1399𓀴ugly oath.
1400𓀴
1401𓀴I have never felt such pity for any one in this wide world as I felt for
1402𓀴that half-witted creature, and it began to come over me that the brig
1403𓀴_Covenant_ (for all her pious name) was little better than a hell upon
1404𓀴the seas.
1405𓀴
1406𓀴“Have you no friends?” said I.
1407𓀴
1408𓀴He said he had a father in some English seaport, I forget which.
1409𓀴
1410𓀴“He was a fine man, too,” he said, “but he’s dead.”
1411𓀴
1412𓀴“In Heaven’s name,” cried I, “can you find no reputable life on shore?”
1413𓀴
1414𓀴“O, no,” says he, winking and looking very sly, “they would put me to a
1415𓀴trade. I know a trick worth two of that, I do!”
1416𓀴
1417𓀴I asked him what trade could be so dreadful as the one he followed,
1418𓀴where he ran the continual peril of his life, not alone from wind and
1419𓀴sea, but by the horrid cruelty of those who were his masters. He said
1420𓀴it was very true; and then began to praise the life, and tell what a
1421𓀴pleasure it was to get on shore with money in his pocket, and spend it
1422𓀴like a man, and buy apples, and swagger, and surprise what he called
1423𓀴stick-in-the-mud boys. “And then it’s not all as bad as that,” says he;
1424𓀴“there’s worse off than me: there’s the twenty-pounders. O, laws!
1425𓀴you should see them taking on. Why, I’ve seen a man as old as you, I
1426𓀴dessay”--(to him I seemed old)--“ah, and he had a beard, too--well, and
1427𓀴as soon as we cleared out of the river, and he had the drug out of his
1428𓀴head--my! how he cried and carried on! I made a fine fool of him, I tell
1429𓀴you! And then there’s little uns, too: oh, little by me! I tell you, I
1430𓀴keep them in order. When we carry little uns, I have a rope’s end of
1431𓀴my own to wollop’em.” And so he ran on, until it came in on me what
1432𓀴he meant by twenty-pounders were those unhappy criminals who were
1433𓀴sent over-seas to slavery in North America, or the still more unhappy
1434𓀴innocents who were kidnapped or trepanned (as the word went) for private
1435𓀴interest or vengeance.
1436𓀴
1437𓀴Just then we came to the top of the hill, and looked down on the Ferry
1438𓀴and the Hope. The Firth of Forth (as is very well known) narrows at this
1439𓀴point to the width of a good-sized river, which makes a convenient ferry
1440𓀴going north, and turns the upper reach into a landlocked haven for all
1441𓀴manner of ships. Right in the midst of the narrows lies an islet with
1442𓀴some ruins; on the south shore they have built a pier for the service
1443𓀴of the Ferry; and at the end of the pier, on the other side of the road,
1444𓀴and backed against a pretty garden of holly-trees and hawthorns, I could
1445𓀴see the building which they called the Hawes Inn.
1446𓀴
1447𓀴The town of Queensferry lies farther west, and the neighbourhood of the
1448𓀴inn looked pretty lonely at that time of day, for the boat had just
1449𓀴gone north with passengers. A skiff, however, lay beside the pier, with
1450𓀴some seamen sleeping on the thwarts; this, as Ransome told me, was the
1451𓀴brig’s boat waiting for the captain; and about half a mile off, and all
1452𓀴alone in the anchorage, he showed me the _Covenant_ herself. There was
1453𓀴a sea-going bustle on board; yards were swinging into place; and as the
1454𓀴wind blew from that quarter, I could hear the song of the sailors as
1455𓀴they pulled upon the ropes. After all I had listened to upon the way, I
1456𓀴looked at that ship with an extreme abhorrence; and from the bottom of
1457𓀴my heart I pitied all poor souls that were condemned to sail in her.
1458𓀴
1459𓀴We had all three pulled up on the brow of the hill; and now I marched
1460𓀴across the road and addressed my uncle. “I think it right to tell
1461𓀴you, sir,” says I, “there’s nothing that will bring me on board that
1462𓀴_Covenant_.”
1463𓀴
1464𓀴He seemed to waken from a dream. “Eh?” he said. “What’s that?”
1465𓀴
1466𓀴I told him over again.
1467𓀴
1468𓀴“Well, well,” he said, “we’ll have to please ye, I suppose. But what
1469𓀴are we standing here for? It’s perishing cold; and if I’m no mistaken,
1470𓀴they’re busking the _Covenant_ for sea.”
1471𓀴
1472𓀴
1473𓀴
1474𓀴
1475𓀴CHAPTER VI
1476𓀴
1477𓀴WHAT BEFELL AT THE QUEEN’S FERRY
1478𓀴
1479𓀴
1480𓀴As soon as we came to the inn, Ransome led us up the stair to a small
1481𓀴room, with a bed in it, and heated like an oven by a great fire of coal.
1482𓀴At a table hard by the chimney, a tall, dark, sober-looking man sat
1483𓀴writing. In spite of the heat of the room, he wore a thick sea-jacket,
1484𓀴buttoned to the neck, and a tall hairy cap drawn down over his ears; yet
1485𓀴I never saw any man, not even a judge upon the bench, look cooler, or
1486𓀴more studious and self-possessed, than this ship-captain.
1487𓀴
1488𓀴He got to his feet at once, and coming forward, offered his large hand
1489𓀴to Ebenezer. “I am proud to see you, Mr. Balfour,” said he, in a fine
1490𓀴deep voice, “and glad that ye are here in time. The wind’s fair, and the
1491𓀴tide upon the turn; we’ll see the old coal-bucket burning on the Isle of
1492𓀴May before to-night.”
1493𓀴
1494𓀴“Captain Hoseason,” returned my uncle, “you keep your room unco hot.”
1495𓀴
1496𓀴“It’s a habit I have, Mr. Balfour,” said the skipper. “I’m a cold-rife
1497𓀴man by my nature; I have a cold blood, sir. There’s neither fur,
1498𓀴nor flannel--no, sir, nor hot rum, will warm up what they call
1499𓀴the temperature. Sir, it’s the same with most men that have been
1500𓀴carbonadoed, as they call it, in the tropic seas.”
1501𓀴
1502𓀴“Well, well, captain,” replied my uncle, “we must all be the way we’re
1503𓀴made.”
1504𓀴
1505𓀴But it chanced that this fancy of the captain’s had a great share in my
1506𓀴misfortunes. For though I had promised myself not to let my kinsman out
1507𓀴of sight, I was both so impatient for a nearer look of the sea, and
1508𓀴so sickened by the closeness of the room, that when he told me to “run
1509𓀴down-stairs and play myself awhile,” I was fool enough to take him at
1510𓀴his word.
1511𓀴
1512𓀴Away I went, therefore, leaving the two men sitting down to a bottle
1513𓀴and a great mass of papers; and crossing the road in front of the inn,
1514𓀴walked down upon the beach. With the wind in that quarter, only little
1515𓀴wavelets, not much bigger than I had seen upon a lake, beat upon the
1516𓀴shore. But the weeds were new to me--some green, some brown and long,
1517𓀴and some with little bladders that crackled between my fingers. Even so
1518𓀴far up the firth, the smell of the sea-water was exceedingly salt and
1519𓀴stirring; the _Covenant_, besides, was beginning to shake out her
1520𓀴sails, which hung upon the yards in clusters; and the spirit of all
1521𓀴that I beheld put me in thoughts of far voyages and foreign places.
1522𓀴
1523𓀴I looked, too, at the seamen with the skiff--big brown fellows, some in
1524𓀴shirts, some with jackets, some with coloured handkerchiefs about their
1525𓀴throats, one with a brace of pistols stuck into his pockets, two or
1526𓀴three with knotty bludgeons, and all with their case-knives. I passed
1527𓀴the time of day with one that looked less desperate than his fellows,
1528𓀴and asked him of the sailing of the brig. He said they would get under
1529𓀴way as soon as the ebb set, and expressed his gladness to be out of
1530𓀴a port where there were no taverns and fiddlers; but all with such
1531𓀴horrifying oaths, that I made haste to get away from him.
1532𓀴
1533𓀴This threw me back on Ransome, who seemed the least wicked of that gang,
1534𓀴and who soon came out of the inn and ran to me, crying for a bowl of
1535𓀴punch. I told him I would give him no such thing, for neither he nor I
1536𓀴was of an age for such indulgences. “But a glass of ale you may have,
1537𓀴and welcome,” said I. He mopped and mowed at me, and called me names;
