1538𓀴but he was glad to get the ale, for all that; and presently we were
1539𓀴set down at a table in the front room of the inn, and both eating and
1540𓀴drinking with a good appetite.
1541𓀴
1542𓀴Here it occurred to me that, as the landlord was a man of that county,
1543𓀴I might do well to make a friend of him. I offered him a share, as was
1544𓀴much the custom in those days; but he was far too great a man to sit
1545𓀴with such poor customers as Ransome and myself, and he was leaving the
1546𓀴room, when I called him back to ask if he knew Mr. Rankeillor.
1547𓀴
1548𓀴“Hoot, ay,” says he, “and a very honest man. And, O, by-the-by,” says
1549𓀴he, “was it you that came in with Ebenezer?” And when I had told him
1550𓀴yes, “Ye’ll be no friend of his?” he asked, meaning, in the Scottish
1551𓀴way, that I would be no relative.
1552𓀴
1553𓀴I told him no, none.
1554𓀴
1555𓀴“I thought not,” said he, “and yet ye have a kind of gliff[6] of Mr.
1556𓀴Alexander.”
1557𓀴
1558𓀴     [6] Look.
1559𓀴
1560𓀴I said it seemed that Ebenezer was ill-seen in the country.
1561𓀴
1562𓀴“Nae doubt,” said the landlord. “He’s a wicked auld man, and there’s
1563𓀴many would like to see him girning in the tow.[7] Jennet Clouston and mony
1564𓀴mair that he has harried out of house and hame. And yet he was ance
1565𓀴a fine young fellow, too. But that was before the sough[8] gaed abroad
1566𓀴about Mr. Alexander, that was like the death of him.”
1567𓀴
1568𓀴     [7] Rope.
1569𓀴
1570𓀴     [8] Report.
1571𓀴
1572𓀴“And what was it?” I asked.
1573𓀴
1574𓀴“Ou, just that he had killed him,” said the landlord. “Did ye never hear
1575𓀴that?”
1576𓀴
1577𓀴“And what would he kill him for?” said I.
1578𓀴
1579𓀴“And what for, but just to get the place,” said he.
1580𓀴
1581𓀴“The place?” said I. “The Shaws?”
1582𓀴
1583𓀴“Nae other place that I ken,” said he.
1584𓀴
1585𓀴“Ay, man?” said I. “Is that so? Was my--was Alexander the eldest son?”
1586𓀴
1587𓀴“‘Deed was he,” said the landlord. “What else would he have killed him
1588𓀴for?”
1589𓀴
1590𓀴And with that he went away, as he had been impatient to do from the
1591𓀴beginning.
1592𓀴
1593𓀴Of course, I had guessed it a long while ago; but it is one thing to
1594𓀴guess, another to know; and I sat stunned with my good fortune, and
1595𓀴could scarce grow to believe that the same poor lad who had trudged in
1596𓀴the dust from Ettrick Forest not two days ago, was now one of the rich
1597𓀴of the earth, and had a house and broad lands, and might mount his horse
1598𓀴tomorrow. All these pleasant things, and a thousand others, crowded into
1599𓀴my mind, as I sat staring before me out of the inn window, and paying
1600𓀴no heed to what I saw; only I remember that my eye lighted on Captain
1601𓀴Hoseason down on the pier among his seamen, and speaking with some
1602𓀴authority. And presently he came marching back towards the house, with
1603𓀴no mark of a sailor’s clumsiness, but carrying his fine, tall figure
1604𓀴with a manly bearing, and still with the same sober, grave expression on
1605𓀴his face. I wondered if it was possible that Ransome’s stories could
1606𓀴be true, and half disbelieved them; they fitted so ill with the man’s
1607𓀴looks. But indeed, he was neither so good as I supposed him, nor quite
1608𓀴so bad as Ransome did; for, in fact, he was two men, and left the better
1609𓀴one behind as soon as he set foot on board his vessel.
1610𓀴
1611𓀴The next thing, I heard my uncle calling me, and found the pair in the
1612𓀴road together. It was the captain who addressed me, and that with an air
1613𓀴(very flattering to a young lad) of grave equality.
1614𓀴
1615𓀴“Sir,” said he, “Mr. Balfour tells me great things of you; and for my
1616𓀴own part, I like your looks. I wish I was for longer here, that we might
1617𓀴make the better friends; but we’ll make the most of what we have. Ye
1618𓀴shall come on board my brig for half an hour, till the ebb sets, and
1619𓀴drink a bowl with me.”
1620𓀴
1621𓀴Now, I longed to see the inside of a ship more than words can tell; but
1622𓀴I was not going to put myself in jeopardy, and I told him my uncle and I
1623𓀴had an appointment with a lawyer.
1624𓀴
1625𓀴“Ay, ay,” said he, “he passed me word of that. But, ye see, the boat’ll
1626𓀴set ye ashore at the town pier, and that’s but a penny stonecast from
1627𓀴Rankeillor’s house.” And here he suddenly leaned down and whispered in
1628𓀴my ear: “Take care of the old tod;[9] he means mischief. Come aboard till
1629𓀴I can get a word with ye.” And then, passing his arm through mine, he
1630𓀴continued aloud, as he set off towards his boat: “But, come, what can I
1631𓀴bring ye from the Carolinas? Any friend of Mr. Balfour’s can command.
1632𓀴A roll of tobacco? Indian feather-work? a skin of a wild beast? a stone
1633𓀴pipe? the mocking-bird that mews for all the world like a cat? the
1634𓀴cardinal bird that is as red as blood?--take your pick and say your
1635𓀴pleasure.”
1636𓀴
1637𓀴     [9] Fox.
1638𓀴
1639𓀴By this time we were at the boat-side, and he was handing me in. I did
1640𓀴not dream of hanging back; I thought (the poor fool!) that I had found
1641𓀴a good friend and helper, and I was rejoiced to see the ship. As soon as
1642𓀴we were all set in our places, the boat was thrust off from the pier
1643𓀴and began to move over the waters: and what with my pleasure in this new
1644𓀴movement and my surprise at our low position, and the appearance of the
1645𓀴shores, and the growing bigness of the brig as we drew near to it, I
1646𓀴could hardly understand what the captain said, and must have answered
1647𓀴him at random.
1648𓀴
1649𓀴As soon as we were alongside (where I sat fairly gaping at the ship’s
1650𓀴height, the strong humming of the tide against its sides, and the
1651𓀴pleasant cries of the seamen at their work) Hoseason, declaring that he
1652𓀴and I must be the first aboard, ordered a tackle to be sent down from
1653𓀴the main-yard. In this I was whipped into the air and set down again on
1654𓀴the deck, where the captain stood ready waiting for me, and instantly
1655𓀴slipped back his arm under mine. There I stood some while, a little
1656𓀴dizzy with the unsteadiness of all around me, perhaps a little afraid,
1657𓀴and yet vastly pleased with these strange sights; the captain meanwhile
1658𓀴pointing out the strangest, and telling me their names and uses.
1659𓀴
1660𓀴“But where is my uncle?” said I suddenly.
1661𓀴
1662𓀴“Ay,” said Hoseason, with a sudden grimness, “that’s the point.”
1663𓀴
1664𓀴I felt I was lost. With all my strength, I plucked myself clear of him
1665𓀴and ran to the bulwarks. Sure enough, there was the boat pulling for the
1666𓀴town, with my uncle sitting in the stern. I gave a piercing cry--“Help,
1667𓀴help! Murder!”--so that both sides of the anchorage rang with it, and
1668𓀴my uncle turned round where he was sitting, and showed me a face full of
1669𓀴cruelty and terror.
1670𓀴
1671𓀴It was the last I saw. Already strong hands had been plucking me back
1672𓀴from the ship’s side; and now a thunderbolt seemed to strike me; I saw a
1673𓀴great flash of fire, and fell senseless.
1674𓀴
1675𓀴
1676𓀴
1677𓀴
1678𓀴CHAPTER VII
1679𓀴
1680𓀴I GO TO SEA IN THE BRIG _COVENANT_ OF DYSART
1681𓀴
1682𓀴
1683𓀴I came to myself in darkness, in great pain, bound hand and foot, and
1684𓀴deafened by many unfamiliar noises. There sounded in my ears a roaring
1685𓀴of water as of a huge mill-dam, the thrashing of heavy sprays, the
1686𓀴thundering of the sails, and the shrill cries of seamen. The whole world
1687𓀴now heaved giddily up, and now rushed giddily downward; and so sick and
1688𓀴hurt was I in body, and my mind so much confounded, that it took me a
1689𓀴long while, chasing my thoughts up and down, and ever stunned again by
1690𓀴a fresh stab of pain, to realise that I must be lying somewhere bound in
1691𓀴the belly of that unlucky ship, and that the wind must have strengthened
1692𓀴to a gale. With the clear perception of my plight, there fell upon me a
1693𓀴blackness of despair, a horror of remorse at my own folly, and a passion
1694𓀴of anger at my uncle, that once more bereft me of my senses.
1695𓀴
1696𓀴When I returned again to life, the same uproar, the same confused and
1697𓀴violent movements, shook and deafened me; and presently, to my other
1698𓀴pains and distresses, there was added the sickness of an unused landsman
1699𓀴on the sea. In that time of my adventurous youth, I suffered many
1700𓀴hardships; but none that was so crushing to my mind and body, or lit by
1701𓀴so few hopes, as these first hours aboard the brig.
1702𓀴
1703𓀴I heard a gun fire, and supposed the storm had proved too strong for us,
1704𓀴and we were firing signals of distress. The thought of deliverance, even
1705𓀴by death in the deep sea, was welcome to me. Yet it was no such matter;
1706𓀴but (as I was afterwards told) a common habit of the captain’s, which
1707𓀴I here set down to show that even the worst man may have his kindlier
1708𓀴side. We were then passing, it appeared, within some miles of Dysart,
1709𓀴where the brig was built, and where old Mrs. Hoseason, the captain’s
1710𓀴mother, had come some years before to live; and whether outward or
1711𓀴inward bound, the _Covenant_ was never suffered to go by that place by
1712𓀴day, without a gun fired and colours shown.
1713𓀴
1714𓀴I had no measure of time; day and night were alike in that ill-smelling
1715𓀴cavern of the ship’s bowels where I lay; and the misery of my situation
1716𓀴drew out the hours to double. How long, therefore, I lay waiting to hear
1717𓀴the ship split upon some rock, or to feel her reel head foremost into
1718𓀴the depths of the sea, I have not the means of computation. But sleep at
1719𓀴length stole from me the consciousness of sorrow.
1720𓀴
1721𓀴I was awakened by the light of a hand-lantern shining in my face. A
1722𓀴small man of about thirty, with green eyes and a tangle of fair hair,
1723𓀴stood looking down at me.
1724𓀴
1725𓀴“Well,” said he, “how goes it?”
1726𓀴
1727𓀴I answered by a sob; and my visitor then felt my pulse and temples, and
1728𓀴set himself to wash and dress the wound upon my scalp.
1729𓀴
1730𓀴“Ay,” said he, “a sore dunt.[10] What, man? Cheer up! The world’s no done;
1731𓀴you’ve made a bad start of it but you’ll make a better. Have you had any
1732𓀴meat?”
1733𓀴
1734𓀴     [10] Stroke.
1735𓀴
1736𓀴I said I could not look at it: and thereupon he gave me some brandy and
1737𓀴water in a tin pannikin, and left me once more to myself.
1738𓀴
1739𓀴The next time he came to see me, I was lying betwixt sleep and waking,
1740𓀴my eyes wide open in the darkness, the sickness quite departed, but
1741𓀴succeeded by a horrid giddiness and swimming that was almost worse
1742𓀴to bear. I ached, besides, in every limb, and the cords that bound me
1743𓀴seemed to be of fire. The smell of the hole in which I lay seemed to
1744𓀴have become a part of me; and during the long interval since his last
1745𓀴visit I had suffered tortures of fear, now from the scurrying of the
1746𓀴ship’s rats, that sometimes pattered on my very face, and now from the
1747𓀴dismal imaginings that haunt the bed of fever.
1748𓀴
1749𓀴The glimmer of the lantern, as a trap opened, shone in like the heaven’s
1750𓀴sunlight; and though it only showed me the strong, dark beams of the
1751𓀴ship that was my prison, I could have cried aloud for gladness. The man
1752𓀴with the green eyes was the first to descend the ladder, and I noticed
1753𓀴that he came somewhat unsteadily. He was followed by the captain.
1754𓀴Neither said a word; but the first set to and examined me, and dressed
1755𓀴my wound as before, while Hoseason looked me in my face with an odd,
1756𓀴black look.
1757𓀴
1758𓀴“Now, sir, you see for yourself,” said the first: “a high fever, no
1759𓀴appetite, no light, no meat: you see for yourself what that means.”
1760𓀴
1761𓀴“I am no conjurer, Mr. Riach,” said the captain.
1762𓀴
1763𓀴“Give me leave, sir,” said Riach; “you’ve a good head upon your
1764𓀴shoulders, and a good Scotch tongue to ask with; but I will leave you no
1765𓀴manner of excuse; I want that boy taken out of this hole and put in the
1766𓀴forecastle.”
1767𓀴
1768𓀴“What ye may want, sir, is a matter of concern to nobody but yoursel’,”
1769𓀴 returned the captain; “but I can tell ye that which is to be. Here he
1770𓀴is; here he shall bide.”
1771𓀴
1772𓀴“Admitting that you have been paid in a proportion,” said the other, “I
1773𓀴will crave leave humbly to say that I have not. Paid I am, and none too
1774𓀴much, to be the second officer of this old tub, and you ken very well if
1775𓀴I do my best to earn it. But I was paid for nothing more.”
1776𓀴
1777𓀴“If ye could hold back your hand from the tin-pan, Mr. Riach, I would
1778𓀴have no complaint to make of ye,” returned the skipper; “and instead
1779𓀴of asking riddles, I make bold to say that ye would keep your breath to
1780𓀴cool your porridge. We’ll be required on deck,” he added, in a sharper
1781𓀴note, and set one foot upon the ladder.
1782𓀴
1783𓀴But Mr. Riach caught him by the sleeve.
1784𓀴
1785𓀴“Admitting that you have been paid to do a murder----” he began.
1786𓀴
1787𓀴Hoseason turned upon him with a flash.
1788𓀴
1789𓀴“What’s that?” he cried. “What kind of talk is that?”
1790𓀴
1791𓀴“It seems it is the talk that you can understand,” said Mr. Riach,
1792𓀴looking him steadily in the face.
1793𓀴
1794𓀴“Mr. Riach, I have sailed with ye three cruises,” replied the captain.
1795𓀴“In all that time, sir, ye should have learned to know me: I’m a stiff
1796𓀴man, and a dour man; but for what ye say the now--fie, fie!--it comes
1797𓀴from a bad heart and a black conscience. If ye say the lad will die----”
1798𓀴
1799𓀴“Ay, will he!” said Mr. Riach.
1800𓀴
1801𓀴“Well, sir, is not that enough?” said Hoseason. “Flit him where ye
1802𓀴please!”
1803𓀴
1804𓀴Thereupon the captain ascended the ladder; and I, who had lain silent
1805𓀴throughout this strange conversation, beheld Mr. Riach turn after him
1806𓀴and bow as low as to his knees in what was plainly a spirit of derision.
1807𓀴Even in my then state of sickness, I perceived two things: that the
1808𓀴mate was touched with liquor, as the captain hinted, and that (drunk or
1809𓀴sober) he was like to prove a valuable friend.
1810𓀴
1811𓀴Five minutes afterwards my bonds were cut, I was hoisted on a man’s
1812𓀴back, carried up to the forecastle, and laid in a bunk on some
1813𓀴sea-blankets; where the first thing that I did was to lose my senses.
1814𓀴
1815𓀴It was a blessed thing indeed to open my eyes again upon the daylight,
1816𓀴and to find myself in the society of men. The forecastle was a roomy
1817𓀴place enough, set all about with berths, in which the men of the watch
1818𓀴below were seated smoking, or lying down asleep. The day being calm and
1819𓀴the wind fair, the scuttle was open, and not only the good daylight, but
1820𓀴from time to time (as the ship rolled) a dusty beam of sunlight shone
1821𓀴in, and dazzled and delighted me. I had no sooner moved, moreover, than
1822𓀴one of the men brought me a drink of something healing which Mr. Riach
1823𓀴had prepared, and bade me lie still and I should soon be well again.
1824𓀴There were no bones broken, he explained: “A clour[11] on the head was
1825𓀴naething. Man,” said he, “it was me that gave it ye!”
1826𓀴
1827𓀴     [11] Blow.
1828𓀴
1829𓀴Here I lay for the space of many days a close prisoner, and not only got
1830𓀴my health again, but came to know my companions. They were a rough lot
1831𓀴indeed, as sailors mostly are: being men rooted out of all the kindly
1832𓀴parts of life, and condemned to toss together on the rough seas, with
1833𓀴masters no less cruel. There were some among them that had sailed with
1834𓀴the pirates and seen things it would be a shame even to speak of; some
1835𓀴were men that had run from the king’s ships, and went with a halter
1836𓀴round their necks, of which they made no secret; and all, as the saying
1837𓀴goes, were “at a word and a blow” with their best friends. Yet I had
1838𓀴not been many days shut up with them before I began to be ashamed of my
1839𓀴first judgment, when I had drawn away from them at the Ferry pier, as
1840𓀴though they had been unclean beasts. No class of man is altogether bad,
1841𓀴but each has its own faults and virtues; and these shipmates of mine
1842𓀴were no exception to the rule. Rough they were, sure enough; and bad, I
1843𓀴suppose; but they had many virtues. They were kind when it occurred to
1844𓀴them, simple even beyond the simplicity of a country lad like me, and
1845𓀴had some glimmerings of honesty.
1846𓀴
1847𓀴There was one man, of maybe forty, that would sit on my berthside for
1848𓀴hours and tell me of his wife and child. He was a fisher that had lost
1849𓀴his boat, and thus been driven to the deep-sea voyaging. Well, it is
1850𓀴years ago now: but I have never forgotten him. His wife (who was “young
1851𓀴by him,” as he often told me) waited in vain to see her man return; he
1852𓀴would never again make the fire for her in the morning, nor yet keep
1853𓀴the bairn when she was sick. Indeed, many of these poor fellows (as the
1854𓀴event proved) were upon their last cruise; the deep seas and cannibal
1855𓀴fish received them; and it is a thankless business to speak ill of the
1856𓀴dead.
1857𓀴
1858𓀴Among other good deeds that they did, they returned my money, which had
1859𓀴been shared among them; and though it was about a third short, I was
1860𓀴very glad to get it, and hoped great good from it in the land I was
1861𓀴going to. The ship was bound for the Carolinas; and you must not suppose
1862𓀴that I was going to that place merely as an exile. The trade was even
1863𓀴then much depressed; since that, and with the rebellion of the colonies
1864𓀴and the formation of the United States, it has, of course, come to
1865𓀴an end; but in those days of my youth, white men were still sold into
1866𓀴slavery on the plantations, and that was the destiny to which my wicked
1867𓀴uncle had condemned me.
1868𓀴
1869𓀴The cabin-boy Ransome (from whom I had first heard of these atrocities)
1870𓀴came in at times from the round-house, where he berthed and served, now
1871𓀴nursing a bruised limb in silent agony, now raving against the cruelty
1872𓀴of Mr. Shuan. It made my heart bleed; but the men had a great respect
1873𓀴for the chief mate, who was, as they said, “the only seaman of the whole
1874𓀴jing-bang, and none such a bad man when he was sober.” Indeed, I found
1875𓀴there was a strange peculiarity about our two mates: that Mr. Riach was
1876𓀴sullen, unkind, and harsh when he was sober, and Mr. Shuan would not
1877𓀴hurt a fly except when he was drinking. I asked about the captain; but I
1878𓀴was told drink made no difference upon that man of iron.
1879𓀴
1880𓀴I did my best in the small time allowed me to make some thing like a
1881𓀴man, or rather I should say something like a boy, of the poor creature,
1882𓀴Ransome. But his mind was scarce truly human. He could remember nothing
1883𓀴of the time before he came to sea; only that his father had made clocks,
1884𓀴and had a starling in the parlour, which could whistle “The North
1885𓀴Countrie;” all else had been blotted out in these years of hardship
1886𓀴and cruelties. He had a strange notion of the dry land, picked up from
1887𓀴sailor’s stories: that it was a place where lads were put to some kind
1888𓀴of slavery called a trade, and where apprentices were continually lashed
1889𓀴and clapped into foul prisons. In a town, he thought every second person
1890𓀴a decoy, and every third house a place in which seamen would be drugged
1891𓀴and murdered. To be sure, I would tell him how kindly I had myself been
1892𓀴used upon that dry land he was so much afraid of, and how well fed and
1893𓀴carefully taught both by my friends and my parents: and if he had been
1894𓀴recently hurt, he would weep bitterly and swear to run away; but if
1895𓀴he was in his usual crackbrain humour, or (still more) if he had had a
1896𓀴glass of spirits in the roundhouse, he would deride the notion.
1897𓀴
1898𓀴It was Mr. Riach (Heaven forgive him!) who gave the boy drink; and
1899𓀴it was, doubtless, kindly meant; but besides that it was ruin to his
1900𓀴health, it was the pitifullest thing in life to see this unhappy,
1901𓀴unfriended creature staggering, and dancing, and talking he knew not
1902𓀴what. Some of the men laughed, but not all; others would grow as black
1903𓀴as thunder (thinking, perhaps, of their own childhood or their own
1904𓀴children) and bid him stop that nonsense, and think what he was doing.
1905𓀴As for me, I felt ashamed to look at him, and the poor child still comes
1906𓀴about me in my dreams.
1907𓀴
1908𓀴All this time, you should know, the _Covenant_ was meeting continual
1909𓀴head-winds and tumbling up and down against head-seas, so that the
1910𓀴scuttle was almost constantly shut, and the forecastle lighted only by a
1911𓀴swinging lantern on a beam. There was constant labour for all hands; the
1912𓀴sails had to be made and shortened every hour; the strain told on the
1913𓀴men’s temper; there was a growl of quarrelling all day long from berth
1914𓀴to berth; and as I was never allowed to set my foot on deck, you
1915𓀴can picture to yourselves how weary of my life I grew to be, and how
1916𓀴impatient for a change.
1917𓀴
1918𓀴And a change I was to get, as you shall hear; but I must first tell of
1919𓀴a conversation I had with Mr. Riach, which put a little heart in me to
1920𓀴bear my troubles. Getting him in a favourable stage of drink (for indeed
1921𓀴he never looked near me when he was sober), I pledged him to secrecy,
1922𓀴and told him my whole story.
1923𓀴
1924𓀴He declared it was like a ballad; that he would do his best to help
1925𓀴me; that I should have paper, pen, and ink, and write one line to Mr.
1926𓀴Campbell and another to Mr. Rankeillor; and that if I had told the
1927𓀴truth, ten to one he would be able (with their help) to pull me through
1928𓀴and set me in my rights.
1929𓀴
1930𓀴“And in the meantime,” says he, “keep your heart up. You’re not the only
1931𓀴one, I’ll tell you that. There’s many a man hoeing tobacco over-seas
1932𓀴that should be mounting his horse at his own door at home; many and
1933𓀴many! And life is all a variorum, at the best. Look at me: I’m a laird’s
1934𓀴son and more than half a doctor, and here I am, man-Jack to Hoseason!”
1935𓀴
1936𓀴I thought it would be civil to ask him for his story.
1937𓀴
1938𓀴He whistled loud.
1939𓀴
1940𓀴“Never had one,” said he. “I like fun, that’s all.” And he skipped out
1941𓀴of the forecastle.
1942𓀴
1943𓀴
1944𓀴
1945𓀴
1946𓀴CHAPTER VIII
1947𓀴
1948𓀴THE ROUND-HOUSE
1949𓀴
1950𓀴
1951𓀴One night, about eleven o’clock, a man of Mr. Riach’s watch (which was
1952𓀴on deck) came below for his jacket; and instantly there began to go
1953𓀴a whisper about the forecastle that “Shuan had done for him at last.”
1954𓀴 There was no need of a name; we all knew who was meant; but we had
1955𓀴scarce time to get the idea rightly in our heads, far less to speak of
1956𓀴it, when the scuttle was again flung open, and Captain Hoseason came
1957𓀴down the ladder. He looked sharply round the bunks in the tossing light
1958𓀴of the lantern; and then, walking straight up to me, he addressed me, to
1959𓀴my surprise, in tones of kindness.
1960𓀴
1961𓀴“My man,” said he, “we want ye to serve in the round-house. You and
1962𓀴Ransome are to change berths. Run away aft with ye.”
1963𓀴
1964𓀴Even as he spoke, two seamen appeared in the scuttle, carrying Ransome
1965𓀴in their arms; and the ship at that moment giving a great sheer into the
1966𓀴sea, and the lantern swinging, the light fell direct on the boy’s face.
1967𓀴It was as white as wax, and had a look upon it like a dreadful smile.
1968𓀴The blood in me ran cold, and I drew in my breath as if I had been
1969𓀴struck.
1970𓀴
1971𓀴“Run away aft; run away aft with ye!” cried Hoseason.
1972𓀴
1973𓀴And at that I brushed by the sailors and the boy (who neither spoke nor
1974𓀴moved), and ran up the ladder on deck.
1975𓀴
1976𓀴The brig was sheering swiftly and giddily through a long, cresting
1977𓀴swell. She was on the starboard tack, and on the left hand, under the
1978𓀴arched foot of the foresail, I could see the sunset still quite bright.
1979𓀴This, at such an hour of the night, surprised me greatly; but I was too
1980𓀴ignorant to draw the true conclusion--that we were going north-about
1981𓀴round Scotland, and were now on the high sea between the Orkney and
1982𓀴Shetland Islands, having avoided the dangerous currents of the Pentland
1983𓀴Firth. For my part, who had been so long shut in the dark and knew
1984𓀴nothing of head-winds, I thought we might be half-way or more across the
1985𓀴Atlantic. And indeed (beyond that I wondered a little at the lateness of
1986𓀴the sunset light) I gave no heed to it, and pushed on across the decks,
1987𓀴running between the seas, catching at ropes, and only saved from going
1988𓀴overboard by one of the hands on deck, who had been always kind to me.
1989𓀴
1990𓀴The round-house, for which I was bound, and where I was now to sleep and
1991𓀴serve, stood some six feet above the decks, and considering the size of
1992𓀴the brig, was of good dimensions. Inside were a fixed table and bench,
1993𓀴and two berths, one for the captain and the other for the two mates,
1994𓀴turn and turn about. It was all fitted with lockers from top to bottom,
1995𓀴so as to stow away the officers’ belongings and a part of the ship’s
1996𓀴stores; there was a second store-room underneath, which you entered by a
1997𓀴hatchway in the middle of the deck; indeed, all the best of the meat and
1998𓀴drink and the whole of the powder were collected in this place; and all
1999𓀴the firearms, except the two pieces of brass ordnance, were set in a
2000𓀴rack in the aftermost wall of the round-house. The most of the cutlasses
2001𓀴were in another place.
2002𓀴
2003𓀴A small window with a shutter on each side, and a skylight in the roof,
2004𓀴gave it light by day; and after dark there was a lamp always burning.
2005𓀴It was burning when I entered, not brightly, but enough to show Mr.
2006𓀴Shuan sitting at the table, with the brandy bottle and a tin pannikin
2007𓀴in front of him. He was a tall man, strongly made and very black; and he
2008𓀴stared before him on the table like one stupid.
2009𓀴
2010𓀴He took no notice of my coming in; nor did he move when the captain
2011𓀴followed and leant on the berth beside me, looking darkly at the mate.
2012𓀴I stood in great fear of Hoseason, and had my reasons for it; but
2013𓀴something told me I need not be afraid of him just then; and I whispered
2014𓀴in his ear: “How is he?” He shook his head like one that does not know
2015𓀴and does not wish to think, and his face was very stern.
2016𓀴
2017𓀴Presently Mr. Riach came in. He gave the captain a glance that meant the
2018𓀴boy was dead as plain as speaking, and took his place like the rest
2019𓀴of us; so that we all three stood without a word, staring down at Mr.
2020𓀴Shuan, and Mr. Shuan (on his side) sat without a word, looking hard upon
2021𓀴the table.
2022𓀴
2023𓀴All of a sudden he put out his hand to take the bottle; and at that Mr.
2024𓀴Riach started forward and caught it away from him, rather by surprise
2025𓀴than violence, crying out, with an oath, that there had been too much of
2026𓀴this work altogether, and that a judgment would fall upon the ship.
2027𓀴And as he spoke (the weather sliding-doors standing open) he tossed the
2028𓀴bottle into the sea.
2029𓀴
2030𓀴Mr. Shuan was on his feet in a trice; he still looked dazed, but he
2031𓀴meant murder, ay, and would have done it, for the second time that
2032𓀴night, had not the captain stepped in between him and his victim.
2033𓀴
2034𓀴“Sit down!” roars the captain. “Ye sot and swine, do ye know what ye’ve
2035𓀴done? Ye’ve murdered the boy!”
2036𓀴
2037𓀴Mr. Shuan seemed to understand; for he sat down again, and put up his
2038𓀴hand to his brow.
2039𓀴
2040𓀴“Well,” he said, “he brought me a dirty pannikin!”
2041𓀴
2042𓀴At that word, the captain and I and Mr. Riach all looked at each other
2043𓀴for a second with a kind of frightened look; and then Hoseason walked
2044𓀴up to his chief officer, took him by the shoulder, led him across to his
2045𓀴bunk, and bade him lie down and go to sleep, as you might speak to a bad
2046𓀴child. The murderer cried a little, but he took off his sea-boots and
2047𓀴obeyed.
2048𓀴
2049𓀴“Ah!” cried Mr. Riach, with a dreadful voice, “ye should have interfered
2050𓀴long syne. It’s too late now.”
2051𓀴
2052𓀴“Mr. Riach,” said the captain, “this night’s work must never be kennt
2053𓀴in Dysart. The boy went overboard, sir; that’s what the story is; and I
2054𓀴would give five pounds out of my pocket it was true!” He turned to the
2055𓀴table. “What made ye throw the good bottle away?” he added. “There was
2056𓀴nae sense in that, sir. Here, David, draw me another. They’re in the
2057𓀴bottom locker;” and he tossed me a key. “Ye’ll need a glass yourself,
2058𓀴sir,” he added to Riach. “Yon was an ugly thing to see.”
2059𓀴
2060𓀴So the pair sat down and hob-a-nobbed; and while they did so, the
2061𓀴murderer, who had been lying and whimpering in his berth, raised himself
2062𓀴upon his elbow and looked at them and at me.
2063𓀴
2064𓀴That was the first night of my new duties; and in the course of the next
2065𓀴day I had got well into the run of them. I had to serve at the meals,
2066𓀴which the captain took at regular hours, sitting down with the officer
2067𓀴who was off duty; all the day through I would be running with a dram
2068𓀴to one or other of my three masters; and at night I slept on a blanket
2069𓀴thrown on the deck boards at the aftermost end of the round-house, and
2070𓀴right in the draught of the two doors. It was a hard and a cold bed;
2071𓀴nor was I suffered to sleep without interruption; for some one would be
2072𓀴always coming in from deck to get a dram, and when a fresh watch was
2073𓀴to be set, two and sometimes all three would sit down and brew a bowl
2074𓀴together. How they kept their health, I know not, any more than how I
2075𓀴kept my own.
2076𓀴
2077𓀴And yet in other ways it was an easy service. There was no cloth to lay;
2078𓀴the meals were either of oatmeal porridge or salt junk, except twice a
2079𓀴week, when there was duff: and though I was clumsy enough and (not being
2080𓀴firm on my sealegs) sometimes fell with what I was bringing them, both
2081𓀴Mr. Riach and the captain were singularly patient. I could not but fancy
2082𓀴they were making up lee-way with their consciences, and that they
2083𓀴would scarce have been so good with me if they had not been worse with
2084𓀴Ransome.
2085𓀴
2086𓀴As for Mr. Shuan, the drink or his crime, or the two together, had
2087𓀴certainly troubled his mind. I cannot say I ever saw him in his proper
2088𓀴wits. He never grew used to my being there, stared at me continually
2089𓀴(sometimes, I could have thought, with terror), and more than once drew
2090𓀴back from my hand when I was serving him. I was pretty sure from the
2091𓀴first that he had no clear mind of what he had done, and on my second
2092𓀴day in the round-house I had the proof of it. We were alone, and he had
2093𓀴been staring at me a long time, when all at once, up he got, as pale as
2094𓀴death, and came close up to me, to my great terror. But I had no cause
2095𓀴to be afraid of him.
2096𓀴
2097𓀴“You were not here before?” he asked.
2098𓀴
2099𓀴“No, sir,” said I.”
2100𓀴
2101𓀴“There was another boy?” he asked again; and when I had answered him,
2102𓀴“Ah!” says he, “I thought that,” and went and sat down, without another
2103𓀴word, except to call for brandy.
2104𓀴
2105𓀴You may think it strange, but for all the horror I had, I was still
2106𓀴sorry for him. He was a married man, with a wife in Leith; but whether
2107𓀴or no he had a family, I have now forgotten; I hope not.
2108𓀴
2109𓀴Altogether it was no very hard life for the time it lasted, which (as
2110𓀴you are to hear) was not long. I was as well fed as the best of them;
2111𓀴even their pickles, which were the great dainty, I was allowed my share
2112𓀴of; and had I liked I might have been drunk from morning to night, like
2113𓀴Mr. Shuan. I had company, too, and good company of its sort. Mr. Riach,
2114𓀴who had been to the college, spoke to me like a friend when he was not
2115𓀴sulking, and told me many curious things, and some that were informing;
2116𓀴and even the captain, though he kept me at the stick’s end the most part
2117𓀴of the time, would sometimes unbuckle a bit, and tell me of the fine
2118𓀴countries he had visited.
2119𓀴
2120𓀴The shadow of poor Ransome, to be sure, lay on all four of us, and on
2121𓀴me and Mr. Shuan in particular, most heavily. And then I had another
2122𓀴trouble of my own. Here I was, doing dirty work for three men that I
2123𓀴looked down upon, and one of whom, at least, should have hung upon a
2124𓀴gallows; that was for the present; and as for the future, I could only
2125𓀴see myself slaving alongside of negroes in the tobacco fields. Mr.
2126𓀴Riach, perhaps from caution, would never suffer me to say another word
2127𓀴about my story; the captain, whom I tried to approach, rebuffed me like
2128𓀴a dog and would not hear a word; and as the days came and went, my heart
2129𓀴sank lower and lower, till I was even glad of the work which kept me
2130𓀴from thinking.
2131𓀴
2132𓀴
2133𓀴
2134𓀴
2135𓀴CHAPTER IX
2136𓀴
2137𓀴THE MAN WITH THE BELT OF GOLD
2138𓀴
2139𓀴
2140𓀴More than a week went by, in which the ill-luck that had hitherto
2141𓀴pursued the _Covenant_ upon this voyage grew yet more strongly marked.
2142𓀴Some days she made a little way; others, she was driven actually back.
2143𓀴At last we were beaten so far to the south that we tossed and tacked to
2144𓀴and fro the whole of the ninth day, within sight of Cape Wrath and the
2145𓀴wild, rocky coast on either hand of it. There followed on that a council
2146𓀴of the officers, and some decision which I did not rightly understand,
2147𓀴seeing only the result: that we had made a fair wind of a foul one and
2148𓀴were running south.
2149𓀴
2150𓀴The tenth afternoon there was a falling swell and a thick, wet, white
2151𓀴fog that hid one end of the brig from the other. All afternoon, when
2152𓀴I went on deck, I saw men and officers listening hard over the
2153𓀴bulwarks--“for breakers,” they said; and though I did not so much as
2154𓀴understand the word, I felt danger in the air, and was excited.
2155𓀴
2156𓀴Maybe about ten at night, I was serving Mr. Riach and the captain at
2157𓀴their supper, when the ship struck something with a great sound, and we
2158𓀴heard voices singing out. My two masters leaped to their feet.
2159𓀴
2160𓀴“She’s struck!” said Mr. Riach.
2161𓀴
2162𓀴“No, sir,” said the captain. “We’ve only run a boat down.”
2163𓀴
2164𓀴And they hurried out.
2165𓀴
2166𓀴The captain was in the right of it. We had run down a boat in the fog,
2167𓀴and she had parted in the midst and gone to the bottom with all her crew
2168𓀴but one. This man (as I heard afterwards) had been sitting in the stern
2169𓀴as a passenger, while the rest were on the benches rowing. At the moment
2170𓀴of the blow, the stern had been thrown into the air, and the man (having
2171𓀴his hands free, and for all he was encumbered with a frieze overcoat
2172𓀴that came below his knees) had leaped up and caught hold of the brig’s
2173𓀴bowsprit. It showed he had luck and much agility and unusual strength,
2174𓀴that he should have thus saved himself from such a pass. And yet, when
2175𓀴the captain brought him into the round-house, and I set eyes on him for
2176𓀴the first time, he looked as cool as I did.
2177𓀴
2178𓀴He was smallish in stature, but well set and as nimble as a goat; his
2179𓀴face was of a good open expression, but sunburnt very dark, and heavily
2180𓀴freckled and pitted with the small-pox; his eyes were unusually light
2181𓀴and had a kind of dancing madness in them, that was both engaging and
2182𓀴alarming; and when he took off his great-coat, he laid a pair of fine
2183𓀴silver-mounted pistols on the table, and I saw that he was belted with
2184𓀴a great sword. His manners, besides, were elegant, and he pledged the
2185𓀴captain handsomely. Altogether I thought of him, at the first sight,
2186𓀴that here was a man I would rather call my friend than my enemy.
2187𓀴
2188𓀴The captain, too, was taking his observations, but rather of the man’s
2189𓀴clothes than his person. And to be sure, as soon as he had taken off
2190𓀴the great-coat, he showed forth mighty fine for the round-house of a
2191𓀴merchant brig: having a hat with feathers, a red waistcoat, breeches
2192𓀴of black plush, and a blue coat with silver buttons and handsome silver
2193𓀴lace; costly clothes, though somewhat spoiled with the fog and being
2194𓀴slept in.
2195𓀴
2196𓀴“I’m vexed, sir, about the boat,” says the captain.
2197𓀴
2198𓀴“There are some pretty men gone to the bottom,” said the stranger, “that
2199𓀴I would rather see on the dry land again than half a score of boats.”
2200𓀴
2201𓀴“Friends of yours?” said Hoseason.
2202𓀴
2203𓀴“You have none such friends in your country,” was the reply. “They would
2204𓀴have died for me like dogs.”
2205𓀴
2206𓀴“Well, sir,” said the captain, still watching him, “there are more men
2207𓀴in the world than boats to put them in.”
2208𓀴
2209𓀴“And that’s true, too,” cried the other, “and ye seem to be a gentleman
2210𓀴of great penetration.”
2211𓀴
2212𓀴“I have been in France, sir,” says the captain, so that it was plain he
2213𓀴meant more by the words than showed upon the face of them.
2214𓀴
2215𓀴“Well, sir,” says the other, “and so has many a pretty man, for the
2216𓀴matter of that.”
2217𓀴
2218𓀴“No doubt, sir,” says the captain, “and fine coats.”
2219𓀴
2220𓀴“Oho!” says the stranger, “is that how the wind sets?” And he laid his
2221𓀴hand quickly on his pistols.
2222𓀴
2223𓀴“Don’t be hasty,” said the captain. “Don’t do a mischief before ye
2224𓀴see the need of it. Ye’ve a French soldier’s coat upon your back and a
2225𓀴Scotch tongue in your head, to be sure; but so has many an honest fellow
2226𓀴in these days, and I dare say none the worse of it.”
2227𓀴
2228𓀴“So?” said the gentleman in the fine coat: “are ye of the honest party?”
2229𓀴 (meaning, Was he a Jacobite? for each side, in these sort of civil
2230𓀴broils, takes the name of honesty for its own).
2231𓀴
2232𓀴“Why, sir,” replied the captain, “I am a true-blue Protestant, and I
2233𓀴thank God for it.” (It was the first word of any religion I had ever
2234𓀴heard from him, but I learnt afterwards he was a great church-goer while
2235𓀴on shore.) “But, for all that,” says he, “I can be sorry to see another
2236𓀴man with his back to the wall.”
2237𓀴
2238𓀴“Can ye so, indeed?” asked the Jacobite. “Well, sir, to be quite plain
2239𓀴with ye, I am one of those honest gentlemen that were in trouble about
2240𓀴the years forty-five and six; and (to be still quite plain with ye) if
2241𓀴I got into the hands of any of the red-coated gentry, it’s like it would
2242𓀴go hard with me. Now, sir, I was for France; and there was a French ship
2243𓀴cruising here to pick me up; but she gave us the go-by in the fog--as I
2244𓀴wish from the heart that ye had done yoursel’! And the best that I can
2245𓀴say is this: If ye can set me ashore where I was going, I have that upon
2246𓀴me will reward you highly for your trouble.”
2247𓀴
2248𓀴“In France?” says the captain. “No, sir; that I cannot do. But where ye
2249𓀴come from--we might talk of that.”
2250𓀴
2251𓀴And then, unhappily, he observed me standing in my corner, and packed
2252𓀴me off to the galley to get supper for the gentleman. I lost no time,
2253𓀴I promise you; and when I came back into the round-house, I found the
2254𓀴gentleman had taken a money-belt from about his waist, and poured out
2255𓀴a guinea or two upon the table. The captain was looking at the guineas,
2256𓀴and then at the belt, and then at the gentleman’s face; and I thought he
2257𓀴seemed excited.
2258𓀴
2259𓀴“Half of it,” he cried, “and I’m your man!”
2260𓀴
2261𓀴The other swept back the guineas into the belt, and put it on again
2262𓀴under his waistcoat. “I have told ye sir,” said he, “that not one doit
2263𓀴of it belongs to me. It belongs to my chieftain,” and here he touched
2264𓀴his hat, “and while I would be but a silly messenger to grudge some of
2265𓀴it that the rest might come safe, I should show myself a hound indeed if
2266𓀴I bought my own carcase any too dear. Thirty guineas on the sea-side, or
2267𓀴sixty if ye set me on the Linnhe Loch. Take it, if ye will; if not, ye
2268𓀴can do your worst.”
2269𓀴
2270𓀴“Ay,” said Hoseason. “And if I give ye over to the soldiers?”
2271𓀴
2272𓀴“Ye would make a fool’s bargain,” said the other. “My chief, let me tell
2273𓀴you, sir, is forfeited, like every honest man in Scotland. His estate
2274𓀴is in the hands of the man they call King George; and it is his officers
2275𓀴that collect the rents, or try to collect them. But for the honour of
2276𓀴Scotland, the poor tenant bodies take a thought upon their chief lying
2277𓀴in exile; and this money is a part of that very rent for which King
2278𓀴George is looking. Now, sir, ye seem to me to be a man that understands
2279𓀴things: bring this money within the reach of Government, and how much of
2280𓀴it’ll come to you?”
2281𓀴
2282𓀴“Little enough, to be sure,” said Hoseason; and then, “if they knew,” he
2283𓀴added, drily. “But I think, if I was to try, that I could hold my tongue
2284𓀴about it.”
2285𓀴
2286𓀴“Ah, but I’ll begowk[12] ye there!” cried the gentleman. “Play me false,
2287𓀴and I’ll play you cunning. If a hand is laid upon me, they shall ken
2288𓀴what money it is.”
2289𓀴
2290𓀴     [12] Befool.
2291𓀴
2292𓀴“Well,” returned the captain, “what must be must. Sixty guineas, and
2293𓀴done. Here’s my hand upon it.”
2294𓀴
2295𓀴“And here’s mine,” said the other.
2296𓀴
2297𓀴And thereupon the captain went out (rather hurriedly, I thought), and
2298𓀴left me alone in the round-house with the stranger.
2299𓀴
2300𓀴At that period (so soon after the forty-five) there were many exiled
2301𓀴gentlemen coming back at the peril of their lives, either to see their
2302𓀴friends or to collect a little money; and as for the Highland chiefs
2303𓀴that had been forfeited, it was a common matter of talk how their
2304𓀴tenants would stint themselves to send them money, and their clansmen
2305𓀴outface the soldiery to get it in, and run the gauntlet of our great
2306𓀴navy to carry it across. All this I had, of course, heard tell of; and
2307𓀴now I had a man under my eyes whose life was forfeit on all these counts
2308𓀴and upon one more, for he was not only a rebel and a smuggler of rents,
2309𓀴but had taken service with King Louis of France. And as if all this
2310𓀴were not enough, he had a belt full of golden guineas round his loins.
2311𓀴Whatever my opinions, I could not look on such a man without a lively
2312𓀴interest.
2313𓀴
2314𓀴“And so you’re a Jacobite?” said I, as I set meat before him.
2315𓀴
2316𓀴“Ay,” said he, beginning to eat. “And you, by your long face, should be
2317𓀴a Whig?”[13]
2318𓀴
2319𓀴     [13] Whig or Whigamore was the cant name for those who were
2320𓀴     loyal to King George.
2321𓀴
2322𓀴“Betwixt and between,” said I, not to annoy him; for indeed I was as
2323𓀴good a Whig as Mr. Campbell could make me.
2324𓀴
2325𓀴“And that’s naething,” said he. “But I’m saying, Mr.
2326𓀴Betwixt-and-Between,” he added, “this bottle of yours is dry; and it’s
2327𓀴hard if I’m to pay sixty guineas and be grudged a dram upon the back of
2328𓀴it.”
2329𓀴
2330𓀴“I’ll go and ask for the key,” said I, and stepped on deck.
2331𓀴
2332𓀴The fog was as close as ever, but the swell almost down. They had laid
2333𓀴the brig to, not knowing precisely where they were, and the wind (what
2334𓀴little there was of it) not serving well for their true course. Some of
2335𓀴the hands were still hearkening for breakers; but the captain and the
2336𓀴two officers were in the waist with their heads together. It struck me
2337𓀴(I don’t know why) that they were after no good; and the first word I
2338𓀴heard, as I drew softly near, more than confirmed me.
2339𓀴
2340𓀴It was Mr. Riach, crying out as if upon a sudden thought: “Couldn’t we
2341𓀴wile him out of the round-house?”
2342𓀴
2343𓀴“He’s better where he is,” returned Hoseason; “he hasn’t room to use his
2344𓀴sword.”
2345𓀴
2346𓀴“Well, that’s true,” said Riach; “but he’s hard to come at.”
2347𓀴
2348𓀴“Hut!” said Hoseason. “We can get the man in talk, one upon each side,
2349𓀴and pin him by the two arms; or if that’ll not hold, sir, we can make a
2350𓀴run by both the doors and get him under hand before he has the time to
2351𓀴draw.”
2352𓀴
2353𓀴At this hearing, I was seized with both fear and anger at these
2354𓀴treacherous, greedy, bloody men that I sailed with. My first mind was to
2355𓀴run away; my second was bolder.
2356𓀴
2357𓀴“Captain,” said I, “the gentleman is seeking a dram, and the bottle’s
2358𓀴out. Will you give me the key?”
2359𓀴
2360𓀴They all started and turned about.
2361𓀴
2362𓀴“Why, here’s our chance to get the firearms!”
2363𓀴
2364𓀴Riach cried; and then to me: “Hark ye, David,” he said, “do ye ken where
2365𓀴the pistols are?”
2366𓀴
2367𓀴“Ay, ay,” put in Hoseason. “David kens; David’s a good lad. Ye see,
2368𓀴David my man, yon wild Hielandman is a danger to the ship, besides being
2369𓀴a rank foe to King George, God bless him!”
2370𓀴
2371𓀴I had never been so be-Davided since I came on board: but I said Yes, as
2372𓀴if all I heard were quite natural.
2373𓀴
2374𓀴“The trouble is,” resumed the captain, “that all our firelocks, great
2375𓀴and little, are in the round-house under this man’s nose; likewise the
2376𓀴powder. Now, if I, or one of the officers, was to go in and take them,
2377𓀴he would fall to thinking. But a lad like you, David, might snap up a
2378𓀴horn and a pistol or two without remark. And if ye can do it cleverly,
2379𓀴I’ll bear it in mind when it’ll be good for you to have friends; and
2380𓀴that’s when we come to Carolina.”
2381𓀴
2382𓀴Here Mr. Riach whispered him a little.
2383𓀴
2384𓀴“Very right, sir,” said the captain; and then to myself: “And see here,
2385𓀴David, yon man has a beltful of gold, and I give you my word that you
2386𓀴shall have your fingers in it.”
2387𓀴
2388𓀴I told him I would do as he wished, though indeed I had scarce breath to
2389𓀴speak with; and upon that he gave me the key of the spirit locker, and I
2390𓀴began to go slowly back to the round-house. What was I to do? They
2391𓀴were dogs and thieves; they had stolen me from my own country; they had
2392𓀴killed poor Ransome; and was I to hold the candle to another murder? But
2393𓀴then, upon the other hand, there was the fear of death very plain before
2394𓀴me; for what could a boy and a man, if they were as brave as lions,
2395𓀴against a whole ship’s company?
2396𓀴
2397𓀴I was still arguing it back and forth, and getting no great clearness,
2398𓀴when I came into the round-house and saw the Jacobite eating his supper
2399𓀴under the lamp; and at that my mind was made up all in a moment. I have
2400𓀴no credit by it; it was by no choice of mine, but as if by compulsion,
2401𓀴that I walked right up to the table and put my hand on his shoulder.
2402𓀴
2403𓀴“Do ye want to be killed?” said I. He sprang to his feet, and looked a
2404𓀴question at me as clear as if he had spoken.
2405𓀴
2406𓀴“O!” cried I, “they’re all murderers here; it’s a ship full of them!
2407𓀴They’ve murdered a boy already. Now it’s you.”
2408𓀴
2409𓀴“Ay, ay,” said he; “but they have n’t got me yet.” And then looking at me
2410𓀴curiously, “Will ye stand with me?”
2411𓀴
2412𓀴“That will I!” said I. “I am no thief, nor yet murderer. I’ll stand by
2413𓀴you.”
2414𓀴
2415𓀴“Why, then,” said he, “what’s your name?”
2416𓀴
2417𓀴“David Balfour,” said I; and then, thinking that a man with so fine a
2418𓀴coat must like fine people, I added for the first time, “of Shaws.”
2419𓀴
2420𓀴It never occurred to him to doubt me, for a Highlander is used to see
2421𓀴great gentlefolk in great poverty; but as he had no estate of his own,
2422𓀴my words nettled a very childish vanity he had.
2423𓀴
2424𓀴“My name is Stewart,” he said, drawing himself up. “Alan Breck, they
2425𓀴call me. A king’s name is good enough for me, though I bear it plain and
2426𓀴have the name of no farm-midden to clap to the hind-end of it.”
2427𓀴
2428𓀴And having administered this rebuke, as though it were something of a
2429𓀴chief importance, he turned to examine our defences.
2430𓀴
2431𓀴The round-house was built very strong, to support the breaching of the
2432𓀴seas. Of its five apertures, only the skylight and the two doors were
2433𓀴large enough for the passage of a man. The doors, besides, could be
2434𓀴drawn close: they were of stout oak, and ran in grooves, and were fitted
2435𓀴with hooks to keep them either shut or open, as the need arose. The
2436𓀴one that was already shut I secured in this fashion; but when I was
2437𓀴proceeding to slide to the other, Alan stopped me.
2438𓀴
2439𓀴“David,” said he--“for I cannae bring to mind the name of your landed
2440𓀴estate, and so will make so bold as to call you David--that door, being
2441𓀴open, is the best part of my defences.”
2442𓀴
2443𓀴“It would be yet better shut,” says I.
2444𓀴
2445𓀴“Not so, David,” says he. “Ye see, I have but one face; but so long as
2446𓀴that door is open and my face to it, the best part of my enemies will be
2447𓀴in front of me, where I would aye wish to find them.”
2448𓀴
2449𓀴Then he gave me from the rack a cutlass (of which there were a few
2450𓀴besides the firearms), choosing it with great care, shaking his head and
2451𓀴saying he had never in all his life seen poorer weapons; and next he set
2452𓀴me down to the table with a powder-horn, a bag of bullets and all the
2453𓀴pistols, which he bade me charge.
2454𓀴
2455𓀴“And that will be better work, let me tell you,” said he, “for a
2456𓀴gentleman of decent birth, than scraping plates and raxing[14] drams to a
2457𓀴wheen tarry sailors.”
2458𓀴
2459𓀴     [14] Reaching.
2460𓀴
2461𓀴Thereupon he stood up in the midst with his face to the door, and
2462𓀴drawing his great sword, made trial of the room he had to wield it in.
2463𓀴
2464𓀴“I must stick to the point,” he said, shaking his head; “and that’s a
2465𓀴pity, too. It doesn’t set my genius, which is all for the upper guard.
2466𓀴And, now,” said he, “do you keep on charging the pistols, and give heed
2467𓀴to me.”
2468𓀴
2469𓀴I told him I would listen closely. My chest was tight, my mouth dry, the
2470𓀴light dark to my eyes; the thought of the numbers that were soon to
2471𓀴leap in upon us kept my heart in a flutter: and the sea, which I heard
2472𓀴washing round the brig, and where I thought my dead body would be cast
2473𓀴ere morning, ran in my mind strangely.
2474𓀴
2475𓀴“First of all,” said he, “how many are against us?”
2476𓀴
2477𓀴I reckoned them up; and such was the hurry of my mind, I had to cast the
2478𓀴numbers twice. “Fifteen,” said I.
2479𓀴
2480𓀴Alan whistled. “Well,” said he, “that can’t be cured. And now follow me.
2481𓀴It is my part to keep this door, where I look for the main battle. In
2482𓀴that, ye have no hand. And mind and dinnae fire to this side unless they
2483𓀴get me down; for I would rather have ten foes in front of me than one
2484𓀴friend like you cracking pistols at my back.”
2485𓀴
2486𓀴I told him, indeed I was no great shot.
2487𓀴
2488𓀴“And that’s very bravely said,” he cried, in a great admiration of my
2489𓀴candour. “There’s many a pretty gentleman that wouldnae dare to say it.”
2490𓀴
2491𓀴“But then, sir,” said I, “there is the door behind you, which they may
2492𓀴perhaps break in.”
2493𓀴
2494𓀴“Ay,” said he, “and that is a part of your work. No sooner the pistols
2495𓀴charged, than ye must climb up into yon bed where ye’re handy at the
2496𓀴window; and if they lift hand against the door, ye’re to shoot. But
2497𓀴that’s not all. Let’s make a bit of a soldier of ye, David. What else
2498𓀴have ye to guard?”
2499𓀴
2500𓀴“There’s the skylight,” said I. “But indeed, Mr. Stewart, I would need
2501𓀴to have eyes upon both sides to keep the two of them; for when my face
2502𓀴is at the one, my back is to the other.”
2503𓀴
2504𓀴“And that’s very true,” said Alan. “But have ye no ears to your head?”
2505𓀴
2506𓀴“To be sure!” cried I. “I must hear the bursting of the glass!”
2507𓀴
2508𓀴“Ye have some rudiments of sense,” said Alan, grimly.
2509𓀴
2510𓀴
2511𓀴
2512𓀴
2513𓀴CHAPTER X
2514𓀴
2515𓀴THE SIEGE OF THE ROUND-HOUSE
2516𓀴
2517𓀴
2518𓀴But now our time of truce was come to an end. Those on deck had waited
2519𓀴for my coming till they grew impatient; and scarce had Alan spoken, when
2520𓀴the captain showed face in the open door.
2521𓀴
2522𓀴“Stand!” cried Alan, and pointed his sword at him. The captain stood,
2523𓀴indeed; but he neither winced nor drew back a foot.
2524𓀴
2525𓀴“A naked sword?” says he. “This is a strange return for hospitality.”
2526𓀴
2527𓀴“Do ye see me?” said Alan. “I am come of kings; I bear a king’s name. My
2528𓀴badge is the oak. Do ye see my sword? It has slashed the heads off mair
2529𓀴Whigamores than you have toes upon your feet. Call up your vermin to
2530𓀴your back, sir, and fall on! The sooner the clash begins, the sooner
2531𓀴ye’ll taste this steel throughout your vitals.”
2532𓀴
2533𓀴The captain said nothing to Alan, but he looked over at me with an ugly
2534𓀴look. “David,” said he, “I’ll mind this;” and the sound of his voice
2535𓀴went through me with a jar.
2536𓀴
2537𓀴Next moment he was gone.
2538𓀴
2539𓀴“And now,” said Alan, “let your hand keep your head, for the grip is
2540𓀴coming.”
2541𓀴
2542𓀴Alan drew a dirk, which he held in his left hand in case they should run
2543𓀴in under his sword. I, on my part, clambered up into the berth with
2544𓀴an armful of pistols and something of a heavy heart, and set open the
2545𓀴window where I was to watch. It was a small part of the deck that I
2546𓀴could overlook, but enough for our purpose. The sea had gone down, and
2547𓀴the wind was steady and kept the sails quiet; so that there was a
2548𓀴great stillness in the ship, in which I made sure I heard the sound of
2549𓀴muttering voices. A little after, and there came a clash of steel upon
2550𓀴the deck, by which I knew they were dealing out the cutlasses and one
2551𓀴had been let fall; and after that, silence again.
2552𓀴
2553𓀴I do not know if I was what you call afraid; but my heart beat like a
2554𓀴bird’s, both quick and little; and there was a dimness came before my
2555𓀴eyes which I continually rubbed away, and which continually returned. As
2556𓀴for hope, I had none; but only a darkness of despair and a sort of anger
2557𓀴against all the world that made me long to sell my life as dear as I was
2558𓀴able. I tried to pray, I remember, but that same hurry of my mind, like
2559𓀴a man running, would not suffer me to think upon the words; and my chief
2560𓀴wish was to have the thing begin and be done with it.
2561𓀴
2562𓀴It came all of a sudden when it did, with a rush of feet and a roar, and
2563𓀴then a shout from Alan, and a sound of blows and some one crying out
2564𓀴as if hurt. I looked back over my shoulder, and saw Mr. Shuan in the
2565𓀴doorway, crossing blades with Alan.
2566𓀴
2567𓀴“That’s him that killed the boy!” I cried.
2568𓀴
2569𓀴“Look to your window!” said Alan; and as I turned back to my place, I
2570𓀴saw him pass his sword through the mate’s body.
2571𓀴
2572𓀴It was none too soon for me to look to my own part; for my head was
2573𓀴scarce back at the window, before five men, carrying a spare yard for
2574𓀴a battering-ram, ran past me and took post to drive the door in. I had
2575𓀴never fired with a pistol in my life, and not often with a gun; far less
2576𓀴against a fellow-creature. But it was now or never; and just as they
2577𓀴swang the yard, I cried out: “Take that!” and shot into their midst.
2578𓀴
2579𓀴I must have hit one of them, for he sang out and gave back a step, and
2580𓀴the rest stopped as if a little disconcerted. Before they had time to
2581𓀴recover, I sent another ball over their heads; and at my third shot
2582𓀴(which went as wide as the second) the whole party threw down the yard
2583𓀴and ran for it.
2584𓀴
2585𓀴Then I looked round again into the deck-house. The whole place was full
2586𓀴of the smoke of my own firing, just as my ears seemed to be burst with
2587𓀴the noise of the shots. But there was Alan, standing as before; only
2588𓀴now his sword was running blood to the hilt, and himself so swelled
2589𓀴with triumph and fallen into so fine an attitude, that he looked to be
2590𓀴invincible. Right before him on the floor was Mr. Shuan, on his hands
2591𓀴and knees; the blood was pouring from his mouth, and he was sinking
2592𓀴slowly lower, with a terrible, white face; and just as I looked, some of
2593𓀴those from behind caught hold of him by the heels and dragged him bodily
2594𓀴out of the round-house. I believe he died as they were doing it.
2595𓀴
2596𓀴“There’s one of your Whigs for ye!” cried Alan; and then turning to me,
2597𓀴he asked if I had done much execution.
2598𓀴
2599𓀴I told him I had winged one, and thought it was the captain.
2600𓀴
2601𓀴“And I’ve settled two,” says he. “No, there’s not enough blood let;
2602𓀴they’ll be back again. To your watch, David. This was but a dram before
2603𓀴meat.”
2604𓀴
2605𓀴I settled back to my place, re-charging the three pistols I had fired,
2606𓀴and keeping watch with both eye and ear.
2607𓀴
2608𓀴Our enemies were disputing not far off upon the deck, and that so loudly
2609𓀴that I could hear a word or two above the washing of the seas.
2610𓀴
2611𓀴“It was Shuan bauchled[15] it,” I heard one say.
2612𓀴
2613𓀴     [15] Bungled.
2614𓀴
2615𓀴And another answered him with a “Wheesht, man! He’s paid the piper.”
2616𓀴
2617𓀴After that the voices fell again into the same muttering as before. Only
2618𓀴now, one person spoke most of the time, as though laying down a plan,
2619𓀴and first one and then another answered him briefly, like men taking
2620𓀴orders. By this, I made sure they were coming on again, and told Alan.
2621𓀴
2622𓀴“It’s what we have to pray for,” said he. “Unless we can give them a
2623𓀴good distaste of us, and done with it, there’ll be nae sleep for either
2624𓀴you or me. But this time, mind, they’ll be in earnest.”
2625𓀴
2626𓀴By this, my pistols were ready, and there was nothing to do but listen
2627𓀴and wait. While the brush lasted, I had not the time to think if I was
2628𓀴frighted; but now, when all was still again, my mind ran upon nothing
2629𓀴else. The thought of the sharp swords and the cold steel was strong in
2630𓀴me; and presently, when I began to hear stealthy steps and a brushing
2631𓀴of men’s clothes against the round-house wall, and knew they were taking
2632𓀴their places in the dark, I could have found it in my mind to cry out
2633𓀴aloud.
2634𓀴
2635𓀴All this was upon Alan’s side; and I had begun to think my share of the
2636𓀴fight was at an end, when I heard some one drop softly on the roof above
2637𓀴me.
2638𓀴
2639𓀴Then there came a single call on the sea-pipe, and that was the signal.
2640𓀴A knot of them made one rush of it, cutlass in hand, against the door;
2641𓀴and at the same moment, the glass of the skylight was dashed in a
2642𓀴thousand pieces, and a man leaped through and landed on the floor.
2643𓀴Before he got his feet, I had clapped a pistol to his back, and might
2644𓀴have shot him, too; only at the touch of him (and him alive) my whole
2645𓀴flesh misgave me, and I could no more pull the trigger than I could have
2646𓀴flown.
2647𓀴
2648𓀴He had dropped his cutlass as he jumped, and when he felt the pistol,
2649𓀴whipped straight round and laid hold of me, roaring out an oath; and at
2650𓀴that either my courage came again, or I grew so much afraid as came to
2651𓀴the same thing; for I gave a shriek and shot him in the midst of the
2652𓀴body. He gave the most horrible, ugly groan and fell to the floor. The
2653𓀴foot of a second fellow, whose legs were dangling through the skylight,
2654𓀴struck me at the same time upon the head; and at that I snatched another
2655𓀴pistol and shot this one through the thigh, so that he slipped through
2656𓀴and tumbled in a lump on his companion’s body. There was no talk of
2657𓀴missing, any more than there was time to aim; I clapped the muzzle to
2658𓀴the very place and fired.
2659𓀴
2660𓀴I might have stood and stared at them for long, but I heard Alan shout
2661𓀴as if for help, and that brought me to my senses.
2662𓀴
2663𓀴He had kept the door so long; but one of the seamen, while he was
2664𓀴engaged with others, had run in under his guard and caught him about the
2665𓀴body. Alan was dirking him with his left hand, but the fellow clung like
2666𓀴a leech. Another had broken in and had his cutlass raised. The door was
2667𓀴thronged with their faces. I thought we were lost, and catching up my
2668𓀴cutlass, fell on them in flank.
2669𓀴
2670𓀴But I had not time to be of help. The wrestler dropped at last; and
2671𓀴Alan, leaping back to get his distance, ran upon the others like a
2672𓀴bull, roaring as he went. They broke before him like water, turning, and
2673𓀴running, and falling one against another in their haste. The sword
2674𓀴in his hands flashed like quicksilver into the huddle of our fleeing
2675𓀴enemies; and at every flash there came the scream of a man hurt. I was
2676𓀴still thinking we were lost, when lo! they were all gone, and Alan was
2677𓀴driving them along the deck as a sheep-dog chases sheep.
2678𓀴
2679𓀴Yet he was no sooner out than he was back again, being as cautious as he
2680𓀴was brave; and meanwhile the seamen continued running and crying out as
2681𓀴if he was still behind them; and we heard them tumble one upon another
2682𓀴into the forecastle, and clap-to the hatch upon the top.
2683𓀴
2684𓀴The round-house was like a shambles; three were dead inside, another
2685𓀴lay in his death agony across the threshold; and there were Alan and I
2686𓀴victorious and unhurt.
2687𓀴
2688𓀴He came up to me with open arms. “Come to my arms!” he cried, and
2689𓀴embraced and kissed me hard upon both cheeks. “David,” said he, “I love
2690𓀴you like a brother. And O, man,” he cried in a kind of ecstasy, “am I no
2691𓀴a bonny fighter?”
2692𓀴
2693𓀴Thereupon he turned to the four enemies, passed his sword clean through
2694𓀴each of them, and tumbled them out of doors one after the other. As he
2695𓀴did so, he kept humming and singing and whistling to himself, like a man
2696𓀴trying to recall an air; only what _he_ was trying was to make one. All
2697𓀴the while, the flush was in his face, and his eyes were as bright as a
2698𓀴five-year-old child’s with a new toy. And presently he sat down upon the
2699𓀴table, sword in hand; the air that he was making all the time began to
2700𓀴run a little clearer, and then clearer still; and then out he burst with
2701𓀴a great voice into a Gaelic song.
2702𓀴
2703𓀴I have translated it here, not in verse (of which I have no skill) but
2704𓀴at least in the king’s English.
2705𓀴
2706𓀴He sang it often afterwards, and the thing became popular; so that I
2707𓀴have heard it and had it explained to me, many’s the time.
2708𓀴
2709𓀴    “This is the song of the sword of Alan;
2710𓀴    The smith made it,
2711𓀴    The fire set it;
2712𓀴    Now it shines in the hand of Alan Breck.
2713𓀴
2714𓀴    “Their eyes were many and bright,
2715𓀴    Swift were they to behold,
2716𓀴    Many the hands they guided:
2717𓀴    The sword was alone.
2718𓀴
2719𓀴    “The dun deer troop over the hill,
2720𓀴    They are many, the hill is one;
2721𓀴    The dun deer vanish,
2722𓀴    The hill remains.
2723𓀴
2724𓀴    “Come to me from the hills of heather,
2725𓀴    Come from the isles of the sea.
2726𓀴    O far-beholding eagles,
2727𓀴    Here is your meat.”
2728𓀴
2729𓀴Now this song which he made (both words and music) in the hour of our
2730𓀴victory, is something less than just to me, who stood beside him in
2731𓀴the tussle. Mr. Shuan and five more were either killed outright or
2732𓀴thoroughly disabled; but of these, two fell by my hand, the two that
2733𓀴came by the skylight. Four more were hurt, and of that number, one (and
2734𓀴he not the least important) got his hurt from me. So that, altogether,
2735𓀴I did my fair share both of the killing and the wounding, and might have
2736𓀴claimed a place in Alan’s verses. But poets have to think upon their
2737𓀴rhymes; and in good prose talk, Alan always did me more than justice.
2738𓀴
2739𓀴In the meanwhile, I was innocent of any wrong being done me. For not
2740𓀴only I knew no word of the Gaelic; but what with the long suspense of
2741𓀴the waiting, and the scurry and strain of our two spirts of fighting,
2742𓀴and more than all, the horror I had of some of my own share in it, the
2743𓀴thing was no sooner over than I was glad to stagger to a seat. There was
2744𓀴that tightness on my chest that I could hardly breathe; the thought
2745𓀴of the two men I had shot sat upon me like a nightmare; and all upon a
2746𓀴sudden, and before I had a guess of what was coming, I began to sob and
2747𓀴cry like any child.
2748𓀴
2749𓀴Alan clapped my shoulder, and said I was a brave lad and wanted nothing
2750𓀴but a sleep.
2751𓀴
2752𓀴“I’ll take the first watch,” said he. “Ye’ve done well by me, David,
2753𓀴first and last; and I wouldn’t lose you for all Appin--no, nor for
2754𓀴Breadalbane.”
2755𓀴
2756𓀴So I made up my bed on the floor; and he took the first spell, pistol
2757𓀴in hand and sword on knee, three hours by the captain’s watch upon the
2758𓀴wall. Then he roused me up, and I took my turn of three hours; before
2759𓀴the end of which it was broad day, and a very quiet morning, with a
2760𓀴smooth, rolling sea that tossed the ship and made the blood run to and
2761𓀴fro on the round-house floor, and a heavy rain that drummed upon the
2762𓀴roof. All my watch there was nothing stirring; and by the banging of the
2763𓀴helm, I knew they had even no one at the tiller. Indeed (as I learned
2764𓀴afterwards) there were so many of them hurt or dead, and the rest in so
2765𓀴ill a temper, that Mr. Riach and the captain had to take turn and turn
2766𓀴like Alan and me, or the brig might have gone ashore and nobody the
2767𓀴wiser. It was a mercy the night had fallen so still, for the wind had
2768𓀴gone down as soon as the rain began. Even as it was, I judged by the
2769𓀴wailing of a great number of gulls that went crying and fishing round
2770𓀴the ship, that she must have drifted pretty near the coast or one of
2771𓀴the islands of the Hebrides; and at last, looking out of the door of the
2772𓀴round-house, I saw the great stone hills of Skye on the right hand, and,
2773𓀴a little more astern, the strange isle of Rum.
2774𓀴
2775𓀴
2776𓀴
2777𓀴
2778𓀴CHAPTER XI
2779𓀴
2780𓀴THE CAPTAIN KNUCKLES UNDER
2781𓀴
2782𓀴
2783𓀴Alan and I sat down to breakfast about six of the clock. The floor was
2784𓀴covered with broken glass and in a horrid mess of blood, which took away
2785𓀴my hunger. In all other ways we were in a situation not only agreeable
2786𓀴but merry; having ousted the officers from their own cabin, and having
2787𓀴at command all the drink in the ship--both wine and spirits--and all the
2788𓀴dainty part of what was eatable, such as the pickles and the fine sort
2789𓀴of bread. This, of itself, was enough to set us in good humour, but the
2790𓀴richest part of it was this, that the two thirstiest men that ever came
2791𓀴out of Scotland (Mr. Shuan being dead) were now shut in the fore-part of
2792𓀴the ship and condemned to what they hated most--cold water.
2793𓀴
2794𓀴“And depend upon it,” Alan said, “we shall hear more of them ere long.
2795𓀴Ye may keep a man from the fighting, but never from his bottle.”
2796𓀴
2797𓀴We made good company for each other. Alan, indeed, expressed himself
2798𓀴most lovingly; and taking a knife from the table, cut me off one of the
2799𓀴silver buttons from his coat.
2800𓀴
2801𓀴“I had them,” says he, “from my father, Duncan Stewart; and now give ye
2802𓀴one of them to be a keepsake for last night’s work. And wherever ye go
2803𓀴and show that button, the friends of Alan Breck will come around you.”
2804𓀴
2805𓀴He said this as if he had been Charlemagne, and commanded armies; and
2806𓀴indeed, much as I admired his courage, I was always in danger of smiling
2807𓀴at his vanity: in danger, I say, for had I not kept my countenance, I
2808𓀴would be afraid to think what a quarrel might have followed.
2809𓀴
2810𓀴As soon as we were through with our meal he rummaged in the captain’s
2811𓀴locker till he found a clothes-brush; and then taking off his coat,
2812𓀴began to visit his suit and brush away the stains, with such care and
2813𓀴labour as I supposed to have been only usual with women. To be sure, he
2814𓀴had no other; and, besides (as he said), it belonged to a king and so
2815𓀴behoved to be royally looked after.
2816𓀴
2817𓀴For all that, when I saw what care he took to pluck out the threads
2818𓀴where the button had been cut away, I put a higher value on his gift.
2819𓀴
2820𓀴He was still so engaged when we were hailed by Mr. Riach from the deck,
2821𓀴asking for a parley; and I, climbing through the skylight and sitting on
2822𓀴the edge of it, pistol in hand and with a bold front, though inwardly in
2823𓀴fear of broken glass, hailed him back again and bade him speak out. He
2824𓀴came to the edge of the round-house, and stood on a coil of rope, so
2825𓀴that his chin was on a level with the roof; and we looked at each other
2826𓀴awhile in silence. Mr. Riach, as I do not think he had been very forward
2827𓀴in the battle, so he had got off with nothing worse than a blow upon the
2828𓀴cheek: but he looked out of heart and very weary, having been all night
2829𓀴afoot, either standing watch or doctoring the wounded.
2830𓀴
2831𓀴“This is a bad job,” said he at last, shaking his head.
2832𓀴
2833𓀴“It was none of our choosing,” said I.
2834𓀴
2835𓀴“The captain,” says he, “would like to speak with your friend. They
2836𓀴might speak at the window.”
2837𓀴
2838𓀴“And how do we know what treachery he means?” cried I.
2839𓀴
2840𓀴“He means none, David,” returned Mr. Riach, “and if he did, I’ll tell ye
2841𓀴the honest truth, we couldnae get the men to follow.”
2842𓀴
2843𓀴“Is that so?” said I.
2844𓀴
2845𓀴“I’ll tell ye more than that,” said he. “It’s not only the men; it’s me.
2846𓀴I’m frich’ened, Davie.” And he smiled across at me. “No,” he continued,
2847𓀴“what we want is to be shut of him.”
2848𓀴
2849𓀴Thereupon I consulted with Alan, and the parley was agreed to and
2850𓀴parole given upon either side; but this was not the whole of Mr. Riach’s
2851𓀴business, and he now begged me for a dram with such instancy and such
2852𓀴reminders of his former kindness, that at last I handed him a pannikin
2853𓀴with about a gill of brandy. He drank a part, and then carried the rest
2854𓀴down upon the deck, to share it (I suppose) with his superior.
2855𓀴
2856𓀴A little after, the captain came (as was agreed) to one of the windows,
2857𓀴and stood there in the rain, with his arm in a sling, and looking stern
2858𓀴and pale, and so old that my heart smote me for having fired upon him.
2859𓀴
2860𓀴Alan at once held a pistol in his face.
2861𓀴
2862𓀴“Put that thing up!” said the captain. “Have I not passed my word, sir?
2863𓀴or do ye seek to affront me?”
2864𓀴
2865𓀴“Captain,” says Alan, “I doubt your word is a breakable. Last night ye
2866𓀴haggled and argle-bargled like an apple-wife; and then passed me your
2867𓀴word, and gave me your hand to back it; and ye ken very well what was
2868𓀴the upshot. Be damned to your word!” says he.
2869𓀴
2870𓀴“Well, well, sir,” said the captain, “ye’ll get little good by
2871𓀴swearing.” (And truly that was a fault of which the captain was quite
2872𓀴free.) “But we have other things to speak,” he continued, bitterly.
2873𓀴“Ye’ve made a sore hash of my brig; I haven’t hands enough left to work
2874𓀴her; and my first officer (whom I could ill spare) has got your sword
2875𓀴throughout his vitals, and passed without speech. There is nothing left
2876𓀴me, sir, but to put back into the port of Glasgow after hands; and there
2877𓀴(by your leave) ye will find them that are better able to talk to you.”
2878𓀴
2879𓀴“Ay?” said Alan; “and faith, I’ll have a talk with them mysel’! Unless
2880𓀴there’s naebody speaks English in that town, I have a bonny tale for
2881𓀴them. Fifteen tarry sailors upon the one side, and a man and a halfling
2882𓀴boy upon the other! O, man, it’s peetiful!”
2883𓀴
2884𓀴Hoseason flushed red.
2885𓀴
2886𓀴“No,” continued Alan, “that’ll no do. Ye’ll just have to set me ashore
2887𓀴as we agreed.”
2888𓀴
2889𓀴“Ay,” said Hoseason, “but my first officer is dead--ye ken best how.
2890𓀴There’s none of the rest of us acquaint with this coast, sir; and it’s
2891𓀴one very dangerous to ships.”
2892𓀴
2893𓀴“I give ye your choice,” says Alan. “Set me on dry ground in Appin,
2894𓀴or Ardgour, or in Morven, or Arisaig, or Morar; or, in brief, where ye
2895𓀴please, within thirty miles of my own country; except in a country of
2896𓀴the Campbells. That’s a broad target. If ye miss that, ye must be as
2897𓀴feckless at the sailoring as I have found ye at the fighting. Why, my
2898𓀴poor country people in their bit cobles[16] pass from island to island in
2899𓀴all weathers, ay, and by night too, for the matter of that.”
2900𓀴
2901𓀴     [16] Coble: a small boat used in fishing.
2902𓀴
2903𓀴“A coble’s not a ship, sir,” said the captain. “It has nae draught of
2904𓀴water.”
2905𓀴
2906𓀴“Well, then, to Glasgow if ye list!” says Alan. “We’ll have the laugh of
2907𓀴ye at the least.”
2908𓀴
2909𓀴“My mind runs little upon laughing,” said the captain. “But all this
2910𓀴will cost money, sir.”
2911𓀴
2912𓀴“Well, sir,” says Alan, “I am nae weathercock. Thirty guineas, if ye land
2913𓀴me on the sea-side; and sixty, if ye put me in the Linnhe Loch.”
2914𓀴
2915𓀴“But see, sir, where we lie, we are but a few hours’ sail from
2916𓀴Ardnamurchan,” said Hoseason. “Give me sixty, and I’ll set ye there.”
2917𓀴
2918𓀴“And I’m to wear my brogues and run jeopardy of the red-coats to please
2919𓀴you?” cries Alan. “No, sir; if ye want sixty guineas earn them, and set
2920𓀴me in my own country.”
2921𓀴
2922𓀴“It’s to risk the brig, sir,” said the captain, “and your own lives
2923𓀴along with her.”
2924𓀴
2925𓀴“Take it or want it,” says Alan.
2926𓀴
2927𓀴“Could ye pilot us at all?” asked the captain, who was frowning to
2928𓀴himself.
2929𓀴
2930𓀴“Well, it’s doubtful,” said Alan. “I’m more of a fighting man (as ye
2931𓀴have seen for yoursel’) than a sailor-man. But I have been often enough
2932𓀴picked up and set down upon this coast, and should ken something of the
2933𓀴lie of it.”
2934𓀴
2935𓀴The captain shook his head, still frowning.
2936𓀴
2937𓀴“If I had lost less money on this unchancy cruise,” says he, “I would
2938𓀴see you in a rope’s end before I risked my brig, sir. But be it as ye
2939𓀴will. As soon as I get a slant of wind (and there’s some coming, or I’m
2940𓀴the more mistaken) I’ll put it in hand. But there’s one thing more. We
2941𓀴may meet in with a king’s ship and she may lay us aboard, sir, with no
2942𓀴blame of mine: they keep the cruisers thick upon this coast, ye ken who
2943𓀴for. Now, sir, if that was to befall, ye might leave the money.”
2944𓀴
2945𓀴“Captain,” says Alan, “if ye see a pennant, it shall be your part to
2946𓀴run away. And now, as I hear you’re a little short of brandy in the
2947𓀴fore-part, I’ll offer ye a change: a bottle of brandy against two
2948𓀴buckets of water.”
2949𓀴
2950𓀴That was the last clause of the treaty, and was duly executed on both
2951𓀴sides; so that Alan and I could at last wash out the round-house and be
2952𓀴quit of the memorials of those whom we had slain, and the captain and
2953𓀴Mr. Riach could be happy again in their own way, the name of which was
2954𓀴drink.
2955𓀴
2956𓀴
2957𓀴
2958𓀴
2959𓀴CHAPTER XII
2960𓀴
2961𓀴I HEAR OF THE “RED FOX”
2962𓀴
2963𓀴
2964𓀴Before we had done cleaning out the round-house, a breeze sprang up from
2965𓀴a little to the east of north. This blew off the rain and brought out
2966𓀴the sun.
2967𓀴
2968𓀴And here I must explain; and the reader would do well to look at a map.
2969𓀴On the day when the fog fell and we ran down Alan’s boat, we had been
2970𓀴running through the Little Minch. At dawn after the battle, we lay
2971𓀴becalmed to the east of the Isle of Canna or between that and Isle
2972𓀴Eriska in the chain of the Long Island. Now to get from there to the
2973𓀴Linnhe Loch, the straight course was through the narrows of the Sound of
2974𓀴Mull. But the captain had no chart; he was afraid to trust his brig so
2975𓀴deep among the islands; and the wind serving well, he preferred to go by
2976𓀴west of Tiree and come up under the southern coast of the great Isle of
2977𓀴Mull.
2978𓀴
2979𓀴All day the breeze held in the same point, and rather freshened than
2980𓀴died down; and towards afternoon, a swell began to set in from round the
2981𓀴outer Hebrides. Our course, to go round about the inner isles, was to
2982𓀴the west of south, so that at first we had this swell upon our beam, and
2983𓀴were much rolled about. But after nightfall, when we had turned the end
2984𓀴of Tiree and began to head more to the east, the sea came right astern.
2985𓀴
2986𓀴Meanwhile, the early part of the day, before the swell came up, was
2987𓀴very pleasant; sailing, as we were, in a bright sunshine and with
2988𓀴many mountainous islands upon different sides. Alan and I sat in the
2989𓀴round-house with the doors open on each side (the wind being straight
2990𓀴astern), and smoked a pipe or two of the captain’s fine tobacco. It was
2991𓀴at this time we heard each other’s stories, which was the more important
2992𓀴to me, as I gained some knowledge of that wild Highland country on which
2993𓀴I was so soon to land. In those days, so close on the back of the great
2994𓀴rebellion, it was needful a man should know what he was doing when he
2995𓀴went upon the heather.
2996𓀴
2997𓀴It was I that showed the example, telling him all my misfortune; which
2998𓀴he heard with great good-nature. Only, when I came to mention that good
2999𓀴friend of mine, Mr. Campbell the minister, Alan fired up and cried out
3000𓀴that he hated all that were of that name.
3001𓀴
3002𓀴“Why,” said I, “he is a man you should be proud to give your hand to.”
3003𓀴
3004𓀴“I know nothing I would help a Campbell to,” says he, “unless it was a
3005𓀴leaden bullet. I would hunt all of that name like blackcocks. If I lay
3006𓀴dying, I would crawl upon my knees to my chamber window for a shot at
3007𓀴one.”
3008𓀴
3009𓀴“Why, Alan,” I cried, “what ails ye at the Campbells?”
3010𓀴
3011𓀴“Well,” says he, “ye ken very well that I am an Appin Stewart, and the
3012𓀴Campbells have long harried and wasted those of my name; ay, and got
3013𓀴lands of us by treachery--but never with the sword,” he cried loudly,
3014𓀴and with the word brought down his fist upon the table. But I paid the
3015𓀴less attention to this, for I knew it was usually said by those who have
3016𓀴the underhand. “There’s more than that,” he continued, “and all in the
3017𓀴same story: lying words, lying papers, tricks fit for a peddler, and the
3018𓀴show of what’s legal over all, to make a man the more angry.”
3019𓀴
3020𓀴“You that are so wasteful of your buttons,” said I, “I can hardly think
3021𓀴you would be a good judge of business.”
3022𓀴
3023𓀴“Ah!” says he, falling again to smiling, “I got my wastefulness from
3024𓀴the same man I got the buttons from; and that was my poor father, Duncan
3025𓀴Stewart, grace be to him! He was the prettiest man of his kindred; and
3026𓀴the best swordsman in the Hielands, David, and that is the same as to
3027𓀴say, in all the world, I should ken, for it was him that taught me.
3028𓀴He was in the Black Watch, when first it was mustered; and, like other
3029𓀴gentlemen privates, had a gillie at his back to carry his firelock for
3030𓀴him on the march. Well, the King, it appears, was wishful to see Hieland
3031𓀴swordsmanship; and my father and three more were chosen out and sent to
3032𓀴London town, to let him see it at the best. So they were had into the
