3033𓀴palace and showed the whole art of the sword for two hours at a stretch,
3034𓀴before King George and Queen Carline, and the Butcher Cumberland, and
3035𓀴many more of whom I havenae mind. And when they were through, the King
3036𓀴(for all he was a rank usurper) spoke them fair and gave each man three
3037𓀴guineas in his hand. Now, as they were going out of the palace, they
3038𓀴had a porter’s lodge to go by; and it came in on my father, as he was
3039𓀴perhaps the first private Hieland gentleman that had ever gone by that
3040𓀴door, it was right he should give the poor porter a proper notion of
3041𓀴their quality. So he gives the King’s three guineas into the man’s hand,
3042𓀴as if it was his common custom; the three others that came behind him
3043𓀴did the same; and there they were on the street, never a penny the
3044𓀴better for their pains. Some say it was one, that was the first to fee
3045𓀴the King’s porter; and some say it was another; but the truth of it is,
3046𓀴that it was Duncan Stewart, as I am willing to prove with either sword
3047𓀴or pistol. And that was the father that I had, God rest him!”
3048𓀴
3049𓀴“I think he was not the man to leave you rich,” said I.
3050𓀴
3051𓀴“And that’s true,” said Alan. “He left me my breeks to cover me, and
3052𓀴little besides. And that was how I came to enlist, which was a black
3053𓀴spot upon my character at the best of times, and would still be a sore
3054𓀴job for me if I fell among the red-coats.”
3055𓀴
3056𓀴“What,” cried I, “were you in the English army?”
3057𓀴
3058𓀴“That was I,” said Alan. “But I deserted to the right side at Preston
3059𓀴Pans--and that’s some comfort.”
3060𓀴
3061𓀴I could scarcely share this view: holding desertion under arms for an
3062𓀴unpardonable fault in honour. But for all I was so young, I was wiser
3063𓀴than say my thought. “Dear, dear,” says I, “the punishment is death.”
3064𓀴
3065𓀴“Ay” said he, “if they got hands on me, it would be a short shrift and
3066𓀴a lang tow for Alan! But I have the King of France’s commission in my
3067𓀴pocket, which would aye be some protection.”
3068𓀴
3069𓀴“I misdoubt it much,” said I.
3070𓀴
3071𓀴“I have doubts mysel’,” said Alan drily.
3072𓀴
3073𓀴“And, good heaven, man,” cried I, “you that are a condemned rebel, and a
3074𓀴deserter, and a man of the French King’s--what tempts ye back into this
3075𓀴country? It’s a braving of Providence.”
3076𓀴
3077𓀴“Tut!” says Alan, “I have been back every year since forty-six!”
3078𓀴
3079𓀴“And what brings ye, man?” cried I.
3080𓀴
3081𓀴“Well, ye see, I weary for my friends and country,” said he. “France is
3082𓀴a braw place, nae doubt; but I weary for the heather and the deer. And
3083𓀴then I have bit things that I attend to. Whiles I pick up a few lads
3084𓀴to serve the King of France: recruits, ye see; and that’s aye a
3085𓀴little money. But the heart of the matter is the business of my chief,
3086𓀴Ardshiel.”
3087𓀴
3088𓀴“I thought they called your chief Appin,” said I.
3089𓀴
3090𓀴“Ay, but Ardshiel is the captain of the clan,” said he, which scarcely
3091𓀴cleared my mind. “Ye see, David, he that was all his life so great a
3092𓀴man, and come of the blood and bearing the name of kings, is now brought
3093𓀴down to live in a French town like a poor and private person. He that
3094𓀴had four hundred swords at his whistle, I have seen, with these eyes
3095𓀴of mine, buying butter in the market-place, and taking it home in a
3096𓀴kale-leaf. This is not only a pain but a disgrace to us of his family
3097𓀴and clan. There are the bairns forby, the children and the hope of
3098𓀴Appin, that must be learned their letters and how to hold a sword, in
3099𓀴that far country. Now, the tenants of Appin have to pay a rent to King
3100𓀴George; but their hearts are staunch, they are true to their chief; and
3101𓀴what with love and a bit of pressure, and maybe a threat or two, the
3102𓀴poor folk scrape up a second rent for Ardshiel. Well, David, I’m the
3103𓀴hand that carries it.” And he struck the belt about his body, so that
3104𓀴the guineas rang.
3105𓀴
3106𓀴“Do they pay both?” cried I.
3107𓀴
3108𓀴“Ay, David, both,” says he.
3109𓀴
3110𓀴“What! two rents?” I repeated.
3111𓀴
3112𓀴“Ay, David,” said he. “I told a different tale to yon captain man; but
3113𓀴this is the truth of it. And it’s wonderful to me how little pressure
3114𓀴is needed. But that’s the handiwork of my good kinsman and my father’s
3115𓀴friend, James of the Glens: James Stewart, that is: Ardshiel’s
3116𓀴half-brother. He it is that gets the money in, and does the management.”
3117𓀴
3118𓀴This was the first time I heard the name of that James Stewart, who was
3119𓀴afterwards so famous at the time of his hanging. But I took little heed
3120𓀴at the moment, for all my mind was occupied with the generosity of these
3121𓀴poor Highlanders.
3122𓀴
3123𓀴“I call it noble,” I cried. “I’m a Whig, or little better; but I call it
3124𓀴noble.”
3125𓀴
3126𓀴“Ay” said he, “ye’re a Whig, but ye’re a gentleman; and that’s what does
3127𓀴it. Now, if ye were one of the cursed race of Campbell, ye would gnash
3128𓀴your teeth to hear tell of it. If ye were the Red Fox...” And at that
3129𓀴name, his teeth shut together, and he ceased speaking. I have seen many
3130𓀴a grim face, but never a grimmer than Alan’s when he had named the Red
3131𓀴Fox.
3132𓀴
3133𓀴“And who is the Red Fox?” I asked, daunted, but still curious.
3134𓀴
3135𓀴“Who is he?” cried Alan. “Well, and I’ll tell you that. When the men of
3136𓀴the clans were broken at Culloden, and the good cause went down, and the
3137𓀴horses rode over the fetlocks in the best blood of the north, Ardshiel
3138𓀴had to flee like a poor deer upon the mountains--he and his lady and his
3139𓀴bairns. A sair job we had of it before we got him shipped; and while he
3140𓀴still lay in the heather, the English rogues, that couldnae come at his
3141𓀴life, were striking at his rights. They stripped him of his powers; they
3142𓀴stripped him of his lands; they plucked the weapons from the hands of
3143𓀴his clansmen, that had borne arms for thirty centuries; ay, and the very
3144𓀴clothes off their backs--so that it’s now a sin to wear a tartan plaid,
3145𓀴and a man may be cast into a gaol if he has but a kilt about his legs.
3146𓀴One thing they couldnae kill. That was the love the clansmen bore their
3147𓀴chief. These guineas are the proof of it. And now, in there steps a man,
3148𓀴a Campbell, red-headed Colin of Glenure----”
3149𓀴
3150𓀴“Is that him you call the Red Fox?” said I.
3151𓀴
3152𓀴“Will ye bring me his brush?” cries Alan, fiercely. “Ay, that’s the man.
3153𓀴In he steps, and gets papers from King George, to be so-called King’s
3154𓀴factor on the lands of Appin. And at first he sings small, and is
3155𓀴hail-fellow-well-met with Sheamus--that’s James of the Glens, my
3156𓀴chieftain’s agent. But by-and-by, that came to his ears that I have just
3157𓀴told you; how the poor commons of Appin, the farmers and the crofters
3158𓀴and the boumen, were wringing their very plaids to get a second rent,
3159𓀴and send it over-seas for Ardshiel and his poor bairns. What was it ye
3160𓀴called it, when I told ye?”
3161𓀴
3162𓀴“I called it noble, Alan,” said I.
3163𓀴
3164𓀴“And you little better than a common Whig!” cries Alan. “But when it
3165𓀴came to Colin Roy, the black Campbell blood in him ran wild. He sat
3166𓀴gnashing his teeth at the wine table. What! should a Stewart get a bite
3167𓀴of bread, and him not be able to prevent it? Ah! Red Fox, if ever I
3168𓀴hold you at a gun’s end, the Lord have pity upon ye!” (Alan stopped to
3169𓀴swallow down his anger.) “Well, David, what does he do? He declares all
3170𓀴the farms to let. And, thinks he, in his black heart, ‘I’ll soon get
3171𓀴other tenants that’ll overbid these Stewarts, and Maccolls, and Macrobs’
3172𓀴(for these are all names in my clan, David); ‘and then,’ thinks he,
3173𓀴‘Ardshiel will have to hold his bonnet on a French roadside.’”
3174𓀴
3175𓀴“Well,” said I, “what followed?”
3176𓀴
3177𓀴Alan laid down his pipe, which he had long since suffered to go out, and
3178𓀴set his two hands upon his knees.
3179𓀴
3180𓀴“Ay,” said he, “ye’ll never guess that! For these same Stewarts, and
3181𓀴Maccolls, and Macrobs (that had two rents to pay, one to King George
3182𓀴by stark force, and one to Ardshiel by natural kindness) offered him a
3183𓀴better price than any Campbell in all broad Scotland; and far he
3184𓀴sent seeking them--as far as to the sides of Clyde and the cross of
3185𓀴Edinburgh--seeking, and fleeching, and begging them to come, where there
3186𓀴was a Stewart to be starved and a red-headed hound of a Campbell to be
3187𓀴pleasured!”
3188𓀴
3189𓀴“Well, Alan,” said I, “that is a strange story, and a fine one, too. And
3190𓀴Whig as I may be, I am glad the man was beaten.”
3191𓀴
3192𓀴“Him beaten?” echoed Alan. “It’s little ye ken of Campbells, and less
3193𓀴of the Red Fox. Him beaten? No: nor will be, till his blood’s on the
3194𓀴hillside! But if the day comes, David man, that I can find time and
3195𓀴leisure for a bit of hunting, there grows not enough heather in all
3196𓀴Scotland to hide him from my vengeance!”
3197𓀴
3198𓀴“Man Alan,” said I, “ye are neither very wise nor very Christian to
3199𓀴blow off so many words of anger. They will do the man ye call the Fox no
3200𓀴harm, and yourself no good. Tell me your tale plainly out. What did he
3201𓀴next?”
3202𓀴
3203𓀴“And that’s a good observe, David,” said Alan. “Troth and indeed,
3204𓀴they will do him no harm; the more’s the pity! And barring that about
3205𓀴Christianity (of which my opinion is quite otherwise, or I would be nae
3206𓀴Christian), I am much of your mind.”
3207𓀴
3208𓀴“Opinion here or opinion there,” said I, “it’s a kent thing that
3209𓀴Christianity forbids revenge.”
3210𓀴
3211𓀴“Ay” said he, “it’s well seen it was a Campbell taught ye! It would be
3212𓀴a convenient world for them and their sort, if there was no such a thing
3213𓀴as a lad and a gun behind a heather bush! But that’s nothing to the
3214𓀴point. This is what he did.”
3215𓀴
3216𓀴“Ay” said I, “come to that.”
3217𓀴
3218𓀴“Well, David,” said he, “since he couldnae be rid of the loyal commons
3219𓀴by fair means, he swore he would be rid of them by foul. Ardshiel was to
3220𓀴starve: that was the thing he aimed at. And since them that fed him in
3221𓀴his exile wouldnae be bought out--right or wrong, he would drive them
3222𓀴out. Therefore he sent for lawyers, and papers, and red-coats to stand
3223𓀴at his back. And the kindly folk of that country must all pack and
3224𓀴tramp, every father’s son out of his father’s house, and out of the
3225𓀴place where he was bred and fed, and played when he was a callant. And
3226𓀴who are to succeed them? Bare-leggit beggars! King George is to whistle
3227𓀴for his rents; he maun dow with less; he can spread his butter thinner:
3228𓀴what cares Red Colin? If he can hurt Ardshiel, he has his wish; if he
3229𓀴can pluck the meat from my chieftain’s table, and the bit toys out of
3230𓀴his children’s hands, he will gang hame singing to Glenure!”
3231𓀴
3232𓀴“Let me have a word,” said I. “Be sure, if they take less rents, be
3233𓀴sure Government has a finger in the pie. It’s not this Campbell’s fault,
3234𓀴man--it’s his orders. And if ye killed this Colin to-morrow, what better
3235𓀴would ye be? There would be another factor in his shoes, as fast as spur
3236𓀴can drive.”
3237𓀴
3238𓀴“Ye’re a good lad in a fight,” said Alan; “but, man! ye have Whig blood
3239𓀴in ye!”
3240𓀴
3241𓀴He spoke kindly enough, but there was so much anger under his contempt
3242𓀴that I thought it was wise to change the conversation. I expressed my
3243𓀴wonder how, with the Highlands covered with troops, and guarded like
3244𓀴a city in a siege, a man in his situation could come and go without
3245𓀴arrest.
3246𓀴
3247𓀴“It’s easier than ye would think,” said Alan. “A bare hillside (ye see)
3248𓀴is like all one road; if there’s a sentry at one place, ye just go by
3249𓀴another. And then the heather’s a great help. And everywhere there are
3250𓀴friends’ houses and friends’ byres and haystacks. And besides, when folk
3251𓀴talk of a country covered with troops, it’s but a kind of a byword at
3252𓀴the best. A soldier covers nae mair of it than his boot-soles. I have
3253𓀴fished a water with a sentry on the other side of the brae, and killed a
3254𓀴fine trout; and I have sat in a heather bush within six feet of another,
3255𓀴and learned a real bonny tune from his whistling. This was it,” said he,
3256𓀴and whistled me the air.
3257𓀴
3258𓀴“And then, besides,” he continued, “it’s no sae bad now as it was in
3259𓀴forty-six. The Hielands are what they call pacified. Small wonder, with
3260𓀴never a gun or a sword left from Cantyre to Cape Wrath, but what tenty[17]
3261𓀴folk have hidden in their thatch! But what I would like to ken, David,
3262𓀴is just how long? Not long, ye would think, with men like Ardshiel in
3263𓀴exile and men like the Red Fox sitting birling the wine and oppressing
3264𓀴the poor at home. But it’s a kittle thing to decide what folk’ll bear,
3265𓀴and what they will not. Or why would Red Colin be riding his horse all
3266𓀴over my poor country of Appin, and never a pretty lad to put a bullet in
3267𓀴him?”
3268𓀴
3269𓀴     [17] Careful.
3270𓀴
3271𓀴And with this Alan fell into a muse, and for a long time sate very sad
3272𓀴and silent.
3273𓀴
3274𓀴I will add the rest of what I have to say about my friend, that he
3275𓀴was skilled in all kinds of music, but principally pipe-music; was a
3276𓀴well-considered poet in his own tongue; had read several books both in
3277𓀴French and English; was a dead shot, a good angler, and an excellent
3278𓀴fencer with the small sword as well as with his own particular weapon.
3279𓀴For his faults, they were on his face, and I now knew them all. But
3280𓀴the worst of them, his childish propensity to take offence and to pick
3281𓀴quarrels, he greatly laid aside in my case, out of regard for the battle
3282𓀴of the round-house. But whether it was because I had done well myself,
3283𓀴or because I had been a witness of his own much greater prowess, is more
3284𓀴than I can tell. For though he had a great taste for courage in other
3285𓀴men, yet he admired it most in Alan Breck.
3286𓀴
3287𓀴
3288𓀴
3289𓀴
3290𓀴CHAPTER XIII
3291𓀴
3292𓀴THE LOSS OF THE BRIG
3293𓀴
3294𓀴
3295𓀴It was already late at night, and as dark as it ever would be at that
3296𓀴season of the year (and that is to say, it was still pretty bright),
3297𓀴when Hoseason clapped his head into the round-house door.
3298𓀴
3299𓀴“Here,” said he, “come out and see if ye can pilot.”
3300𓀴
3301𓀴“Is this one of your tricks?” asked Alan.
3302𓀴
3303𓀴“Do I look like tricks?” cries the captain. “I have other things to
3304𓀴think of--my brig’s in danger!”
3305𓀴
3306𓀴By the concerned look of his face, and, above all, by the sharp tones in
3307𓀴which he spoke of his brig, it was plain to both of us he was in deadly
3308𓀴earnest; and so Alan and I, with no great fear of treachery, stepped on
3309𓀴deck.
3310𓀴
3311𓀴The sky was clear; it blew hard, and was bitter cold; a great deal of
3312𓀴daylight lingered; and the moon, which was nearly full, shone brightly.
3313𓀴The brig was close hauled, so as to round the southwest corner of the
3314𓀴Island of Mull, the hills of which (and Ben More above them all, with a
3315𓀴wisp of mist upon the top of it) lay full upon the lar-board bow. Though
3316𓀴it was no good point of sailing for the _Covenant_, she tore through
3317𓀴the seas at a great rate, pitching and straining, and pursued by the
3318𓀴westerly swell.
3319𓀴
3320𓀴Altogether it was no such ill night to keep the seas in; and I had begun
3321𓀴to wonder what it was that sat so heavily upon the captain, when the
3322𓀴brig rising suddenly on the top of a high swell, he pointed and cried to
3323𓀴us to look. Away on the lee bow, a thing like a fountain rose out of the
3324𓀴moonlit sea, and immediately after we heard a low sound of roaring.
3325𓀴
3326𓀴“What do ye call that?” asked the captain, gloomily.
3327𓀴
3328𓀴“The sea breaking on a reef,” said Alan. “And now ye ken where it is;
3329𓀴and what better would ye have?”
3330𓀴
3331𓀴“Ay,” said Hoseason, “if it was the only one.”
3332𓀴
3333𓀴And sure enough, just as he spoke there came a second fountain farther
3334𓀴to the south.
3335𓀴
3336𓀴“There!” said Hoseason. “Ye see for yourself. If I had kent of these
3337𓀴reefs, if I had had a chart, or if Shuan had been spared, it’s not sixty
3338𓀴guineas, no, nor six hundred, would have made me risk my brig in sic a
3339𓀴stoneyard! But you, sir, that was to pilot us, have ye never a word?”
3340𓀴
3341𓀴“I’m thinking,” said Alan, “these’ll be what they call the Torran
3342𓀴Rocks.”
3343𓀴
3344𓀴“Are there many of them?” says the captain.
3345𓀴
3346𓀴“Truly, sir, I am nae pilot,” said Alan; “but it sticks in my mind there
3347𓀴are ten miles of them.”
3348𓀴
3349𓀴Mr. Riach and the captain looked at each other.
3350𓀴
3351𓀴“There’s a way through them, I suppose?” said the captain.
3352𓀴
3353𓀴“Doubtless,” said Alan, “but where? But it somehow runs in my mind once
3354𓀴more that it is clearer under the land.”
3355𓀴
3356𓀴“So?” said Hoseason. “We’ll have to haul our wind then, Mr. Riach; we’ll
3357𓀴have to come as near in about the end of Mull as we can take her, sir;
3358𓀴and even then we’ll have the land to kep the wind off us, and that
3359𓀴stoneyard on our lee. Well, we’re in for it now, and may as well crack
3360𓀴on.”
3361𓀴
3362𓀴With that he gave an order to the steersman, and sent Riach to the
3363𓀴foretop. There were only five men on deck, counting the officers; these
3364𓀴being all that were fit (or, at least, both fit and willing) for their
3365𓀴work. So, as I say, it fell to Mr. Riach to go aloft, and he sat there
3366𓀴looking out and hailing the deck with news of all he saw.
3367𓀴
3368𓀴“The sea to the south is thick,” he cried; and then, after a while, “it
3369𓀴does seem clearer in by the land.”
3370𓀴
3371𓀴“Well, sir,” said Hoseason to Alan, “we’ll try your way of it. But I
3372𓀴think I might as well trust to a blind fiddler. Pray God you’re right.”
3373𓀴
3374𓀴“Pray God I am!” says Alan to me. “But where did I hear it? Well, well,
3375𓀴it will be as it must.”
3376𓀴
3377𓀴As we got nearer to the turn of the land the reefs began to be sown here
3378𓀴and there on our very path; and Mr. Riach sometimes cried down to us to
3379𓀴change the course. Sometimes, indeed, none too soon; for one reef was
3380𓀴so close on the brig’s weather board that when a sea burst upon it the
3381𓀴lighter sprays fell upon her deck and wetted us like rain.
3382𓀴
3383𓀴The brightness of the night showed us these perils as clearly as by day,
3384𓀴which was, perhaps, the more alarming. It showed me, too, the face of
3385𓀴the captain as he stood by the steersman, now on one foot, now on the
3386𓀴other, and sometimes blowing in his hands, but still listening and
3387𓀴looking and as steady as steel. Neither he nor Mr. Riach had shown
3388𓀴well in the fighting; but I saw they were brave in their own trade, and
3389𓀴admired them all the more because I found Alan very white.
3390𓀴
3391𓀴“Ochone, David,” says he, “this is no the kind of death I fancy!”
3392𓀴
3393𓀴“What, Alan!” I cried, “you’re not afraid?”
3394𓀴
3395𓀴“No,” said he, wetting his lips, “but you’ll allow, yourself, it’s a
3396𓀴cold ending.”
3397𓀴
3398𓀴By this time, now and then sheering to one side or the other to avoid a
3399𓀴reef, but still hugging the wind and the land, we had got round Iona and
3400𓀴begun to come alongside Mull. The tide at the tail of the land ran very
3401𓀴strong, and threw the brig about. Two hands were put to the helm, and
3402𓀴Hoseason himself would sometimes lend a help; and it was strange to
3403𓀴see three strong men throw their weight upon the tiller, and it (like a
3404𓀴living thing) struggle against and drive them back. This would have
3405𓀴been the greater danger had not the sea been for some while free of
3406𓀴obstacles. Mr. Riach, besides, announced from the top that he saw clear
3407𓀴water ahead.
3408𓀴
3409𓀴“Ye were right,” said Hoseason to Alan. “Ye have saved the brig, sir.
3410𓀴I’ll mind that when we come to clear accounts.” And I believe he not
3411𓀴only meant what he said, but would have done it; so high a place did the
3412𓀴_Covenant_ hold in his affections.
3413𓀴
3414𓀴But this is matter only for conjecture, things having gone otherwise
3415𓀴than he forecast.
3416𓀴
3417𓀴“Keep her away a point,” sings out Mr. Riach. “Reef to windward!”
3418𓀴
3419𓀴And just at the same time the tide caught the brig, and threw the wind
3420𓀴out of her sails. She came round into the wind like a top, and the next
3421𓀴moment struck the reef with such a dunch as threw us all flat upon the
3422𓀴deck, and came near to shake Mr. Riach from his place upon the mast.
3423𓀴
3424𓀴I was on my feet in a minute. The reef on which we had struck was close
3425𓀴in under the southwest end of Mull, off a little isle they call Earraid,
3426𓀴which lay low and black upon the larboard. Sometimes the swell broke
3427𓀴clean over us; sometimes it only ground the poor brig upon the reef, so
3428𓀴that we could hear her beat herself to pieces; and what with the great
3429𓀴noise of the sails, and the singing of the wind, and the flying of the
3430𓀴spray in the moonlight, and the sense of danger, I think my head must
3431𓀴have been partly turned, for I could scarcely understand the things I
3432𓀴saw.
3433𓀴
3434𓀴Presently I observed Mr. Riach and the seamen busy round the skiff, and,
3435𓀴still in the same blank, ran over to assist them; and as soon as I set
3436𓀴my hand to work, my mind came clear again. It was no very easy task, for
3437𓀴the skiff lay amidships and was full of hamper, and the breaking of the
3438𓀴heavier seas continually forced us to give over and hold on; but we all
3439𓀴wrought like horses while we could.
3440𓀴
3441𓀴Meanwhile such of the wounded as could move came clambering out of the
3442𓀴fore-scuttle and began to help; while the rest that lay helpless in
3443𓀴their bunks harrowed me with screaming and begging to be saved.
3444𓀴
3445𓀴The captain took no part. It seemed he was struck stupid. He stood
3446𓀴holding by the shrouds, talking to himself and groaning out aloud
3447𓀴whenever the ship hammered on the rock. His brig was like wife and
3448𓀴child to him; he had looked on, day by day, at the mishandling of poor
3449𓀴Ransome; but when it came to the brig, he seemed to suffer along with
3450𓀴her.
3451𓀴
3452𓀴All the time of our working at the boat, I remember only one other
3453𓀴thing: that I asked Alan, looking across at the shore, what country it
3454𓀴was; and he answered, it was the worst possible for him, for it was a
3455𓀴land of the Campbells.
3456𓀴
3457𓀴We had one of the wounded men told off to keep a watch upon the seas and
3458𓀴cry us warning. Well, we had the boat about ready to be launched, when
3459𓀴this man sang out pretty shrill: “For God’s sake, hold on!” We knew
3460𓀴by his tone that it was something more than ordinary; and sure enough,
3461𓀴there followed a sea so huge that it lifted the brig right up and canted
3462𓀴her over on her beam. Whether the cry came too late, or my hold was too
3463𓀴weak, I know not; but at the sudden tilting of the ship I was cast clean
3464𓀴over the bulwarks into the sea.
3465𓀴
3466𓀴I went down, and drank my fill, and then came up, and got a blink of the
3467𓀴moon, and then down again. They say a man sinks a third time for good. I
3468𓀴cannot be made like other folk, then; for I would not like to write how
3469𓀴often I went down, or how often I came up again. All the while, I was
3470𓀴being hurled along, and beaten upon and choked, and then swallowed
3471𓀴whole; and the thing was so distracting to my wits, that I was neither
3472𓀴sorry nor afraid.
3473𓀴
3474𓀴Presently, I found I was holding to a spar, which helped me somewhat.
3475𓀴And then all of a sudden I was in quiet water, and began to come to
3476𓀴myself.
3477𓀴
3478𓀴It was the spare yard I had got hold of, and I was amazed to see how far
3479𓀴I had travelled from the brig. I hailed her, indeed; but it was plain
3480𓀴she was already out of cry. She was still holding together; but whether
3481𓀴or not they had yet launched the boat, I was too far off and too low
3482𓀴down to see.
3483𓀴
3484𓀴While I was hailing the brig, I spied a tract of water lying between
3485𓀴us where no great waves came, but which yet boiled white all over and
3486𓀴bristled in the moon with rings and bubbles. Sometimes the whole tract
3487𓀴swung to one side, like the tail of a live serpent; sometimes, for a
3488𓀴glimpse, it would all disappear and then boil up again. What it was I
3489𓀴had no guess, which for the time increased my fear of it; but I now know
3490𓀴it must have been the roost or tide race, which had carried me away so
3491𓀴fast and tumbled me about so cruelly, and at last, as if tired of that
3492𓀴play, had flung out me and the spare yard upon its landward margin.
3493𓀴
3494𓀴I now lay quite becalmed, and began to feel that a man can die of cold
3495𓀴as well as of drowning. The shores of Earraid were close in; I could see
3496𓀴in the moonlight the dots of heather and the sparkling of the mica in
3497𓀴the rocks.
3498𓀴
3499𓀴“Well,” thought I to myself, “if I cannot get as far as that, it’s
3500𓀴strange!”
3501𓀴
3502𓀴I had no skill of swimming, Essen Water being small in our
3503𓀴neighbourhood; but when I laid hold upon the yard with both arms, and
3504𓀴kicked out with both feet, I soon begun to find that I was moving. Hard
3505𓀴work it was, and mortally slow; but in about an hour of kicking
3506𓀴and splashing, I had got well in between the points of a sandy bay
3507𓀴surrounded by low hills.
3508𓀴
3509𓀴The sea was here quite quiet; there was no sound of any surf; the moon
3510𓀴shone clear; and I thought in my heart I had never seen a place so
3511𓀴desert and desolate. But it was dry land; and when at last it grew so
3512𓀴shallow that I could leave the yard and wade ashore upon my feet, I
3513𓀴cannot tell if I was more tired or more grateful. Both, at least, I was:
3514𓀴tired as I never was before that night; and grateful to God as I trust I
3515𓀴have been often, though never with more cause.
3516𓀴
3517𓀴
3518𓀴
3519𓀴
3520𓀴CHAPTER XIV
3521𓀴
3522𓀴THE ISLET
3523𓀴
3524𓀴
3525𓀴With my stepping ashore I began the most unhappy part of my adventures.
3526𓀴It was half-past twelve in the morning, and though the wind was broken
3527𓀴by the land, it was a cold night. I dared not sit down (for I thought
3528𓀴I should have frozen), but took off my shoes and walked to and fro upon
3529𓀴the sand, bare-foot, and beating my breast with infinite weariness.
3530𓀴There was no sound of man or cattle; not a cock crew, though it was
3531𓀴about the hour of their first waking; only the surf broke outside in the
3532𓀴distance, which put me in mind of my perils and those of my friend.
3533𓀴To walk by the sea at that hour of the morning, and in a place so
3534𓀴desert-like and lonesome, struck me with a kind of fear.
3535𓀴
3536𓀴As soon as the day began to break I put on my shoes and climbed a
3537𓀴hill--the ruggedest scramble I ever undertook--falling, the whole way,
3538𓀴between big blocks of granite, or leaping from one to another. When I
3539𓀴got to the top the dawn was come. There was no sign of the brig, which
3540𓀴must have lifted from the reef and sunk. The boat, too, was nowhere to
3541𓀴be seen. There was never a sail upon the ocean; and in what I could see
3542𓀴of the land was neither house nor man.
3543𓀴
3544𓀴I was afraid to think what had befallen my shipmates, and afraid to look
3545𓀴longer at so empty a scene. What with my wet clothes and weariness, and
3546𓀴my belly that now began to ache with hunger, I had enough to trouble
3547𓀴me without that. So I set off eastward along the south coast, hoping to
3548𓀴find a house where I might warm myself, and perhaps get news of those I
3549𓀴had lost. And at the worst, I considered the sun would soon rise and dry
3550𓀴my clothes.
3551𓀴
3552𓀴After a little, my way was stopped by a creek or inlet of the sea, which
3553𓀴seemed to run pretty deep into the land; and as I had no means to get
3554𓀴across, I must needs change my direction to go about the end of it. It
3555𓀴was still the roughest kind of walking; indeed the whole, not only of
3556𓀴Earraid, but of the neighbouring part of Mull (which they call the Ross)
3557𓀴is nothing but a jumble of granite rocks with heather in among. At first
3558𓀴the creek kept narrowing as I had looked to see; but presently to my
3559𓀴surprise it began to widen out again. At this I scratched my head,
3560𓀴but had still no notion of the truth: until at last I came to a rising
3561𓀴ground, and it burst upon me all in a moment that I was cast upon a
3562𓀴little barren isle, and cut off on every side by the salt seas.
3563𓀴
3564𓀴Instead of the sun rising to dry me, it came on to rain, with a thick
3565𓀴mist; so that my case was lamentable.
3566𓀴
3567𓀴I stood in the rain, and shivered, and wondered what to do, till it
3568𓀴occurred to me that perhaps the creek was fordable. Back I went to the
3569𓀴narrowest point and waded in. But not three yards from shore, I plumped
3570𓀴in head over ears; and if ever I was heard of more, it was rather by
3571𓀴God’s grace than my own prudence. I was no wetter (for that could hardly
3572𓀴be), but I was all the colder for this mishap; and having lost another
3573𓀴hope was the more unhappy.
3574𓀴
3575𓀴And now, all at once, the yard came in my head. What had carried me
3576𓀴through the roost would surely serve me to cross this little quiet creek
3577𓀴in safety. With that I set off, undaunted, across the top of the isle,
3578𓀴to fetch and carry it back. It was a weary tramp in all ways, and if
3579𓀴hope had not buoyed me up, I must have cast myself down and given up.
3580𓀴Whether with the sea salt, or because I was growing fevered, I was
3581𓀴distressed with thirst, and had to stop, as I went, and drink the peaty
3582𓀴water out of the hags.
3583𓀴
3584𓀴I came to the bay at last, more dead than alive; and at the first
3585𓀴glance, I thought the yard was something farther out than when I left
3586𓀴it. In I went, for the third time, into the sea. The sand was smooth
3587𓀴and firm, and shelved gradually down, so that I could wade out till the
3588𓀴water was almost to my neck and the little waves splashed into my face.
3589𓀴But at that depth my feet began to leave me, and I durst venture in no
3590𓀴farther. As for the yard, I saw it bobbing very quietly some twenty feet
3591𓀴beyond.
3592𓀴
3593𓀴I had borne up well until this last disappointment; but at that I came
3594𓀴ashore, and flung myself down upon the sands and wept.
3595𓀴
3596𓀴The time I spent upon the island is still so horrible a thought to me,
3597𓀴that I must pass it lightly over. In all the books I have read of people
3598𓀴cast away, they had either their pockets full of tools, or a chest of
3599𓀴things would be thrown upon the beach along with them, as if on purpose.
3600𓀴My case was very different. I had nothing in my pockets but money and
3601𓀴Alan’s silver button; and being inland bred, I was as much short of
3602𓀴knowledge as of means.
3603𓀴
3604𓀴I knew indeed that shell-fish were counted good to eat; and among the
3605𓀴rocks of the isle I found a great plenty of limpets, which at first I
3606𓀴could scarcely strike from their places, not knowing quickness to be
3607𓀴needful. There were, besides, some of the little shells that we call
3608𓀴buckies; I think periwinkle is the English name. Of these two I made my
3609𓀴whole diet, devouring them cold and raw as I found them; and so hungry
3610𓀴was I, that at first they seemed to me delicious.
3611𓀴
3612𓀴Perhaps they were out of season, or perhaps there was something wrong
3613𓀴in the sea about my island. But at least I had no sooner eaten my first
3614𓀴meal than I was seized with giddiness and retching, and lay for a long
3615𓀴time no better than dead. A second trial of the same food (indeed I had
3616𓀴no other) did better with me, and revived my strength. But as long as
3617𓀴I was on the island, I never knew what to expect when I had eaten;
3618𓀴sometimes all was well, and sometimes I was thrown into a miserable
3619𓀴sickness; nor could I ever distinguish what particular fish it was that
3620𓀴hurt me.
3621𓀴
3622𓀴All day it streamed rain; the island ran like a sop, there was no dry
3623𓀴spot to be found; and when I lay down that night, between two boulders
3624𓀴that made a kind of roof, my feet were in a bog.
3625𓀴
3626𓀴The second day I crossed the island to all sides. There was no one part
3627𓀴of it better than another; it was all desolate and rocky; nothing living
3628𓀴on it but game birds which I lacked the means to kill, and the gulls
3629𓀴which haunted the outlying rocks in a prodigious number. But the creek,
3630𓀴or strait, that cut off the isle from the main-land of the Ross, opened
3631𓀴out on the north into a bay, and the bay again opened into the Sound of
3632𓀴Iona; and it was the neighbourhood of this place that I chose to be my
3633𓀴home; though if I had thought upon the very name of home in such a spot,
3634𓀴I must have burst out weeping.
3635𓀴
3636𓀴I had good reasons for my choice. There was in this part of the isle a
3637𓀴little hut of a house like a pig’s hut, where fishers used to sleep when
3638𓀴they came there upon their business; but the turf roof of it had fallen
3639𓀴entirely in; so that the hut was of no use to me, and gave me less
3640𓀴shelter than my rocks. What was more important, the shell-fish on which
3641𓀴I lived grew there in great plenty; when the tide was out I could gather
3642𓀴a peck at a time: and this was doubtless a convenience. But the other
3643𓀴reason went deeper. I had become in no way used to the horrid solitude
3644𓀴of the isle, but still looked round me on all sides (like a man that
3645𓀴was hunted), between fear and hope that I might see some human creature
3646𓀴coming. Now, from a little up the hillside over the bay, I could catch a
3647𓀴sight of the great, ancient church and the roofs of the people’s houses
3648𓀴in Iona. And on the other hand, over the low country of the Ross, I saw
3649𓀴smoke go up, morning and evening, as if from a homestead in a hollow of
3650𓀴the land.
3651𓀴
3652𓀴I used to watch this smoke, when I was wet and cold, and had my head
3653𓀴half turned with loneliness; and think of the fireside and the
3654𓀴company, till my heart burned. It was the same with the roofs of Iona.
3655𓀴Altogether, this sight I had of men’s homes and comfortable lives,
3656𓀴although it put a point on my own sufferings, yet it kept hope alive,
3657𓀴and helped me to eat my raw shell-fish (which had soon grown to be a
3658𓀴disgust), and saved me from the sense of horror I had whenever I was
3659𓀴quite alone with dead rocks, and fowls, and the rain, and the cold sea.
3660𓀴
3661𓀴I say it kept hope alive; and indeed it seemed impossible that I should
3662𓀴be left to die on the shores of my own country, and within view of a
3663𓀴church-tower and the smoke of men’s houses. But the second day passed;
3664𓀴and though as long as the light lasted I kept a bright look-out for
3665𓀴boats on the Sound or men passing on the Ross, no help came near me. It
3666𓀴still rained, and I turned in to sleep, as wet as ever, and with a cruel
3667𓀴sore throat, but a little comforted, perhaps, by having said good-night
3668𓀴to my next neighbours, the people of Iona.
3669𓀴
3670𓀴Charles the Second declared a man could stay outdoors more days in the
3671𓀴year in the climate of England than in any other. This was very like a
3672𓀴king, with a palace at his back and changes of dry clothes. But he must
3673𓀴have had better luck on his flight from Worcester than I had on that
3674𓀴miserable isle. It was the height of the summer; yet it rained for more
3675𓀴than twenty-four hours, and did not clear until the afternoon of the
3676𓀴third day.
3677𓀴
3678𓀴This was the day of incidents. In the morning I saw a red deer, a buck
3679𓀴with a fine spread of antlers, standing in the rain on the top of the
3680𓀴island; but he had scarce seen me rise from under my rock, before
3681𓀴he trotted off upon the other side. I supposed he must have swum the
3682𓀴strait; though what should bring any creature to Earraid, was more than
3683𓀴I could fancy.
3684𓀴
3685𓀴A little after, as I was jumping about after my limpets, I was startled
3686𓀴by a guinea-piece, which fell upon a rock in front of me and glanced off
3687𓀴into the sea. When the sailors gave me my money again, they kept back
3688𓀴not only about a third of the whole sum, but my father’s leather purse;
3689𓀴so that from that day out, I carried my gold loose in a pocket with a
3690𓀴button. I now saw there must be a hole, and clapped my hand to the place
3691𓀴in a great hurry. But this was to lock the stable door after the steed
3692𓀴was stolen. I had left the shore at Queensferry with near on fifty
3693𓀴pounds; now I found no more than two guinea-pieces and a silver
3694𓀴shilling.
3695𓀴
3696𓀴It is true I picked up a third guinea a little after, where it lay
3697𓀴shining on a piece of turf. That made a fortune of three pounds and four
3698𓀴shillings, English money, for a lad, the rightful heir of an estate, and
3699𓀴now starving on an isle at the extreme end of the wild Highlands.
3700𓀴
3701𓀴This state of my affairs dashed me still further; and, indeed my plight
3702𓀴on that third morning was truly pitiful. My clothes were beginning to
3703𓀴rot; my stockings in particular were quite worn through, so that my
3704𓀴shanks went naked; my hands had grown quite soft with the continual
3705𓀴soaking; my throat was very sore, my strength had much abated, and my
3706𓀴heart so turned against the horrid stuff I was condemned to eat, that
3707𓀴the very sight of it came near to sicken me.
3708𓀴
3709𓀴And yet the worst was not yet come.
3710𓀴
3711𓀴There is a pretty high rock on the northwest of Earraid, which (because
3712𓀴it had a flat top and overlooked the Sound) I was much in the habit of
3713𓀴frequenting; not that ever I stayed in one place, save when asleep, my
3714𓀴misery giving me no rest. Indeed, I wore myself down with continual and
3715𓀴aimless goings and comings in the rain.
3716𓀴
3717𓀴As soon, however, as the sun came out, I lay down on the top of that
3718𓀴rock to dry myself. The comfort of the sunshine is a thing I cannot
3719𓀴tell. It set me thinking hopefully of my deliverance, of which I had
3720𓀴begun to despair; and I scanned the sea and the Ross with a fresh
3721𓀴interest. On the south of my rock, a part of the island jutted out and
3722𓀴hid the open ocean, so that a boat could thus come quite near me upon
3723𓀴that side, and I be none the wiser.
3724𓀴
3725𓀴Well, all of a sudden, a coble with a brown sail and a pair of fishers
3726𓀴aboard of it, came flying round that corner of the isle, bound for Iona.
3727𓀴I shouted out, and then fell on my knees on the rock and reached up my
3728𓀴hands and prayed to them. They were near enough to hear--I could even
3729𓀴see the colour of their hair; and there was no doubt but they observed
3730𓀴me, for they cried out in the Gaelic tongue, and laughed. But the boat
3731𓀴never turned aside, and flew on, right before my eyes, for Iona.
3732𓀴
3733𓀴I could not believe such wickedness, and ran along the shore from rock
3734𓀴to rock, crying on them piteously even after they were out of reach
3735𓀴of my voice, I still cried and waved to them; and when they were quite
3736𓀴gone, I thought my heart would have burst. All the time of my troubles
3737𓀴I wept only twice. Once, when I could not reach the yard, and now, the
3738𓀴second time, when these fishers turned a deaf ear to my cries. But this
3739𓀴time I wept and roared like a wicked child, tearing up the turf with
3740𓀴my nails, and grinding my face in the earth. If a wish would kill men,
3741𓀴those two fishers would never have seen morning, and I should likely
3742𓀴have died upon my island.
3743𓀴
3744𓀴When I was a little over my anger, I must eat again, but with such
3745𓀴loathing of the mess as I could now scarce control. Sure enough, I
3746𓀴should have done as well to fast, for my fishes poisoned me again. I had
3747𓀴all my first pains; my throat was so sore I could scarce swallow; I had
3748𓀴a fit of strong shuddering, which clucked my teeth together; and there
3749𓀴came on me that dreadful sense of illness, which we have no name for
3750𓀴either in Scotch or English. I thought I should have died, and made my
3751𓀴peace with God, forgiving all men, even my uncle and the fishers; and as
3752𓀴soon as I had thus made up my mind to the worst, clearness came upon me;
3753𓀴I observed the night was falling dry; my clothes were dried a good deal;
3754𓀴truly, I was in a better case than ever before, since I had landed on
3755𓀴the isle; and so I got to sleep at last, with a thought of gratitude.
3756𓀴
3757𓀴The next day (which was the fourth of this horrible life of mine) I
3758𓀴found my bodily strength run very low. But the sun shone, the air was
3759𓀴sweet, and what I managed to eat of the shell-fish agreed well with me
3760𓀴and revived my courage.
3761𓀴
3762𓀴I was scarce back on my rock (where I went always the first thing after
3763𓀴I had eaten) before I observed a boat coming down the Sound, and with
3764𓀴her head, as I thought, in my direction.
3765𓀴
3766𓀴I began at once to hope and fear exceedingly; for I thought these men
3767𓀴might have thought better of their cruelty and be coming back to my
3768𓀴assistance. But another disappointment, such as yesterday’s, was more
3769𓀴than I could bear. I turned my back, accordingly, upon the sea, and
3770𓀴did not look again till I had counted many hundreds. The boat was still
3771𓀴heading for the island. The next time I counted the full thousand, as
3772𓀴slowly as I could, my heart beating so as to hurt me. And then it was
3773𓀴out of all question. She was coming straight to Earraid!
3774𓀴
3775𓀴I could no longer hold myself back, but ran to the seaside and out,
3776𓀴from one rock to another, as far as I could go. It is a marvel I was not
3777𓀴drowned; for when I was brought to a stand at last, my legs shook under
3778𓀴me, and my mouth was so dry, I must wet it with the sea-water before I
3779𓀴was able to shout.
3780𓀴
3781𓀴All this time the boat was coming on; and now I was able to perceive
3782𓀴it was the same boat and the same two men as yesterday. This I knew by
3783𓀴their hair, which the one had of a bright yellow and the other black.
3784𓀴But now there was a third man along with them, who looked to be of a
3785𓀴better class.
3786𓀴
3787𓀴As soon as they were come within easy speech, they let down their sail
3788𓀴and lay quiet. In spite of my supplications, they drew no nearer in, and
3789𓀴what frightened me most of all, the new man tee-hee’d with laughter as
3790𓀴he talked and looked at me.
3791𓀴
3792𓀴Then he stood up in the boat and addressed me a long while, speaking
3793𓀴fast and with many wavings of his hand. I told him I had no Gaelic; and
3794𓀴at this he became very angry, and I began to suspect he thought he was
3795𓀴talking English. Listening very close, I caught the word “whateffer”
3796𓀴 several times; but all the rest was Gaelic and might have been Greek and
3797𓀴Hebrew for me.
3798𓀴
3799𓀴“Whatever,” said I, to show him I had caught a word.
3800𓀴
3801𓀴“Yes, yes--yes, yes,” says he, and then he looked at the other men, as
3802𓀴much as to say, “I told you I spoke English,” and began again as hard as
3803𓀴ever in the Gaelic.
3804𓀴
3805𓀴This time I picked out another word, “tide.” Then I had a flash of hope.
3806𓀴I remembered he was always waving his hand towards the mainland of the
3807𓀴Ross.
3808𓀴
3809𓀴“Do you mean when the tide is out--?” I cried, and could not finish.
3810𓀴
3811𓀴“Yes, yes,” said he. “Tide.”
3812𓀴
3813𓀴At that I turned tail upon their boat (where my adviser had once more
3814𓀴begun to tee-hee with laughter), leaped back the way I had come, from
3815𓀴one stone to another, and set off running across the isle as I had never
3816𓀴run before. In about half an hour I came out upon the shores of the
3817𓀴creek; and, sure enough, it was shrunk into a little trickle of water,
3818𓀴through which I dashed, not above my knees, and landed with a shout on
3819𓀴the main island.
3820𓀴
3821𓀴A sea-bred boy would not have stayed a day on Earraid; which is only
3822𓀴what they call a tidal islet, and except in the bottom of the neaps, can
3823𓀴be entered and left twice in every twenty-four hours, either dry-shod,
3824𓀴or at the most by wading. Even I, who had the tide going out and in
3825𓀴before me in the bay, and even watched for the ebbs, the better to get
3826𓀴my shellfish--even I (I say) if I had sat down to think, instead of
3827𓀴raging at my fate, must have soon guessed the secret, and got free. It
3828𓀴was no wonder the fishers had not understood me. The wonder was rather
3829𓀴that they had ever guessed my pitiful illusion, and taken the trouble to
3830𓀴come back. I had starved with cold and hunger on that island for close
3831𓀴upon one hundred hours. But for the fishers, I might have left my bones
3832𓀴there, in pure folly. And even as it was, I had paid for it pretty dear,
3833𓀴not only in past sufferings, but in my present case; being clothed like
3834𓀴a beggar-man, scarce able to walk, and in great pain of my sore throat.
3835𓀴
3836𓀴I have seen wicked men and fools, a great many of both; and I believe
3837𓀴they both get paid in the end; but the fools first.
3838𓀴
3839𓀴
3840𓀴
3841𓀴
3842𓀴CHAPTER XV
3843𓀴
3844𓀴THE LAD WITH THE SILVER BUTTON: THROUGH THE ISLE OF MULL
3845𓀴
3846𓀴
3847𓀴The Ross of Mull, which I had now got upon, was rugged and trackless,
3848𓀴like the isle I had just left; being all bog, and brier, and big stone.
3849𓀴There may be roads for them that know that country well; but for my part
3850𓀴I had no better guide than my own nose, and no other landmark than Ben
3851𓀴More.
3852𓀴
3853𓀴I aimed as well as I could for the smoke I had seen so often from the
3854𓀴island; and with all my great weariness and the difficulty of the way
3855𓀴came upon the house in the bottom of a little hollow about five or
3856𓀴six at night. It was low and longish, roofed with turf and built of
3857𓀴unmortared stones; and on a mound in front of it, an old gentleman sat
3858𓀴smoking his pipe in the sun.
3859𓀴
3860𓀴With what little English he had, he gave me to understand that my
3861𓀴shipmates had got safe ashore, and had broken bread in that very house
3862𓀴on the day after.
3863𓀴
3864𓀴“Was there one,” I asked, “dressed like a gentleman?”
3865𓀴
3866𓀴He said they all wore rough great-coats; but to be sure, the first of
3867𓀴them, the one that came alone, wore breeches and stockings, while the
3868𓀴rest had sailors’ trousers.
3869𓀴
3870𓀴“Ah,” said I, “and he would have a feathered hat?”
3871𓀴
3872𓀴He told me, no, that he was bareheaded like myself.
3873𓀴
3874𓀴At first I thought Alan might have lost his hat; and then the rain came
3875𓀴in my mind, and I judged it more likely he had it out of harm’s way
3876𓀴under his great-coat. This set me smiling, partly because my friend was
3877𓀴safe, partly to think of his vanity in dress.
3878𓀴
3879𓀴And then the old gentleman clapped his hand to his brow, and cried out
3880𓀴that I must be the lad with the silver button.
3881𓀴
3882𓀴“Why, yes!” said I, in some wonder.
3883𓀴
3884𓀴“Well, then,” said the old gentleman, “I have a word for you, that you
3885𓀴are to follow your friend to his country, by Torosay.”
3886𓀴
3887𓀴He then asked me how I had fared, and I told him my tale. A
3888𓀴south-country man would certainly have laughed; but this old gentleman
3889𓀴(I call him so because of his manners, for his clothes were dropping off
3890𓀴his back) heard me all through with nothing but gravity and pity. When I
3891𓀴had done, he took me by the hand, led me into his hut (it was no better)
3892𓀴and presented me before his wife, as if she had been the Queen and I a
3893𓀴duke.
3894𓀴
3895𓀴The good woman set oat-bread before me and a cold grouse, patting my
3896𓀴shoulder and smiling to me all the time, for she had no English; and the
3897𓀴old gentleman (not to be behind) brewed me a strong punch out of their
3898𓀴country spirit. All the while I was eating, and after that when I was
3899𓀴drinking the punch, I could scarce come to believe in my good fortune;
3900𓀴and the house, though it was thick with the peat-smoke and as full of
3901𓀴holes as a colander, seemed like a palace.
3902𓀴
3903𓀴The punch threw me in a strong sweat and a deep slumber; the good people
3904𓀴let me lie; and it was near noon of the next day before I took the road,
3905𓀴my throat already easier and my spirits quite restored by good fare and
3906𓀴good news. The old gentleman, although I pressed him hard, would take no
3907𓀴money, and gave me an old bonnet for my head; though I am free to own I
3908𓀴was no sooner out of view of the house than I very jealously washed this
3909𓀴gift of his in a wayside fountain.
3910𓀴
3911𓀴Thought I to myself: “If these are the wild Highlanders, I could wish my
3912𓀴own folk wilder.”
3913𓀴
3914𓀴I not only started late, but I must have wandered nearly half the time.
3915𓀴True, I met plenty of people, grubbing in little miserable fields that
3916𓀴would not keep a cat, or herding little kine about the bigness of asses.
3917𓀴The Highland dress being forbidden by law since the rebellion, and the
3918𓀴people condemned to the Lowland habit, which they much disliked, it was
3919𓀴strange to see the variety of their array. Some went bare, only for a
3920𓀴hanging cloak or great-coat, and carried their trousers on their backs
3921𓀴like a useless burthen: some had made an imitation of the tartan with
3922𓀴little parti-coloured stripes patched together like an old wife’s quilt;
3923𓀴others, again, still wore the Highland philabeg, but by putting a few
3924𓀴stitches between the legs transformed it into a pair of trousers like
3925𓀴a Dutchman’s. All those makeshifts were condemned and punished, for the
3926𓀴law was harshly applied, in hopes to break up the clan spirit; but in
3927𓀴that out-of-the-way, sea-bound isle, there were few to make remarks and
3928𓀴fewer to tell tales.
3929𓀴
3930𓀴They seemed in great poverty; which was no doubt natural, now that
3931𓀴rapine was put down, and the chiefs kept no longer an open house;
3932𓀴and the roads (even such a wandering, country by-track as the one
3933𓀴I followed) were infested with beggars. And here again I marked
3934𓀴a difference from my own part of the country. For our Lowland
3935𓀴beggars--even the gownsmen themselves, who beg by patent--had a louting,
3936𓀴flattering way with them, and if you gave them a plaek and asked change,
3937𓀴would very civilly return you a boddle. But these Highland beggars stood
3938𓀴on their dignity, asked alms only to buy snuff (by their account) and
3939𓀴would give no change.
3940𓀴
3941𓀴To be sure, this was no concern of mine, except in so far as it
3942𓀴entertained me by the way. What was much more to the purpose, few had
3943𓀴any English, and these few (unless they were of the brotherhood of
3944𓀴beggars) not very anxious to place it at my service. I knew Torosay
3945𓀴to be my destination, and repeated the name to them and pointed; but
3946𓀴instead of simply pointing in reply, they would give me a screed of the
3947𓀴Gaelic that set me foolish; so it was small wonder if I went out of my
3948𓀴road as often as I stayed in it.
3949𓀴
3950𓀴At last, about eight at night, and already very weary, I came to a lone
3951𓀴house, where I asked admittance, and was refused, until I bethought
3952𓀴me of the power of money in so poor a country, and held up one of my
3953𓀴guineas in my finger and thumb. Thereupon, the man of the house, who had
3954𓀴hitherto pretended to have no English, and driven me from his door by
3955𓀴signals, suddenly began to speak as clearly as was needful, and agreed
3956𓀴for five shillings to give me a night’s lodging and guide me the next
3957𓀴day to Torosay.
3958𓀴
3959𓀴I slept uneasily that night, fearing I should be robbed; but I might
3960𓀴have spared myself the pain; for my host was no robber, only miserably
3961𓀴poor and a great cheat. He was not alone in his poverty; for the next
3962𓀴morning, we must go five miles about to the house of what he called a
3963𓀴rich man to have one of my guineas changed. This was perhaps a rich man
3964𓀴for Mull; he would have scarce been thought so in the south; for it
3965𓀴took all he had--the whole house was turned upside down, and a neighbour
3966𓀴brought under contribution, before he could scrape together twenty
3967𓀴shillings in silver. The odd shilling he kept for himself, protesting he
3968𓀴could ill afford to have so great a sum of money lying “locked up.” For
3969𓀴all that he was very courteous and well spoken, made us both sit down
3970𓀴with his family to dinner, and brewed punch in a fine china bowl, over
3971𓀴which my rascal guide grew so merry that he refused to start.
3972𓀴
3973𓀴I was for getting angry, and appealed to the rich man (Hector Maclean
3974𓀴was his name), who had been a witness to our bargain and to my payment
3975𓀴of the five shillings. But Maclean had taken his share of the punch,
3976𓀴and vowed that no gentleman should leave his table after the bowl was
3977𓀴brewed; so there was nothing for it but to sit and hear Jacobite toasts
3978𓀴and Gaelic songs, till all were tipsy and staggered off to the bed or
3979𓀴the barn for their night’s rest.
3980𓀴
3981𓀴Next day (the fourth of my travels) we were up before five upon the
3982𓀴clock; but my rascal guide got to the bottle at once, and it was three
3983𓀴hours before I had him clear of the house, and then (as you shall hear)
3984𓀴only for a worse disappointment.
3985𓀴
3986𓀴As long as we went down a heathery valley that lay before Mr. Maclean’s
3987𓀴house, all went well; only my guide looked constantly over his shoulder,
3988𓀴and when I asked him the cause, only grinned at me. No sooner, however,
3989𓀴had we crossed the back of a hill, and got out of sight of the house
3990𓀴windows, than he told me Torosay lay right in front, and that a hill-top
3991𓀴(which he pointed out) was my best landmark.
3992𓀴
3993𓀴“I care very little for that,” said I, “since you are going with me.”
3994𓀴
3995𓀴The impudent cheat answered me in the Gaelic that he had no English.
3996𓀴
3997𓀴“My fine fellow,” I said, “I know very well your English comes and goes.
3998𓀴Tell me what will bring it back? Is it more money you wish?”
3999𓀴
4000𓀴“Five shillings mair,” said he, “and hersel’ will bring ye there.”
4001𓀴
4002𓀴I reflected awhile and then offered him two, which he accepted greedily,
4003𓀴and insisted on having in his hands at once “for luck,” as he said, but
4004𓀴I think it was rather for my misfortune.
4005𓀴
4006𓀴The two shillings carried him not quite as many miles; at the end of
4007𓀴which distance, he sat down upon the wayside and took off his brogues
4008𓀴from his feet, like a man about to rest.
4009𓀴
4010𓀴I was now red-hot. “Ha!” said I, “have you no more English?”
4011𓀴
4012𓀴He said impudently, “No.”
4013𓀴
4014𓀴At that I boiled over, and lifted my hand to strike him; and he, drawing
4015𓀴a knife from his rags, squatted back and grinned at me like a wildcat.
4016𓀴At that, forgetting everything but my anger, I ran in upon him, put
4017𓀴aside his knife with my left, and struck him in the mouth with the
4018𓀴right. I was a strong lad and very angry, and he but a little man; and
4019𓀴he went down before me heavily. By good luck, his knife flew out of his
4020𓀴hand as he fell.
4021𓀴
4022𓀴I picked up both that and his brogues, wished him a good morning, and
4023𓀴set off upon my way, leaving him barefoot and disarmed. I chuckled to
4024𓀴myself as I went, being sure I was done with that rogue, for a variety
4025𓀴of reasons. First, he knew he could have no more of my money; next, the
4026𓀴brogues were worth in that country only a few pence; and, lastly, the
4027𓀴knife, which was really a dagger, it was against the law for him to
4028𓀴carry.
4029𓀴
4030𓀴In about half an hour of walk, I overtook a great, ragged man, moving
4031𓀴pretty fast but feeling before him with a staff. He was quite blind, and
4032𓀴told me he was a catechist, which should have put me at my ease. But
4033𓀴his face went against me; it seemed dark and dangerous and secret; and
4034𓀴presently, as we began to go on alongside, I saw the steel butt of a
4035𓀴pistol sticking from under the flap of his coat-pocket. To carry such a
4036𓀴thing meant a fine of fifteen pounds sterling upon a first offence, and
4037𓀴transportation to the colonies upon a second. Nor could I quite see why
4038𓀴a religious teacher should go armed, or what a blind man could be doing
4039𓀴with a pistol.
4040𓀴
4041𓀴I told him about my guide, for I was proud of what I had done, and my
4042𓀴vanity for once got the heels of my prudence. At the mention of the
4043𓀴five shillings he cried out so loud that I made up my mind I should say
4044𓀴nothing of the other two, and was glad he could not see my blushes.
4045𓀴
4046𓀴“Was it too much?” I asked, a little faltering.
4047𓀴
4048𓀴“Too much!” cries he. “Why, I will guide you to Torosay myself for a
4049𓀴dram of brandy. And give you the great pleasure of my company (me that
4050𓀴is a man of some learning) in the bargain.”
4051𓀴
4052𓀴I said I did not see how a blind man could be a guide; but at that he
4053𓀴laughed aloud, and said his stick was eyes enough for an eagle.
4054𓀴
4055𓀴“In the Isle of Mull, at least,” says he, “where I know every stone and
4056𓀴heather-bush by mark of head. See, now,” he said, striking right and
4057𓀴left, as if to make sure, “down there a burn is running; and at the head
4058𓀴of it there stands a bit of a small hill with a stone cocked upon the
4059𓀴top of that; and it’s hard at the foot of the hill, that the way runs by
4060𓀴to Torosay; and the way here, being for droves, is plainly trodden, and
4061𓀴will show grassy through the heather.”
4062𓀴
4063𓀴I had to own he was right in every feature, and told my wonder.
4064𓀴
4065𓀴“Ha!” says he, “that’s nothing. Would ye believe me now, that before
4066𓀴the Act came out, and when there were weepons in this country, I could
4067𓀴shoot? Ay, could I!” cries he, and then with a leer: “If ye had such a
4068𓀴thing as a pistol here to try with, I would show ye how it’s done.”
4069𓀴
4070𓀴I told him I had nothing of the sort, and gave him a wider berth. If
4071𓀴he had known, his pistol stuck at that time quite plainly out of his
4072𓀴pocket, and I could see the sun twinkle on the steel of the butt. But
4073𓀴by the better luck for me, he knew nothing, thought all was covered, and
4074𓀴lied on in the dark.
4075𓀴
4076𓀴He then began to question me cunningly, where I came from, whether I
4077𓀴was rich, whether I could change a five-shilling piece for him (which
4078𓀴he declared he had that moment in his sporran), and all the time he kept
4079𓀴edging up to me and I avoiding him. We were now upon a sort of green
4080𓀴cattle-track which crossed the hills towards Torosay, and we kept
4081𓀴changing sides upon that like dancers in a reel. I had so plainly the
4082𓀴upper-hand that my spirits rose, and indeed I took a pleasure in this
4083𓀴game of blindman’s buff; but the catechist grew angrier and angrier,
4084𓀴and at last began to swear in Gaelic and to strike for my legs with his
4085𓀴staff.
4086𓀴
4087𓀴Then I told him that, sure enough, I had a pistol in my pocket as well
4088𓀴as he, and if he did not strike across the hill due south I would even
4089𓀴blow his brains out.
4090𓀴
4091𓀴He became at once very polite, and after trying to soften me for some
4092𓀴time, but quite in vain, he cursed me once more in Gaelic and took
4093𓀴himself off. I watched him striding along, through bog and brier,
4094𓀴tapping with his stick, until he turned the end of a hill and
4095𓀴disappeared in the next hollow. Then I struck on again for Torosay, much
4096𓀴better pleased to be alone than to travel with that man of learning.
4097𓀴This was an unlucky day; and these two, of whom I had just rid myself,
4098𓀴one after the other, were the two worst men I met with in the Highlands.
4099𓀴
4100𓀴At Torosay, on the Sound of Mull and looking over to the mainland
4101𓀴of Morven, there was an inn with an innkeeper, who was a Maclean, it
4102𓀴appeared, of a very high family; for to keep an inn is thought even more
4103𓀴genteel in the Highlands than it is with us, perhaps as partaking of
4104𓀴hospitality, or perhaps because the trade is idle and drunken. He spoke
4105𓀴good English, and finding me to be something of a scholar, tried me
4106𓀴first in French, where he easily beat me, and then in the Latin, in
4107𓀴which I don’t know which of us did best. This pleasant rivalry put us at
4108𓀴once upon friendly terms; and I sat up and drank punch with him (or to
4109𓀴be more correct, sat up and watched him drink it), until he was so tipsy
4110𓀴that he wept upon my shoulder.
4111𓀴
4112𓀴I tried him, as if by accident, with a sight of Alan’s button; but it
4113𓀴was plain he had never seen or heard of it. Indeed, he bore some grudge
4114𓀴against the family and friends of Ardshiel, and before he was drunk
4115𓀴he read me a lampoon, in very good Latin, but with a very ill meaning,
4116𓀴which he had made in elegiac verses upon a person of that house.
4117𓀴
4118𓀴When I told him of my catechist, he shook his head, and said I was lucky
4119𓀴to have got clear off. “That is a very dangerous man,” he said; “Duncan
4120𓀴Mackiegh is his name; he can shoot by the ear at several yards, and has
4121𓀴been often accused of highway robberies, and once of murder.”
4122𓀴
4123𓀴“The cream of it is,” says I, “that he called himself a catechist.”
4124𓀴
4125𓀴“And why should he not?” says he, “when that is what he is. It was
4126𓀴Maclean of Duart gave it to him because he was blind. But perhaps it was
4127𓀴a peety,” says my host, “for he is always on the road, going from
4128𓀴one place to another to hear the young folk say their religion; and,
4129𓀴doubtless, that is a great temptation to the poor man.”
4130𓀴
4131𓀴At last, when my landlord could drink no more, he showed me to a bed,
4132𓀴and I lay down in very good spirits; having travelled the greater part
4133𓀴of that big and crooked Island of Mull, from Earraid to Torosay, fifty
4134𓀴miles as the crow flies, and (with my wanderings) much nearer a hundred,
4135𓀴in four days and with little fatigue. Indeed I was by far in better
4136𓀴heart and health of body at the end of that long tramp than I had been
4137𓀴at the beginning.
4138𓀴
4139𓀴
4140𓀴
4141𓀴
4142𓀴CHAPTER XVI
4143𓀴
4144𓀴THE LAD WITH THE SILVER BUTTON: ACROSS MORVEN
4145𓀴
4146𓀴
4147𓀴There is a regular ferry from Torosay to Kinlochaline on the mainland.
4148𓀴Both shores of the Sound are in the country of the strong clan of the
4149𓀴Macleans, and the people that passed the ferry with me were almost all
4150𓀴of that clan. The skipper of the boat, on the other hand, was called
4151𓀴Neil Roy Macrob; and since Macrob was one of the names of Alan’s
4152𓀴clansmen, and Alan himself had sent me to that ferry, I was eager to
4153𓀴come to private speech of Neil Roy.
4154𓀴
4155𓀴In the crowded boat this was of course impossible, and the passage was
4156𓀴a very slow affair. There was no wind, and as the boat was wretchedly
4157𓀴equipped, we could pull but two oars on one side, and one on the other.
4158𓀴The men gave way, however, with a good will, the passengers taking
4159𓀴spells to help them, and the whole company giving the time in
4160𓀴Gaelic boat-songs. And what with the songs, and the sea-air, and the
4161𓀴good-nature and spirit of all concerned, and the bright weather, the
4162𓀴passage was a pretty thing to have seen.
4163𓀴
4164𓀴But there was one melancholy part. In the mouth of Loch Aline we found
4165𓀴a great sea-going ship at anchor; and this I supposed at first to be one
4166𓀴of the King’s cruisers which were kept along that coast, both summer
4167𓀴and winter, to prevent communication with the French. As we got a little
4168𓀴nearer, it became plain she was a ship of merchandise; and what still
4169𓀴more puzzled me, not only her decks, but the sea-beach also, were quite
4170𓀴black with people, and skiffs were continually plying to and fro between
4171𓀴them. Yet nearer, and there began to come to our ears a great sound
4172𓀴of mourning, the people on board and those on the shore crying and
4173𓀴lamenting one to another so as to pierce the heart.
4174𓀴
4175𓀴Then I understood this was an emigrant ship bound for the American
4176𓀴colonies.
4177𓀴
4178𓀴We put the ferry-boat alongside, and the exiles leaned over the
4179𓀴bulwarks, weeping and reaching out their hands to my fellow-passengers,
4180𓀴among whom they counted some near friends. How long this might have gone
4181𓀴on I do not know, for they seemed to have no sense of time: but at last
4182𓀴the captain of the ship, who seemed near beside himself (and no great
4183𓀴wonder) in the midst of this crying and confusion, came to the side and
4184𓀴begged us to depart.
4185𓀴
4186𓀴Thereupon Neil sheered off; and the chief singer in our boat struck into
4187𓀴a melancholy air, which was presently taken up both by the emigrants and
4188𓀴their friends upon the beach, so that it sounded from all sides like a
4189𓀴lament for the dying. I saw the tears run down the cheeks of the men and
4190𓀴women in the boat, even as they bent at the oars; and the circumstances
4191𓀴and the music of the song (which is one called “Lochaber no more”) were
4192𓀴highly affecting even to myself.
4193𓀴
4194𓀴At Kinlochaline I got Neil Roy upon one side on the beach, and said I
4195𓀴made sure he was one of Appin’s men.
4196𓀴
4197𓀴“And what for no?” said he.
4198𓀴
4199𓀴“I am seeking somebody,” said I; “and it comes in my mind that you will
4200𓀴have news of him. Alan Breck Stewart is his name.” And very foolishly,
4201𓀴instead of showing him the button, I sought to pass a shilling in his
4202𓀴hand.
4203𓀴
4204𓀴At this he drew back. “I am very much affronted,” he said; “and this is
4205𓀴not the way that one shentleman should behave to another at all. The man
4206𓀴you ask for is in France; but if he was in my sporran,” says he, “and
4207𓀴your belly full of shillings, I would not hurt a hair upon his body.”
4208𓀴
4209𓀴I saw I had gone the wrong way to work, and without wasting time upon
4210𓀴apologies, showed him the button lying in the hollow of my palm.
4211𓀴
4212𓀴“Aweel, aweel,” said Neil; “and I think ye might have begun with that
4213𓀴end of the stick, whatever! But if ye are the lad with the silver
4214𓀴button, all is well, and I have the word to see that ye come safe. But
4215𓀴if ye will pardon me to speak plainly,” says he, “there is a name that
4216𓀴you should never take into your mouth, and that is the name of Alan
4217𓀴Breck; and there is a thing that ye would never do, and that is to offer
4218𓀴your dirty money to a Hieland shentleman.”
4219𓀴
4220𓀴It was not very easy to apologise; for I could scarce tell him (what was
4221𓀴the truth) that I had never dreamed he would set up to be a gentleman
4222𓀴until he told me so. Neil on his part had no wish to prolong his
4223𓀴dealings with me, only to fulfil his orders and be done with it; and
4224𓀴he made haste to give me my route. This was to lie the night in
4225𓀴Kinlochaline in the public inn; to cross Morven the next day to Ardgour,
4226𓀴and lie the night in the house of one John of the Claymore, who was
4227𓀴warned that I might come; the third day, to be set across one loch at
4228𓀴Corran and another at Balachulish, and then ask my way to the house of
4229𓀴James of the Glens, at Aucharn in Duror of Appin. There was a good deal
4230𓀴of ferrying, as you hear; the sea in all this part running deep into the
4231𓀴mountains and winding about their roots. It makes the country strong to
4232𓀴hold and difficult to travel, but full of prodigious wild and dreadful
4233𓀴prospects.
4234𓀴
4235𓀴I had some other advice from Neil: to speak with no one by the way, to
4236𓀴avoid Whigs, Campbells, and the “red-soldiers;” to leave the road and
4237𓀴lie in a bush if I saw any of the latter coming, “for it was never
4238𓀴chancy to meet in with them;” and in brief, to conduct myself like a
4239𓀴robber or a Jacobite agent, as perhaps Neil thought me.
4240𓀴
4241𓀴The inn at Kinlochaline was the most beggarly vile place that ever pigs
4242𓀴were styed in, full of smoke, vermin, and silent Highlanders. I was not
4243𓀴only discontented with my lodging, but with myself for my mismanagement
4244𓀴of Neil, and thought I could hardly be worse off. But very wrongly, as I
4245𓀴was soon to see; for I had not been half an hour at the inn (standing in
4246𓀴the door most of the time, to ease my eyes from the peat smoke) when a
4247𓀴thunderstorm came close by, the springs broke in a little hill on which
4248𓀴the inn stood, and one end of the house became a running water. Places
4249𓀴of public entertainment were bad enough all over Scotland in those days;
4250𓀴yet it was a wonder to myself, when I had to go from the fireside to the
4251𓀴bed in which I slept, wading over the shoes.
4252𓀴
4253𓀴Early in my next day’s journey I overtook a little, stout, solemn man,
4254𓀴walking very slowly with his toes turned out, sometimes reading in
4255𓀴a book and sometimes marking the place with his finger, and dressed
4256𓀴decently and plainly in something of a clerical style.
4257𓀴
4258𓀴This I found to be another catechist, but of a different order from the
4259𓀴blind man of Mull: being indeed one of those sent out by the Edinburgh
4260𓀴Society for Propagating Christian Knowledge, to evangelise the more
4261𓀴savage places of the Highlands. His name was Henderland; he spoke with
4262𓀴the broad south-country tongue, which I was beginning to weary for the
4263𓀴sound of; and besides common countryship, we soon found we had a
4264𓀴more particular bond of interest. For my good friend, the minister of
4265𓀴Essendean, had translated into the Gaelic in his by-time a number of
4266𓀴hymns and pious books which Henderland used in his work, and held in
4267𓀴great esteem. Indeed, it was one of these he was carrying and reading
4268𓀴when we met.
4269𓀴
4270𓀴We fell in company at once, our ways lying together as far as to
4271𓀴Kingairloch. As we went, he stopped and spoke with all the wayfarers
4272𓀴and workers that we met or passed; and though of course I could not tell
4273𓀴what they discoursed about, yet I judged Mr. Henderland must be well
4274𓀴liked in the countryside, for I observed many of them to bring out their
4275𓀴mulls and share a pinch of snuff with him.
4276𓀴
4277𓀴I told him as far in my affairs as I judged wise; as far, that is,
4278𓀴as they were none of Alan’s; and gave Balachulish as the place I was
4279𓀴travelling to, to meet a friend; for I thought Aucharn, or even Duror,
4280𓀴would be too particular, and might put him on the scent.
4281𓀴
4282𓀴On his part, he told me much of his work and the people he worked among,
4283𓀴the hiding priests and Jacobites, the Disarming Act, the dress, and many
4284𓀴other curiosities of the time and place. He seemed moderate; blaming
4285𓀴Parliament in several points, and especially because they had framed the
4286𓀴Act more severely against those who wore the dress than against those
4287𓀴who carried weapons.
4288𓀴
4289𓀴This moderation put it in my mind to question him of the Red Fox and the
4290𓀴Appin tenants; questions which, I thought, would seem natural enough in
4291𓀴the mouth of one travelling to that country.
4292𓀴
4293𓀴
4294𓀴
4295𓀴He said it was a bad business. “It’s wonderful,” said he, “where the
4296𓀴tenants find the money, for their life is mere starvation. (Ye don’t
4297𓀴carry such a thing as snuff, do ye, Mr. Balfour? No. Well, I’m better
4298𓀴wanting it.) But these tenants (as I was saying) are doubtless partly
4299𓀴driven to it. James Stewart in Duror (that’s him they call James of the
4300𓀴Glens) is half-brother to Ardshiel, the captain of the clan; and he is
4301𓀴a man much looked up to, and drives very hard. And then there’s one they
4302𓀴call Alan Breck--”
4303𓀴
4304𓀴“Ah!” I cried, “what of him?”
4305𓀴
4306𓀴“What of the wind that bloweth where it listeth?” said Henderland. “He’s
4307𓀴here and awa; here to-day and gone to-morrow: a fair heather-cat. He
4308𓀴might be glowering at the two of us out of yon whin-bush, and I wouldnae
4309𓀴wonder! Ye’ll no carry such a thing as snuff, will ye?”
4310𓀴
4311𓀴I told him no, and that he had asked the same thing more than once.
4312𓀴
4313𓀴“It’s highly possible,” said he, sighing. “But it seems strange ye
4314𓀴shouldnae carry it. However, as I was saying, this Alan Breck is a bold,
4315𓀴desperate customer, and well kent to be James’s right hand. His life
4316𓀴is forfeit already; he would boggle at naething; and maybe, if a
4317𓀴tenant-body was to hang back he would get a dirk in his wame.”
4318𓀴
4319𓀴“You make a poor story of it all, Mr. Henderland,” said I. “If it is all
4320𓀴fear upon both sides, I care to hear no more of it.”
4321𓀴
4322𓀴“Na,” said Mr. Henderland, “but there’s love too, and self-denial that
4323𓀴should put the like of you and me to shame. There’s something fine about
4324𓀴it; no perhaps Christian, but humanly fine. Even Alan Breck, by all that
4325𓀴I hear, is a chield to be respected. There’s many a lying sneck-draw
4326𓀴sits close in kirk in our own part of the country, and stands well in
4327𓀴the world’s eye, and maybe is a far worse man, Mr. Balfour, than yon
4328𓀴misguided shedder of man’s blood. Ay, ay, we might take a lesson by
4329𓀴them.--Ye’ll perhaps think I’ve been too long in the Hielands?” he
4330𓀴added, smiling to me.
4331𓀴
4332𓀴I told him not at all; that I had seen much to admire among the
4333𓀴Highlanders; and if he came to that, Mr. Campbell himself was a
4334𓀴Highlander.
4335𓀴
4336𓀴“Ay,” said he, “that’s true. It’s a fine blood.”
4337𓀴
4338𓀴“And what is the King’s agent about?” I asked.
4339𓀴
4340𓀴“Colin Campbell?” says Henderland. “Putting his head in a bees’ byke!”
4341𓀴
4342𓀴“He is to turn the tenants out by force, I hear?” said I.
4343𓀴
4344𓀴“Yes,” says he, “but the business has gone back and forth, as folk say.
4345𓀴First, James of the Glens rode to Edinburgh, and got some lawyer (a
4346𓀴Stewart, nae doubt--they all hing together like bats in a steeple) and
4347𓀴had the proceedings stayed. And then Colin Campbell cam’ in again, and
4348𓀴had the upper-hand before the Barons of Exchequer. And now they tell me
4349𓀴the first of the tenants are to flit to-morrow. It’s to begin at Duror
4350𓀴under James’s very windows, which doesnae seem wise by my humble way of
4351𓀴it.”
4352𓀴
4353𓀴“Do you think they’ll fight?” I asked.
4354𓀴
4355𓀴“Well,” says Henderland, “they’re disarmed--or supposed to be--for
4356𓀴there’s still a good deal of cold iron lying by in quiet places. And
4357𓀴then Colin Campbell has the sogers coming. But for all that, if I was
4358𓀴his lady wife, I wouldnae be well pleased till I got him home again.
4359𓀴They’re queer customers, the Appin Stewarts.”
4360𓀴
4361𓀴I asked if they were worse than their neighbours.
4362𓀴
4363𓀴“No they,” said he. “And that’s the worst part of it. For if Colin Roy
4364𓀴can get his business done in Appin, he has it all to begin again in the
4365𓀴next country, which they call Mamore, and which is one of the countries
4366𓀴of the Camerons. He’s King’s Factor upon both, and from both he has to
4367𓀴drive out the tenants; and indeed, Mr. Balfour (to be open with ye),
4368𓀴it’s my belief that if he escapes the one lot, he’ll get his death by
4369𓀴the other.”
4370𓀴
4371𓀴So we continued talking and walking the great part of the day; until
4372𓀴at last, Mr. Henderland after expressing his delight in my company, and
4373𓀴satisfaction at meeting with a friend of Mr. Campbell’s (“whom,” says
4374𓀴he, “I will make bold to call that sweet singer of our covenanted
4375𓀴Zion”), proposed that I should make a short stage, and lie the night in
4376𓀴his house a little beyond Kingairloch. To say truth, I was overjoyed;
4377𓀴for I had no great desire for John of the Claymore, and since my double
4378𓀴misadventure, first with the guide and next with the gentleman skipper,
4379𓀴I stood in some fear of any Highland stranger. Accordingly we shook
4380𓀴hands upon the bargain, and came in the afternoon to a small house,
4381𓀴standing alone by the shore of the Linnhe Loch. The sun was already gone
4382𓀴from the desert mountains of Ardgour upon the hither side, but shone on
4383𓀴those of Appin on the farther; the loch lay as still as a lake, only
4384𓀴the gulls were crying round the sides of it; and the whole place seemed
4385𓀴solemn and uncouth.
4386𓀴
4387𓀴We had no sooner come to the door of Mr. Henderland’s dwelling, than to
4388𓀴my great surprise (for I was now used to the politeness of Highlanders)
4389𓀴he burst rudely past me, dashed into the room, caught up a jar and
4390𓀴a small horn-spoon, and began ladling snuff into his nose in most
4391𓀴excessive quantities. Then he had a hearty fit of sneezing, and looked
4392𓀴round upon me with a rather silly smile.
4393𓀴
4394𓀴“It’s a vow I took,” says he. “I took a vow upon me that I wouldnae
4395𓀴carry it. Doubtless it’s a great privation; but when I think upon
4396𓀴the martyrs, not only to the Scottish Covenant but to other points of
4397𓀴Christianity, I think shame to mind it.”
4398𓀴
4399𓀴As soon as we had eaten (and porridge and whey was the best of the good
4400𓀴man’s diet) he took a grave face and said he had a duty to perform by
4401𓀴Mr. Campbell, and that was to inquire into my state of mind towards God.
4402𓀴I was inclined to smile at him since the business of the snuff; but he
4403𓀴had not spoken long before he brought the tears into my eyes. There are
4404𓀴two things that men should never weary of, goodness and humility; we get
4405𓀴none too much of them in this rough world among cold, proud people; but
4406𓀴Mr. Henderland had their very speech upon his tongue. And though I was a
4407𓀴good deal puffed up with my adventures and with having come off, as the
4408𓀴saying is, with flying colours; yet he soon had me on my knees beside a
4409𓀴simple, poor old man, and both proud and glad to be there.
4410𓀴
4411𓀴Before we went to bed he offered me sixpence to help me on my way, out
4412𓀴of a scanty store he kept in the turf wall of his house; at which excess
4413𓀴of goodness I knew not what to do. But at last he was so earnest with me
4414𓀴that I thought it the more mannerly part to let him have his way, and so
4415𓀴left him poorer than myself.
4416𓀴
4417𓀴
4418𓀴
4419𓀴
4420𓀴CHAPTER XVII
4421𓀴
4422𓀴THE DEATH OF THE RED FOX
4423𓀴
4424𓀴
4425𓀴The next day Mr. Henderland found for me a man who had a boat of his own
4426𓀴and was to cross the Linnhe Loch that afternoon into Appin, fishing. Him
4427𓀴he prevailed on to take me, for he was one of his flock; and in this way
4428𓀴I saved a long day’s travel and the price of the two public ferries I
4429𓀴must otherwise have passed.
4430𓀴
4431𓀴It was near noon before we set out; a dark day with clouds, and the sun
4432𓀴shining upon little patches. The sea was here very deep and still,
4433𓀴and had scarce a wave upon it; so that I must put the water to my lips
4434𓀴before I could believe it to be truly salt. The mountains on either side
4435𓀴were high, rough and barren, very black and gloomy in the shadow of
4436𓀴the clouds, but all silver-laced with little watercourses where the sun
4437𓀴shone upon them. It seemed a hard country, this of Appin, for people to
4438𓀴care as much about as Alan did.
4439𓀴
4440𓀴There was but one thing to mention. A little after we had started,
4441𓀴the sun shone upon a little moving clump of scarlet close in along the
4442𓀴water-side to the north. It was much of the same red as soldiers’ coats;
4443𓀴every now and then, too, there came little sparks and lightnings, as
4444𓀴though the sun had struck upon bright steel.
4445𓀴
4446𓀴I asked my boatman what it should be, and he answered he supposed it was
