4447𓀴some of the red soldiers coming from Fort William into Appin, against
4448𓀴the poor tenantry of the country. Well, it was a sad sight to me;
4449𓀴and whether it was because of my thoughts of Alan, or from something
4450𓀴prophetic in my bosom, although this was but the second time I had seen
4451𓀴King George’s troops, I had no good will to them.
4452𓀴
4453𓀴At last we came so near the point of land at the entering in of Loch
4454𓀴Leven that I begged to be set on shore. My boatman (who was an honest
4455𓀴fellow and mindful of his promise to the catechist) would fain have
4456𓀴carried me on to Balachulish; but as this was to take me farther from my
4457𓀴secret destination, I insisted, and was set on shore at last under the
4458𓀴wood of Lettermore (or Lettervore, for I have heard it both ways) in
4459𓀴Alan’s country of Appin.
4460𓀴
4461𓀴This was a wood of birches, growing on a steep, craggy side of a
4462𓀴mountain that overhung the loch. It had many openings and ferny howes;
4463𓀴and a road or bridle track ran north and south through the midst of
4464𓀴it, by the edge of which, where was a spring, I sat down to eat some
4465𓀴oat-bread of Mr. Henderland’s and think upon my situation.
4466𓀴
4467𓀴Here I was not only troubled by a cloud of stinging midges, but far more
4468𓀴by the doubts of my mind. What I ought to do, why I was going to join
4469𓀴myself with an outlaw and a would-be murderer like Alan, whether I
4470𓀴should not be acting more like a man of sense to tramp back to the south
4471𓀴country direct, by my own guidance and at my own charges, and what Mr.
4472𓀴Campbell or even Mr. Henderland would think of me if they should ever
4473𓀴learn my folly and presumption: these were the doubts that now began to
4474𓀴come in on me stronger than ever.
4475𓀴
4476𓀴As I was so sitting and thinking, a sound of men and horses came to me
4477𓀴through the wood; and presently after, at a turning of the road, I saw
4478𓀴four travellers come into view. The way was in this part so rough and
4479𓀴narrow that they came single and led their horses by the reins. The
4480𓀴first was a great, red-headed gentleman, of an imperious and flushed
4481𓀴face, who carried his hat in his hand and fanned himself, for he was in
4482𓀴a breathing heat. The second, by his decent black garb and white wig,
4483𓀴I correctly took to be a lawyer. The third was a servant, and wore some
4484𓀴part of his clothes in tartan, which showed that his master was of a
4485𓀴Highland family, and either an outlaw or else in singular good odour
4486𓀴with the Government, since the wearing of tartan was against the Act. If
4487𓀴I had been better versed in these things, I would have known the tartan
4488𓀴to be of the Argyle (or Campbell) colours. This servant had a good-sized
4489𓀴portmanteau strapped on his horse, and a net of lemons (to brew punch
4490𓀴with) hanging at the saddle-bow; as was often enough the custom with
4491𓀴luxurious travellers in that part of the country.
4492𓀴
4493𓀴As for the fourth, who brought up the tail, I had seen his like before,
4494𓀴and knew him at once to be a sheriff’s officer.
4495𓀴
4496𓀴I had no sooner seen these people coming than I made up my mind (for no
4497𓀴reason that I can tell) to go through with my adventure; and when the
4498𓀴first came alongside of me, I rose up from the bracken and asked him the
4499𓀴way to Aucharn.
4500𓀴
4501𓀴He stopped and looked at me, as I thought, a little oddly; and then,
4502𓀴turning to the lawyer, “Mungo,” said he, “there’s many a man would think
4503𓀴this more of a warning than two pyats. Here am I on my road to Duror on
4504𓀴the job ye ken; and here is a young lad starts up out of the bracken,
4505𓀴and speers if I am on the way to Aucharn.”
4506𓀴
4507𓀴“Glenure,” said the other, “this is an ill subject for jesting.”
4508𓀴
4509𓀴These two had now drawn close up and were gazing at me, while the two
4510𓀴followers had halted about a stone-cast in the rear.
4511𓀴
4512𓀴“And what seek ye in Aucharn?” said Colin Roy Campbell of Glenure, him
4513𓀴they called the Red Fox; for he it was that I had stopped.
4514𓀴
4515𓀴“The man that lives there,” said I.
4516𓀴
4517𓀴“James of the Glens,” says Glenure, musingly; and then to the lawyer:
4518𓀴“Is he gathering his people, think ye?”
4519𓀴
4520𓀴“Anyway,” says the lawyer, “we shall do better to bide where we are, and
4521𓀴let the soldiers rally us.”
4522𓀴
4523𓀴“If you are concerned for me,” said I, “I am neither of his people nor
4524𓀴yours, but an honest subject of King George, owing no man and fearing no
4525𓀴man.”
4526𓀴
4527𓀴“Why, very well said,” replies the Factor. “But if I may make so bold as
4528𓀴ask, what does this honest man so far from his country? and why does
4529𓀴he come seeking the brother of Ardshiel? I have power here, I must tell
4530𓀴you. I am King’s Factor upon several of these estates, and have twelve
4531𓀴files of soldiers at my back.”
4532𓀴
4533𓀴“I have heard a waif word in the country,” said I, a little nettled,
4534𓀴“that you were a hard man to drive.”
4535𓀴
4536𓀴He still kept looking at me, as if in doubt.
4537𓀴
4538𓀴“Well,” said he, at last, “your tongue is bold; but I am no unfriend to
4539𓀴plainness. If ye had asked me the way to the door of James Stewart on
4540𓀴any other day but this, I would have set ye right and bidden ye God
4541𓀴speed. But to-day--eh, Mungo?” And he turned again to look at the
4542𓀴lawyer.
4543𓀴
4544𓀴But just as he turned there came the shot of a firelock from higher up
4545𓀴the hill; and with the very sound of it Glenure fell upon the road.
4546𓀴
4547𓀴“O, I am dead!” he cried, several times over.
4548𓀴
4549𓀴The lawyer had caught him up and held him in his arms, the servant
4550𓀴standing over and clasping his hands. And now the wounded man looked
4551𓀴from one to another with scared eyes, and there was a change in his
4552𓀴voice, that went to the heart.
4553𓀴
4554𓀴“Take care of yourselves,” says he. “I am dead.”
4555𓀴
4556𓀴He tried to open his clothes as if to look for the wound, but his
4557𓀴fingers slipped on the buttons. With that he gave a great sigh, his head
4558𓀴rolled on his shoulder, and he passed away.
4559𓀴
4560𓀴The lawyer said never a word, but his face was as sharp as a pen and
4561𓀴as white as the dead man’s; the servant broke out into a great noise of
4562𓀴crying and weeping, like a child; and I, on my side, stood staring at
4563𓀴them in a kind of horror. The sheriff’s officer had run back at the
4564𓀴first sound of the shot, to hasten the coming of the soldiers.
4565𓀴
4566𓀴At last the lawyer laid down the dead man in his blood upon the road,
4567𓀴and got to his own feet with a kind of stagger.
4568𓀴
4569𓀴I believe it was his movement that brought me to my senses; for he had
4570𓀴no sooner done so than I began to scramble up the hill, crying out, “The
4571𓀴murderer! the murderer!”
4572𓀴
4573𓀴So little a time had elapsed, that when I got to the top of the first
4574𓀴steepness, and could see some part of the open mountain, the murderer
4575𓀴was still moving away at no great distance. He was a big man, in a black
4576𓀴coat, with metal buttons, and carried a long fowling-piece.
4577𓀴
4578𓀴“Here!” I cried. “I see him!”
4579𓀴
4580𓀴At that the murderer gave a little, quick look over his shoulder, and
4581𓀴began to run. The next moment he was lost in a fringe of birches; then
4582𓀴he came out again on the upper side, where I could see him climbing like
4583𓀴a jackanapes, for that part was again very steep; and then he dipped
4584𓀴behind a shoulder, and I saw him no more.
4585𓀴
4586𓀴All this time I had been running on my side, and had got a good way up,
4587𓀴when a voice cried upon me to stand.
4588𓀴
4589𓀴I was at the edge of the upper wood, and so now, when I halted and
4590𓀴looked back, I saw all the open part of the hill below me.
4591𓀴
4592𓀴The lawyer and the sheriff’s officer were standing just above the road,
4593𓀴crying and waving on me to come back; and on their left, the red-coats,
4594𓀴musket in hand, were beginning to struggle singly out of the lower wood.
4595𓀴
4596𓀴“Why should I come back?” I cried. “Come you on!”
4597𓀴
4598𓀴“Ten pounds if ye take that lad!” cried the lawyer. “He’s an accomplice.
4599𓀴He was posted here to hold us in talk.”
4600𓀴
4601𓀴At that word (which I could hear quite plainly, though it was to the
4602𓀴soldiers and not to me that he was crying it) my heart came in my mouth
4603𓀴with quite a new kind of terror. Indeed, it is one thing to stand the
4604𓀴danger of your life, and quite another to run the peril of both life and
4605𓀴character. The thing, besides, had come so suddenly, like thunder out of
4606𓀴a clear sky, that I was all amazed and helpless.
4607𓀴
4608𓀴The soldiers began to spread, some of them to run, and others to put up
4609𓀴their pieces and cover me; and still I stood.
4610𓀴
4611𓀴“Jouk[18] in here among the trees,” said a voice close by.
4612𓀴
4613𓀴     [18] Duck.
4614𓀴
4615𓀴Indeed, I scarce knew what I was doing, but I obeyed; and as I did so, I
4616𓀴heard the firelocks bang and the balls whistle in the birches.
4617𓀴
4618𓀴Just inside the shelter of the trees I found Alan Breck standing, with
4619𓀴a fishing-rod. He gave me no salutation; indeed it was no time for
4620𓀴civilities; only “Come!” says he, and set off running along the side of
4621𓀴the mountain towards Balachulish; and I, like a sheep, to follow him.
4622𓀴
4623𓀴Now we ran among the birches; now stooping behind low humps upon the
4624𓀴mountain-side; now crawling on all fours among the heather. The pace was
4625𓀴deadly: my heart seemed bursting against my ribs; and I had neither time
4626𓀴to think nor breath to speak with. Only I remember seeing with wonder,
4627𓀴that Alan every now and then would straighten himself to his full height
4628𓀴and look back; and every time he did so, there came a great far-away
4629𓀴cheering and crying of the soldiers.
4630𓀴
4631𓀴Quarter of an hour later, Alan stopped, clapped down flat in the
4632𓀴heather, and turned to me.
4633𓀴
4634𓀴“Now,” said he, “it’s earnest. Do as I do, for your life.”
4635𓀴
4636𓀴And at the same speed, but now with infinitely more precaution, we
4637𓀴traced back again across the mountain-side by the same way that we had
4638𓀴come, only perhaps higher; till at last Alan threw himself down in the
4639𓀴upper wood of Lettermore, where I had found him at the first, and lay,
4640𓀴with his face in the bracken, panting like a dog.
4641𓀴
4642𓀴My own sides so ached, my head so swam, my tongue so hung out of my
4643𓀴mouth with heat and dryness, that I lay beside him like one dead.
4644𓀴
4645𓀴
4646𓀴
4647𓀴
4648𓀴CHAPTER XVIII
4649𓀴
4650𓀴I TALK WITH ALAN IN THE WOOD OF LETTERMORE
4651𓀴
4652𓀴
4653𓀴Alan was the first to come round. He rose, went to the border of the
4654𓀴wood, peered out a little, and then returned and sat down.
4655𓀴
4656𓀴“Well,” said he, “yon was a hot burst, David.”
4657𓀴
4658𓀴I said nothing, nor so much as lifted my face. I had seen murder done,
4659𓀴and a great, ruddy, jovial gentleman struck out of life in a moment; the
4660𓀴pity of that sight was still sore within me, and yet that was but a part
4661𓀴of my concern. Here was murder done upon the man Alan hated; here was
4662𓀴Alan skulking in the trees and running from the troops; and whether his
4663𓀴was the hand that fired or only the head that ordered, signified
4664𓀴but little. By my way of it, my only friend in that wild country was
4665𓀴blood-guilty in the first degree; I held him in horror; I could not look
4666𓀴upon his face; I would have rather lain alone in the rain on my cold
4667𓀴isle, than in that warm wood beside a murderer.
4668𓀴
4669𓀴“Are ye still wearied?” he asked again.
4670𓀴
4671𓀴“No,” said I, still with my face in the bracken; “no, I am not wearied
4672𓀴now, and I can speak. You and me must twine,”[19] I said. “I liked you very
4673𓀴well, Alan, but your ways are not mine, and they’re not God’s: and the
4674𓀴short and the long of it is just that we must twine.”
4675𓀴
4676𓀴     [19] Part.
4677𓀴
4678𓀴“I will hardly twine from ye, David, without some kind of reason for
4679𓀴the same,” said Alan, mighty gravely. “If ye ken anything against
4680𓀴my reputation, it’s the least thing that ye should do, for old
4681𓀴acquaintance’ sake, to let me hear the name of it; and if ye have only
4682𓀴taken a distaste to my society, it will be proper for me to judge if I’m
4683𓀴insulted.”
4684𓀴
4685𓀴“Alan,” said I, “what is the sense of this? Ye ken very well yon
4686𓀴Campbell-man lies in his blood upon the road.”
4687𓀴
4688𓀴He was silent for a little; then says he, “Did ever ye hear tell of the
4689𓀴story of the Man and the Good People?”--by which he meant the fairies.
4690𓀴
4691𓀴“No,” said I, “nor do I want to hear it.”
4692𓀴
4693𓀴“With your permission, Mr. Balfour, I will tell it you, whatever,” says
4694𓀴Alan. “The man, ye should ken, was cast upon a rock in the sea, where
4695𓀴it appears the Good People were in use to come and rest as they went
4696𓀴through to Ireland. The name of this rock is called the Skerryvore, and
4697𓀴it’s not far from where we suffered ship-wreck. Well, it seems the man
4698𓀴cried so sore, if he could just see his little bairn before he died!
4699𓀴that at last the king of the Good People took peety upon him, and sent
4700𓀴one flying that brought back the bairn in a poke[20] and laid it down
4701𓀴beside the man where he lay sleeping. So when the man woke, there was a
4702𓀴poke beside him and something into the inside of it that moved. Well, it
4703𓀴seems he was one of these gentry that think aye the worst of things; and
4704𓀴for greater security, he stuck his dirk throughout that poke before he
4705𓀴opened it, and there was his bairn dead. I am thinking to myself, Mr.
4706𓀴Balfour, that you and the man are very much alike.”
4707𓀴
4708𓀴     [20] Bag.
4709𓀴
4710𓀴“Do you mean you had no hand in it?” cried I, sitting up.
4711𓀴
4712𓀴“I will tell you first of all, Mr. Balfour of Shaws, as one friend to
4713𓀴another,” said Alan, “that if I were going to kill a gentleman, it would
4714𓀴not be in my own country, to bring trouble on my clan; and I would not
4715𓀴go wanting sword and gun, and with a long fishing-rod upon my back.”
4716𓀴
4717𓀴“Well,” said I, “that’s true!”
4718𓀴
4719𓀴“And now,” continued Alan, taking out his dirk and laying his hand upon
4720𓀴it in a certain manner, “I swear upon the Holy Iron I had neither art
4721𓀴nor part, act nor thought in it.”
4722𓀴
4723𓀴“I thank God for that!” cried I, and offered him my hand.
4724𓀴
4725𓀴He did not appear to see it.
4726𓀴
4727𓀴“And here is a great deal of work about a Campbell!” said he. “They are
4728𓀴not so scarce, that I ken!”
4729𓀴
4730𓀴“At least,” said I, “you cannot justly blame me, for you know very
4731𓀴well what you told me in the brig. But the temptation and the act are
4732𓀴different, I thank God again for that. We may all be tempted; but
4733𓀴to take a life in cold blood, Alan!” And I could say no more for the
4734𓀴moment. “And do you know who did it?” I added. “Do you know that man in
4735𓀴the black coat?”
4736𓀴
4737𓀴“I have nae clear mind about his coat,” said Alan cunningly, “but it
4738𓀴sticks in my head that it was blue.”
4739𓀴
4740𓀴“Blue or black, did ye know him?” said I.
4741𓀴
4742𓀴“I couldnae just conscientiously swear to him,” says Alan. “He gaed very
4743𓀴close by me, to be sure, but it’s a strange thing that I should just
4744𓀴have been tying my brogues.”
4745𓀴
4746𓀴“Can you swear that you don’t know him, Alan?” I cried, half angered,
4747𓀴half in a mind to laugh at his evasions.
4748𓀴
4749𓀴“Not yet,” says he; “but I’ve a grand memory for forgetting, David.”
4750𓀴
4751𓀴“And yet there was one thing I saw clearly,” said I; “and that was, that
4752𓀴you exposed yourself and me to draw the soldiers.”
4753𓀴
4754𓀴“It’s very likely,” said Alan; “and so would any gentleman. You and me
4755𓀴were innocent of that transaction.”
4756𓀴
4757𓀴“The better reason, since we were falsely suspected, that we should get
4758𓀴clear,” I cried. “The innocent should surely come before the guilty.”
4759𓀴
4760𓀴“Why, David,” said he, “the innocent have aye a chance to get assoiled
4761𓀴in court; but for the lad that shot the bullet, I think the best place
4762𓀴for him will be the heather. Them that havenae dipped their hands in any
4763𓀴little difficulty, should be very mindful of the case of them that have.
4764𓀴And that is the good Christianity. For if it was the other way round
4765𓀴about, and the lad whom I couldnae just clearly see had been in our
4766𓀴shoes, and we in his (as might very well have been), I think we would be
4767𓀴a good deal obliged to him oursel’s if he would draw the soldiers.”
4768𓀴
4769𓀴When it came to this, I gave Alan up. But he looked so innocent all the
4770𓀴time, and was in such clear good faith in what he said, and so ready to
4771𓀴sacrifice himself for what he deemed his duty, that my mouth was closed.
4772𓀴Mr. Henderland’s words came back to me: that we ourselves might take a
4773𓀴lesson by these wild Highlanders. Well, here I had taken mine. Alan’s
4774𓀴morals were all tail-first; but he was ready to give his life for them,
4775𓀴such as they were.
4776𓀴
4777𓀴“Alan,” said I, “I’ll not say it’s the good Christianity as I understand
4778𓀴it, but it’s good enough. And here I offer ye my hand for the second
4779𓀴time.”
4780𓀴
4781𓀴Whereupon he gave me both of his, saying surely I had cast a spell upon
4782𓀴him, for he could forgive me anything. Then he grew very grave, and said
4783𓀴we had not much time to throw away, but must both flee that country: he,
4784𓀴because he was a deserter, and the whole of Appin would now be searched
4785𓀴like a chamber, and every one obliged to give a good account of himself;
4786𓀴and I, because I was certainly involved in the murder.
4787𓀴
4788𓀴“O!” says I, willing to give him a little lesson, “I have no fear of the
4789𓀴justice of my country.”
4790𓀴
4791𓀴“As if this was your country!” said he. “Or as if ye would be tried
4792𓀴here, in a country of Stewarts!”
4793𓀴
4794𓀴“It’s all Scotland,” said I.
4795𓀴
4796𓀴“Man, I whiles wonder at ye,” said Alan. “This is a Campbell that’s been
4797𓀴killed. Well, it’ll be tried in Inverara, the Campbells’ head place;
4798𓀴with fifteen Campbells in the jury-box and the biggest Campbell of all
4799𓀴(and that’s the Duke) sitting cocking on the bench. Justice, David?
4800𓀴The same justice, by all the world, as Glenure found awhile ago at the
4801𓀴roadside.”
4802𓀴
4803𓀴This frightened me a little, I confess, and would have frightened me
4804𓀴more if I had known how nearly exact were Alan’s predictions; indeed
4805𓀴it was but in one point that he exaggerated, there being but eleven
4806𓀴Campbells on the jury; though as the other four were equally in the
4807𓀴Duke’s dependence, it mattered less than might appear. Still, I cried
4808𓀴out that he was unjust to the Duke of Argyle, who (for all he was a
4809𓀴Whig) was yet a wise and honest nobleman.
4810𓀴
4811𓀴“Hoot!” said Alan, “the man’s a Whig, nae doubt; but I would never deny
4812𓀴he was a good chieftain to his clan. And what would the clan think if
4813𓀴there was a Campbell shot, and naebody hanged, and their own chief
4814𓀴the Justice General? But I have often observed,” says Alan, “that you
4815𓀴Low-country bodies have no clear idea of what’s right and wrong.”
4816𓀴
4817𓀴At this I did at last laugh out aloud, when to my surprise, Alan joined
4818𓀴in, and laughed as merrily as myself.
4819𓀴
4820𓀴“Na, na,” said he, “we’re in the Hielands, David; and when I tell ye
4821𓀴to run, take my word and run. Nae doubt it’s a hard thing to skulk and
4822𓀴starve in the Heather, but it’s harder yet to lie shackled in a red-coat
4823𓀴prison.”
4824𓀴
4825𓀴I asked him whither we should flee; and as he told me “to the Lowlands,”
4826𓀴 I was a little better inclined to go with him; for, indeed, I was
4827𓀴growing impatient to get back and have the upper-hand of my uncle.
4828𓀴Besides, Alan made so sure there would be no question of justice in the
4829𓀴matter, that I began to be afraid he might be right. Of all deaths, I
4830𓀴would truly like least to die by the gallows; and the picture of that
4831𓀴uncanny instrument came into my head with extraordinary clearness (as I
4832𓀴had once seen it engraved at the top of a pedlar’s ballad) and took away
4833𓀴my appetite for courts of justice.
4834𓀴
4835𓀴“I’ll chance it, Alan,” said I. “I’ll go with you.”
4836𓀴
4837𓀴“But mind you,” said Alan, “it’s no small thing. Ye maun lie bare and
4838𓀴hard, and brook many an empty belly. Your bed shall be the moorcock’s,
4839𓀴and your life shall be like the hunted deer’s, and ye shall sleep with
4840𓀴your hand upon your weapons. Ay, man, ye shall taigle many a weary foot,
4841𓀴or we get clear! I tell ye this at the start, for it’s a life that I ken
4842𓀴well. But if ye ask what other chance ye have, I answer: Nane. Either
4843𓀴take to the heather with me, or else hang.”
4844𓀴
4845𓀴“And that’s a choice very easily made,” said I; and we shook hands upon
4846𓀴it.
4847𓀴
4848𓀴“And now let’s take another keek at the red-coats,” says Alan, and he
4849𓀴led me to the north-eastern fringe of the wood.
4850𓀴
4851𓀴Looking out between the trees, we could see a great side of mountain,
4852𓀴running down exceeding steep into the waters of the loch. It was a rough
4853𓀴part, all hanging stone, and heather, and big scrogs of birchwood; and
4854𓀴away at the far end towards Balachulish, little wee red soldiers were
4855𓀴dipping up and down over hill and howe, and growing smaller every
4856𓀴minute. There was no cheering now, for I think they had other uses
4857𓀴for what breath was left them; but they still stuck to the trail, and
4858𓀴doubtless thought that we were close in front of them.
4859𓀴
4860𓀴Alan watched them, smiling to himself.
4861𓀴
4862𓀴“Ay,” said he, “they’ll be gey weary before they’ve got to the end of
4863𓀴that employ! And so you and me, David, can sit down and eat a bite, and
4864𓀴breathe a bit longer, and take a dram from my bottle. Then we’ll strike
4865𓀴for Aucharn, the house of my kinsman, James of the Glens, where I must
4866𓀴get my clothes, and my arms, and money to carry us along; and then,
4867𓀴David, we’ll cry, ‘Forth, Fortune!’ and take a cast among the heather.”
4868𓀴
4869𓀴So we sat again and ate and drank, in a place whence we could see the
4870𓀴sun going down into a field of great, wild, and houseless mountains,
4871𓀴such as I was now condemned to wander in with my companion. Partly as
4872𓀴we so sat, and partly afterwards, on the way to Aucharn, each of us
4873𓀴narrated his adventures; and I shall here set down so much of Alan’s as
4874𓀴seems either curious or needful.
4875𓀴
4876𓀴It appears he ran to the bulwarks as soon as the wave was passed; saw
4877𓀴me, and lost me, and saw me again, as I tumbled in the roost; and at
4878𓀴last had one glimpse of me clinging on the yard. It was this that put
4879𓀴him in some hope I would maybe get to land after all, and made him leave
4880𓀴those clues and messages which had brought me (for my sins) to that
4881𓀴unlucky country of Appin.
4882𓀴
4883𓀴In the meanwhile, those still on the brig had got the skiff launched,
4884𓀴and one or two were on board of her already, when there came a second
4885𓀴wave greater than the first, and heaved the brig out of her place, and
4886𓀴would certainly have sent her to the bottom, had she not struck and
4887𓀴caught on some projection of the reef. When she had struck first, it had
4888𓀴been bows-on, so that the stern had hitherto been lowest. But now her
4889𓀴stern was thrown in the air, and the bows plunged under the sea; and
4890𓀴with that, the water began to pour into the fore-scuttle like the
4891𓀴pouring of a mill-dam.
4892𓀴
4893𓀴It took the colour out of Alan’s face, even to tell what followed.
4894𓀴For there were still two men lying impotent in their bunks; and these,
4895𓀴seeing the water pour in and thinking the ship had foundered, began to
4896𓀴cry out aloud, and that with such harrowing cries that all who were on
4897𓀴deck tumbled one after another into the skiff and fell to their oars.
4898𓀴They were not two hundred yards away, when there came a third great sea;
4899𓀴and at that the brig lifted clean over the reef; her canvas filled for
4900𓀴a moment, and she seemed to sail in chase of them, but settling all the
4901𓀴while; and presently she drew down and down, as if a hand was drawing
4902𓀴her; and the sea closed over the _Covenant_ of Dysart.
4903𓀴
4904𓀴Never a word they spoke as they pulled ashore, being stunned with the
4905𓀴horror of that screaming; but they had scarce set foot upon the beach
4906𓀴when Hoseason woke up, as if out of a muse, and bade them lay hands upon
4907𓀴Alan. They hung back indeed, having little taste for the employment;
4908𓀴but Hoseason was like a fiend, crying that Alan was alone, that he had
4909𓀴a great sum about him, that he had been the means of losing the brig and
4910𓀴drowning all their comrades, and that here was both revenge and wealth
4911𓀴upon a single cast. It was seven against one; in that part of the shore
4912𓀴there was no rock that Alan could set his back to; and the sailors began
4913𓀴to spread out and come behind him.
4914𓀴
4915𓀴“And then,” said Alan, “the little man with the red head--I havenae mind
4916𓀴of the name that he is called.”
4917𓀴
4918𓀴“Riach,” said I.
4919𓀴
4920𓀴“Ay” said Alan, “Riach! Well, it was him that took up the clubs for me,
4921𓀴asked the men if they werenae feared of a judgment, and, says he ‘Dod,
4922𓀴I’ll put my back to the Hielandman’s mysel’.’ That’s none such an
4923𓀴entirely bad little man, yon little man with the red head,” said Alan.
4924𓀴“He has some spunks of decency.”
4925𓀴
4926𓀴“Well,” said I, “he was kind to me in his way.”
4927𓀴
4928𓀴“And so he was to Alan,” said he; “and by my troth, I found his way a
4929𓀴very good one! But ye see, David, the loss of the ship and the cries of
4930𓀴these poor lads sat very ill upon the man; and I’m thinking that would
4931𓀴be the cause of it.”
4932𓀴
4933𓀴“Well, I would think so,” says I; “for he was as keen as any of the rest
4934𓀴at the beginning. But how did Hoseason take it?”
4935𓀴
4936𓀴“It sticks in my mind that he would take it very ill,” says Alan. “But
4937𓀴the little man cried to me to run, and indeed I thought it was a good
4938𓀴observe, and ran. The last that I saw they were all in a knot upon the
4939𓀴beach, like folk that were not agreeing very well together.”
4940𓀴
4941𓀴“What do you mean by that?” said I.
4942𓀴
4943𓀴“Well, the fists were going,” said Alan; “and I saw one man go down like
4944𓀴a pair of breeks. But I thought it would be better no to wait. Ye see
4945𓀴there’s a strip of Campbells in that end of Mull, which is no good
4946𓀴company for a gentleman like me. If it hadnae been for that I would have
4947𓀴waited and looked for ye mysel’, let alone giving a hand to the little
4948𓀴man.” (It was droll how Alan dwelt on Mr. Riach’s stature, for, to say
4949𓀴the truth, the one was not much smaller than the other.) “So,” says he,
4950𓀴continuing, “I set my best foot forward, and whenever I met in with any
4951𓀴one I cried out there was a wreck ashore. Man, they didnae stop to fash
4952𓀴with me! Ye should have seen them linking for the beach! And when they
4953𓀴got there they found they had had the pleasure of a run, which is aye
4954𓀴good for a Campbell. I’m thinking it was a judgment on the clan that the
4955𓀴brig went down in the lump and didnae break. But it was a very unlucky
4956𓀴thing for you, that same; for if any wreck had come ashore they would
4957𓀴have hunted high and low, and would soon have found ye.”
4958𓀴
4959𓀴
4960𓀴
4961𓀴
4962𓀴CHAPTER XIX
4963𓀴
4964𓀴THE HOUSE OF FEAR
4965𓀴
4966𓀴
4967𓀴Night fell as we were walking, and the clouds, which had broken up in
4968𓀴the afternoon, settled in and thickened, so that it fell, for the
4969𓀴season of the year, extremely dark. The way we went was over rough
4970𓀴mountainsides; and though Alan pushed on with an assured manner, I could
4971𓀴by no means see how he directed himself.
4972𓀴
4973𓀴At last, about half-past ten of the clock, we came to the top of a brae,
4974𓀴and saw lights below us. It seemed a house door stood open and let out a
4975𓀴beam of fire and candle-light; and all round the house and steading
4976𓀴five or six persons were moving hurriedly about, each carrying a lighted
4977𓀴brand.
4978𓀴
4979𓀴“James must have tint his wits,” said Alan. “If this was the soldiers
4980𓀴instead of you and me, he would be in a bonny mess. But I dare say he’ll
4981𓀴have a sentry on the road, and he would ken well enough no soldiers
4982𓀴would find the way that we came.”
4983𓀴
4984𓀴Hereupon he whistled three times, in a particular manner. It was strange
4985𓀴to see how, at the first sound of it, all the moving torches came to
4986𓀴a stand, as if the bearers were affrighted; and how, at the third, the
4987𓀴bustle began again as before.
4988𓀴
4989𓀴Having thus set folks’ minds at rest, we came down the brae, and were
4990𓀴met at the yard gate (for this place was like a well-doing farm) by
4991𓀴a tall, handsome man of more than fifty, who cried out to Alan in the
4992𓀴Gaelic.
4993𓀴
4994𓀴“James Stewart,” said Alan, “I will ask ye to speak in Scotch, for here
4995𓀴is a young gentleman with me that has nane of the other. This is him,”
4996𓀴 he added, putting his arm through mine, “a young gentleman of the
4997𓀴Lowlands, and a laird in his country too, but I am thinking it will be
4998𓀴the better for his health if we give his name the go-by.”
4999𓀴
5000𓀴James of the Glens turned to me for a moment, and greeted me courteously
5001𓀴enough; the next he had turned to Alan.
5002𓀴
5003𓀴“This has been a dreadful accident,” he cried. “It will bring trouble on
5004𓀴the country.” And he wrung his hands.
5005𓀴
5006𓀴“Hoots!” said Alan, “ye must take the sour with the sweet, man. Colin
5007𓀴Roy is dead, and be thankful for that!”
5008𓀴
5009𓀴“Ay” said James, “and by my troth, I wish he was alive again! It’s all
5010𓀴very fine to blow and boast beforehand; but now it’s done, Alan; and
5011𓀴who’s to bear the wyte[21] of it? The accident fell out in Appin--mind ye
5012𓀴that, Alan; it’s Appin that must pay; and I am a man that has a family.”
5013𓀴
5014𓀴     [21] Blame.
5015𓀴
5016𓀴While this was going on I looked about me at the servants. Some were on
5017𓀴ladders, digging in the thatch of the house or the farm buildings,
5018𓀴from which they brought out guns, swords, and different weapons of
5019𓀴war; others carried them away; and by the sound of mattock blows from
5020𓀴somewhere farther down the brae, I suppose they buried them. Though they
5021𓀴were all so busy, there prevailed no kind of order in their efforts; men
5022𓀴struggled together for the same gun and ran into each other with their
5023𓀴burning torches; and James was continually turning about from his talk
5024𓀴with Alan, to cry out orders which were apparently never understood. The
5025𓀴faces in the torchlight were like those of people overborne with hurry
5026𓀴and panic; and though none spoke above his breath, their speech sounded
5027𓀴both anxious and angry.
5028𓀴
5029𓀴It was about this time that a lassie came out of the house carrying
5030𓀴a pack or bundle; and it has often made me smile to think how Alan’s
5031𓀴instinct awoke at the mere sight of it.
5032𓀴
5033𓀴“What’s that the lassie has?” he asked.
5034𓀴
5035𓀴“We’re just setting the house in order, Alan,” said James, in his
5036𓀴frightened and somewhat fawning way. “They’ll search Appin with candles,
5037𓀴and we must have all things straight. We’re digging the bit guns and
5038𓀴swords into the moss, ye see; and these, I am thinking, will be your ain
5039𓀴French clothes. We’ll be to bury them, I believe.”
5040𓀴
5041𓀴“Bury my French clothes!” cried Alan. “Troth, no!” And he laid hold upon
5042𓀴the packet and retired into the barn to shift himself, recommending me
5043𓀴in the meanwhile to his kinsman.
5044𓀴
5045𓀴James carried me accordingly into the kitchen, and sat down with me at
5046𓀴table, smiling and talking at first in a very hospitable manner. But
5047𓀴presently the gloom returned upon him; he sat frowning and biting his
5048𓀴fingers; only remembered me from time to time; and then gave me but a
5049𓀴word or two and a poor smile, and back into his private terrors. His
5050𓀴wife sat by the fire and wept, with her face in her hands; his eldest
5051𓀴son was crouched upon the floor, running over a great mass of papers and
5052𓀴now and again setting one alight and burning it to the bitter end; all
5053𓀴the while a servant lass with a red face was rummaging about the room,
5054𓀴in a blind hurry of fear, and whimpering as she went; and every now and
5055𓀴again one of the men would thrust in his face from the yard, and cry for
5056𓀴orders.
5057𓀴
5058𓀴At last James could keep his seat no longer, and begged my permission to
5059𓀴be so unmannerly as walk about. “I am but poor company altogether, sir,”
5060𓀴 says he, “but I can think of nothing but this dreadful accident, and the
5061𓀴trouble it is like to bring upon quite innocent persons.”
5062𓀴
5063𓀴A little after he observed his son burning a paper which he thought
5064𓀴should have been kept; and at that his excitement burst out so that it
5065𓀴was painful to witness. He struck the lad repeatedly.
5066𓀴
5067𓀴“Are you gone gyte?”[22] he cried. “Do you wish to hang your father?” and
5068𓀴forgetful of my presence, carried on at him a long time together in the
5069𓀴Gaelic, the young man answering nothing; only the wife, at the name of
5070𓀴hanging, throwing her apron over her face and sobbing out louder than
5071𓀴before.
5072𓀴
5073𓀴     [22] Mad.
5074𓀴
5075𓀴This was all wretched for a stranger like myself to hear and see; and
5076𓀴I was right glad when Alan returned, looking like himself in his fine
5077𓀴French clothes, though (to be sure) they were now grown almost too
5078𓀴battered and withered to deserve the name of fine. I was then taken out
5079𓀴in my turn by another of the sons, and given that change of clothing of
5080𓀴which I had stood so long in need, and a pair of Highland brogues made
5081𓀴of deer-leather, rather strange at first, but after a little practice
5082𓀴very easy to the feet.
5083𓀴
5084𓀴By the time I came back Alan must have told his story; for it seemed
5085𓀴understood that I was to fly with him, and they were all busy upon our
5086𓀴equipment. They gave us each a sword and pistols, though I professed my
5087𓀴inability to use the former; and with these, and some ammunition, a bag
5088𓀴of oatmeal, an iron pan, and a bottle of right French brandy, we were
5089𓀴ready for the heather. Money, indeed, was lacking. I had about two
5090𓀴guineas left; Alan’s belt having been despatched by another hand, that
5091𓀴trusty messenger had no more than seventeen-pence to his whole fortune;
5092𓀴and as for James, it appears he had brought himself so low with journeys
5093𓀴to Edinburgh and legal expenses on behalf of the tenants, that he could
5094𓀴only scrape together three-and-five-pence-halfpenny, the most of it in
5095𓀴coppers.
5096𓀴
5097𓀴“This’ll no do,” said Alan.
5098𓀴
5099𓀴“Ye must find a safe bit somewhere near by,” said James, “and get word
5100𓀴sent to me. Ye see, ye’ll have to get this business prettily off, Alan.
5101𓀴This is no time to be stayed for a guinea or two. They’re sure to get
5102𓀴wind of ye, sure to seek ye, and by my way of it, sure to lay on ye the
5103𓀴wyte of this day’s accident. If it falls on you, it falls on me that am
5104𓀴your near kinsman and harboured ye while ye were in the country. And if
5105𓀴it comes on me----” he paused, and bit his fingers, with a white face.
5106𓀴“It would be a painful thing for our friends if I was to hang,” said he.
5107𓀴
5108𓀴“It would be an ill day for Appin,” says Alan.
5109𓀴
5110𓀴“It’s a day that sticks in my throat,” said James. “O man, man, man--man
5111𓀴Alan! you and me have spoken like two fools!” he cried, striking his
5112𓀴hand upon the wall so that the house rang again.
5113𓀴
5114𓀴“Well, and that’s true, too,” said Alan; “and my friend from the
5115𓀴Lowlands here” (nodding at me) “gave me a good word upon that head, if I
5116𓀴would only have listened to him.”
5117𓀴
5118𓀴“But see here,” said James, returning to his former manner, “if they lay
5119𓀴me by the heels, Alan, it’s then that you’ll be needing the money. For
5120𓀴with all that I have said and that you have said, it will look very
5121𓀴black against the two of us; do ye mark that? Well, follow me out, and
5122𓀴ye’ll, I’ll see that I’ll have to get a paper out against ye mysel’;
5123𓀴have to offer a reward for ye; ay, will I! It’s a sore thing to do
5124𓀴between such near friends; but if I get the dirdum[23] of this dreadful
5125𓀴accident, I’ll have to fend for myself, man. Do ye see that?”
5126𓀴
5127𓀴     [23] Blame.
5128𓀴
5129𓀴He spoke with a pleading earnestness, taking Alan by the breast of the
5130𓀴coat.
5131𓀴
5132𓀴“Ay” said Alan, “I see that.”
5133𓀴
5134𓀴“And ye’ll have to be clear of the country, Alan--ay, and clear of
5135𓀴Scotland--you and your friend from the Lowlands, too. For I’ll have to
5136𓀴paper your friend from the Lowlands. Ye see that, Alan--say that ye see
5137𓀴that!”
5138𓀴
5139𓀴I thought Alan flushed a bit. “This is unco hard on me that brought him
5140𓀴here, James,” said he, throwing his head back. “It’s like making me a
5141𓀴traitor!”
5142𓀴
5143𓀴“Now, Alan, man!” cried James. “Look things in the face! He’ll be
5144𓀴papered anyway; Mungo Campbell’ll be sure to paper him; what matters
5145𓀴if I paper him too? And then, Alan, I am a man that has a family.” And
5146𓀴then, after a little pause on both sides, “And, Alan, it’ll be a jury of
5147𓀴Campbells,” said he.
5148𓀴
5149𓀴“There’s one thing,” said Alan, musingly, “that naebody kens his name.”
5150𓀴
5151𓀴“Nor yet they shallnae, Alan! There’s my hand on that,” cried James, for
5152𓀴all the world as if he had really known my name and was foregoing some
5153𓀴advantage. “But just the habit he was in, and what he looked like, and
5154𓀴his age, and the like? I couldnae well do less.”
5155𓀴
5156𓀴“I wonder at your father’s son,” cried Alan, sternly. “Would ye sell the
5157𓀴lad with a gift? Would ye change his clothes and then betray him?”
5158𓀴
5159𓀴“No, no, Alan,” said James. “No, no: the habit he took off--the habit
5160𓀴Mungo saw him in.” But I thought he seemed crestfallen; indeed, he was
5161𓀴clutching at every straw, and all the time, I dare say, saw the faces of
5162𓀴his hereditary foes on the bench, and in the jury-box, and the gallows
5163𓀴in the background.
5164𓀴
5165𓀴“Well, sir,” says Alan, turning to me, “what say ye to that? Ye are here
5166𓀴under the safeguard of my honour; and it’s my part to see nothing done
5167𓀴but what shall please you.”
5168𓀴
5169𓀴“I have but one word to say,” said I; “for to all this dispute I am a
5170𓀴perfect stranger. But the plain common-sense is to set the blame where
5171𓀴it belongs, and that is on the man who fired the shot. Paper him, as ye
5172𓀴call it, set the hunt on him; and let honest, innocent folk show their
5173𓀴faces in safety.” But at this both Alan and James cried out in horror;
5174𓀴bidding me hold my tongue, for that was not to be thought of; and asking
5175𓀴me what the Camerons would think? (which confirmed me, it must have been
5176𓀴a Cameron from Mamore that did the act) and if I did not see that the
5177𓀴lad might be caught? “Ye havenae surely thought of that?” said they,
5178𓀴with such innocent earnestness, that my hands dropped at my side and I
5179𓀴despaired of argument.
5180𓀴
5181𓀴“Very well, then,” said I, “paper me, if you please, paper Alan, paper
5182𓀴King George! We’re all three innocent, and that seems to be what’s
5183𓀴wanted. But at least, sir,” said I to James, recovering from my little
5184𓀴fit of annoyance, “I am Alan’s friend, and if I can be helpful to
5185𓀴friends of his, I will not stumble at the risk.”
5186𓀴
5187𓀴I thought it best to put a fair face on my consent, for I saw Alan
5188𓀴troubled; and, besides (thinks I to myself), as soon as my back is
5189𓀴turned, they will paper me, as they call it, whether I consent or not.
5190𓀴But in this I saw I was wrong; for I had no sooner said the words, than
5191𓀴Mrs. Stewart leaped out of her chair, came running over to us, and wept
5192𓀴first upon my neck and then on Alan’s, blessing God for our goodness to
5193𓀴her family.
5194𓀴
5195𓀴“As for you, Alan, it was no more than your bounden duty,” she said.
5196𓀴“But for this lad that has come here and seen us at our worst, and seen
5197𓀴the goodman fleeching like a suitor, him that by rights should give his
5198𓀴commands like any king--as for you, my lad,” she says, “my heart is wae
5199𓀴not to have your name, but I have your face; and as long as my heart
5200𓀴beats under my bosom, I will keep it, and think of it, and bless it.”
5201𓀴 And with that she kissed me, and burst once more into such sobbing, that
5202𓀴I stood abashed.
5203𓀴
5204𓀴“Hoot, hoot,” said Alan, looking mighty silly. “The day comes unco soon
5205𓀴in this month of July; and to-morrow there’ll be a fine to-do in Appin,
5206𓀴a fine riding of dragoons, and crying of ‘Cruachan!’[24] and running of
5207𓀴red-coats; and it behoves you and me to the sooner be gone.”
5208𓀴
5209𓀴     [24] The rallying-word of the Campbells.
5210𓀴
5211𓀴Thereupon we said farewell, and set out again, bending somewhat
5212𓀴eastwards, in a fine mild dark night, and over much the same broken
5213𓀴country as before.
5214𓀴
5215𓀴
5216𓀴
5217𓀴
5218𓀴CHAPTER XX
5219𓀴
5220𓀴THE FLIGHT IN THE HEATHER: THE ROCKS
5221𓀴
5222𓀴
5223𓀴Sometimes we walked, sometimes ran; and as it drew on to morning, walked
5224𓀴ever the less and ran the more. Though, upon its face, that country
5225𓀴appeared to be a desert, yet there were huts and houses of the people,
5226𓀴of which we must have passed more than twenty, hidden in quiet places of
5227𓀴the hills. When we came to one of these, Alan would leave me in the way,
5228𓀴and go himself and rap upon the side of the house and speak awhile at
5229𓀴the window with some sleeper awakened. This was to pass the news; which,
5230𓀴in that country, was so much of a duty that Alan must pause to attend to
5231𓀴it even while fleeing for his life; and so well attended to by others,
5232𓀴that in more than half of the houses where we called they had heard
5233𓀴already of the murder. In the others, as well as I could make out
5234𓀴(standing back at a distance and hearing a strange tongue), the news was
5235𓀴received with more of consternation than surprise.
5236𓀴
5237𓀴For all our hurry, day began to come in while we were still far from any
5238𓀴shelter. It found us in a prodigious valley, strewn with rocks and where
5239𓀴ran a foaming river. Wild mountains stood around it; there grew there
5240𓀴neither grass nor trees; and I have sometimes thought since then, that
5241𓀴it may have been the valley called Glencoe, where the massacre was in
5242𓀴the time of King William. But for the details of our itinerary, I am all
5243𓀴to seek; our way lying now by short cuts, now by great detours; our pace
5244𓀴being so hurried, our time of journeying usually by night; and the names
5245𓀴of such places as I asked and heard being in the Gaelic tongue and the
5246𓀴more easily forgotten.
5247𓀴
5248𓀴The first peep of morning, then, showed us this horrible place, and I
5249𓀴could see Alan knit his brow.
5250𓀴
5251𓀴“This is no fit place for you and me,” he said. “This is a place they’re
5252𓀴bound to watch.”
5253𓀴
5254𓀴And with that he ran harder than ever down to the water-side, in a part
5255𓀴where the river was split in two among three rocks. It went through with
5256𓀴a horrid thundering that made my belly quake; and there hung over the
5257𓀴lynn a little mist of spray. Alan looked neither to the right nor to the
5258𓀴left, but jumped clean upon the middle rock and fell there on his hands
5259𓀴and knees to check himself, for that rock was small and he might have
5260𓀴pitched over on the far side. I had scarce time to measure the distance
5261𓀴or to understand the peril before I had followed him, and he had caught
5262𓀴and stopped me.
5263𓀴
5264𓀴So there we stood, side by side upon a small rock slippery with spray,
5265𓀴a far broader leap in front of us, and the river dinning upon all sides.
5266𓀴When I saw where I was, there came on me a deadly sickness of fear,
5267𓀴and I put my hand over my eyes. Alan took me and shook me; I saw he
5268𓀴was speaking, but the roaring of the falls and the trouble of my mind
5269𓀴prevented me from hearing; only I saw his face was red with anger, and
5270𓀴that he stamped upon the rock. The same look showed me the water raging
5271𓀴by, and the mist hanging in the air: and with that I covered my eyes
5272𓀴again and shuddered.
5273𓀴
5274𓀴The next minute Alan had set the brandy bottle to my lips, and forced
5275𓀴me to drink about a gill, which sent the blood into my head again. Then,
5276𓀴putting his hands to his mouth, and his mouth to my ear, he shouted,
5277𓀴“Hang or drown!” and turning his back upon me, leaped over the farther
5278𓀴branch of the stream, and landed safe.
5279𓀴
5280𓀴I was now alone upon the rock, which gave me the more room; the brandy
5281𓀴was singing in my ears; I had this good example fresh before me, and
5282𓀴just wit enough to see that if I did not leap at once, I should never
5283𓀴leap at all. I bent low on my knees and flung myself forth, with
5284𓀴that kind of anger of despair that has sometimes stood me in stead of
5285𓀴courage. Sure enough, it was but my hands that reached the full length;
5286𓀴these slipped, caught again, slipped again; and I was sliddering back
5287𓀴into the lynn, when Alan seized me, first by the hair, then by the
5288𓀴collar, and with a great strain dragged me into safety.
5289𓀴
5290𓀴Never a word he said, but set off running again for his life, and I must
5291𓀴stagger to my feet and run after him. I had been weary before, but now
5292𓀴I was sick and bruised, and partly drunken with the brandy; I kept
5293𓀴stumbling as I ran, I had a stitch that came near to overmaster me; and
5294𓀴when at last Alan paused under a great rock that stood there among a
5295𓀴number of others, it was none too soon for David Balfour.
5296𓀴
5297𓀴A great rock I have said; but by rights it was two rocks leaning
5298𓀴together at the top, both some twenty feet high, and at the first sight
5299𓀴inaccessible. Even Alan (though you may say he had as good as four
5300𓀴hands) failed twice in an attempt to climb them; and it was only at the
5301𓀴third trial, and then by standing on my shoulders and leaping up with
5302𓀴such force as I thought must have broken my collar-bone, that he secured
5303𓀴a lodgment. Once there, he let down his leathern girdle; and with the
5304𓀴aid of that and a pair of shallow footholds in the rock, I scrambled up
5305𓀴beside him.
5306𓀴
5307𓀴Then I saw why we had come there; for the two rocks, being both somewhat
5308𓀴hollow on the top and sloping one to the other, made a kind of dish or
5309𓀴saucer, where as many as three or four men might have lain hidden.
5310𓀴
5311𓀴All this while Alan had not said a word, and had run and climbed with
5312𓀴such a savage, silent frenzy of hurry, that I knew that he was in mortal
5313𓀴fear of some miscarriage. Even now we were on the rock he said nothing,
5314𓀴nor so much as relaxed the frowning look upon his face; but clapped flat
5315𓀴down, and keeping only one eye above the edge of our place of shelter
5316𓀴scouted all round the compass. The dawn had come quite clear; we could
5317𓀴see the stony sides of the valley, and its bottom, which was bestrewed
5318𓀴with rocks, and the river, which went from one side to another, and made
5319𓀴white falls; but nowhere the smoke of a house, nor any living creature
5320𓀴but some eagles screaming round a cliff.
5321𓀴
5322𓀴Then at last Alan smiled.
5323𓀴
5324𓀴“Ay” said he, “now we have a chance;” and then looking at me with some
5325𓀴amusement, “Ye’re no very gleg[25] at the jumping,” said he.
5326𓀴
5327𓀴     [25] Brisk.
5328𓀴
5329𓀴At this I suppose I coloured with mortification, for he added at once,
5330𓀴“Hoots! small blame to ye! To be feared of a thing and yet to do it, is
5331𓀴what makes the prettiest kind of a man. And then there was water there,
5332𓀴and water’s a thing that dauntons even me. No, no,” said Alan, “it’s no
5333𓀴you that’s to blame, it’s me.”
5334𓀴
5335𓀴I asked him why.
5336𓀴
5337𓀴“Why,” said he, “I have proved myself a gomeral this night. For first
5338𓀴of all I take a wrong road, and that in my own country of Appin; so that
5339𓀴the day has caught us where we should never have been; and thanks to
5340𓀴that, we lie here in some danger and mair discomfort. And next (which is
5341𓀴the worst of the two, for a man that has been so much among the heather
5342𓀴as myself) I have come wanting a water-bottle, and here we lie for a
5343𓀴long summer’s day with naething but neat spirit. Ye may think that a
5344𓀴small matter; but before it comes night, David, ye’ll give me news of
5345𓀴it.”
5346𓀴
5347𓀴I was anxious to redeem my character, and offered, if he would pour out
5348𓀴the brandy, to run down and fill the bottle at the river.
5349𓀴
5350𓀴“I wouldnae waste the good spirit either,” says he. “It’s been a good
5351𓀴friend to you this night; or in my poor opinion, ye would still be
5352𓀴cocking on yon stone. And what’s mair,” says he, “ye may have observed
5353𓀴(you that’s a man of so much penetration) that Alan Breck Stewart was
5354𓀴perhaps walking quicker than his ordinar’.”
5355𓀴
5356𓀴“You!” I cried, “you were running fit to burst.”
5357𓀴
5358𓀴“Was I so?” said he. “Well, then, ye may depend upon it, there was nae
5359𓀴time to be lost. And now here is enough said; gang you to your sleep,
5360𓀴lad, and I’ll watch.”
5361𓀴
5362𓀴Accordingly, I lay down to sleep; a little peaty earth had drifted in
5363𓀴between the top of the two rocks, and some bracken grew there, to be a
5364𓀴bed to me; the last thing I heard was still the crying of the eagles.
5365𓀴
5366𓀴I dare say it would be nine in the morning when I was roughly awakened,
5367𓀴and found Alan’s hand pressed upon my mouth.
5368𓀴
5369𓀴“Wheesht!” he whispered. “Ye were snoring.”
5370𓀴
5371𓀴“Well,” said I, surprised at his anxious and dark face, “and why not?”
5372𓀴
5373𓀴He peered over the edge of the rock, and signed to me to do the like.
5374𓀴
5375𓀴It was now high day, cloudless, and very hot. The valley was as clear as
5376𓀴in a picture. About half a mile up the water was a camp of red-coats; a
5377𓀴big fire blazed in their midst, at which some were cooking; and near by,
5378𓀴on the top of a rock about as high as ours, there stood a sentry, with
5379𓀴the sun sparkling on his arms. All the way down along the river-side
5380𓀴were posted other sentries; here near together, there widelier
5381𓀴scattered; some planted like the first, on places of command, some
5382𓀴on the ground level and marching and counter-marching, so as to meet
5383𓀴half-way. Higher up the glen, where the ground was more open, the chain
5384𓀴of posts was continued by horse-soldiers, whom we could see in the
5385𓀴distance riding to and fro. Lower down, the infantry continued; but
5386𓀴as the stream was suddenly swelled by the confluence of a considerable
5387𓀴burn, they were more widely set, and only watched the fords and
5388𓀴stepping-stones.
5389𓀴
5390𓀴I took but one look at them, and ducked again into my place. It was
5391𓀴strange indeed to see this valley, which had lain so solitary in the
5392𓀴hour of dawn, bristling with arms and dotted with the red coats and
5393𓀴breeches.
5394𓀴
5395𓀴“Ye see,” said Alan, “this was what I was afraid of, Davie: that they
5396𓀴would watch the burn-side. They began to come in about two hours ago,
5397𓀴and, man! but ye’re a grand hand at the sleeping! We’re in a narrow
5398𓀴place. If they get up the sides of the hill, they could easy spy us with
5399𓀴a glass; but if they’ll only keep in the foot of the valley, we’ll do
5400𓀴yet. The posts are thinner down the water; and, come night, we’ll try
5401𓀴our hand at getting by them.”
5402𓀴
5403𓀴“And what are we to do till night?” I asked.
5404𓀴
5405𓀴“Lie here,” says he, “and birstle.”
5406𓀴
5407𓀴That one good Scotch word, “birstle,” was indeed the most of the story
5408𓀴of the day that we had now to pass. You are to remember that we lay on
5409𓀴the bare top of a rock, like scones upon a girdle; the sun beat upon us
5410𓀴cruelly; the rock grew so heated, a man could scarce endure the touch of
5411𓀴it; and the little patch of earth and fern, which kept cooler, was only
5412𓀴large enough for one at a time. We took turn about to lie on the naked
5413𓀴rock, which was indeed like the position of that saint that was martyred
5414𓀴on a gridiron; and it ran in my mind how strange it was, that in the
5415𓀴same climate and at only a few days’ distance, I should have suffered
5416𓀴so cruelly, first from cold upon my island and now from heat upon this
5417𓀴rock.
5418𓀴
5419𓀴All the while we had no water, only raw brandy for a drink, which was
5420𓀴worse than nothing; but we kept the bottle as cool as we could, burying
5421𓀴it in the earth, and got some relief by bathing our breasts and temples.
5422𓀴
5423𓀴The soldiers kept stirring all day in the bottom of the valley, now
5424𓀴changing guard, now in patrolling parties hunting among the rocks. These
5425𓀴lay round in so great a number, that to look for men among them was like
5426𓀴looking for a needle in a bottle of hay; and being so hopeless a task,
5427𓀴it was gone about with the less care. Yet we could see the soldiers
5428𓀴pike their bayonets among the heather, which sent a cold thrill into my
5429𓀴vitals; and they would sometimes hang about our rock, so that we scarce
5430𓀴dared to breathe.
5431𓀴
5432𓀴It was in this way that I first heard the right English speech; one
5433𓀴fellow as he went by actually clapping his hand upon the sunny face of
5434𓀴the rock on which we lay, and plucking it off again with an oath. “I
5435𓀴tell you it’s ‘ot,” says he; and I was amazed at the clipping tones and
5436𓀴the odd sing-song in which he spoke, and no less at that strange trick
5437𓀴of dropping out the letter “h.” To be sure, I had heard Ransome; but he
5438𓀴had taken his ways from all sorts of people, and spoke so imperfectly
5439𓀴at the best, that I set down the most of it to childishness. My surprise
5440𓀴was all the greater to hear that manner of speaking in the mouth of a
5441𓀴grown man; and indeed I have never grown used to it; nor yet altogether
5442𓀴with the English grammar, as perhaps a very critical eye might here and
5443𓀴there spy out even in these memoirs.
5444𓀴
5445𓀴The tediousness and pain of these hours upon the rock grew only the
5446𓀴greater as the day went on; the rock getting still the hotter and the
5447𓀴sun fiercer. There were giddiness, and sickness, and sharp pangs like
5448𓀴rheumatism, to be supported. I minded then, and have often minded since,
5449𓀴on the lines in our Scotch psalm:--
5450𓀴
5451𓀴     “The moon by night thee shall not smite,
5452𓀴     Nor yet the sun by day;”
5453𓀴
5454𓀴and indeed it was only by God’s blessing that we were neither of us
5455𓀴sun-smitten.
5456𓀴
5457𓀴At last, about two, it was beyond men’s bearing, and there was now
5458𓀴temptation to resist, as well as pain to thole. For the sun being now
5459𓀴got a little into the west, there came a patch of shade on the east side
5460𓀴of our rock, which was the side sheltered from the soldiers.
5461𓀴
5462𓀴“As well one death as another,” said Alan, and slipped over the edge and
5463𓀴dropped on the ground on the shadowy side.
5464𓀴
5465𓀴I followed him at once, and instantly fell all my length, so weak was I
5466𓀴and so giddy with that long exposure. Here, then, we lay for an hour or
5467𓀴two, aching from head to foot, as weak as water, and lying quite naked
5468𓀴to the eye of any soldier who should have strolled that way. None came,
5469𓀴however, all passing by on the other side; so that our rock continued to
5470𓀴be our shield even in this new position.
5471𓀴
5472𓀴Presently we began again to get a little strength; and as the soldiers
5473𓀴were now lying closer along the river-side, Alan proposed that we should
5474𓀴try a start. I was by this time afraid of but one thing in the world;
5475𓀴and that was to be set back upon the rock; anything else was welcome
5476𓀴to me; so we got ourselves at once in marching order, and began to slip
5477𓀴from rock to rock one after the other, now crawling flat on our bellies
5478𓀴in the shade, now making a run for it, heart in mouth.
5479𓀴
5480𓀴The soldiers, having searched this side of the valley after a fashion,
5481𓀴and being perhaps somewhat sleepy with the sultriness of the afternoon,
5482𓀴had now laid by much of their vigilance, and stood dozing at their posts
5483𓀴or only kept a look-out along the banks of the river; so that in this
5484𓀴way, keeping down the valley and at the same time towards the mountains,
5485𓀴we drew steadily away from their neighbourhood. But the business was the
5486𓀴most wearing I had ever taken part in. A man had need of a hundred
5487𓀴eyes in every part of him, to keep concealed in that uneven country and
5488𓀴within cry of so many and scattered sentries. When we must pass an open
5489𓀴place, quickness was not all, but a swift judgment not only of the lie
5490𓀴of the whole country, but of the solidity of every stone on which we
5491𓀴must set foot; for the afternoon was now fallen so breathless that the
5492𓀴rolling of a pebble sounded abroad like a pistol shot, and would start
5493𓀴the echo calling among the hills and cliffs.
5494𓀴
5495𓀴By sundown we had made some distance, even by our slow rate of progress,
5496𓀴though to be sure the sentry on the rock was still plainly in our view.
5497𓀴But now we came on something that put all fears out of season; and that
5498𓀴was a deep rushing burn, that tore down, in that part, to join the glen
5499𓀴river. At the sight of this we cast ourselves on the ground and plunged
5500𓀴head and shoulders in the water; and I cannot tell which was the more
5501𓀴pleasant, the great shock as the cool stream went over us, or the greed
5502𓀴with which we drank of it.
5503𓀴
5504𓀴We lay there (for the banks hid us), drank again and again, bathed our
5505𓀴chests, let our wrists trail in the running water till they ached
5506𓀴with the chill; and at last, being wonderfully renewed, we got out the
5507𓀴meal-bag and made drammach in the iron pan. This, though it is but cold
5508𓀴water mingled with oatmeal, yet makes a good enough dish for a hungry
5509𓀴man; and where there are no means of making fire, or (as in our case)
5510𓀴good reason for not making one, it is the chief stand-by of those who
5511𓀴have taken to the heather.
5512𓀴
5513𓀴As soon as the shadow of the night had fallen, we set forth again, at
5514𓀴first with the same caution, but presently with more boldness, standing
5515𓀴our full height and stepping out at a good pace of walking. The way
5516𓀴was very intricate, lying up the steep sides of mountains and along the
5517𓀴brows of cliffs; clouds had come in with the sunset, and the night was
5518𓀴dark and cool; so that I walked without much fatigue, but in continual
5519𓀴fear of falling and rolling down the mountains, and with no guess at our
5520𓀴direction.
5521𓀴
5522𓀴The moon rose at last and found us still on the road; it was in its last
5523𓀴quarter, and was long beset with clouds; but after awhile shone out and
5524𓀴showed me many dark heads of mountains, and was reflected far underneath
5525𓀴us on the narrow arm of a sea-loch.
5526𓀴
5527𓀴At this sight we both paused: I struck with wonder to find myself so
5528𓀴high and walking (as it seemed to me) upon clouds; Alan to make sure of
5529𓀴his direction.
5530𓀴
5531𓀴Seemingly he was well pleased, and he must certainly have judged us
5532𓀴out of ear-shot of all our enemies; for throughout the rest of our
5533𓀴night-march he beguiled the way with whistling of many tunes, warlike,
5534𓀴merry, plaintive; reel tunes that made the foot go faster; tunes of my
5535𓀴own south country that made me fain to be home from my adventures; and
5536𓀴all these, on the great, dark, desert mountains, making company upon the
5537𓀴way.
5538𓀴
5539𓀴
5540𓀴
5541𓀴
5542𓀴CHAPTER XXI
5543𓀴
5544𓀴THE FLIGHT IN THE HEATHER: THE HEUGH OF CORRYNAKIEGH
5545𓀴
5546𓀴
5547𓀴Early as day comes in the beginning of July, it was still dark when we
5548𓀴reached our destination, a cleft in the head of a great mountain, with a
5549𓀴water running through the midst, and upon the one hand a shallow cave
5550𓀴in a rock. Birches grew there in a thin, pretty wood, which a little
5551𓀴farther on was changed into a wood of pines. The burn was full of trout;
5552𓀴the wood of cushat-doves; on the open side of the mountain beyond,
5553𓀴whaups would be always whistling, and cuckoos were plentiful. From the
5554𓀴mouth of the cleft we looked down upon a part of Mamore, and on the
5555𓀴sea-loch that divides that country from Appin; and this from so great
5556𓀴a height as made it my continual wonder and pleasure to sit and behold
5557𓀴them.
5558𓀴
5559𓀴The name of the cleft was the Heugh of Corrynakiegh; and although from
5560𓀴its height and being so near upon the sea, it was often beset with
5561𓀴clouds, yet it was on the whole a pleasant place, and the five days we
5562𓀴lived in it went happily.
5563𓀴
5564𓀴We slept in the cave, making our bed of heather bushes which we cut for
5565𓀴that purpose, and covering ourselves with Alan’s great-coat. There was a
5566𓀴low concealed place, in a turning of the glen, where we were so bold as
5567𓀴to make fire: so that we could warm ourselves when the clouds set in,
5568𓀴and cook hot porridge, and grill the little trouts that we caught with
5569𓀴our hands under the stones and overhanging banks of the burn. This was
5570𓀴indeed our chief pleasure and business; and not only to save our meal
5571𓀴against worse times, but with a rivalry that much amused us, we spent
5572𓀴a great part of our days at the water-side, stripped to the waist and
5573𓀴groping about or (as they say) guddling for these fish. The largest we
5574𓀴got might have been a quarter of a pound; but they were of good flesh
5575𓀴and flavour, and when broiled upon the coals, lacked only a little salt
5576𓀴to be delicious.
5577𓀴
5578𓀴In any by-time Alan must teach me to use my sword, for my ignorance
5579𓀴had much distressed him; and I think besides, as I had sometimes
5580𓀴the upper-hand of him in the fishing, he was not sorry to turn to an
5581𓀴exercise where he had so much the upper-hand of me. He made it somewhat
5582𓀴more of a pain than need have been, for he stormed at me all through the
5583𓀴lessons in a very violent manner of scolding, and would push me so close
5584𓀴that I made sure he must run me through the body. I was often tempted
5585𓀴to turn tail, but held my ground for all that, and got some profit of
5586𓀴my lessons; if it was but to stand on guard with an assured countenance,
5587𓀴which is often all that is required. So, though I could never in the
5588𓀴least please my master, I was not altogether displeased with myself.
5589𓀴
5590𓀴In the meanwhile, you are not to suppose that we neglected our chief
5591𓀴business, which was to get away.
5592𓀴
5593𓀴“It will be many a long day,” Alan said to me on our first morning,
5594𓀴“before the red-coats think upon seeking Corrynakiegh; so now we must
5595𓀴get word sent to James, and he must find the siller for us.”
5596𓀴
5597𓀴“And how shall we send that word?” says I. “We are here in a desert
5598𓀴place, which yet we dare not leave; and unless ye get the fowls of the
5599𓀴air to be your messengers, I see not what we shall be able to do.”
5600𓀴
5601𓀴“Ay?” said Alan. “Ye’re a man of small contrivance, David.”
5602𓀴
5603𓀴Thereupon he fell in a muse, looking in the embers of the fire; and
5604𓀴presently, getting a piece of wood, he fashioned it in a cross, the four
5605𓀴ends of which he blackened on the coals. Then he looked at me a little
5606𓀴shyly.
5607𓀴
5608𓀴“Could ye lend me my button?” says he. “It seems a strange thing to ask
5609𓀴a gift again, but I own I am laith to cut another.”
5610𓀴
5611𓀴I gave him the button; whereupon he strung it on a strip of his
5612𓀴great-coat which he had used to bind the cross; and tying in a little
5613𓀴sprig of birch and another of fir, he looked upon his work with
5614𓀴satisfaction.
5615𓀴
5616𓀴“Now,” said he, “there is a little clachan” (what is called a hamlet
5617𓀴in the English) “not very far from Corrynakiegh, and it has the name of
5618𓀴Koalisnacoan. There there are living many friends of mine whom I could
5619𓀴trust with my life, and some that I am no just so sure of. Ye see,
5620𓀴David, there will be money set upon our heads; James himsel’ is to set
5621𓀴money on them; and as for the Campbells, they would never spare siller
5622𓀴where there was a Stewart to be hurt. If it was otherwise, I would go
5623𓀴down to Koalisnacoan whatever, and trust my life into these people’s
5624𓀴hands as lightly as I would trust another with my glove.”
5625𓀴
5626𓀴“But being so?” said I.
5627𓀴
5628𓀴“Being so,” said he, “I would as lief they didnae see me. There’s bad
5629𓀴folk everywhere, and what’s far worse, weak ones. So when it comes dark
5630𓀴again, I will steal down into that clachan, and set this that I have
5631𓀴been making in the window of a good friend of mine, John Breck Maccoll,
5632𓀴a bouman[26] of Appin’s.”
5633𓀴
5634𓀴     [26] A bouman is a tenant who takes stock from the landlord and
5635𓀴     shares with him the increase.
5636𓀴
5637𓀴“With all my heart,” says I; “and if he finds it, what is he to think?”
5638𓀴
5639𓀴“Well,” says Alan, “I wish he was a man of more penetration, for by my
5640𓀴troth I am afraid he will make little enough of it! But this is what I
5641𓀴have in my mind. This cross is something in the nature of the
5642𓀴crosstarrie, or fiery cross, which is the signal of gathering in our
5643𓀴clans; yet he will know well enough the clan is not to rise, for there
5644𓀴it is standing in his window, and no word with it. So he will say to
5645𓀴himsel’, _The clan is not to rise, but there is something_. Then he
5646𓀴will see my button, and that was Duncan Stewart’s. And then he will say
5647𓀴to himsel’, _The son of Duncan is in the heather and has need of me_.”
5648𓀴
5649𓀴“Well,” said I, “it may be. But even supposing so, there is a good deal
5650𓀴of heather between here and the Forth.”
5651𓀴
5652𓀴“And that is a very true word,” says Alan. “But then John Breck will
5653𓀴see the sprig of birch and the sprig of pine; and he will say to
5654𓀴himsel’ (if he is a man of any penetration at all, which I misdoubt),
5655𓀴_Alan will be lying in a wood which is both of pines and birches_. Then
5656𓀴he will think to himsel’, _That is not so very rife hereabout;_ and
5657𓀴then he will come and give us a look up in Corrynakiegh. And if he does
5658𓀴not, David, the devil may fly away with him, for what I care; for he
5659𓀴will no be worth the salt to his porridge.”
5660𓀴
5661𓀴“Eh, man,” said I, drolling with him a little, “you’re very ingenious!
5662𓀴But would it not be simpler for you to write him a few words in black
5663𓀴and white?”
5664𓀴
5665𓀴“And that is an excellent observe, Mr. Balfour of Shaws,” says Alan,
5666𓀴drolling with me; “and it would certainly be much simpler for me to
5667𓀴write to him, but it would be a sore job for John Breck to read it. He
5668𓀴would have to go to the school for two-three years; and it’s possible we
5669𓀴might be wearied waiting on him.”
5670𓀴
5671𓀴So that night Alan carried down his fiery cross and set it in the
5672𓀴bouman’s window. He was troubled when he came back; for the dogs had
5673𓀴barked and the folk run out from their houses; and he thought he had
5674𓀴heard a clatter of arms and seen a red-coat come to one of the doors. On
5675𓀴all accounts we lay the next day in the borders of the wood and kept a
5676𓀴close look-out, so that if it was John Breck that came we might be ready
5677𓀴to guide him, and if it was the red-coats we should have time to get
5678𓀴away.
5679𓀴
5680𓀴About noon a man was to be spied, straggling up the open side of the
5681𓀴mountain in the sun, and looking round him as he came, from under his
5682𓀴hand. No sooner had Alan seen him than he whistled; the man turned and
5683𓀴came a little towards us: then Alan would give another “peep!” and the
5684𓀴man would come still nearer; and so by the sound of whistling, he was
5685𓀴guided to the spot where we lay.
5686𓀴
5687𓀴He was a ragged, wild, bearded man, about forty, grossly disfigured with
5688𓀴the small pox, and looked both dull and savage. Although his English
5689𓀴was very bad and broken, yet Alan (according to his very handsome use,
5690𓀴whenever I was by) would suffer him to speak no Gaelic. Perhaps the
5691𓀴strange language made him appear more backward than he really was; but
5692𓀴I thought he had little good-will to serve us, and what he had was the
5693𓀴child of terror.
5694𓀴
5695𓀴Alan would have had him carry a message to James; but the bouman would
5696𓀴hear of no message. “She was forget it,” he said in his screaming voice;
5697𓀴and would either have a letter or wash his hands of us.
5698𓀴
5699𓀴I thought Alan would be gravelled at that, for we lacked the means of
5700𓀴writing in that desert.
5701𓀴
5702𓀴But he was a man of more resources than I knew; searched the wood until
5703𓀴he found the quill of a cushat-dove, which he shaped into a pen; made
5704𓀴himself a kind of ink with gunpowder from his horn and water from the
5705𓀴running stream; and tearing a corner from his French military commission
5706𓀴(which he carried in his pocket, like a talisman to keep him from the
5707𓀴gallows), he sat down and wrote as follows:
5708𓀴
5709𓀴
5710𓀴“DEAR KINSMAN,--Please send the money by the bearer to the place he kens
5711𓀴of.
5712𓀴
5713𓀴“Your affectionate cousin,
5714𓀴
5715𓀴“A. S.”
5716𓀴
5717𓀴
5718𓀴This he intrusted to the bouman, who promised to make what manner of
5719𓀴speed he best could, and carried it off with him down the hill.
5720𓀴
5721𓀴He was three full days gone, but about five in the evening of the third,
5722𓀴we heard a whistling in the wood, which Alan answered; and presently the
5723𓀴bouman came up the water-side, looking for us, right and left. He seemed
5724𓀴less sulky than before, and indeed he was no doubt well pleased to have
5725𓀴got to the end of such a dangerous commission.
5726𓀴
5727𓀴He gave us the news of the country; that it was alive with red-coats;
5728𓀴that arms were being found, and poor folk brought in trouble daily; and
5729𓀴that James and some of his servants were already clapped in prison at
5730𓀴Fort William, under strong suspicion of complicity. It seemed it was
5731𓀴noised on all sides that Alan Breck had fired the shot; and there was a
5732𓀴bill issued for both him and me, with one hundred pounds reward.
5733𓀴
5734𓀴This was all as bad as could be; and the little note the bouman had
5735𓀴carried us from Mrs. Stewart was of a miserable sadness. In it she
5736𓀴besought Alan not to let himself be captured, assuring him, if he fell
5737𓀴in the hands of the troops, both he and James were no better than dead
5738𓀴men. The money she had sent was all that she could beg or borrow, and
5739𓀴she prayed heaven we could be doing with it. Lastly, she said, she
5740𓀴enclosed us one of the bills in which we were described.
5741𓀴
5742𓀴This we looked upon with great curiosity and not a little fear, partly
5743𓀴as a man may look in a mirror, partly as he might look into the barrel
5744𓀴of an enemy’s gun to judge if it be truly aimed. Alan was advertised as
5745𓀴“a small, pock-marked, active man of thirty-five or thereby, dressed
5746𓀴in a feathered hat, a French side-coat of blue with silver buttons,
5747𓀴and lace a great deal tarnished, a red waistcoat and breeches of black,
5748𓀴shag;” and I as “a tall strong lad of about eighteen, wearing an
5749𓀴old blue coat, very ragged, an old Highland bonnet, a long homespun
5750𓀴waistcoat, blue breeches; his legs bare, low-country shoes, wanting the
5751𓀴toes; speaks like a Lowlander, and has no beard.”
5752𓀴
5753𓀴Alan was well enough pleased to see his finery so fully remembered and
5754𓀴set down; only when he came to the word tarnish, he looked upon his lace
5755𓀴like one a little mortified. As for myself, I thought I cut a miserable
5756𓀴figure in the bill; and yet was well enough pleased too, for since I had
5757𓀴changed these rags, the description had ceased to be a danger and become
5758𓀴a source of safety.
5759𓀴
5760𓀴“Alan,” said I, “you should change your clothes.”
5761𓀴
5762𓀴“Na, troth!” said Alan, “I have nae others. A fine sight I would be, if
5763𓀴I went back to France in a bonnet!”
5764𓀴
5765𓀴This put a second reflection in my mind: that if I were to separate
5766𓀴from Alan and his tell-tale clothes I should be safe against arrest, and
5767𓀴might go openly about my business. Nor was this all; for suppose I was
5768𓀴arrested when I was alone, there was little against me; but suppose I
5769𓀴was taken in company with the reputed murderer, my case would begin to
5770𓀴be grave. For generosity’s sake I dare not speak my mind upon this head;
5771𓀴but I thought of it none the less.
5772𓀴
5773𓀴I thought of it all the more, too, when the bouman brought out a green
5774𓀴purse with four guineas in gold, and the best part of another in small
5775𓀴change. True, it was more than I had. But then Alan, with less than
5776𓀴five guineas, had to get as far as France; I, with my less than two, not
5777𓀴beyond Queensferry; so that taking things in their proportion, Alan’s
5778𓀴society was not only a peril to my life, but a burden on my purse.
5779𓀴
5780𓀴But there was no thought of the sort in the honest head of my companion.
5781𓀴He believed he was serving, helping, and protecting me. And what could I
5782𓀴do but hold my peace, and chafe, and take my chance of it?
5783𓀴
5784𓀴“It’s little enough,” said Alan, putting the purse in his pocket, “but
5785𓀴it’ll do my business. And now, John Breck, if ye will hand me over my
5786𓀴button, this gentleman and me will be for taking the road.”
5787𓀴
5788𓀴But the bouman, after feeling about in a hairy purse that hung in front
5789𓀴of him in the Highland manner (though he wore otherwise the Lowland
5790𓀴habit, with sea-trousers), began to roll his eyes strangely, and at last
5791𓀴said, “Her nainsel will loss it,” meaning he thought he had lost it.
5792𓀴
5793𓀴“What!” cried Alan, “you will lose my button, that was my father’s
5794𓀴before me? Now I will tell you what is in my mind, John Breck: it is
5795𓀴in my mind this is the worst day’s work that ever ye did since ye was
5796𓀴born.”
5797𓀴
5798𓀴And as Alan spoke, he set his hands on his knees and looked at the
5799𓀴bouman with a smiling mouth, and that dancing light in his eyes that
5800𓀴meant mischief to his enemies.
5801𓀴
5802𓀴Perhaps the bouman was honest enough; perhaps he had meant to cheat and
5803𓀴then, finding himself alone with two of us in a desert place, cast back
5804𓀴to honesty as being safer; at least, and all at once, he seemed to find
5805𓀴that button and handed it to Alan.
5806𓀴
5807𓀴“Well, and it is a good thing for the honour of the Maccolls,” said
5808𓀴Alan, and then to me, “Here is my button back again, and I thank you for
5809𓀴parting with it, which is of a piece with all your friendships to me.”
5810𓀴 Then he took the warmest parting of the bouman. “For,” says he, “ye have
5811𓀴done very well by me, and set your neck at a venture, and I will always
5812𓀴give you the name of a good man.”
5813𓀴
5814𓀴Lastly, the bouman took himself off by one way; and Alan and I (getting our
5815𓀴chattels together) struck into another to resume our flight.
5816𓀴
5817𓀴
5818𓀴
5819𓀴
5820𓀴CHAPTER XXII
5821𓀴
5822𓀴THE FLIGHT IN THE HEATHER: THE MOOR
5823𓀴
5824𓀴
5825𓀴Some seven hours’ incessant, hard travelling brought us early in the
5826𓀴morning to the end of a range of mountains. In front of us there lay a
5827𓀴piece of low, broken, desert land, which we must now cross. The sun was
5828𓀴not long up, and shone straight in our eyes; a little, thin mist went up
5829𓀴from the face of the moorland like a smoke; so that (as Alan said) there
5830𓀴might have been twenty squadron of dragoons there and we none the wiser.
5831𓀴
5832𓀴We sat down, therefore, in a howe of the hill-side till the mist should
5833𓀴have risen, and made ourselves a dish of drammach, and held a council of
5834𓀴war.
5835𓀴
5836𓀴“David,” said Alan, “this is the kittle bit. Shall we lie here till it
5837𓀴comes night, or shall we risk it, and stave on ahead?”
5838𓀴
5839𓀴“Well,” said I, “I am tired indeed, but I could walk as far again, if
5840𓀴that was all.”
5841𓀴
5842𓀴“Ay, but it isnae,” said Alan, “nor yet the half. This is how we stand:
5843𓀴Appin’s fair death to us. To the south it’s all Campbells, and no to be
5844𓀴thought of. To the north; well, there’s no muckle to be gained by going
5845𓀴north; neither for you, that wants to get to Queensferry, nor yet for
5846𓀴me, that wants to get to France. Well, then, we’ll can strike east.”
5847𓀴
5848𓀴“East be it!” says I, quite cheerily; but I was thinking in to myself:
5849𓀴“O, man, if you would only take one point of the compass and let me take
5850𓀴any other, it would be the best for both of us.”
5851𓀴
5852𓀴“Well, then, east, ye see, we have the muirs,” said Alan. “Once there,
5853𓀴David, it’s mere pitch-and-toss. Out on yon bald, naked, flat place,
5854𓀴where can a body turn to? Let the red-coats come over a hill, they can
5855𓀴spy you miles away; and the sorrow’s in their horses’ heels, they would
5856𓀴soon ride you down. It’s no good place, David; and I’m free to say, it’s
5857𓀴worse by daylight than by dark.”
5858𓀴
5859𓀴“Alan,” said I, “hear my way of it. Appin’s death for us; we have none
5860𓀴too much money, nor yet meal; the longer they seek, the nearer they
5861𓀴may guess where we are; it’s all a risk; and I give my word to go ahead
5862𓀴until we drop.”
5863𓀴
5864𓀴Alan was delighted. “There are whiles,” said he, “when ye are altogether
5865𓀴too canny and Whiggish to be company for a gentleman like me; but there
5866𓀴come other whiles when ye show yoursel’ a mettle spark; and it’s then,
5867𓀴David, that I love ye like a brother.”
5868𓀴
5869𓀴The mist rose and died away, and showed us that country lying as waste
5870𓀴as the sea; only the moorfowl and the pewees crying upon it, and far
5871𓀴over to the east, a herd of deer, moving like dots. Much of it was red
5872𓀴with heather; much of the rest broken up with bogs and hags and peaty
5873𓀴pools; some had been burnt black in a heath fire; and in another place
5874𓀴there was quite a forest of dead firs, standing like skeletons. A
5875𓀴wearier-looking desert man never saw; but at least it was clear of
5876𓀴troops, which was our point.
5877𓀴
5878𓀴We went down accordingly into the waste, and began to make our toilsome
5879𓀴and devious travel towards the eastern verge. There were the tops of
5880𓀴mountains all round (you are to remember) from whence we might be spied
5881𓀴at any moment; so it behoved us to keep in the hollow parts of the moor,
5882𓀴and when these turned aside from our direction to move upon its naked
5883𓀴face with infinite care. Sometimes, for half an hour together, we must
5884𓀴crawl from one heather bush to another, as hunters do when they are hard
5885𓀴upon the deer. It was a clear day again, with a blazing sun; the water
5886𓀴in the brandy bottle was soon gone; and altogether, if I had guessed
5887𓀴what it would be to crawl half the time upon my belly and to walk much
5888𓀴of the rest stooping nearly to the knees, I should certainly have held
5889𓀴back from such a killing enterprise.
5890𓀴
5891𓀴Toiling and resting and toiling again, we wore away the morning; and
5892𓀴about noon lay down in a thick bush of heather to sleep. Alan took the
5893𓀴first watch; and it seemed to me I had scarce closed my eyes before I
5894𓀴was shaken up to take the second. We had no clock to go by; and Alan
