5895𓀴stuck a sprig of heath in the ground to serve instead; so that as soon
5896𓀴as the shadow of the bush should fall so far to the east, I might know
5897𓀴to rouse him. But I was by this time so weary that I could have slept
5898𓀴twelve hours at a stretch; I had the taste of sleep in my throat; my
5899𓀴joints slept even when my mind was waking; the hot smell of the heather,
5900𓀴and the drone of the wild bees, were like possets to me; and every now
5901𓀴and again I would give a jump and find I had been dozing.
5902𓀴
5903𓀴The last time I woke I seemed to come back from farther away, and
5904𓀴thought the sun had taken a great start in the heavens. I looked at the
5905𓀴sprig of heath, and at that I could have cried aloud: for I saw I had
5906𓀴betrayed my trust. My head was nearly turned with fear and shame; and at
5907𓀴what I saw, when I looked out around me on the moor, my heart was like
5908𓀴dying in my body. For sure enough, a body of horse-soldiers had come
5909𓀴down during my sleep, and were drawing near to us from the south-east,
5910𓀴spread out in the shape of a fan and riding their horses to and fro in
5911𓀴the deep parts of the heather.
5912𓀴
5913𓀴When I waked Alan, he glanced first at the soldiers, then at the mark
5914𓀴and the position of the sun, and knitted his brows with a sudden, quick
5915𓀴look, both ugly and anxious, which was all the reproach I had of him.
5916𓀴
5917𓀴“What are we to do now?” I asked.
5918𓀴
5919𓀴“We’ll have to play at being hares,” said he. “Do ye see yon mountain?”
5920𓀴 pointing to one on the north-eastern sky.
5921𓀴
5922𓀴“Ay,” said I.
5923𓀴
5924𓀴“Well, then,” says he, “let us strike for that. Its name is Ben Alder.
5925𓀴it is a wild, desert mountain full of hills and hollows, and if we can
5926𓀴win to it before the morn, we may do yet.”
5927𓀴
5928𓀴“But, Alan,” cried I, “that will take us across the very coming of the
5929𓀴soldiers!”
5930𓀴
5931𓀴“I ken that fine,” said he; “but if we are driven back on Appin, we are
5932𓀴two dead men. So now, David man, be brisk!”
5933𓀴
5934𓀴With that he began to run forward on his hands and knees with an
5935𓀴incredible quickness, as though it were his natural way of going. All
5936𓀴the time, too, he kept winding in and out in the lower parts of the
5937𓀴moorland where we were the best concealed. Some of these had been burned
5938𓀴or at least scathed with fire; and there rose in our faces (which were
5939𓀴close to the ground) a blinding, choking dust as fine as smoke. The
5940𓀴water was long out; and this posture of running on the hands and knees
5941𓀴brings an overmastering weakness and weariness, so that the joints ache
5942𓀴and the wrists faint under your weight.
5943𓀴
5944𓀴Now and then, indeed, where was a big bush of heather, we lay awhile,
5945𓀴and panted, and putting aside the leaves, looked back at the dragoons.
5946𓀴They had not spied us, for they held straight on; a half-troop, I think,
5947𓀴covering about two miles of ground, and beating it mighty thoroughly as
5948𓀴they went. I had awakened just in time; a little later, and we must have
5949𓀴fled in front of them, instead of escaping on one side. Even as it was,
5950𓀴the least misfortune might betray us; and now and again, when a grouse
5951𓀴rose out of the heather with a clap of wings, we lay as still as the
5952𓀴dead and were afraid to breathe.
5953𓀴
5954𓀴The aching and faintness of my body, the labouring of my heart, the
5955𓀴soreness of my hands, and the smarting of my throat and eyes in the
5956𓀴continual smoke of dust and ashes, had soon grown to be so unbearable
5957𓀴that I would gladly have given up. Nothing but the fear of Alan lent me
5958𓀴enough of a false kind of courage to continue. As for himself (and you
5959𓀴are to bear in mind that he was cumbered with a great-coat) he had first
5960𓀴turned crimson, but as time went on the redness began to be mingled
5961𓀴with patches of white; his breath cried and whistled as it came; and his
5962𓀴voice, when he whispered his observations in my ear during our halts,
5963𓀴sounded like nothing human. Yet he seemed in no way dashed in spirits,
5964𓀴nor did he at all abate in his activity, so that I was driven to marvel
5965𓀴at the man’s endurance.
5966𓀴
5967𓀴At length, in the first gloaming of the night, we heard a trumpet sound,
5968𓀴and looking back from among the heather, saw the troop beginning to
5969𓀴collect. A little after, they had built a fire and camped for the night,
5970𓀴about the middle of the waste.
5971𓀴
5972𓀴At this I begged and besought that we might lie down and sleep.
5973𓀴
5974𓀴“There shall be no sleep the night!” said Alan. “From now on, these
5975𓀴weary dragoons of yours will keep the crown of the muirland, and none
5976𓀴will get out of Appin but winged fowls. We got through in the nick
5977𓀴of time, and shall we jeopard what we’ve gained? Na, na, when the day
5978𓀴comes, it shall find you and me in a fast place on Ben Alder.”
5979𓀴
5980𓀴“Alan,” I said, “it’s not the want of will: it’s the strength that I
5981𓀴want. If I could, I would; but as sure as I’m alive I cannot.”
5982𓀴
5983𓀴“Very well, then,” said Alan. “I’ll carry ye.”
5984𓀴
5985𓀴I looked to see if he were jesting; but no, the little man was in dead
5986𓀴earnest; and the sight of so much resolution shamed me.
5987𓀴
5988𓀴“Lead away!” said I. “I’ll follow.”
5989𓀴
5990𓀴He gave me one look as much as to say, “Well done, David!” and off he
5991𓀴set again at his top speed.
5992𓀴
5993𓀴It grew cooler and even a little darker (but not much) with the coming
5994𓀴of the night. The sky was cloudless; it was still early in July, and
5995𓀴pretty far north; in the darkest part of that night, you would have
5996𓀴needed pretty good eyes to read, but for all that, I have often seen it
5997𓀴darker in a winter mid-day. Heavy dew fell and drenched the moor like
5998𓀴rain; and this refreshed me for a while. When we stopped to breathe,
5999𓀴and I had time to see all about me, the clearness and sweetness of
6000𓀴the night, the shapes of the hills like things asleep, and the fire
6001𓀴dwindling away behind us, like a bright spot in the midst of the moor,
6002𓀴anger would come upon me in a clap that I must still drag myself in
6003𓀴agony and eat the dust like a worm.
6004𓀴
6005𓀴By what I have read in books, I think few that have held a pen were ever
6006𓀴really wearied, or they would write of it more strongly. I had no care
6007𓀴of my life, neither past nor future, and I scarce remembered there was
6008𓀴such a lad as David Balfour. I did not think of myself, but just of each
6009𓀴fresh step which I was sure would be my last, with despair--and of Alan,
6010𓀴who was the cause of it, with hatred. Alan was in the right trade as a
6011𓀴soldier; this is the officer’s part to make men continue to do things,
6012𓀴they know not wherefore, and when, if the choice was offered, they would
6013𓀴lie down where they were and be killed. And I dare say I would have made
6014𓀴a good enough private; for in these last hours it never occurred to me
6015𓀴that I had any choice but just to obey as long as I was able, and die
6016𓀴obeying.
6017𓀴
6018𓀴Day began to come in, after years, I thought; and by that time we were
6019𓀴past the greatest danger, and could walk upon our feet like men, instead
6020𓀴of crawling like brutes. But, dear heart have mercy! what a pair we must
6021𓀴have made, going double like old grandfathers, stumbling like babes,
6022𓀴and as white as dead folk. Never a word passed between us; each set his
6023𓀴mouth and kept his eyes in front of him, and lifted up his foot and set
6024𓀴it down again, like people lifting weights at a country play;[27] all the
6025𓀴while, with the moorfowl crying “peep!” in the heather, and the light
6026𓀴coming slowly clearer in the east.
6027𓀴
6028𓀴     [27] Village fair.
6029𓀴
6030𓀴I say Alan did as I did. Not that ever I looked at him, for I had enough
6031𓀴ado to keep my feet; but because it is plain he must have been as stupid
6032𓀴with weariness as myself, and looked as little where we were going, or
6033𓀴we should not have walked into an ambush like blind men.
6034𓀴
6035𓀴It fell in this way. We were going down a heathery brae, Alan leading
6036𓀴and I following a pace or two behind, like a fiddler and his wife; when
6037𓀴upon a sudden the heather gave a rustle, three or four ragged men leaped
6038𓀴out, and the next moment we were lying on our backs, each with a dirk at
6039𓀴his throat.
6040𓀴
6041𓀴I don’t think I cared; the pain of this rough handling was quite
6042𓀴swallowed up by the pains of which I was already full; and I was too
6043𓀴glad to have stopped walking to mind about a dirk. I lay looking up in
6044𓀴the face of the man that held me; and I mind his face was black with the
6045𓀴sun, and his eyes very light, but I was not afraid of him. I heard Alan
6046𓀴and another whispering in the Gaelic; and what they said was all one to
6047𓀴me.
6048𓀴
6049𓀴Then the dirks were put up, our weapons were taken away, and we were set
6050𓀴face to face, sitting in the heather.
6051𓀴
6052𓀴“They are Cluny’s men,” said Alan. “We couldnae have fallen better.
6053𓀴We’re just to bide here with these, which are his out-sentries, till
6054𓀴they can get word to the chief of my arrival.”
6055𓀴
6056𓀴Now Cluny Macpherson, the chief of the clan Vourich, had been one of the
6057𓀴leaders of the great rebellion six years before; there was a price on
6058𓀴his life; and I had supposed him long ago in France, with the rest of
6059𓀴the heads of that desperate party. Even tired as I was, the surprise of
6060𓀴what I heard half wakened me.
6061𓀴
6062𓀴“What,” I cried, “is Cluny still here?”
6063𓀴
6064𓀴“Ay, is he so!” said Alan. “Still in his own country and kept by his own
6065𓀴clan. King George can do no more.”
6066𓀴
6067𓀴I think I would have asked farther, but Alan gave me the put-off. “I am
6068𓀴rather wearied,” he said, “and I would like fine to get a sleep.” And
6069𓀴without more words, he rolled on his face in a deep heather bush, and
6070𓀴seemed to sleep at once.
6071𓀴
6072𓀴There was no such thing possible for me. You have heard grasshoppers
6073𓀴whirring in the grass in the summer time? Well, I had no sooner closed
6074𓀴my eyes, than my body, and above all my head, belly, and wrists, seemed
6075𓀴to be filled with whirring grasshoppers; and I must open my eyes again
6076𓀴at once, and tumble and toss, and sit up and lie down; and look at the
6077𓀴sky which dazzled me, or at Cluny’s wild and dirty sentries, peering out
6078𓀴over the top of the brae and chattering to each other in the Gaelic.
6079𓀴
6080𓀴That was all the rest I had, until the messenger returned; when, as it
6081𓀴appeared that Cluny would be glad to receive us, we must get once more
6082𓀴upon our feet and set forward. Alan was in excellent good spirits, much
6083𓀴refreshed by his sleep, very hungry, and looking pleasantly forward to
6084𓀴a dram and a dish of hot collops, of which, it seems, the messenger had
6085𓀴brought him word. For my part, it made me sick to hear of eating. I had
6086𓀴been dead-heavy before, and now I felt a kind of dreadful lightness,
6087𓀴which would not suffer me to walk. I drifted like a gossamer; the ground
6088𓀴seemed to me a cloud, the hills a feather-weight, the air to have a
6089𓀴current, like a running burn, which carried me to and fro. With all
6090𓀴that, a sort of horror of despair sat on my mind, so that I could have
6091𓀴wept at my own helplessness.
6092𓀴
6093𓀴I saw Alan knitting his brows at me, and supposed it was in anger; and
6094𓀴that gave me a pang of light-headed fear, like what a child may have. I
6095𓀴remember, too, that I was smiling, and could not stop smiling, hard as
6096𓀴I tried; for I thought it was out of place at such a time. But my good
6097𓀴companion had nothing in his mind but kindness; and the next moment,
6098𓀴two of the gillies had me by the arms, and I began to be carried forward
6099𓀴with great swiftness (or so it appeared to me, although I dare say it
6100𓀴was slowly enough in truth), through a labyrinth of dreary glens and
6101𓀴hollows and into the heart of that dismal mountain of Ben Alder.
6102𓀴
6103𓀴
6104𓀴
6105𓀴
6106𓀴CHAPTER XXIII
6107𓀴
6108𓀴CLUNY’S CAGE
6109𓀴
6110𓀴
6111𓀴We came at last to the foot of an exceeding steep wood, which scrambled
6112𓀴up a craggy hillside, and was crowned by a naked precipice.
6113𓀴
6114𓀴“It’s here,” said one of the guides, and we struck up hill.
6115𓀴
6116𓀴The trees clung upon the slope, like sailors on the shrouds of a ship,
6117𓀴and their trunks were like the rounds of a ladder, by which we mounted.
6118𓀴
6119𓀴Quite at the top, and just before the rocky face of the cliff sprang
6120𓀴above the foliage, we found that strange house which was known in the
6121𓀴country as “Cluny’s Cage.” The trunks of several trees had been wattled
6122𓀴across, the intervals strengthened with stakes, and the ground behind
6123𓀴this barricade levelled up with earth to make the floor. A tree, which
6124𓀴grew out from the hillside, was the living centre-beam of the roof.
6125𓀴The walls were of wattle and covered with moss. The whole house had
6126𓀴something of an egg shape; and it half hung, half stood in that steep,
6127𓀴hillside thicket, like a wasp’s nest in a green hawthorn.
6128𓀴
6129𓀴Within, it was large enough to shelter five or six persons with some
6130𓀴comfort. A projection of the cliff had been cunningly employed to be the
6131𓀴fireplace; and the smoke rising against the face of the rock, and being
6132𓀴not dissimilar in colour, readily escaped notice from below.
6133𓀴
6134𓀴This was but one of Cluny’s hiding-places; he had caves, besides, and
6135𓀴underground chambers in several parts of his country; and following the
6136𓀴reports of his scouts, he moved from one to another as the soldiers
6137𓀴drew near or moved away. By this manner of living, and thanks to the
6138𓀴affection of his clan, he had not only stayed all this time in safety,
6139𓀴while so many others had fled or been taken and slain: but stayed four
6140𓀴or five years longer, and only went to France at last by the express
6141𓀴command of his master. There he soon died; and it is strange to reflect
6142𓀴that he may have regretted his Cage upon Ben Alder.
6143𓀴
6144𓀴When we came to the door he was seated by his rock chimney, watching a
6145𓀴gillie about some cookery. He was mighty plainly habited, with a knitted
6146𓀴nightcap drawn over his ears, and smoked a foul cutty pipe. For all that
6147𓀴he had the manners of a king, and it was quite a sight to see him rise
6148𓀴out of his place to welcome us.
6149𓀴
6150𓀴“Well, Mr. Stewart, come awa’, sir!” said he, “and bring in your friend
6151𓀴that as yet I dinna ken the name of.”
6152𓀴
6153𓀴“And how is yourself, Cluny?” said Alan. “I hope ye do brawly, sir. And
6154𓀴I am proud to see ye, and to present to ye my friend the Laird of Shaws,
6155𓀴Mr. David Balfour.”
6156𓀴
6157𓀴Alan never referred to my estate without a touch of a sneer, when we
6158𓀴were alone; but with strangers, he rang the words out like a herald.
6159𓀴
6160𓀴“Step in by, the both of ye, gentlemen,” says Cluny. “I make ye welcome
6161𓀴to my house, which is a queer, rude place for certain, but one where I
6162𓀴have entertained a royal personage, Mr. Stewart--ye doubtless ken the
6163𓀴personage I have in my eye. We’ll take a dram for luck, and as soon as
6164𓀴this handless man of mine has the collops ready, we’ll dine and take a
6165𓀴hand at the cartes as gentlemen should. My life is a bit driegh,” says
6166𓀴he, pouring out the brandy; “I see little company, and sit and twirl my
6167𓀴thumbs, and mind upon a great day that is gone by, and weary for another
6168𓀴great day that we all hope will be upon the road. And so here’s a toast
6169𓀴to ye: The Restoration!”
6170𓀴
6171𓀴Thereupon we all touched glasses and drank. I am sure I wished no ill
6172𓀴to King George; and if he had been there himself in proper person, it’s
6173𓀴like he would have done as I did. No sooner had I taken out the drain
6174𓀴than I felt hugely better, and could look on and listen, still a little
6175𓀴mistily perhaps, but no longer with the same groundless horror and
6176𓀴distress of mind.
6177𓀴
6178𓀴It was certainly a strange place, and we had a strange host. In his long
6179𓀴hiding, Cluny had grown to have all manner of precise habits, like those
6180𓀴of an old maid. He had a particular place, where no one else must sit;
6181𓀴the Cage was arranged in a particular way, which none must disturb;
6182𓀴cookery was one of his chief fancies, and even while he was greeting us
6183𓀴in, he kept an eye to the collops.
6184𓀴
6185𓀴It appears, he sometimes visited or received visits from his wife and
6186𓀴one or two of his nearest friends, under the cover of night; but for the
6187𓀴more part lived quite alone, and communicated only with his sentinels
6188𓀴and the gillies that waited on him in the Cage. The first thing in the
6189𓀴morning, one of them, who was a barber, came and shaved him, and gave
6190𓀴him the news of the country, of which he was immoderately greedy. There
6191𓀴was no end to his questions; he put them as earnestly as a child; and
6192𓀴at some of the answers, laughed out of all bounds of reason, and would
6193𓀴break out again laughing at the mere memory, hours after the barber was
6194𓀴gone.
6195𓀴
6196𓀴To be sure, there might have been a purpose in his questions; for
6197𓀴though he was thus sequestered, and like the other landed gentlemen of
6198𓀴Scotland, stripped by the late Act of Parliament of legal powers, he
6199𓀴still exercised a patriarchal justice in his clan. Disputes were brought
6200𓀴to him in his hiding-hole to be decided; and the men of his country,
6201𓀴who would have snapped their fingers at the Court of Session, laid
6202𓀴aside revenge and paid down money at the bare word of this forfeited and
6203𓀴hunted outlaw. When he was angered, which was often enough, he gave
6204𓀴his commands and breathed threats of punishment like any king; and his
6205𓀴gillies trembled and crouched away from him like children before a hasty
6206𓀴father. With each of them, as he entered, he ceremoniously shook hands,
6207𓀴both parties touching their bonnets at the same time in a military
6208𓀴manner. Altogether, I had a fair chance to see some of the inner
6209𓀴workings of a Highland clan; and this with a proscribed, fugitive chief;
6210𓀴his country conquered; the troops riding upon all sides in quest of
6211𓀴him, sometimes within a mile of where he lay; and when the least of the
6212𓀴ragged fellows whom he rated and threatened, could have made a fortune
6213𓀴by betraying him.
6214𓀴
6215𓀴On that first day, as soon as the collops were ready, Cluny gave them
6216𓀴with his own hand a squeeze of a lemon (for he was well supplied with
6217𓀴luxuries) and bade us draw in to our meal.
6218𓀴
6219𓀴“They,” said he, meaning the collops, “are such as I gave his Royal
6220𓀴Highness in this very house; bating the lemon juice, for at that time we
6221𓀴were glad to get the meat and never fashed for kitchen.[28] Indeed, there
6222𓀴were mair dragoons than lemons in my country in the year forty-six.”
6223𓀴
6224𓀴     [28] Condiment.
6225𓀴
6226𓀴I do not know if the collops were truly very good, but my heart rose
6227𓀴against the sight of them, and I could eat but little. All the while
6228𓀴Cluny entertained us with stories of Prince Charlie’s stay in the Cage,
6229𓀴giving us the very words of the speakers, and rising from his place
6230𓀴to show us where they stood. By these, I gathered the Prince was a
6231𓀴gracious, spirited boy, like the son of a race of polite kings, but not
6232𓀴so wise as Solomon. I gathered, too, that while he was in the Cage, he
6233𓀴was often drunk; so the fault that has since, by all accounts, made such
6234𓀴a wreck of him, had even then begun to show itself.
6235𓀴
6236𓀴We were no sooner done eating than Cluny brought out an old, thumbed,
6237𓀴greasy pack of cards, such as you may find in a mean inn; and his eyes
6238𓀴brightened in his face as he proposed that we should fall to playing.
6239𓀴
6240𓀴Now this was one of the things I had been brought up to eschew like
6241𓀴disgrace; it being held by my father neither the part of a Christian
6242𓀴nor yet of a gentleman to set his own livelihood and fish for that of
6243𓀴others, on the cast of painted pasteboard. To be sure, I might have
6244𓀴pleaded my fatigue, which was excuse enough; but I thought it behoved
6245𓀴that I should bear a testimony. I must have got very red in the face,
6246𓀴but I spoke steadily, and told them I had no call to be a judge
6247𓀴of others, but for my own part, it was a matter in which I had no
6248𓀴clearness.
6249𓀴
6250𓀴Cluny stopped mingling the cards. “What in deil’s name is this?” says
6251𓀴he. “What kind of Whiggish, canting talk is this, for the house of Cluny
6252𓀴Macpherson?”
6253𓀴
6254𓀴“I will put my hand in the fire for Mr. Balfour,” says Alan. “He is an
6255𓀴honest and a mettle gentleman, and I would have ye bear in mind who says
6256𓀴it. I bear a king’s name,” says he, cocking his hat; “and I and any that
6257𓀴I call friend are company for the best. But the gentleman is tired, and
6258𓀴should sleep; if he has no mind to the cartes, it will never hinder you
6259𓀴and me. And I’m fit and willing, sir, to play ye any game that ye can
6260𓀴name.”
6261𓀴
6262𓀴“Sir,” says Cluny, “in this poor house of mine I would have you to ken
6263𓀴that any gentleman may follow his pleasure. If your friend would like to
6264𓀴stand on his head, he is welcome. And if either he, or you, or any other
6265𓀴man, is not preceesely satisfied, I will be proud to step outside with
6266𓀴him.”
6267𓀴
6268𓀴I had no will that these two friends should cut their throats for my
6269𓀴sake.
6270𓀴
6271𓀴“Sir,” said I, “I am very wearied, as Alan says; and what’s more, as
6272𓀴you are a man that likely has sons of your own, I may tell you it was a
6273𓀴promise to my father.”
6274𓀴
6275𓀴“Say nae mair, say nae mair,” said Cluny, and pointed me to a bed of
6276𓀴heather in a corner of the Cage. For all that he was displeased enough,
6277𓀴looked at me askance, and grumbled when he looked. And indeed it must
6278𓀴be owned that both my scruples and the words in which I declared them,
6279𓀴smacked somewhat of the Covenanter, and were little in their place among
6280𓀴wild Highland Jacobites.
6281𓀴
6282𓀴What with the brandy and the venison, a strange heaviness had come over
6283𓀴me; and I had scarce lain down upon the bed before I fell into a kind
6284𓀴of trance, in which I continued almost the whole time of our stay in the
6285𓀴Cage. Sometimes I was broad awake and understood what passed; sometimes
6286𓀴I only heard voices, or men snoring, like the voice of a silly river;
6287𓀴and the plaids upon the wall dwindled down and swelled out again, like
6288𓀴firelight shadows on the roof. I must sometimes have spoken or cried
6289𓀴out, for I remember I was now and then amazed at being answered; yet
6290𓀴I was conscious of no particular nightmare, only of a general, black,
6291𓀴abiding horror--a horror of the place I was in, and the bed I lay in,
6292𓀴and the plaids on the wall, and the voices, and the fire, and myself.
6293𓀴
6294𓀴The barber-gillie, who was a doctor too, was called in to prescribe
6295𓀴for me; but as he spoke in the Gaelic, I understood not a word of his
6296𓀴opinion, and was too sick even to ask for a translation. I knew well
6297𓀴enough I was ill, and that was all I cared about.
6298𓀴
6299𓀴I paid little heed while I lay in this poor pass. But Alan and Cluny
6300𓀴were most of the time at the cards, and I am clear that Alan must have
6301𓀴begun by winning; for I remember sitting up, and seeing them hard at it,
6302𓀴and a great glittering pile of as much as sixty or a hundred guineas on
6303𓀴the table. It looked strange enough, to see all this wealth in a nest
6304𓀴upon a cliff-side, wattled about growing trees. And even then, I
6305𓀴thought it seemed deep water for Alan to be riding, who had no better
6306𓀴battle-horse than a green purse and a matter of five pounds.
6307𓀴
6308𓀴The luck, it seems, changed on the second day. About noon I was wakened
6309𓀴as usual for dinner, and as usual refused to eat, and was given a dram
6310𓀴with some bitter infusion which the barber had prescribed. The sun was
6311𓀴shining in at the open door of the Cage, and this dazzled and offended
6312𓀴me. Cluny sat at the table, biting the pack of cards. Alan had stooped
6313𓀴over the bed, and had his face close to my eyes; to which, troubled as
6314𓀴they were with the fever, it seemed of the most shocking bigness.
6315𓀴
6316𓀴He asked me for a loan of my money.
6317𓀴
6318𓀴“What for?” said I.
6319𓀴
6320𓀴“O, just for a loan,” said he.
6321𓀴
6322𓀴“But why?” I repeated. “I don’t see.”
6323𓀴
6324𓀴“Hut, David!” said Alan, “ye wouldnae grudge me a loan?”
6325𓀴
6326𓀴I would, though, if I had had my senses! But all I thought of then was
6327𓀴to get his face away, and I handed him my money.
6328𓀴
6329𓀴On the morning of the third day, when we had been forty-eight hours in
6330𓀴the Cage, I awoke with a great relief of spirits, very weak and weary
6331𓀴indeed, but seeing things of the right size and with their honest,
6332𓀴everyday appearance. I had a mind to eat, moreover, rose from bed of my
6333𓀴own movement, and as soon as we had breakfasted, stepped to the entry of
6334𓀴the Cage and sat down outside in the top of the wood. It was a grey day
6335𓀴with a cool, mild air: and I sat in a dream all morning, only disturbed
6336𓀴by the passing by of Cluny’s scouts and servants coming with provisions
6337𓀴and reports; for as the coast was at that time clear, you might almost
6338𓀴say he held court openly.
6339𓀴
6340𓀴When I returned, he and Alan had laid the cards aside, and were
6341𓀴questioning a gillie; and the chief turned about and spoke to me in the
6342𓀴Gaelic.
6343𓀴
6344𓀴“I have no Gaelic, sir,” said I.
6345𓀴
6346𓀴Now since the card question, everything I said or did had the power of
6347𓀴annoying Cluny. “Your name has more sense than yourself, then,” said he
6348𓀴angrily, “for it’s good Gaelic. But the point is this. My scout reports
6349𓀴all clear in the south, and the question is, have ye the strength to
6350𓀴go?”
6351𓀴
6352𓀴I saw cards on the table, but no gold; only a heap of little written
6353𓀴papers, and these all on Cluny’s side. Alan, besides, had an odd
6354𓀴look, like a man not very well content; and I began to have a strong
6355𓀴misgiving.
6356𓀴
6357𓀴“I do not know if I am as well as I should be,” said I, looking at Alan;
6358𓀴“but the little money we have has a long way to carry us.”
6359𓀴
6360𓀴Alan took his under-lip into his mouth, and looked upon the ground.
6361𓀴
6362𓀴“David,” says he at last, “I’ve lost it; there’s the naked truth.”
6363𓀴
6364𓀴“My money too?” said I.
6365𓀴
6366𓀴“Your money too,” says Alan, with a groan. “Ye shouldnae have given it
6367𓀴me. I’m daft when I get to the cartes.”
6368𓀴
6369𓀴“Hoot-toot! hoot-toot!” said Cluny. “It was all daffing; it’s all
6370𓀴nonsense. Of course you’ll have your money back again, and the double of
6371𓀴it, if ye’ll make so free with me. It would be a singular thing for me
6372𓀴to keep it. It’s not to be supposed that I would be any hindrance to
6373𓀴gentlemen in your situation; that would be a singular thing!” cries he,
6374𓀴and began to pull gold out of his pocket with a mighty red face.
6375𓀴
6376𓀴Alan said nothing, only looked on the ground.
6377𓀴
6378𓀴“Will you step to the door with me, sir?” said I.
6379𓀴
6380𓀴Cluny said he would be very glad, and followed me readily enough, but he
6381𓀴looked flustered and put out.
6382𓀴
6383𓀴“And now, sir,” says I, “I must first acknowledge your generosity.”
6384𓀴
6385𓀴“Nonsensical nonsense!” cries Cluny. “Where’s the generosity? This is
6386𓀴just a most unfortunate affair; but what would ye have me do--boxed
6387𓀴up in this bee-skep of a cage of mine--but just set my friends to the
6388𓀴cartes, when I can get them? And if they lose, of course, it’s not to be
6389𓀴supposed----” And here he came to a pause.
6390𓀴
6391𓀴“Yes,” said I, “if they lose, you give them back their money; and if
6392𓀴they win, they carry away yours in their pouches! I have said before
6393𓀴that I grant your generosity; but to me, sir, it’s a very painful thing
6394𓀴to be placed in this position.”
6395𓀴
6396𓀴There was a little silence, in which Cluny seemed always as if he was
6397𓀴about to speak, but said nothing. All the time he grew redder and redder
6398𓀴in the face.
6399𓀴
6400𓀴“I am a young man,” said I, “and I ask your advice. Advise me as you
6401𓀴would your son. My friend fairly lost his money, after having fairly
6402𓀴gained a far greater sum of yours; can I accept it back again? Would
6403𓀴that be the right part for me to play? Whatever I do, you can see for
6404𓀴yourself it must be hard upon a man of any pride.”
6405𓀴
6406𓀴“It’s rather hard on me, too, Mr. Balfour,” said Cluny, “and ye give
6407𓀴me very much the look of a man that has entrapped poor people to their
6408𓀴hurt. I wouldnae have my friends come to any house of mine to accept
6409𓀴affronts; no,” he cried, with a sudden heat of anger, “nor yet to give
6410𓀴them!”
6411𓀴
6412𓀴“And so you see, sir,” said I, “there is something to be said upon my
6413𓀴side; and this gambling is a very poor employ for gentlefolks. But I am
6414𓀴still waiting your opinion.”
6415𓀴
6416𓀴I am sure if ever Cluny hated any man it was David Balfour. He looked
6417𓀴me all over with a warlike eye, and I saw the challenge at his lips.
6418𓀴But either my youth disarmed him, or perhaps his own sense of justice.
6419𓀴Certainly it was a mortifying matter for all concerned, and not least
6420𓀴Cluny; the more credit that he took it as he did.
6421𓀴
6422𓀴“Mr. Balfour,” said he, “I think you are too nice and covenanting, but
6423𓀴for all that you have the spirit of a very pretty gentleman. Upon my
6424𓀴honest word, ye may take this money--it’s what I would tell my son--and
6425𓀴here’s my hand along with it!”
6426𓀴
6427𓀴
6428𓀴
6429𓀴
6430𓀴CHAPTER XXIV
6431𓀴
6432𓀴THE FLIGHT IN THE HEATHER: THE QUARREL
6433𓀴
6434𓀴
6435𓀴Alan and I were put across Loch Errocht under cloud of night, and went
6436𓀴down its eastern shore to another hiding-place near the head of Loch
6437𓀴Rannoch, whither we were led by one of the gillies from the Cage. This
6438𓀴fellow carried all our luggage and Alan’s great-coat in the bargain,
6439𓀴trotting along under the burthen, far less than the half of which used
6440𓀴to weigh me to the ground, like a stout hill pony with a feather; yet he
6441𓀴was a man that, in plain contest, I could have broken on my knee.
6442𓀴
6443𓀴Doubtless it was a great relief to walk disencumbered; and perhaps
6444𓀴without that relief, and the consequent sense of liberty and lightness,
6445𓀴I could not have walked at all. I was but new risen from a bed of
6446𓀴sickness; and there was nothing in the state of our affairs to hearten
6447𓀴me for much exertion; travelling, as we did, over the most dismal
6448𓀴deserts in Scotland, under a cloudy heaven, and with divided hearts
6449𓀴among the travellers.
6450𓀴
6451𓀴For long, we said nothing; marching alongside or one behind the other,
6452𓀴each with a set countenance: I, angry and proud, and drawing what
6453𓀴strength I had from these two violent and sinful feelings; Alan angry
6454𓀴and ashamed, ashamed that he had lost my money, angry that I should take
6455𓀴it so ill.
6456𓀴
6457𓀴The thought of a separation ran always the stronger in my mind; and the
6458𓀴more I approved of it, the more ashamed I grew of my approval. It would
6459𓀴be a fine, handsome, generous thing, indeed, for Alan to turn round and
6460𓀴say to me: “Go, I am in the most danger, and my company only increases
6461𓀴yours.” But for me to turn to the friend who certainly loved me, and say
6462𓀴to him: “You are in great danger, I am in but little; your friendship
6463𓀴is a burden; go, take your risks and bear your hardships alone----” no,
6464𓀴that was impossible; and even to think of it privily to myself, made my
6465𓀴cheeks to burn.
6466𓀴
6467𓀴And yet Alan had behaved like a child, and (what is worse) a treacherous
6468𓀴child. Wheedling my money from me while I lay half-conscious was scarce
6469𓀴better than theft; and yet here he was trudging by my side, without a
6470𓀴penny to his name, and by what I could see, quite blithe to sponge upon
6471𓀴the money he had driven me to beg. True, I was ready to share it with
6472𓀴him; but it made me rage to see him count upon my readiness.
6473𓀴
6474𓀴These were the two things uppermost in my mind; and I could open my
6475𓀴mouth upon neither without black ungenerosity. So I did the next worst,
6476𓀴and said nothing, nor so much as looked once at my companion, save with
6477𓀴the tail of my eye.
6478𓀴
6479𓀴At last, upon the other side of Loch Errocht, going over a smooth, rushy
6480𓀴place, where the walking was easy, he could bear it no longer, and came
6481𓀴close to me.
6482𓀴
6483𓀴“David,” says he, “this is no way for two friends to take a small
6484𓀴accident. I have to say that I’m sorry; and so that’s said. And now if
6485𓀴you have anything, ye’d better say it.”
6486𓀴
6487𓀴“O,” says I, “I have nothing.”
6488𓀴
6489𓀴He seemed disconcerted; at which I was meanly pleased.
6490𓀴
6491𓀴“No,” said he, with rather a trembling voice, “but when I say I was to
6492𓀴blame?”
6493𓀴
6494𓀴“Why, of course, ye were to blame,” said I, coolly; “and you will bear
6495𓀴me out that I have never reproached you.”
6496𓀴
6497𓀴“Never,” says he; “but ye ken very well that ye’ve done worse. Are we to
6498𓀴part? Ye said so once before. Are ye to say it again? There’s hills and
6499𓀴heather enough between here and the two seas, David; and I will own I’m
6500𓀴no very keen to stay where I’m no wanted.”
6501𓀴
6502𓀴This pierced me like a sword, and seemed to lay bare my private
6503𓀴disloyalty.
6504𓀴
6505𓀴“Alan Breck!” I cried; and then: “Do you think I am one to turn my
6506𓀴back on you in your chief need? You dursn’t say it to my face. My whole
6507𓀴conduct’s there to give the lie to it. It’s true, I fell asleep upon
6508𓀴the muir; but that was from weariness, and you do wrong to cast it up to
6509𓀴me----”
6510𓀴
6511𓀴“Which is what I never did,” said Alan.
6512𓀴
6513𓀴“But aside from that,” I continued, “what have I done that you should
6514𓀴even me to dogs by such a supposition? I never yet failed a friend, and
6515𓀴it’s not likely I’ll begin with you. There are things between us that I
6516𓀴can never forget, even if you can.”
6517𓀴
6518𓀴“I will only say this to ye, David,” said Alan, very quietly, “that I
6519𓀴have long been owing ye my life, and now I owe ye money. Ye should try
6520𓀴to make that burden light for me.”
6521𓀴
6522𓀴This ought to have touched me, and in a manner it did, but the wrong
6523𓀴manner. I felt I was behaving badly; and was now not only angry with
6524𓀴Alan, but angry with myself in the bargain; and it made me the more
6525𓀴cruel.
6526𓀴
6527𓀴“You asked me to speak,” said I. “Well, then, I will. You own yourself
6528𓀴that you have done me a disservice; I have had to swallow an affront: I
6529𓀴have never reproached you, I never named the thing till you did. And
6530𓀴now you blame me,” cried I, “because I cannae laugh and sing as if I was
6531𓀴glad to be affronted. The next thing will be that I’m to go down upon my
6532𓀴knees and thank you for it! Ye should think more of others, Alan
6533𓀴Breck. If ye thought more of others, ye would perhaps speak less about
6534𓀴yourself; and when a friend that likes you very well has passed over an
6535𓀴offence without a word, you would be blithe to let it lie, instead of
6536𓀴making it a stick to break his back with. By your own way of it, it was
6537𓀴you that was to blame; then it shouldnae be you to seek the quarrel.”
6538𓀴
6539𓀴“Aweel,” said Alan, “say nae mair.”
6540𓀴
6541𓀴And we fell back into our former silence; and came to our journey’s end,
6542𓀴and supped, and lay down to sleep, without another word.
6543𓀴
6544𓀴The gillie put us across Loch Rannoch in the dusk of the next day, and
6545𓀴gave us his opinion as to our best route. This was to get us up at once
6546𓀴into the tops of the mountains: to go round by a circuit, turning the
6547𓀴heads of Glen Lyon, Glen Lochay, and Glen Dochart, and come down upon
6548𓀴the lowlands by Kippen and the upper waters of the Forth. Alan was
6549𓀴little pleased with a route which led us through the country of his
6550𓀴blood-foes, the Glenorchy Campbells. He objected that by turning to the
6551𓀴east, we should come almost at once among the Athole Stewarts, a race of
6552𓀴his own name and lineage, although following a different chief, and come
6553𓀴besides by a far easier and swifter way to the place whither we were
6554𓀴bound. But the gillie, who was indeed the chief man of Cluny’s scouts,
6555𓀴had good reasons to give him on all hands, naming the force of troops
6556𓀴in every district, and alleging finally (as well as I could understand)
6557𓀴that we should nowhere be so little troubled as in a country of the
6558𓀴Campbells.
6559𓀴
6560𓀴Alan gave way at last, but with only half a heart. “It’s one of the
6561𓀴dowiest countries in Scotland,” said he. “There’s naething there that I
6562𓀴ken, but heath, and crows, and Campbells. But I see that ye’re a man of
6563𓀴some penetration; and be it as ye please!”
6564𓀴
6565𓀴We set forth accordingly by this itinerary; and for the best part of
6566𓀴three nights travelled on eerie mountains and among the well-heads of
6567𓀴wild rivers; often buried in mist, almost continually blown and rained
6568𓀴upon, and not once cheered by any glimpse of sunshine. By day, we lay
6569𓀴and slept in the drenching heather; by night, incessantly clambered upon
6570𓀴break-neck hills and among rude crags. We often wandered; we were often
6571𓀴so involved in fog, that we must lie quiet till it lightened. A fire was
6572𓀴never to be thought of. Our only food was drammach and a portion of cold
6573𓀴meat that we had carried from the Cage; and as for drink, Heaven knows
6574𓀴we had no want of water.
6575𓀴
6576𓀴This was a dreadful time, rendered the more dreadful by the gloom of
6577𓀴the weather and the country. I was never warm; my teeth chattered in my
6578𓀴head; I was troubled with a very sore throat, such as I had on the isle;
6579𓀴I had a painful stitch in my side, which never left me; and when I slept
6580𓀴in my wet bed, with the rain beating above and the mud oozing below me,
6581𓀴it was to live over again in fancy the worst part of my adventures--to
6582𓀴see the tower of Shaws lit by lightning, Ransome carried below on the
6583𓀴men’s backs, Shuan dying on the round-house floor, or Colin Campbell
6584𓀴grasping at the bosom of his coat. From such broken slumbers, I would be
6585𓀴aroused in the gloaming, to sit up in the same puddle where I had slept,
6586𓀴and sup cold drammach; the rain driving sharp in my face or running
6587𓀴down my back in icy trickles; the mist enfolding us like as in a gloomy
6588𓀴chamber--or, perhaps, if the wind blew, falling suddenly apart and
6589𓀴showing us the gulf of some dark valley where the streams were crying
6590𓀴aloud.
6591𓀴
6592𓀴The sound of an infinite number of rivers came up from all round. In
6593𓀴this steady rain the springs of the mountain were broken up; every glen
6594𓀴gushed water like a cistern; every stream was in high spate, and had
6595𓀴filled and overflowed its channel. During our night tramps, it was
6596𓀴solemn to hear the voice of them below in the valleys, now booming like
6597𓀴thunder, now with an angry cry. I could well understand the story of the
6598𓀴Water Kelpie, that demon of the streams, who is fabled to keep wailing
6599𓀴and roaring at the ford until the coming of the doomed traveller. Alan I
6600𓀴saw believed it, or half believed it; and when the cry of the river rose
6601𓀴more than usually sharp, I was little surprised (though, of course, I
6602𓀴would still be shocked) to see him cross himself in the manner of the
6603𓀴Catholics.
6604𓀴
6605𓀴During all these horrid wanderings we had no familiarity, scarcely even
6606𓀴that of speech. The truth is that I was sickening for my grave, which
6607𓀴is my best excuse. But besides that I was of an unforgiving disposition
6608𓀴from my birth, slow to take offence, slower to forget it, and now
6609𓀴incensed both against my companion and myself. For the best part of two
6610𓀴days he was unweariedly kind; silent, indeed, but always ready to help,
6611𓀴and always hoping (as I could very well see) that my displeasure would
6612𓀴blow by. For the same length of time I stayed in myself, nursing my
6613𓀴anger, roughly refusing his services, and passing him over with my eyes
6614𓀴as if he had been a bush or a stone.
6615𓀴
6616𓀴The second night, or rather the peep of the third day, found us upon a
6617𓀴very open hill, so that we could not follow our usual plan and lie down
6618𓀴immediately to eat and sleep. Before we had reached a place of shelter,
6619𓀴the grey had come pretty clear, for though it still rained, the clouds
6620𓀴ran higher; and Alan, looking in my face, showed some marks of concern.
6621𓀴
6622𓀴“Ye had better let me take your pack,” said he, for perhaps the ninth
6623𓀴time since we had parted from the scout beside Loch Rannoch.
6624𓀴
6625𓀴“I do very well, I thank you,” said I, as cold as ice.
6626𓀴
6627𓀴Alan flushed darkly. “I’ll not offer it again,” he said. “I’m not a
6628𓀴patient man, David.”
6629𓀴
6630𓀴“I never said you were,” said I, which was exactly the rude, silly
6631𓀴speech of a boy of ten.
6632𓀴
6633𓀴Alan made no answer at the time, but his conduct answered for him.
6634𓀴Henceforth, it is to be thought, he quite forgave himself for the affair
6635𓀴at Cluny’s; cocked his hat again, walked jauntily, whistled airs, and
6636𓀴looked at me upon one side with a provoking smile.
6637𓀴
6638𓀴The third night we were to pass through the western end of the country
6639𓀴of Balquhidder. It came clear and cold, with a touch in the air like
6640𓀴frost, and a northerly wind that blew the clouds away and made the stars
6641𓀴bright. The streams were full, of course, and still made a great noise
6642𓀴among the hills; but I observed that Alan thought no more upon the
6643𓀴Kelpie, and was in high good spirits. As for me, the change of weather
6644𓀴came too late; I had lain in the mire so long that (as the Bible has it)
6645𓀴my very clothes “abhorred me.” I was dead weary, deadly sick and full
6646𓀴of pains and shiverings; the chill of the wind went through me, and the
6647𓀴sound of it confused my ears. In this poor state I had to bear from
6648𓀴my companion something in the nature of a persecution. He spoke a good
6649𓀴deal, and never without a taunt. “Whig” was the best name he had to give
6650𓀴me. “Here,” he would say, “here’s a dub for ye to jump, my Whiggie! I
6651𓀴ken you’re a fine jumper!” And so on; all the time with a gibing voice
6652𓀴and face.
6653𓀴
6654𓀴I knew it was my own doing, and no one else’s; but I was too miserable
6655𓀴to repent. I felt I could drag myself but little farther; pretty soon, I
6656𓀴must lie down and die on these wet mountains like a sheep or a fox, and
6657𓀴my bones must whiten there like the bones of a beast. My head was light
6658𓀴perhaps; but I began to love the prospect, I began to glory in the
6659𓀴thought of such a death, alone in the desert, with the wild eagles
6660𓀴besieging my last moments. Alan would repent then, I thought; he would
6661𓀴remember, when I was dead, how much he owed me, and the remembrance
6662𓀴would be torture. So I went like a sick, silly, and bad-hearted
6663𓀴schoolboy, feeding my anger against a fellow-man, when I would have
6664𓀴been better on my knees, crying on God for mercy. And at each of Alan’s
6665𓀴taunts, I hugged myself. “Ah!” thinks I to myself, “I have a better
6666𓀴taunt in readiness; when I lie down and die, you will feel it like a
6667𓀴buffet in your face; ah, what a revenge! ah, how you will regret your
6668𓀴ingratitude and cruelty!”
6669𓀴
6670𓀴All the while, I was growing worse and worse. Once I had fallen, my leg
6671𓀴simply doubling under me, and this had struck Alan for the moment; but I
6672𓀴was afoot so briskly, and set off again with such a natural manner,
6673𓀴that he soon forgot the incident. Flushes of heat went over me, and then
6674𓀴spasms of shuddering. The stitch in my side was hardly bearable. At last
6675𓀴I began to feel that I could trail myself no farther: and with that,
6676𓀴there came on me all at once the wish to have it out with Alan, let my
6677𓀴anger blaze, and be done with my life in a more sudden manner. He had
6678𓀴just called me “Whig.” I stopped.
6679𓀴
6680𓀴“Mr. Stewart,” said I, in a voice that quivered like a fiddle-string,
6681𓀴“you are older than I am, and should know your manners. Do you think
6682𓀴it either very wise or very witty to cast my politics in my teeth? I
6683𓀴thought, where folk differed, it was the part of gentlemen to differ
6684𓀴civilly; and if I did not, I may tell you I could find a better taunt
6685𓀴than some of yours.”
6686𓀴
6687𓀴Alan had stopped opposite to me, his hat cocked, his hands in his
6688𓀴breeches pockets, his head a little on one side. He listened, smiling
6689𓀴evilly, as I could see by the starlight; and when I had done he began to
6690𓀴whistle a Jacobite air. It was the air made in mockery of General Cope’s
6691𓀴defeat at Preston Pans:
6692𓀴
6693𓀴     “Hey, Johnnie Cope, are ye waukin’ yet?
6694𓀴     And are your drums a-beatin’ yet?”
6695𓀴
6696𓀴And it came in my mind that Alan, on the day of that battle, had been
6697𓀴engaged upon the royal side.
6698𓀴
6699𓀴“Why do ye take that air, Mr. Stewart?” said I. “Is that to remind me
6700𓀴you have been beaten on both sides?”
6701𓀴
6702𓀴The air stopped on Alan’s lips. “David!” said he.
6703𓀴
6704𓀴“But it’s time these manners ceased,” I continued; “and I mean you shall
6705𓀴henceforth speak civilly of my King and my good friends the Campbells.”
6706𓀴
6707𓀴“I am a Stewart--” began Alan.
6708𓀴
6709𓀴“O!” says I, “I ken ye bear a king’s name. But you are to remember,
6710𓀴since I have been in the Highlands, I have seen a good many of those
6711𓀴that bear it; and the best I can say of them is this, that they would be
6712𓀴none the worse of washing.”
6713𓀴
6714𓀴“Do you know that you insult me?” said Alan, very low.
6715𓀴
6716𓀴“I am sorry for that,” said I, “for I am not done; and if you distaste
6717𓀴the sermon, I doubt the pirliecue[29] will please you as little. You have
6718𓀴been chased in the field by the grown men of my party; it seems a poor
6719𓀴kind of pleasure to out-face a boy. Both the Campbells and the Whigs
6720𓀴have beaten you; you have run before them like a hare. It behoves you to
6721𓀴speak of them as of your betters.”
6722𓀴
6723𓀴     [29] A second sermon.
6724𓀴
6725𓀴Alan stood quite still, the tails of his great-coat clapping behind him
6726𓀴in the wind.
6727𓀴
6728𓀴“This is a pity,” he said at last. “There are things said that cannot be
6729𓀴passed over.”
6730𓀴
6731𓀴“I never asked you to,” said I. “I am as ready as yourself.”
6732𓀴
6733𓀴“Ready?” said he.
6734𓀴
6735𓀴“Ready,” I repeated. “I am no blower and boaster like some that I could
6736𓀴name. Come on!” And drawing my sword, I fell on guard as Alan himself
6737𓀴had taught me.
6738𓀴
6739𓀴“David!” he cried. “Are ye daft? I cannae draw upon ye, David. It’s
6740𓀴fair murder.”
6741𓀴
6742𓀴“That was your look-out when you insulted me,” said I.
6743𓀴
6744𓀴“It’s the truth!” cried Alan, and he stood for a moment, wringing his
6745𓀴mouth in his hand like a man in sore perplexity. “It’s the bare truth,”
6746𓀴 he said, and drew his sword. But before I could touch his blade with
6747𓀴mine, he had thrown it from him and fallen to the ground. “Na, na,” he
6748𓀴kept saying, “na, na--I cannae, I cannae.”
6749𓀴
6750𓀴At this the last of my anger oozed all out of me; and I found myself
6751𓀴only sick, and sorry, and blank, and wondering at myself. I would have
6752𓀴given the world to take back what I had said; but a word once spoken,
6753𓀴who can recapture it? I minded me of all Alan’s kindness and courage in
6754𓀴the past, how he had helped and cheered and borne with me in our evil
6755𓀴days; and then recalled my own insults, and saw that I had lost for ever
6756𓀴that doughty friend. At the same time, the sickness that hung upon
6757𓀴me seemed to redouble, and the pang in my side was like a sword for
6758𓀴sharpness. I thought I must have swooned where I stood.
6759𓀴
6760𓀴This it was that gave me a thought. No apology could blot out what I had
6761𓀴said; it was needless to think of one, none could cover the offence; but
6762𓀴where an apology was vain, a mere cry for help might bring Alan back to
6763𓀴my side. I put my pride away from me. “Alan!” I said; “if ye cannae help
6764𓀴me, I must just die here.”
6765𓀴
6766𓀴He started up sitting, and looked at me.
6767𓀴
6768𓀴“It’s true,” said I. “I’m by with it. O, let me get into the bield of a
6769𓀴house--I’ll can die there easier.” I had no need to pretend; whether I
6770𓀴chose or not, I spoke in a weeping voice that would have melted a heart
6771𓀴of stone.
6772𓀴
6773𓀴“Can ye walk?” asked Alan.
6774𓀴
6775𓀴“No,” said I, “not without help. This last hour my legs have been
6776𓀴fainting under me; I’ve a stitch in my side like a red-hot iron; I
6777𓀴cannae breathe right. If I die, ye’ll can forgive me, Alan? In my heart,
6778𓀴I liked ye fine--even when I was the angriest.”
6779𓀴
6780𓀴“Wheesht, wheesht!” cried Alan. “Dinna say that! David man, ye ken--” He
6781𓀴shut his mouth upon a sob. “Let me get my arm about ye,” he continued;
6782𓀴“that’s the way! Now lean upon me hard. Gude kens where there’s a house!
6783𓀴We’re in Balwhidder, too; there should be no want of houses, no, nor
6784𓀴friends’ houses here. Do ye gang easier so, Davie?”
6785𓀴
6786𓀴“Ay,” said I, “I can be doing this way;” and I pressed his arm with my
6787𓀴hand.
6788𓀴
6789𓀴Again he came near sobbing. “Davie,” said he, “I’m no a right man at
6790𓀴all; I have neither sense nor kindness; I could nae remember ye were
6791𓀴just a bairn, I couldnae see ye were dying on your feet; Davie, ye’ll
6792𓀴have to try and forgive me.”
6793𓀴
6794𓀴“O man, let’s say no more about it!” said I. “We’re neither one of us
6795𓀴to mend the other--that’s the truth! We must just bear and forbear, man
6796𓀴Alan. O, but my stitch is sore! Is there nae house?”
6797𓀴
6798𓀴“I’ll find a house to ye, David,” he said, stoutly. “We’ll follow down
6799𓀴the burn, where there’s bound to be houses. My poor man, will ye no be
6800𓀴better on my back?”
6801𓀴
6802𓀴“O, Alan,” says I, “and me a good twelve inches taller?”
6803𓀴
6804𓀴“Ye’re no such a thing,” cried Alan, with a start. “There may be a
6805𓀴trifling matter of an inch or two; I’m no saying I’m just exactly what
6806𓀴ye would call a tall man, whatever; and I dare say,” he added, his voice
6807𓀴tailing off in a laughable manner, “now when I come to think of it, I
6808𓀴dare say ye’ll be just about right. Ay, it’ll be a foot, or near hand;
6809𓀴or may be even mair!”
6810𓀴
6811𓀴It was sweet and laughable to hear Alan eat his words up in the fear of
6812𓀴some fresh quarrel. I could have laughed, had not my stitch caught me so
6813𓀴hard; but if I had laughed, I think I must have wept too.
6814𓀴
6815𓀴“Alan,” cried I, “what makes ye so good to me? What makes ye care for
6816𓀴such a thankless fellow?”
6817𓀴
6818𓀴“‘Deed, and I don’t know” said Alan. “For just precisely what I thought
6819𓀴I liked about ye, was that ye never quarrelled:--and now I like ye
6820𓀴better!”
6821𓀴
6822𓀴
6823𓀴
6824𓀴
6825𓀴CHAPTER XXV
6826𓀴
6827𓀴IN BALQUHIDDER
6828𓀴
6829𓀴
6830𓀴At the door of the first house we came to, Alan knocked, which was of
6831𓀴no very safe enterprise in such a part of the Highlands as the Braes of
6832𓀴Balquhidder. No great clan held rule there; it was filled and disputed
6833𓀴by small septs, and broken remnants, and what they call “chiefless
6834𓀴folk,” driven into the wild country about the springs of Forth and Teith
6835𓀴by the advance of the Campbells. Here were Stewarts and Maclarens, which
6836𓀴came to the same thing, for the Maclarens followed Alan’s chief in war,
6837𓀴and made but one clan with Appin. Here, too, were many of that old,
6838𓀴proscribed, nameless, red-handed clan of the Macgregors. They had always
6839𓀴been ill-considered, and now worse than ever, having credit with no side
6840𓀴or party in the whole country of Scotland. Their chief, Macgregor of
6841𓀴Macgregor, was in exile; the more immediate leader of that part of them
6842𓀴about Balquhidder, James More, Rob Roy’s eldest son, lay waiting his
6843𓀴trial in Edinburgh Castle; they were in ill-blood with Highlander and
6844𓀴Lowlander, with the Grahames, the Maclarens, and the Stewarts; and Alan,
6845𓀴who took up the quarrel of any friend, however distant, was extremely
6846𓀴wishful to avoid them.
6847𓀴
6848𓀴Chance served us very well; for it was a household of Maclarens that we
6849𓀴found, where Alan was not only welcome for his name’s sake but known
6850𓀴by reputation. Here then I was got to bed without delay, and a doctor
6851𓀴fetched, who found me in a sorry plight. But whether because he was a
6852𓀴very good doctor, or I a very young, strong man, I lay bedridden for no
6853𓀴more than a week, and before a month I was able to take the road again
6854𓀴with a good heart.
6855𓀴
6856𓀴All this time Alan would not leave me though I often pressed him, and
6857𓀴indeed his foolhardiness in staying was a common subject of outcry with
6858𓀴the two or three friends that were let into the secret. He hid by day
6859𓀴in a hole of the braes under a little wood; and at night, when the coast
6860𓀴was clear, would come into the house to visit me. I need not say if I
6861𓀴was pleased to see him; Mrs. Maclaren, our hostess, thought nothing good
6862𓀴enough for such a guest; and as Duncan Dhu (which was the name of our
6863𓀴host) had a pair of pipes in his house, and was much of a lover of
6864𓀴music, this time of my recovery was quite a festival, and we commonly
6865𓀴turned night into day.
6866𓀴
6867𓀴The soldiers let us be; although once a party of two companies and some
6868𓀴dragoons went by in the bottom of the valley, where I could see them
6869𓀴through the window as I lay in bed. What was much more astonishing, no
6870𓀴magistrate came near me, and there was no question put of whence I came
6871𓀴or whither I was going; and in that time of excitement, I was as free of
6872𓀴all inquiry as though I had lain in a desert. Yet my presence was known
6873𓀴before I left to all the people in Balquhidder and the adjacent parts;
6874𓀴many coming about the house on visits and these (after the custom of the
6875𓀴country) spreading the news among their neighbours. The bills, too, had
6876𓀴now been printed. There was one pinned near the foot of my bed, where
6877𓀴I could read my own not very flattering portrait and, in larger
6878𓀴characters, the amount of the blood money that had been set upon my
6879𓀴life. Duncan Dhu and the rest that knew that I had come there in Alan’s
6880𓀴company, could have entertained no doubt of who I was; and many others
6881𓀴must have had their guess. For though I had changed my clothes, I could
6882𓀴not change my age or person; and Lowland boys of eighteen were not so
6883𓀴rife in these parts of the world, and above all about that time, that
6884𓀴they could fail to put one thing with another, and connect me with the
6885𓀴bill. So it was, at least. Other folk keep a secret among two or three
6886𓀴near friends, and somehow it leaks out; but among these clansmen, it is
6887𓀴told to a whole countryside, and they will keep it for a century.
6888𓀴
6889𓀴There was but one thing happened worth narrating; and that is the visit
6890𓀴I had of Robin Oig, one of the sons of the notorious Rob Roy. He was
6891𓀴sought upon all sides on a charge of carrying a young woman from
6892𓀴Balfron and marrying her (as was alleged) by force; yet he stepped about
6893𓀴Balquhidder like a gentleman in his own walled policy. It was he who had
6894𓀴shot James Maclaren at the plough stilts, a quarrel never satisfied; yet
6895𓀴he walked into the house of his blood enemies as a rider[30] might into a
6896𓀴public inn.
6897𓀴
6898𓀴     [30] Commercial traveller.
6899𓀴
6900𓀴Duncan had time to pass me word of who it was; and we looked at one
6901𓀴another in concern. You should understand, it was then close upon the
6902𓀴time of Alan’s coming; the two were little likely to agree; and yet if
6903𓀴we sent word or sought to make a signal, it was sure to arouse suspicion
6904𓀴in a man under so dark a cloud as the Macgregor.
6905𓀴
6906𓀴He came in with a great show of civility, but like a man among
6907𓀴inferiors; took off his bonnet to Mrs. Maclaren, but clapped it on his
6908𓀴head again to speak to Duncan; and having thus set himself (as he would
6909𓀴have thought) in a proper light, came to my bedside and bowed.
6910𓀴
6911𓀴“I am given to know, sir,” says he, “that your name is Balfour.”
6912𓀴
6913𓀴“They call me David Balfour,” said I, “at your service.”
6914𓀴
6915𓀴“I would give ye my name in return, sir,” he replied, “but it’s one
6916𓀴somewhat blown upon of late days; and it’ll perhaps suffice if I tell
6917𓀴ye that I am own brother to James More Drummond or Macgregor, of whom ye
6918𓀴will scarce have failed to hear.”
6919𓀴
6920𓀴“No, sir,” said I, a little alarmed; “nor yet of your father,
6921𓀴Macgregor-Campbell.” And I sat up and bowed in bed; for I thought best
6922𓀴to compliment him, in case he was proud of having had an outlaw to his
6923𓀴father.
6924𓀴
6925𓀴He bowed in return. “But what I am come to say, sir,” he went on, “is
6926𓀴this. In the year ‘45, my brother raised a part of the ‘Gregara’ and
6927𓀴marched six companies to strike a stroke for the good side; and the
6928𓀴surgeon that marched with our clan and cured my brother’s leg when it
6929𓀴was broken in the brush at Preston Pans, was a gentleman of the same
6930𓀴name precisely as yourself. He was brother to Balfour of Baith; and if
6931𓀴you are in any reasonable degree of nearness one of that gentleman’s
6932𓀴kin, I have come to put myself and my people at your command.”
6933𓀴
6934𓀴You are to remember that I knew no more of my descent than any cadger’s
6935𓀴dog; my uncle, to be sure, had prated of some of our high connections,
6936𓀴but nothing to the present purpose; and there was nothing left me but
6937𓀴that bitter disgrace of owning that I could not tell.
6938𓀴
6939𓀴Robin told me shortly he was sorry he had put himself about, turned his
6940𓀴back upon me without a sign of salutation, and as he went towards the
6941𓀴door, I could hear him telling Duncan that I was “only some kinless loon
6942𓀴that didn’t know his own father.” Angry as I was at these words, and
6943𓀴ashamed of my own ignorance, I could scarce keep from smiling that a
6944𓀴man who was under the lash of the law (and was indeed hanged some three
6945𓀴years later) should be so nice as to the descent of his acquaintances.
6946𓀴
6947𓀴Just in the door, he met Alan coming in; and the two drew back and
6948𓀴looked at each other like strange dogs. They were neither of them big
6949𓀴men, but they seemed fairly to swell out with pride. Each wore a sword,
6950𓀴and by a movement of his haunch, thrust clear the hilt of it, so that it
6951𓀴might be the more readily grasped and the blade drawn.
6952𓀴
6953𓀴“Mr. Stewart, I am thinking,” says Robin.
6954𓀴
6955𓀴“Troth, Mr. Macgregor, it’s not a name to be ashamed of,” answered Alan.
6956𓀴
6957𓀴“I did not know ye were in my country, sir,” says Robin.
6958𓀴
6959𓀴“It sticks in my mind that I am in the country of my friends the
6960𓀴Maclarens,” says Alan.
6961𓀴
6962𓀴“That’s a kittle point,” returned the other. “There may be two words to
6963𓀴say to that. But I think I will have heard that you are a man of your
6964𓀴sword?”
6965𓀴
6966𓀴“Unless ye were born deaf, Mr. Macgregor, ye will have heard a good deal
6967𓀴more than that,” says Alan. “I am not the only man that can draw steel
6968𓀴in Appin; and when my kinsman and captain, Ardshiel, had a talk with a
6969𓀴gentleman of your name, not so many years back, I could never hear that
6970𓀴the Macgregor had the best of it.”
6971𓀴
6972𓀴“Do ye mean my father, sir?” says Robin.
6973𓀴
6974𓀴“Well, I wouldnae wonder,” said Alan. “The gentleman I have in my mind
6975𓀴had the ill-taste to clap Campbell to his name.”
6976𓀴
6977𓀴“My father was an old man,” returned Robin.
6978𓀴
6979𓀴“The match was unequal. You and me would make a better pair, sir.”
6980𓀴
6981𓀴“I was thinking that,” said Alan.
6982𓀴
6983𓀴I was half out of bed, and Duncan had been hanging at the elbow of these
6984𓀴fighting cocks, ready to intervene upon the least occasion. But when
6985𓀴that word was uttered, it was a case of now or never; and Duncan, with
6986𓀴something of a white face to be sure, thrust himself between.
6987𓀴
6988𓀴“Gentlemen,” said he, “I will have been thinking of a very different
6989𓀴matter, whateffer. Here are my pipes, and here are you two gentlemen who
6990𓀴are baith acclaimed pipers. It’s an auld dispute which one of ye’s the
6991𓀴best. Here will be a braw chance to settle it.”
6992𓀴
6993𓀴“Why, sir,” said Alan, still addressing Robin, from whom indeed he had
6994𓀴not so much as shifted his eyes, nor yet Robin from him, “why, sir,”
6995𓀴 says Alan, “I think I will have heard some sough[31] of the sort. Have ye
6996𓀴music, as folk say? Are ye a bit of a piper?”
6997𓀴
6998𓀴     [31] Rumour.
6999𓀴
7000𓀴“I can pipe like a Macrimmon!” cries Robin.
7001𓀴
7002𓀴“And that is a very bold word,” quoth Alan.
7003𓀴
7004𓀴“I have made bolder words good before now,” returned Robin, “and that
7005𓀴against better adversaries.”
7006𓀴
7007𓀴“It is easy to try that,” says Alan.
7008𓀴
7009𓀴Duncan Dhu made haste to bring out the pair of pipes that was his
7010𓀴principal possession, and to set before his guests a mutton-ham and a
7011𓀴bottle of that drink which they call Athole brose, and which is made of
7012𓀴old whiskey, strained honey and sweet cream, slowly beaten together in
7013𓀴the right order and proportion. The two enemies were still on the very
7014𓀴breach of a quarrel; but down they sat, one upon each side of the peat
7015𓀴fire, with a mighty show of politeness. Maclaren pressed them to taste
7016𓀴his mutton-ham and “the wife’s brose,” reminding them the wife was out
7017𓀴of Athole and had a name far and wide for her skill in that confection.
7018𓀴But Robin put aside these hospitalities as bad for the breath.
7019𓀴
7020𓀴“I would have ye to remark, sir,” said Alan, “that I havenae broken
7021𓀴bread for near upon ten hours, which will be worse for the breath than
7022𓀴any brose in Scotland.”
7023𓀴
7024𓀴“I will take no advantages, Mr. Stewart,” replied Robin. “Eat and drink;
7025𓀴I’ll follow you.”
7026𓀴
7027𓀴Each ate a small portion of the ham and drank a glass of the brose to
7028𓀴Mrs. Maclaren; and then after a great number of civilities, Robin took
7029𓀴the pipes and played a little spring in a very ranting manner.
7030𓀴
7031𓀴“Ay, ye can blow” said Alan; and taking the instrument from his rival,
7032𓀴he first played the same spring in a manner identical with Robin’s; and
7033𓀴then wandered into variations, which, as he went on, he decorated with
7034𓀴a perfect flight of grace-notes, such as pipers love, and call the
7035𓀴“warblers.”
7036𓀴
7037𓀴I had been pleased with Robin’s playing, Alan’s ravished me.
7038𓀴
7039𓀴“That’s no very bad, Mr. Stewart,” said the rival, “but ye show a poor
7040𓀴device in your warblers.”
7041𓀴
7042𓀴“Me!” cried Alan, the blood starting to his face. “I give ye the lie.”
7043𓀴
7044𓀴“Do ye own yourself beaten at the pipes, then,” said Robin, “that ye
7045𓀴seek to change them for the sword?”
7046𓀴
7047𓀴“And that’s very well said, Mr. Macgregor,” returned Alan; “and in the
7048𓀴meantime” (laying a strong accent on the word) “I take back the lie. I
7049𓀴appeal to Duncan.”
7050𓀴
7051𓀴“Indeed, ye need appeal to naebody,” said Robin. “Ye’re a far better
7052𓀴judge than any Maclaren in Balquhidder: for it’s a God’s truth that
7053𓀴you’re a very creditable piper for a Stewart. Hand me the pipes.” Alan
7054𓀴did as he asked; and Robin proceeded to imitate and correct some part of
7055𓀴Alan’s variations, which it seemed that he remembered perfectly.
7056𓀴
7057𓀴“Ay, ye have music,” said Alan, gloomily.
7058𓀴
7059𓀴“And now be the judge yourself, Mr. Stewart,” said Robin; and taking up
7060𓀴the variations from the beginning, he worked them throughout to so new a
7061𓀴purpose, with such ingenuity and sentiment, and with so odd a fancy and
7062𓀴so quick a knack in the grace-notes, that I was amazed to hear him.
7063𓀴
7064𓀴As for Alan, his face grew dark and hot, and he sat and gnawed his
7065𓀴fingers, like a man under some deep affront. “Enough!” he cried. “Ye can
7066𓀴blow the pipes--make the most of that.” And he made as if to rise.
7067𓀴
7068𓀴But Robin only held out his hand as if to ask for silence, and struck
7069𓀴into the slow measure of a pibroch. It was a fine piece of music in
7070𓀴itself, and nobly played; but it seems, besides, it was a piece peculiar
7071𓀴to the Appin Stewarts and a chief favourite with Alan. The first notes
7072𓀴were scarce out, before there came a change in his face; when the time
7073𓀴quickened, he seemed to grow restless in his seat; and long before that
7074𓀴piece was at an end, the last signs of his anger died from him, and he
7075𓀴had no thought but for the music.
7076𓀴
7077𓀴“Robin Oig,” he said, when it was done, “ye are a great piper. I am not
7078𓀴fit to blow in the same kingdom with ye. Body of me! ye have mair music
7079𓀴in your sporran than I have in my head! And though it still sticks in
7080𓀴my mind that I could maybe show ye another of it with the cold steel,
7081𓀴I warn ye beforehand--it’ll no be fair! It would go against my heart to
7082𓀴haggle a man that can blow the pipes as you can!”
7083𓀴
7084𓀴Thereupon that quarrel was made up; all night long the brose was going
7085𓀴and the pipes changing hands; and the day had come pretty bright, and
7086𓀴the three men were none the better for what they had been taking, before
7087𓀴Robin as much as thought upon the road.
7088𓀴
7089𓀴
7090𓀴
7091𓀴
7092𓀴CHAPTER XXVI
7093𓀴
7094𓀴END OF THE FLIGHT: WE PASS THE FORTH
7095𓀴
7096𓀴
7097𓀴The month, as I have said, was not yet out, but it was already far
7098𓀴through August, and beautiful warm weather, with every sign of an early
7099𓀴and great harvest, when I was pronounced able for my journey. Our money
7100𓀴was now run to so low an ebb that we must think first of all on speed;
7101𓀴for if we came not soon to Mr. Rankeillor’s, or if when we came there he
7102𓀴should fail to help me, we must surely starve. In Alan’s view, besides,
7103𓀴the hunt must have now greatly slackened; and the line of the Forth and
7104𓀴even Stirling Bridge, which is the main pass over that river, would be
7105𓀴watched with little interest.
7106𓀴
7107𓀴“It’s a chief principle in military affairs,” said he, “to go where
7108𓀴ye are least expected. Forth is our trouble; ye ken the saying, ‘Forth
7109𓀴bridles the wild Hielandman.’ Well, if we seek to creep round about
7110𓀴the head of that river and come down by Kippen or Balfron, it’s just
7111𓀴precisely there that they’ll be looking to lay hands on us. But if we
7112𓀴stave on straight to the auld Brig of Stirling, I’ll lay my sword they
7113𓀴let us pass unchallenged.”
7114𓀴
7115𓀴The first night, accordingly, we pushed to the house of a Maclaren in
7116𓀴Strathire, a friend of Duncan’s, where we slept the twenty-first of the
7117𓀴month, and whence we set forth again about the fall of night to make
7118𓀴another easy stage. The twenty-second we lay in a heather bush on the
7119𓀴hillside in Uam Var, within view of a herd of deer, the happiest ten
7120𓀴hours of sleep in a fine, breathing sunshine and on bone-dry ground,
7121𓀴that I have ever tasted. That night we struck Allan Water, and followed
7122𓀴it down; and coming to the edge of the hills saw the whole Carse of
7123𓀴Stirling underfoot, as flat as a pancake, with the town and castle on a
7124𓀴hill in the midst of it, and the moon shining on the Links of Forth.
7125𓀴
7126𓀴“Now,” said Alan, “I kenna if ye care, but ye’re in your own land again.
7127𓀴We passed the Hieland Line in the first hour; and now if we could but
7128𓀴pass yon crooked water, we might cast our bonnets in the air.”
7129𓀴
7130𓀴In Allan Water, near by where it falls into the Forth, we found a little
7131𓀴sandy islet, overgrown with burdock, butterbur and the like low plants,
7132𓀴that would just cover us if we lay flat. Here it was we made our camp,
7133𓀴within plain view of Stirling Castle, whence we could hear the drums
7134𓀴beat as some part of the garrison paraded. Shearers worked all day in
7135𓀴a field on one side of the river, and we could hear the stones going
7136𓀴on the hooks and the voices and even the words of the men talking. It
7137𓀴behoved to lie close and keep silent. But the sand of the little isle
7138𓀴was sun-warm, the green plants gave us shelter for our heads, we had
7139𓀴food and drink in plenty; and to crown all, we were within sight of
7140𓀴safety.
7141𓀴
7142𓀴As soon as the shearers quit their work and the dusk began to fall,
7143𓀴we waded ashore and struck for the Bridge of Stirling, keeping to the
7144𓀴fields and under the field fences.
7145𓀴
7146𓀴The bridge is close under the castle hill, an old, high, narrow bridge
7147𓀴with pinnacles along the parapet; and you may conceive with how much
7148𓀴interest I looked upon it, not only as a place famous in history, but as
7149𓀴the very doors of salvation to Alan and myself. The moon was not yet up
7150𓀴when we came there; a few lights shone along the front of the fortress,
7151𓀴and lower down a few lighted windows in the town; but it was all mighty
7152𓀴still, and there seemed to be no guard upon the passage.
7153𓀴
7154𓀴I was for pushing straight across; but Alan was more wary.
7155𓀴
7156𓀴“It looks unco’ quiet,” said he; “but for all that we’ll lie down here
7157𓀴cannily behind a dyke, and make sure.”
7158𓀴
7159𓀴So we lay for about a quarter of an hour, whiles whispering, whiles
7160𓀴lying still and hearing nothing earthly but the washing of the water on
7161𓀴the piers. At last there came by an old, hobbling woman with a crutch
7162𓀴stick; who first stopped a little, close to where we lay, and bemoaned
7163𓀴herself and the long way she had travelled; and then set forth again up
7164𓀴the steep spring of the bridge. The woman was so little, and the night
7165𓀴still so dark, that we soon lost sight of her; only heard the sound of
7166𓀴her steps, and her stick, and a cough that she had by fits, draw slowly
7167𓀴farther away.
7168𓀴
7169𓀴“She’s bound to be across now,” I whispered.
7170𓀴
7171𓀴“Na,” said Alan, “her foot still sounds boss[32] upon the bridge.”
7172𓀴
7173𓀴     [32] Hollow.
7174𓀴
7175𓀴And just then--“Who goes?” cried a voice, and we heard the butt of
7176𓀴a musket rattle on the stones. I must suppose the sentry had been
7177𓀴sleeping, so that had we tried, we might have passed unseen; but he was
7178𓀴awake now, and the chance forfeited.
7179𓀴
7180𓀴“This’ll never do,” said Alan. “This’ll never, never do for us, David.”
7181𓀴
7182𓀴And without another word, he began to crawl away through the fields; and
7183𓀴a little after, being well out of eye-shot, got to his feet again, and
7184𓀴struck along a road that led to the eastward. I could not conceive what
7185𓀴he was doing; and indeed I was so sharply cut by the disappointment,
7186𓀴that I was little likely to be pleased with anything. A moment back
7187𓀴and I had seen myself knocking at Mr. Rankeillor’s door to claim my
7188𓀴inheritance, like a hero in a ballad; and here was I back again, a
7189𓀴wandering, hunted blackguard, on the wrong side of Forth.
7190𓀴
7191𓀴“Well?” said I.
7192𓀴
7193𓀴“Well,” said Alan, “what would ye have? They’re none such fools as I
7194𓀴took them for. We have still the Forth to pass, Davie--weary fall the
7195𓀴rains that fed and the hillsides that guided it!”
7196𓀴
7197𓀴“And why go east?” said I.
7198𓀴
7199𓀴“Ou, just upon the chance!” said he. “If we cannae pass the river, we’ll
7200𓀴have to see what we can do for the firth.”
7201𓀴
7202𓀴“There are fords upon the river, and none upon the firth,” said I.
7203𓀴
7204𓀴“To be sure there are fords, and a bridge forbye,” quoth Alan; “and of
7205𓀴what service, when they are watched?”
7206𓀴
7207𓀴“Well,” said I, “but a river can be swum.”
7208𓀴
7209𓀴“By them that have the skill of it,” returned he; “but I have yet to
7210𓀴hear that either you or me is much of a hand at that exercise; and for
7211𓀴my own part, I swim like a stone.”
7212𓀴
7213𓀴“I’m not up to you in talking back, Alan,” I said; “but I can see we’re
7214𓀴making bad worse. If it’s hard to pass a river, it stands to reason it
7215𓀴must be worse to pass a sea.”
7216𓀴
7217𓀴“But there’s such a thing as a boat,” says Alan, “or I’m the more
7218𓀴deceived.”
7219𓀴
7220𓀴“Ay, and such a thing as money,” says I. “But for us that have neither
7221𓀴one nor other, they might just as well not have been invented.”
7222𓀴
7223𓀴“Ye think so?” said Alan.
7224𓀴
7225𓀴“I do that,” said I.
7226𓀴
7227𓀴“David,” says he, “ye’re a man of small invention and less faith. But
7228𓀴let me set my wits upon the hone, and if I cannae beg, borrow, nor yet
7229𓀴steal a boat, I’ll make one!”
7230𓀴
7231𓀴“I think I see ye!” said I. “And what’s more than all that: if ye pass a
7232𓀴bridge, it can tell no tales; but if we pass the firth, there’s the boat
7233𓀴on the wrong side--somebody must have brought it--the country-side will
7234𓀴all be in a bizz---”
7235𓀴
7236𓀴“Man!” cried Alan, “if I make a boat, I’ll make a body to take it back
7237𓀴again! So deave me with no more of your nonsense, but walk (for that’s
7238𓀴what you’ve got to do)--and let Alan think for ye.”
7239𓀴
7240𓀴All night, then, we walked through the north side of the Carse under
7241𓀴the high line of the Ochil mountains; and by Alloa and Clackmannan and
7242𓀴Culross, all of which we avoided: and about ten in the morning, mighty
7243𓀴hungry and tired, came to the little clachan of Limekilns. This is a
7244𓀴place that sits near in by the water-side, and looks across the Hope to
7245𓀴the town of the Queensferry. Smoke went up from both of these, and from
7246𓀴other villages and farms upon all hands. The fields were being reaped;
7247𓀴two ships lay anchored, and boats were coming and going on the Hope.
7248𓀴It was altogether a right pleasant sight to me; and I could not take
7249𓀴my fill of gazing at these comfortable, green, cultivated hills and the
7250𓀴busy people both of the field and sea.
7251𓀴
7252𓀴For all that, there was Mr. Rankeillor’s house on the south shore, where
7253𓀴I had no doubt wealth awaited me; and here was I upon the north, clad in
7254𓀴poor enough attire of an outlandish fashion, with three silver shillings
7255𓀴left to me of all my fortune, a price set upon my head, and an outlawed
7256𓀴man for my sole company.
7257𓀴
7258𓀴“O, Alan!” said I, “to think of it! Over there, there’s all that heart
7259𓀴could want waiting me; and the birds go over, and the boats go over--all
7260𓀴that please can go, but just me only! O, man, but it’s a heart-break!”
7261𓀴
7262𓀴In Limekilns we entered a small change-house, which we only knew to be a
7263𓀴public by the wand over the door, and bought some bread and cheese from
7264𓀴a good-looking lass that was the servant. This we carried with us in a
7265𓀴bundle, meaning to sit and eat it in a bush of wood on the sea-shore,
7266𓀴that we saw some third part of a mile in front. As we went, I kept
7267𓀴looking across the water and sighing to myself; and though I took no
7268𓀴heed of it, Alan had fallen into a muse. At last he stopped in the way.
7269𓀴
7270𓀴“Did ye take heed of the lass we bought this of?” says he, tapping on
7271𓀴the bread and cheese.
7272𓀴
7273𓀴“To be sure,” said I, “and a bonny lass she was.”
7274𓀴
7275𓀴“Ye thought that?” cries he. “Man, David, that’s good news.”
7276𓀴
7277𓀴“In the name of all that’s wonderful, why so?” says I. “What good can
7278𓀴that do?”
7279𓀴
7280𓀴“Well,” said Alan, with one of his droll looks, “I was rather in hopes
7281𓀴it would maybe get us that boat.”
7282𓀴
7283𓀴“If it were the other way about, it would be liker it,” said I.
7284𓀴
7285𓀴“That’s all that you ken, ye see,” said Alan. “I don’t want the lass to
7286𓀴fall in love with ye, I want her to be sorry for ye, David; to which end
7287𓀴there is no manner of need that she should take you for a beauty. Let me
7288𓀴see” (looking me curiously over). “I wish ye were a wee thing paler; but
7289𓀴apart from that ye’ll do fine for my purpose--ye have a fine, hang-dog,
7290𓀴rag-and-tatter, clappermaclaw kind of a look to ye, as if ye had
7291𓀴stolen the coat from a potato-bogle. Come; right about, and back to the
7292𓀴change-house for that boat of ours.”
7293𓀴
7294𓀴I followed him, laughing.
7295𓀴
7296𓀴“David Balfour,” said he, “ye’re a very funny gentleman by your way of
7297𓀴it, and this is a very funny employ for ye, no doubt. For all that, if
7298𓀴ye have any affection for my neck (to say nothing of your own) ye will
7299𓀴perhaps be kind enough to take this matter responsibly. I am going to
7300𓀴do a bit of play-acting, the bottom ground of which is just exactly as
7301𓀴serious as the gallows for the pair of us. So bear it, if ye please, in
7302𓀴mind, and conduct yourself according.”
7303𓀴
7304𓀴“Well, well,” said I, “have it as you will.”
7305𓀴
7306𓀴As we got near the clachan, he made me take his arm and hang upon it
7307𓀴like one almost helpless with weariness; and by the time he pushed
7308𓀴open the change-house door, he seemed to be half carrying me. The maid
7309𓀴appeared surprised (as well she might be) at our speedy return; but
7310𓀴Alan had no words to spare for her in explanation, helped me to a chair,
7311𓀴called for a tass of brandy with which he fed me in little sips,
7312𓀴and then breaking up the bread and cheese helped me to eat it like
7313𓀴a nursery-lass; the whole with that grave, concerned, affectionate
7314𓀴countenance, that might have imposed upon a judge. It was small wonder
7315𓀴if the maid were taken with the picture we presented, of a poor, sick,
7316𓀴overwrought lad and his most tender comrade. She drew quite near, and
7317𓀴stood leaning with her back on the next table.
7318𓀴
7319𓀴“What’s like wrong with him?” said she at last.
7320𓀴
7321𓀴Alan turned upon her, to my great wonder, with a kind of fury. “Wrong?”
7322𓀴 cries he. “He’s walked more hundreds of miles than he has hairs upon his
7323𓀴chin, and slept oftener in wet heather than dry sheets. Wrong, quo’ she!
7324𓀴Wrong enough, I would think! Wrong, indeed!” and he kept grumbling to
7325𓀴himself as he fed me, like a man ill-pleased.
7326𓀴
7327𓀴“He’s young for the like of that,” said the maid.
7328𓀴
7329𓀴“Ower young,” said Alan, with his back to her.
7330𓀴
7331𓀴“He would be better riding,” says she.
7332𓀴
7333𓀴“And where could I get a horse to him?” cried Alan, turning on her with
7334𓀴the same appearance of fury. “Would ye have me steal?”
7335𓀴
7336𓀴I thought this roughness would have sent her off in dudgeon, as indeed
7337𓀴it closed her mouth for the time. But my companion knew very well what
7338𓀴he was doing; and for as simple as he was in some things of life, had a
7339𓀴great fund of roguishness in such affairs as these.
7340𓀴
7341𓀴“Ye neednae tell me,” she said at last--“ye’re gentry.”
7342𓀴
7343𓀴“Well,” said Alan, softened a little (I believe against his will) by
7344𓀴this artless comment, “and suppose we were? Did ever you hear that
7345𓀴gentrice put money in folk’s pockets?”
7346𓀴
7347𓀴She sighed at this, as if she were herself some disinherited great lady.
7348𓀴“No,” says she, “that’s true indeed.”
7349𓀴
7350𓀴I was all this while chafing at the part I played, and sitting
7351𓀴tongue-tied between shame and merriment; but somehow at this I could
7352𓀴hold in no longer, and bade Alan let me be, for I was better already. My
7353𓀴voice stuck in my throat, for I ever hated to take part in lies; but my
