7354𓀴very embarrassment helped on the plot, for the lass no doubt set down my
7355𓀴husky voice to sickness and fatigue.
7356𓀴
7357𓀴“Has he nae friends?” said she, in a tearful voice.
7358𓀴
7359𓀴“That has he so!” cried Alan, “if we could but win to them!--friends and
7360𓀴rich friends, beds to lie in, food to eat, doctors to see to him--and
7361𓀴here he must tramp in the dubs and sleep in the heather like a
7362𓀴beggarman.”
7363𓀴
7364𓀴“And why that?” says the lass.
7365𓀴
7366𓀴“My dear,” said Alan, “I cannae very safely say; but I’ll tell ye what
7367𓀴I’ll do instead,” says he, “I’ll whistle ye a bit tune.” And with that
7368𓀴he leaned pretty far over the table, and in a mere breath of a whistle,
7369𓀴but with a wonderful pretty sentiment, gave her a few bars of “Charlie
7370𓀴is my darling.”
7371𓀴
7372𓀴“Wheesht,” says she, and looked over her shoulder to the door.
7373𓀴
7374𓀴“That’s it,” said Alan.
7375𓀴
7376𓀴“And him so young!” cries the lass.
7377𓀴
7378𓀴“He’s old enough to----” and Alan struck his forefinger on the back part
7379𓀴of his neck, meaning that I was old enough to lose my head.
7380𓀴
7381𓀴“It would be a black shame,” she cried, flushing high.
7382𓀴
7383𓀴“It’s what will be, though,” said Alan, “unless we manage the better.”
7384𓀴
7385𓀴At this the lass turned and ran out of that part of the house, leaving
7386𓀴us alone together. Alan in high good humour at the furthering of his
7387𓀴schemes, and I in bitter dudgeon at being called a Jacobite and treated
7388𓀴like a child.
7389𓀴
7390𓀴“Alan,” I cried, “I can stand no more of this.”
7391𓀴
7392𓀴“Ye’ll have to sit it then, Davie,” said he. “For if ye upset the pot
7393𓀴now, ye may scrape your own life out of the fire, but Alan Breck is a
7394𓀴dead man.”
7395𓀴
7396𓀴This was so true that I could only groan; and even my groan served
7397𓀴Alan’s purpose, for it was overheard by the lass as she came flying in
7398𓀴again with a dish of white puddings and a bottle of strong ale.
7399𓀴
7400𓀴“Poor lamb!” says she, and had no sooner set the meat before us, than
7401𓀴she touched me on the shoulder with a little friendly touch, as much as
7402𓀴to bid me cheer up. Then she told us to fall to, and there would be no
7403𓀴more to pay; for the inn was her own, or at least her father’s, and he
7404𓀴was gone for the day to Pittencrieff. We waited for no second bidding,
7405𓀴for bread and cheese is but cold comfort and the puddings smelt
7406𓀴excellently well; and while we sat and ate, she took up that same place
7407𓀴by the next table, looking on, and thinking, and frowning to herself,
7408𓀴and drawing the string of her apron through her hand.
7409𓀴
7410𓀴“I’m thinking ye have rather a long tongue,” she said at last to Alan.
7411𓀴
7412𓀴“Ay” said Alan; “but ye see I ken the folk I speak to.”
7413𓀴
7414𓀴“I would never betray ye,” said she, “if ye mean that.”
7415𓀴
7416𓀴“No,” said he, “ye’re not that kind. But I’ll tell ye what ye would do,
7417𓀴ye would help.”
7418𓀴
7419𓀴“I couldnae,” said she, shaking her head. “Na, I couldnae.”
7420𓀴
7421𓀴“No,” said he, “but if ye could?”
7422𓀴
7423𓀴She answered him nothing.
7424𓀴
7425𓀴“Look here, my lass,” said Alan, “there are boats in the Kingdom of
7426𓀴Fife, for I saw two (no less) upon the beach, as I came in by your
7427𓀴town’s end. Now if we could have the use of a boat to pass under cloud
7428𓀴of night into Lothian, and some secret, decent kind of a man to bring
7429𓀴that boat back again and keep his counsel, there would be two souls
7430𓀴saved--mine to all likelihood--his to a dead surety. If we lack that
7431𓀴boat, we have but three shillings left in this wide world; and where
7432𓀴to go, and how to do, and what other place there is for us except the
7433𓀴chains of a gibbet--I give you my naked word, I kenna! Shall we go
7434𓀴wanting, lassie? Are ye to lie in your warm bed and think upon us, when
7435𓀴the wind gowls in the chimney and the rain tirls on the roof? Are ye to
7436𓀴eat your meat by the cheeks of a red fire, and think upon this poor sick
7437𓀴lad of mine, biting his finger ends on a blae muir for cauld and hunger?
7438𓀴Sick or sound, he must aye be moving; with the death grapple at his
7439𓀴throat he must aye be trailing in the rain on the lang roads; and when
7440𓀴he gants his last on a rickle of cauld stanes, there will be nae friends
7441𓀴near him but only me and God.”
7442𓀴
7443𓀴At this appeal, I could see the lass was in great trouble of mind,
7444𓀴being tempted to help us, and yet in some fear she might be helping
7445𓀴malefactors; and so now I determined to step in myself and to allay her
7446𓀴scruples with a portion of the truth.
7447𓀴
7448𓀴“Did ever you hear,” said I, “of Mr. Rankeillor of the Ferry?”
7449𓀴
7450𓀴“Rankeillor the writer?” said she. “I daur say that!”
7451𓀴
7452𓀴“Well,” said I, “it’s to his door that I am bound, so you may judge by
7453𓀴that if I am an ill-doer; and I will tell you more, that though I am
7454𓀴indeed, by a dreadful error, in some peril of my life, King George has
7455𓀴no truer friend in all Scotland than myself.”
7456𓀴
7457𓀴Her face cleared up mightily at this, although Alan’s darkened.
7458𓀴
7459𓀴“That’s more than I would ask,” said she. “Mr. Rankeillor is a kennt
7460𓀴man.” And she bade us finish our meat, get clear of the clachan as soon
7461𓀴as might be, and lie close in the bit wood on the sea-beach. “And ye can
7462𓀴trust me,” says she, “I’ll find some means to put you over.”
7463𓀴
7464𓀴At this we waited for no more, but shook hands with her upon the
7465𓀴bargain, made short work of the puddings, and set forth again from
7466𓀴Limekilns as far as to the wood. It was a small piece of perhaps a score
7467𓀴of elders and hawthorns and a few young ashes, not thick enough to veil
7468𓀴us from passersby upon the road or beach. Here we must lie, however,
7469𓀴making the best of the brave warm weather and the good hopes we now had
7470𓀴of a deliverance, and planing more particularly what remained for us to
7471𓀴do.
7472𓀴
7473𓀴We had but one trouble all day; when a strolling piper came and sat in
7474𓀴the same wood with us; a red-nosed, bleareyed, drunken dog, with a great
7475𓀴bottle of whisky in his pocket, and a long story of wrongs that had been
7476𓀴done him by all sorts of persons, from the Lord President of the
7477𓀴Court of Session, who had denied him justice, down to the Bailies of
7478𓀴Inverkeithing who had given him more of it than he desired. It was
7479𓀴impossible but he should conceive some suspicion of two men lying all
7480𓀴day concealed in a thicket and having no business to allege. As long as
7481𓀴he stayed there he kept us in hot water with prying questions; and after
7482𓀴he was gone, as he was a man not very likely to hold his tongue, we were
7483𓀴in the greater impatience to be gone ourselves.
7484𓀴
7485𓀴The day came to an end with the same brightness; the night fell quiet
7486𓀴and clear; lights came out in houses and hamlets and then, one after
7487𓀴another, began to be put out; but it was past eleven, and we were long
7488𓀴since strangely tortured with anxieties, before we heard the grinding
7489𓀴of oars upon the rowing-pins. At that, we looked out and saw the lass
7490𓀴herself coming rowing to us in a boat. She had trusted no one with our
7491𓀴affairs, not even her sweetheart, if she had one; but as soon as her
7492𓀴father was asleep, had left the house by a window, stolen a neighbour’s
7493𓀴boat, and come to our assistance single-handed.
7494𓀴
7495𓀴I was abashed how to find expression for my thanks; but she was no less
7496𓀴abashed at the thought of hearing them; begged us to lose no time and to
7497𓀴hold our peace, saying (very properly) that the heart of our matter was
7498𓀴in haste and silence; and so, what with one thing and another, she had
7499𓀴set us on the Lothian shore not far from Carriden, had shaken hands with
7500𓀴us, and was out again at sea and rowing for Limekilns, before there was
7501𓀴one word said either of her service or our gratitude.
7502𓀴
7503𓀴Even after she was gone, we had nothing to say, as indeed nothing was
7504𓀴enough for such a kindness. Only Alan stood a great while upon the shore
7505𓀴shaking his head.
7506𓀴
7507𓀴“It is a very fine lass,” he said at last. “David, it is a very fine
7508𓀴lass.” And a matter of an hour later, as we were lying in a den on
7509𓀴the sea-shore and I had been already dozing, he broke out again in
7510𓀴commendations of her character. For my part, I could say nothing, she
7511𓀴was so simple a creature that my heart smote me both with remorse and
7512𓀴fear: remorse because we had traded upon her ignorance; and fear lest we
7513𓀴should have anyway involved her in the dangers of our situation.
7514𓀴
7515𓀴
7516𓀴
7517𓀴
7518𓀴CHAPTER XXVII
7519𓀴
7520𓀴I COME TO MR. RANKEILLOR
7521𓀴
7522𓀴
7523𓀴The next day it was agreed that Alan should fend for himself till
7524𓀴sunset; but as soon as it began to grow dark, he should lie in the
7525𓀴fields by the roadside near to Newhalls, and stir for naught until he
7526𓀴heard me whistling. At first I proposed I should give him for a signal
7527𓀴the “Bonnie House of Airlie,” which was a favourite of mine; but he
7528𓀴objected that as the piece was very commonly known, any ploughman might
7529𓀴whistle it by accident; and taught me instead a little fragment of a
7530𓀴Highland air, which has run in my head from that day to this, and will
7531𓀴likely run in my head when I lie dying. Every time it comes to me, it
7532𓀴takes me off to that last day of my uncertainty, with Alan sitting up in
7533𓀴the bottom of the den, whistling and beating the measure with a finger,
7534𓀴and the grey of the dawn coming on his face.
7535𓀴
7536𓀴I was in the long street of Queensferry before the sun was up. It was a
7537𓀴fairly built burgh, the houses of good stone, many slated; the town-hall
7538𓀴not so fine, I thought, as that of Peebles, nor yet the street so noble;
7539𓀴but take it altogether, it put me to shame for my foul tatters.
7540𓀴
7541𓀴
7542𓀴
7543𓀴As the morning went on, and the fires began to be kindled, and the
7544𓀴windows to open, and the people to appear out of the houses, my concern
7545𓀴and despondency grew ever the blacker. I saw now that I had no grounds
7546𓀴to stand upon; and no clear proof of my rights, nor so much as of my own
7547𓀴identity. If it was all a bubble, I was indeed sorely cheated and left
7548𓀴in a sore pass. Even if things were as I conceived, it would in all
7549𓀴likelihood take time to establish my contentions; and what time had I
7550𓀴to spare with less than three shillings in my pocket, and a condemned,
7551𓀴hunted man upon my hands to ship out of the country? Truly, if my hope
7552𓀴broke with me, it might come to the gallows yet for both of us. And as I
7553𓀴continued to walk up and down, and saw people looking askance at me upon
7554𓀴the street or out of windows, and nudging or speaking one to another
7555𓀴with smiles, I began to take a fresh apprehension: that it might be no
7556𓀴easy matter even to come to speech of the lawyer, far less to convince
7557𓀴him of my story.
7558𓀴
7559𓀴For the life of me I could not muster up the courage to address any of
7560𓀴these reputable burghers; I thought shame even to speak with them in
7561𓀴such a pickle of rags and dirt; and if I had asked for the house of such
7562𓀴a man as Mr. Rankeillor, I suppose they would have burst out laughing in
7563𓀴my face. So I went up and down, and through the street, and down to
7564𓀴the harbour-side, like a dog that has lost its master, with a strange
7565𓀴gnawing in my inwards, and every now and then a movement of despair.
7566𓀴It grew to be high day at last, perhaps nine in the forenoon; and I was
7567𓀴worn with these wanderings, and chanced to have stopped in front of
7568𓀴a very good house on the landward side, a house with beautiful, clear
7569𓀴glass windows, flowering knots upon the sills, the walls new-harled[33] and
7570𓀴a chase-dog sitting yawning on the step like one that was at home. Well,
7571𓀴I was even envying this dumb brute, when the door fell open and
7572𓀴there issued forth a shrewd, ruddy, kindly, consequential man in a
7573𓀴well-powdered wig and spectacles. I was in such a plight that no one set
7574𓀴eyes on me once, but he looked at me again; and this gentleman, as it
7575𓀴proved, was so much struck with my poor appearance that he came straight
7576𓀴up to me and asked me what I did.
7577𓀴
7578𓀴     [33] Newly rough-cast.
7579𓀴
7580𓀴I told him I was come to the Queensferry on business, and taking heart
7581𓀴of grace, asked him to direct me to the house of Mr. Rankeillor.
7582𓀴
7583𓀴“Why,” said he, “that is his house that I have just come out of; and for
7584𓀴a rather singular chance, I am that very man.”
7585𓀴
7586𓀴“Then, sir,” said I, “I have to beg the favour of an interview.”
7587𓀴
7588𓀴“I do not know your name,” said he, “nor yet your face.”
7589𓀴
7590𓀴“My name is David Balfour,” said I.
7591𓀴
7592𓀴“David Balfour?” he repeated, in rather a high tone, like one surprised.
7593𓀴“And where have you come from, Mr. David Balfour?” he asked, looking me
7594𓀴pretty drily in the face.
7595𓀴
7596𓀴“I have come from a great many strange places, sir,” said I; “but I
7597𓀴think it would be as well to tell you where and how in a more private
7598𓀴manner.”
7599𓀴
7600𓀴He seemed to muse awhile, holding his lip in his hand, and looking now
7601𓀴at me and now upon the causeway of the street.
7602𓀴
7603𓀴“Yes,” says he, “that will be the best, no doubt.” And he led me back
7604𓀴with him into his house, cried out to some one whom I could not see
7605𓀴that he would be engaged all morning, and brought me into a little
7606𓀴dusty chamber full of books and documents. Here he sate down, and bade
7607𓀴me be seated; though I thought he looked a little ruefully from his
7608𓀴clean chair to my muddy rags. “And now,” says he, “if you have any
7609𓀴business, pray be brief and come swiftly to the point. _Nec gemino
7610𓀴bellum Trojanum orditur ab ovo_--do you understand that?” says he, with
7611𓀴a keen look.
7612𓀴
7613𓀴“I will even do as Horace says, sir,” I answered, smiling, “and carry
7614𓀴you _in medias res_.” He nodded as if he was well pleased, and indeed
7615𓀴his scrap of Latin had been set to test me. For all that, and though I
7616𓀴was somewhat encouraged, the blood came in my face when I added: “I
7617𓀴have reason to believe myself some rights on the estate of Shaws.”
7618𓀴
7619𓀴He got a paper book out of a drawer and set it before him open. “Well?”
7620𓀴 said he.
7621𓀴
7622𓀴But I had shot my bolt and sat speechless.
7623𓀴
7624𓀴“Come, come, Mr. Balfour,” said he, “you must continue. Where were you
7625𓀴born?”
7626𓀴
7627𓀴“In Essendean, sir,” said I, “the year 1733, the 12th of March.”
7628𓀴
7629𓀴He seemed to follow this statement in his paper book; but what that
7630𓀴meant I knew not. “Your father and mother?” said he.
7631𓀴
7632𓀴“My father was Alexander Balfour, schoolmaster of that place,” said I,
7633𓀴“and my mother Grace Pitarrow; I think her people were from Angus.”
7634𓀴
7635𓀴“Have you any papers proving your identity?” asked Mr. Rankeillor.
7636𓀴
7637𓀴“No, sir,” said I, “but they are in the hands of Mr. Campbell, the
7638𓀴minister, and could be readily produced. Mr. Campbell, too, would give
7639𓀴me his word; and for that matter, I do not think my uncle would deny
7640𓀴me.”
7641𓀴
7642𓀴“Meaning Mr. Ebenezer Balfour?” says he.
7643𓀴
7644𓀴“The same,” said I.
7645𓀴
7646𓀴“Whom you have seen?” he asked.
7647𓀴
7648𓀴“By whom I was received into his own house,” I answered.
7649𓀴
7650𓀴“Did you ever meet a man of the name of Hoseason?” asked Mr. Rankeillor.
7651𓀴
7652𓀴“I did so, sir, for my sins,” said I; “for it was by his means and the
7653𓀴procurement of my uncle, that I was kidnapped within sight of this town,
7654𓀴carried to sea, suffered shipwreck and a hundred other hardships, and
7655𓀴stand before you to-day in this poor accoutrement.”
7656𓀴
7657𓀴“You say you were shipwrecked,” said Rankeillor; “where was that?”
7658𓀴
7659𓀴“Off the south end of the Isle of Mull,” said I. “The name of the isle
7660𓀴on which I was cast up is the Island Earraid.”
7661𓀴
7662𓀴“Ah!” says he, smiling, “you are deeper than me in the geography. But so
7663𓀴far, I may tell you, this agrees pretty exactly with other informations
7664𓀴that I hold. But you say you were kidnapped; in what sense?”
7665𓀴
7666𓀴“In the plain meaning of the word, sir,” said I. “I was on my way to
7667𓀴your house, when I was trepanned on board the brig, cruelly struck down,
7668𓀴thrown below, and knew no more of anything till we were far at sea. I
7669𓀴was destined for the plantations; a fate that, in God’s providence, I
7670𓀴have escaped.”
7671𓀴
7672𓀴“The brig was lost on June the 27th,” says he, looking in his book,
7673𓀴“and we are now at August the 24th. Here is a considerable hiatus, Mr.
7674𓀴Balfour, of near upon two months. It has already caused a vast amount
7675𓀴of trouble to your friends; and I own I shall not be very well contented
7676𓀴until it is set right.”
7677𓀴
7678𓀴“Indeed, sir,” said I, “these months are very easily filled up; but yet
7679𓀴before I told my story, I would be glad to know that I was talking to a
7680𓀴friend.”
7681𓀴
7682𓀴“This is to argue in a circle,” said the lawyer. “I cannot be convinced
7683𓀴till I have heard you. I cannot be your friend till I am properly
7684𓀴informed. If you were more trustful, it would better befit your time of
7685𓀴life. And you know, Mr. Balfour, we have a proverb in the country that
7686𓀴evil-doers are aye evil-dreaders.”
7687𓀴
7688𓀴“You are not to forget, sir,” said I, “that I have already suffered by
7689𓀴my trustfulness; and was shipped off to be a slave by the very man that
7690𓀴(if I rightly understand) is your employer?”
7691𓀴
7692𓀴All this while I had been gaining ground with Mr. Rankeillor, and in
7693𓀴proportion as I gained ground, gaining confidence. But at this sally,
7694𓀴which I made with something of a smile myself, he fairly laughed aloud.
7695𓀴
7696𓀴“No, no,” said he, “it is not so bad as that. _Fui, non sum_. I was
7697𓀴indeed your uncle’s man of business; but while you (_imberbis juvenis
7698𓀴custode remoto_) were gallivanting in the west, a good deal of water
7699𓀴has run under the bridges; and if your ears did not sing, it was not
7700𓀴for lack of being talked about. On the very day of your sea disaster,
7701𓀴Mr. Campbell stalked into my office, demanding you from all the winds.
7702𓀴I had never heard of your existence; but I had known your father; and
7703𓀴from matters in my competence (to be touched upon hereafter) I was
7704𓀴disposed to fear the worst. Mr. Ebenezer admitted having seen you;
7705𓀴declared (what seemed improbable) that he had given you considerable
7706𓀴sums; and that you had started for the continent of Europe, intending
7707𓀴to fulfil your education, which was probable and praiseworthy.
7708𓀴Interrogated how you had come to send no word to Mr. Campbell, he
7709𓀴deponed that you had expressed a great desire to break with your past
7710𓀴life. Further interrogated where you now were, protested ignorance, but
7711𓀴believed you were in Leyden. That is a close sum of his replies. I am
7712𓀴not exactly sure that any one believed him,” continued Mr. Rankeillor
7713𓀴with a smile; “and in particular he so much disrelished me expressions
7714𓀴of mine that (in a word) he showed me to the door. We were then at a
7715𓀴full stand; for whatever shrewd suspicions we might entertain, we had
7716𓀴no shadow of probation. In the very article, comes Captain Hoseason
7717𓀴with the story of your drowning; whereupon all fell through; with no
7718𓀴consequences but concern to Mr. Campbell, injury to my pocket, and
7719𓀴another blot upon your uncle’s character, which could very ill afford
7720𓀴it. And now, Mr. Balfour,” said he, “you understand the whole process
7721𓀴of these matters, and can judge for yourself to what extent I may be
7722𓀴trusted.”
7723𓀴
7724𓀴Indeed he was more pedantic than I can represent him, and placed more
7725𓀴scraps of Latin in his speech; but it was all uttered with a fine
7726𓀴geniality of eye and manner which went far to conquer my distrust.
7727𓀴Moreover, I could see he now treated me as if I was myself beyond a
7728𓀴doubt; so that first point of my identity seemed fully granted.
7729𓀴
7730𓀴“Sir,” said I, “if I tell you my story, I must commit a friend’s life
7731𓀴to your discretion. Pass me your word it shall be sacred; and for what
7732𓀴touches myself, I will ask no better guarantee than just your face.”
7733𓀴
7734𓀴He passed me his word very seriously. “But,” said he, “these are rather
7735𓀴alarming prolocutions; and if there are in your story any little jostles
7736𓀴to the law, I would beg you to bear in mind that I am a lawyer, and pass
7737𓀴lightly.”
7738𓀴
7739𓀴Thereupon I told him my story from the first, he listening with his
7740𓀴spectacles thrust up and his eyes closed, so that I sometimes feared
7741𓀴he was asleep. But no such matter! he heard every word (as I found
7742𓀴afterward) with such quickness of hearing and precision of memory as
7743𓀴often surprised me. Even strange outlandish Gaelic names, heard for that
7744𓀴time only, he remembered and would remind me of, years after. Yet when I
7745𓀴called Alan Breck in full, we had an odd scene. The name of Alan had of
7746𓀴course rung through Scotland, with the news of the Appin murder and the
7747𓀴offer of the reward; and it had no sooner escaped me than the lawyer
7748𓀴moved in his seat and opened his eyes.
7749𓀴
7750𓀴“I would name no unnecessary names, Mr. Balfour,” said he; “above all of
7751𓀴Highlanders, many of whom are obnoxious to the law.”
7752𓀴
7753𓀴“Well, it might have been better not,” said I, “but since I have let it
7754𓀴slip, I may as well continue.”
7755𓀴
7756𓀴“Not at all,” said Mr. Rankeillor. “I am somewhat dull of hearing, as
7757𓀴you may have remarked; and I am far from sure I caught the name exactly.
7758𓀴We will call your friend, if you please, Mr. Thomson--that there may
7759𓀴be no reflections. And in future, I would take some such way with any
7760𓀴Highlander that you may have to mention--dead or alive.”
7761𓀴
7762𓀴By this, I saw he must have heard the name all too clearly, and had
7763𓀴already guessed I might be coming to the murder. If he chose to play
7764𓀴this part of ignorance, it was no matter of mine; so I smiled, said it
7765𓀴was no very Highland-sounding name, and consented. Through all the rest
7766𓀴of my story Alan was Mr. Thomson; which amused me the more, as it was a
7767𓀴piece of policy after his own heart. James Stewart, in like manner,
7768𓀴was mentioned under the style of Mr. Thomson’s kinsman; Colin Campbell
7769𓀴passed as a Mr. Glen; and to Cluny, when I came to that part of my tale,
7770𓀴I gave the name of “Mr. Jameson, a Highland chief.” It was truly the
7771𓀴most open farce, and I wondered that the lawyer should care to keep it
7772𓀴up; but, after all, it was quite in the taste of that age, when there
7773𓀴were two parties in the state, and quiet persons, with no very high
7774𓀴opinions of their own, sought out every cranny to avoid offence to
7775𓀴either.
7776𓀴
7777𓀴“Well, well,” said the lawyer, when I had quite done, “this is a great
7778𓀴epic, a great Odyssey of yours. You must tell it, sir, in a sound
7779𓀴Latinity when your scholarship is riper; or in English if you please,
7780𓀴though for my part I prefer the stronger tongue. You have rolled much;
7781𓀴_quæ regio in terris_--what parish in Scotland (to make a homely
7782𓀴translation) has not been filled with your wanderings? You have shown,
7783𓀴besides, a singular aptitude for getting into false positions; and,
7784𓀴yes, upon the whole, for behaving well in them. This Mr. Thomson seems
7785𓀴to me a gentleman of some choice qualities, though perhaps a trifle
7786𓀴bloody-minded. It would please me none the worse, if (with all his
7787𓀴merits) he were soused in the North Sea, for the man, Mr. David, is a
7788𓀴sore embarrassment. But you are doubtless quite right to adhere to him;
7789𓀴indubitably, he adhered to you. _It comes_--we may say--he was your
7790𓀴true companion; nor less _paribus curis vestigia figit_, for I dare say
7791𓀴you would both take an orra thought upon the gallows. Well, well, these
7792𓀴days are fortunately by; and I think (speaking humanly) that you are
7793𓀴near the end of your troubles.”
7794𓀴
7795𓀴As he thus moralised on my adventures, he looked upon me with so much
7796𓀴humour and benignity that I could scarce contain my satisfaction. I had
7797𓀴been so long wandering with lawless people, and making my bed upon the
7798𓀴hills and under the bare sky, that to sit once more in a clean, covered
7799𓀴house, and to talk amicably with a gentleman in broadcloth, seemed
7800𓀴mighty elevations. Even as I thought so, my eye fell on my unseemly
7801𓀴tatters, and I was once more plunged in confusion. But the lawyer saw
7802𓀴and understood me. He rose, called over the stair to lay another plate,
7803𓀴for Mr. Balfour would stay to dinner, and led me into a bedroom in the
7804𓀴upper part of the house. Here he set before me water and soap, and a
7805𓀴comb; and laid out some clothes that belonged to his son; and here, with
7806𓀴another apposite tag, he left me to my toilet.
7807𓀴
7808𓀴
7809𓀴
7810𓀴
7811𓀴CHAPTER XXVIII
7812𓀴
7813𓀴I GO IN QUEST OF MY INHERITANCE
7814𓀴
7815𓀴
7816𓀴Having made what change I could in my appearance; and blithe was I to look in
7817𓀴the glass and find the beggarman a thing of the past, and David Balfour
7818𓀴come to life again. And yet I was ashamed of the change too, and, above
7819𓀴all, of the borrowed clothes. When I had done, Mr. Rankeillor caught
7820𓀴me on the stair, made me his compliments, and had me again into the
7821𓀴cabinet.
7822𓀴
7823𓀴“Sit ye down, Mr. David,” said he, “and now that you are looking a
7824𓀴little more like yourself, let me see if I can find you any news. You
7825𓀴will be wondering, no doubt, about your father and your uncle? To be
7826𓀴sure it is a singular tale; and the explanation is one that I blush to
7827𓀴have to offer you. For,” says he, really with embarrassment, “the matter
7828𓀴hinges on a love affair.”
7829𓀴
7830𓀴“Truly,” said I, “I cannot very well join that notion with my uncle.”
7831𓀴
7832𓀴“But your uncle, Mr. David, was not always old,” replied the lawyer,
7833𓀴“and what may perhaps surprise you more, not always ugly. He had a fine,
7834𓀴gallant air; people stood in their doors to look after him, as he
7835𓀴went by upon a mettle horse. I have seen it with these eyes, and I
7836𓀴ingenuously confess, not altogether without envy; for I was a plain lad
7837𓀴myself and a plain man’s son; and in those days it was a case of _Odi
7838𓀴te, qui bellus es, Sabelle_.”
7839𓀴
7840𓀴“It sounds like a dream,” said I.
7841𓀴
7842𓀴“Ay, ay,” said the lawyer, “that is how it is with youth and age. Nor
7843𓀴was that all, but he had a spirit of his own that seemed to promise
7844𓀴great things in the future. In 1715, what must he do but run away to
7845𓀴join the rebels? It was your father that pursued him, found him in a
7846𓀴ditch, and brought him back _multum gementem;_ to the mirth of the
7847𓀴whole country. However, _majora canamus_--the two lads fell in love,
7848𓀴and that with the same lady. Mr. Ebenezer, who was the admired and the
7849𓀴beloved, and the spoiled one, made, no doubt, mighty certain of the
7850𓀴victory; and when he found he had deceived himself, screamed like a
7851𓀴peacock. The whole country heard of it; now he lay sick at home, with
7852𓀴his silly family standing round the bed in tears; now he rode from
7853𓀴public-house to public-house, and shouted his sorrows into the lug of
7854𓀴Tom, Dick, and Harry. Your father, Mr. David, was a kind gentleman; but
7855𓀴he was weak, dolefully weak; took all this folly with a long
7856𓀴countenance; and one day--by your leave!--resigned the lady. She was no
7857𓀴such fool, however; it’s from her you must inherit your excellent good
7858𓀴sense; and she refused to be bandied from one to another. Both got upon
7859𓀴their knees to her; and the upshot of the matter for that while was
7860𓀴that she showed both of them the door. That was in August; dear me! the
7861𓀴same year I came from college. The scene must have been highly
7862𓀴farcical.”
7863𓀴
7864𓀴I thought myself it was a silly business, but I could not forget my
7865𓀴father had a hand in it. “Surely, sir, it had some note of tragedy,”
7866𓀴 said I.
7867𓀴
7868𓀴“Why, no, sir, not at all,” returned the lawyer. “For tragedy implies
7869𓀴some ponderable matter in dispute, some _dignus vindice nodus;_ and
7870𓀴this piece of work was all about the petulance of a young ass that had
7871𓀴been spoiled, and wanted nothing so much as to be tied up and soundly
7872𓀴belted. However, that was not your father’s view; and the end of it
7873𓀴was, that from concession to concession on your father’s part, and from
7874𓀴one height to another of squalling, sentimental selfishness upon your
7875𓀴uncle’s, they came at last to drive a sort of bargain, from whose ill
7876𓀴results you have recently been smarting. The one man took the lady, the
7877𓀴other the estate. Now, Mr. David, they talk a great deal of charity and
7878𓀴generosity; but in this disputable state of life, I often think the
7879𓀴happiest consequences seem to flow when a gentleman consults his
7880𓀴lawyer, and takes all the law allows him. Anyhow, this piece of
7881𓀴Quixotry on your father’s part, as it was unjust in itself, has brought
7882𓀴forth a monstrous family of injustices. Your father and mother lived
7883𓀴and died poor folk; you were poorly reared; and in the meanwhile, what
7884𓀴a time it has been for the tenants on the estate of Shaws! And I might
7885𓀴add (if it was a matter I cared much about) what a time for Mr.
7886𓀴Ebenezer!”
7887𓀴
7888𓀴“And yet that is certainly the strangest part of all,” said I, “that a
7889𓀴man’s nature should thus change.”
7890𓀴
7891𓀴“True,” said Mr. Rankeillor. “And yet I imagine it was natural enough.
7892𓀴He could not think that he had played a handsome part. Those who knew
7893𓀴the story gave him the cold shoulder; those who knew it not, seeing one
7894𓀴brother disappear, and the other succeed in the estate, raised a cry of
7895𓀴murder; so that upon all sides he found himself evited. Money was all
7896𓀴he got by his bargain; well, he came to think the more of money. He was
7897𓀴selfish when he was young, he is selfish now that he is old; and the
7898𓀴latter end of all these pretty manners and fine feelings you have seen
7899𓀴for yourself.”
7900𓀴
7901𓀴“Well, sir,” said I, “and in all this, what is my position?”
7902𓀴
7903𓀴“The estate is yours beyond a doubt,” replied the lawyer. “It matters
7904𓀴nothing what your father signed, you are the heir of entail. But your
7905𓀴uncle is a man to fight the indefensible; and it would be likely your
7906𓀴identity that he would call in question. A lawsuit is always expensive,
7907𓀴and a family lawsuit always scandalous; besides which, if any of your
7908𓀴doings with your friend Mr. Thomson were to come out, we might find that
7909𓀴we had burned our fingers. The kidnapping, to be sure, would be a court
7910𓀴card upon our side, if we could only prove it. But it may be difficult
7911𓀴to prove; and my advice (upon the whole) is to make a very easy bargain
7912𓀴with your uncle, perhaps even leaving him at Shaws where he has
7913𓀴taken root for a quarter of a century, and contenting yourself in the
7914𓀴meanwhile with a fair provision.”
7915𓀴
7916𓀴I told him I was very willing to be easy, and that to carry family
7917𓀴concerns before the public was a step from which I was naturally much
7918𓀴averse. In the meantime (thinking to myself) I began to see the outlines
7919𓀴of that scheme on which we afterwards acted.
7920𓀴
7921𓀴“The great affair,” I asked, “is to bring home to him the kidnapping?”
7922𓀴
7923𓀴“Surely,” said Mr. Rankeillor, “and if possible, out of court. For mark
7924𓀴you here, Mr. David: we could no doubt find some men of the _Covenant_
7925𓀴who would swear to your reclusion; but once they were in the box, we
7926𓀴could no longer check their testimony, and some word of your friend Mr.
7927𓀴Thomson must certainly crop out. Which (from what you have let fall) I
7928𓀴cannot think to be desirable.”
7929𓀴
7930𓀴“Well, sir,” said I, “here is my way of it.” And I opened my plot to
7931𓀴him.
7932𓀴
7933𓀴“But this would seem to involve my meeting the man Thomson?” says he,
7934𓀴when I had done.
7935𓀴
7936𓀴“I think so, indeed, sir,” said I.
7937𓀴
7938𓀴“Dear doctor!” cries he, rubbing his brow. “Dear doctor! No, Mr. David,
7939𓀴I am afraid your scheme is inadmissible. I say nothing against your
7940𓀴friend, Mr. Thomson: I know nothing against him; and if I did--mark
7941𓀴this, Mr. David!--it would be my duty to lay hands on him. Now I put it
7942𓀴to you: is it wise to meet? He may have matters to his charge. He may
7943𓀴not have told you all. His name may not be even Thomson!” cries the
7944𓀴lawyer, twinkling; “for some of these fellows will pick up names by the
7945𓀴roadside as another would gather haws.”
7946𓀴
7947𓀴“You must be the judge, sir,” said I.
7948𓀴
7949𓀴But it was clear my plan had taken hold upon his fancy, for he kept
7950𓀴musing to himself till we were called to dinner and the company of Mrs.
7951𓀴Rankeillor; and that lady had scarce left us again to ourselves and a
7952𓀴bottle of wine, ere he was back harping on my proposal. When and where
7953𓀴was I to meet my friend Mr. Thomson; was I sure of Mr. T.’s discretion;
7954𓀴supposing we could catch the old fox tripping, would I consent to such
7955𓀴and such a term of an agreement--these and the like questions he kept
7956𓀴asking at long intervals, while he thoughtfully rolled his wine upon his
7957𓀴tongue. When I had answered all of them, seemingly to his contentment,
7958𓀴he fell into a still deeper muse, even the claret being now forgotten.
7959𓀴Then he got a sheet of paper and a pencil, and set to work writing and
7960𓀴weighing every word; and at last touched a bell and had his clerk into
7961𓀴the chamber.
7962𓀴
7963𓀴“Torrance,” said he, “I must have this written out fair against
7964𓀴to-night; and when it is done, you will be so kind as put on your hat
7965𓀴and be ready to come along with this gentleman and me, for you will
7966𓀴probably be wanted as a witness.”
7967𓀴
7968𓀴“What, sir,” cried I, as soon as the clerk was gone, “are you to venture
7969𓀴it?”
7970𓀴
7971𓀴“Why, so it would appear,” says he, filling his glass. “But let us speak
7972𓀴no more of business. The very sight of Torrance brings in my head a
7973𓀴little droll matter of some years ago, when I had made a tryst with the
7974𓀴poor oaf at the cross of Edinburgh. Each had gone his proper errand; and
7975𓀴when it came four o’clock, Torrance had been taking a glass and did
7976𓀴not know his master, and I, who had forgot my spectacles, was so blind
7977𓀴without them, that I give you my word I did not know my own clerk.” And
7978𓀴thereupon he laughed heartily.
7979𓀴
7980𓀴I said it was an odd chance, and smiled out of politeness; but what held
7981𓀴me all the afternoon in wonder, he kept returning and dwelling on this
7982𓀴story, and telling it again with fresh details and laughter; so that I
7983𓀴began at last to be quite put out of countenance and feel ashamed for my
7984𓀴friend’s folly.
7985𓀴
7986𓀴Towards the time I had appointed with Alan, we set out from the house,
7987𓀴Mr. Rankeillor and I arm in arm, and Torrance following behind with the
7988𓀴deed in his pocket and a covered basket in his hand. All through the
7989𓀴town, the lawyer was bowing right and left, and continually being
7990𓀴button-holed by gentlemen on matters of burgh or private business; and I
7991𓀴could see he was one greatly looked up to in the county. At last we were
7992𓀴clear of the houses, and began to go along the side of the haven and
7993𓀴towards the Hawes Inn and the Ferry pier, the scene of my misfortune. I
7994𓀴could not look upon the place without emotion, recalling how many that
7995𓀴had been there with me that day were now no more: Ransome taken, I could
7996𓀴hope, from the evil to come; Shuan passed where I dared not follow him;
7997𓀴and the poor souls that had gone down with the brig in her last plunge.
7998𓀴All these, and the brig herself, I had outlived; and come through these
7999𓀴hardships and fearful perils without scath. My only thought should have
8000𓀴been of gratitude; and yet I could not behold the place without sorrow
8001𓀴for others and a chill of recollected fear.
8002𓀴
8003𓀴I was so thinking when, upon a sudden, Mr. Rankeillor cried out, clapped
8004𓀴his hand to his pockets, and began to laugh.
8005𓀴
8006𓀴“Why,” he cries, “if this be not a farcical adventure! After all that I
8007𓀴said, I have forgot my glasses!”
8008𓀴
8009𓀴At that, of course, I understood the purpose of his anecdote, and knew
8010𓀴that if he had left his spectacles at home, it had been done on purpose,
8011𓀴so that he might have the benefit of Alan’s help without the awkwardness
8012𓀴of recognising him. And indeed it was well thought upon; for now
8013𓀴(suppose things to go the very worst) how could Rankeillor swear to
8014𓀴my friend’s identity, or how be made to bear damaging evidence against
8015𓀴myself? For all that, he had been a long while of finding out his want,
8016𓀴and had spoken to and recognised a good few persons as we came through
8017𓀴the town; and I had little doubt myself that he saw reasonably well.
8018𓀴
8019𓀴As soon as we were past the Hawes (where I recognised the landlord
8020𓀴smoking his pipe in the door, and was amazed to see him look no older)
8021𓀴Mr. Rankeillor changed the order of march, walking behind with Torrance
8022𓀴and sending me forward in the manner of a scout. I went up the hill,
8023𓀴whistling from time to time my Gaelic air; and at length I had the
8024𓀴pleasure to hear it answered and to see Alan rise from behind a bush. He
8025𓀴was somewhat dashed in spirits, having passed a long day alone skulking
8026𓀴in the county, and made but a poor meal in an alehouse near Dundas. But
8027𓀴at the mere sight of my clothes, he began to brighten up; and as soon as
8028𓀴I had told him in what a forward state our matters were and the part I
8029𓀴looked to him to play in what remained, he sprang into a new man.
8030𓀴
8031𓀴“And that is a very good notion of yours,” says he; “and I dare to say
8032𓀴that you could lay your hands upon no better man to put it through than
8033𓀴Alan Breck. It is not a thing (mark ye) that any one could do, but takes
8034𓀴a gentleman of penetration. But it sticks in my head your lawyer-man
8035𓀴will be somewhat wearying to see me,” says Alan.
8036𓀴
8037𓀴Accordingly I cried and waved on Mr. Rankeillor, who came up alone and
8038𓀴was presented to my friend, Mr. Thomson.
8039𓀴
8040𓀴“Mr. Thomson, I am pleased to meet you,” said he. “But I have forgotten
8041𓀴my glasses; and our friend, Mr. David here” (clapping me on the
8042𓀴shoulder), “will tell you that I am little better than blind, and that
8043𓀴you must not be surprised if I pass you by to-morrow.”
8044𓀴
8045𓀴This he said, thinking that Alan would be pleased; but the Highlandman’s
8046𓀴vanity was ready to startle at a less matter than that.
8047𓀴
8048𓀴“Why, sir,” says he, stiffly, “I would say it mattered the less as we
8049𓀴are met here for a particular end, to see justice done to Mr. Balfour;
8050𓀴and by what I can see, not very likely to have much else in common. But
8051𓀴I accept your apology, which was a very proper one to make.”
8052𓀴
8053𓀴“And that is more than I could look for, Mr. Thomson,” said Rankeillor,
8054𓀴heartily. “And now as you and I are the chief actors in this enterprise,
8055𓀴I think we should come into a nice agreement; to which end, I propose
8056𓀴that you should lend me your arm, for (what with the dusk and the want
8057𓀴of my glasses) I am not very clear as to the path; and as for you, Mr.
8058𓀴David, you will find Torrance a pleasant kind of body to speak with.
8059𓀴Only let me remind you, it’s quite needless he should hear more of your
8060𓀴adventures or those of--ahem--Mr. Thomson.”
8061𓀴
8062𓀴Accordingly these two went on ahead in very close talk, and Torrance and
8063𓀴I brought up the rear.
8064𓀴
8065𓀴Night was quite come when we came in view of the house of Shaws. Ten
8066𓀴had been gone some time; it was dark and mild, with a pleasant, rustling
8067𓀴wind in the south-west that covered the sound of our approach; and as we
8068𓀴drew near we saw no glimmer of light in any portion of the building. It
8069𓀴seemed my uncle was already in bed, which was indeed the best thing for
8070𓀴our arrangements. We made our last whispered consultations some fifty
8071𓀴yards away; and then the lawyer and Torrance and I crept quietly up and
8072𓀴crouched down beside the corner of the house; and as soon as we were
8073𓀴in our places, Alan strode to the door without concealment and began to
8074𓀴knock.
8075𓀴
8076𓀴
8077𓀴
8078𓀴
8079𓀴CHAPTER XXIX
8080𓀴
8081𓀴I COME INTO MY KINGDOM
8082𓀴
8083𓀴
8084𓀴For some time Alan volleyed upon the door, and his knocking only roused
8085𓀴the echoes of the house and neighbourhood. At last, however, I could
8086𓀴hear the noise of a window gently thrust up, and knew that my uncle
8087𓀴had come to his observatory. By what light there was, he would see Alan
8088𓀴standing, like a dark shadow, on the steps; the three witnesses were
8089𓀴hidden quite out of his view; so that there was nothing to alarm an
8090𓀴honest man in his own house. For all that, he studied his visitor awhile
8091𓀴in silence, and when he spoke his voice had a quaver of misgiving.
8092𓀴
8093𓀴“What’s this?” says he. “This is nae kind of time of night for decent
8094𓀴folk; and I hae nae trokings[34] wi’ night-hawks. What brings ye here? I
8095𓀴have a blunderbush.”
8096𓀴
8097𓀴     [34] Dealings.
8098𓀴
8099𓀴“Is that yoursel’, Mr. Balfour?” returned Alan, stepping back and
8100𓀴looking up into the darkness. “Have a care of that blunderbuss; they’re
8101𓀴nasty things to burst.”
8102𓀴
8103𓀴“What brings ye here? and whae are ye?” says my uncle, angrily.
8104𓀴
8105𓀴“I have no manner of inclination to rowt out my name to the
8106𓀴country-side,” said Alan; “but what brings me here is another story,
8107𓀴being more of your affair than mine; and if ye’re sure it’s what ye
8108𓀴would like, I’ll set it to a tune and sing it to you.”
8109𓀴
8110𓀴“And what is’t?” asked my uncle.
8111𓀴
8112𓀴“David,” says Alan.
8113𓀴
8114𓀴“What was that?” cried my uncle, in a mighty changed voice.
8115𓀴
8116𓀴“Shall I give ye the rest of the name, then?” said Alan.
8117𓀴
8118𓀴There was a pause; and then, “I’m thinking I’ll better let ye in,” says
8119𓀴my uncle, doubtfully.
8120𓀴
8121𓀴“I dare say that,” said Alan; “but the point is, Would I go? Now I will
8122𓀴tell you what I am thinking. I am thinking that it is here upon this
8123𓀴doorstep that we must confer upon this business; and it shall be here or
8124𓀴nowhere at all whatever; for I would have you to understand that I am as
8125𓀴stiffnecked as yoursel’, and a gentleman of better family.”
8126𓀴
8127𓀴This change of note disconcerted Ebenezer; he was a little while
8128𓀴digesting it, and then says he, “Weel, weel, what must be must,” and
8129𓀴shut the window. But it took him a long time to get down-stairs, and a
8130𓀴still longer to undo the fastenings, repenting (I dare say) and taken
8131𓀴with fresh claps of fear at every second step and every bolt and bar. At
8132𓀴last, however, we heard the creak of the hinges, and it seems my uncle
8133𓀴slipped gingerly out and (seeing that Alan had stepped back a pace or
8134𓀴two) sate him down on the top doorstep with the blunderbuss ready in his
8135𓀴hands.
8136𓀴
8137𓀴“And, now” says he, “mind I have my blunderbush, and if ye take a step
8138𓀴nearer ye’re as good as deid.”
8139𓀴
8140𓀴“And a very civil speech,” says Alan, “to be sure.”
8141𓀴
8142𓀴“Na,” says my uncle, “but this is no a very chanty kind of a proceeding,
8143𓀴and I’m bound to be prepared. And now that we understand each other,
8144𓀴ye’ll can name your business.”
8145𓀴
8146𓀴“Why,” says Alan, “you that are a man of so much understanding, will
8147𓀴doubtless have perceived that I am a Hieland gentleman. My name has nae
8148𓀴business in my story; but the county of my friends is no very far from
8149𓀴the Isle of Mull, of which ye will have heard. It seems there was a
8150𓀴ship lost in those parts; and the next day a gentleman of my family was
8151𓀴seeking wreck-wood for his fire along the sands, when he came upon a lad
8152𓀴that was half drowned. Well, he brought him to; and he and some other
8153𓀴gentleman took and clapped him in an auld, ruined castle, where from
8154𓀴that day to this he has been a great expense to my friends. My friends
8155𓀴are a wee wild-like, and not so particular about the law as some that
8156𓀴I could name; and finding that the lad owned some decent folk, and was
8157𓀴your born nephew, Mr. Balfour, they asked me to give ye a bit call and
8158𓀴confer upon the matter. And I may tell ye at the off-go, unless we can
8159𓀴agree upon some terms, ye are little likely to set eyes upon him. For my
8160𓀴friends,” added Alan, simply, “are no very well off.”
8161𓀴
8162𓀴My uncle cleared his throat. “I’m no very caring,” says he. “He wasnae a
8163𓀴good lad at the best of it, and I’ve nae call to interfere.”
8164𓀴
8165𓀴“Ay, ay,” said Alan, “I see what ye would be at: pretending ye don’t
8166𓀴care, to make the ransom smaller.”
8167𓀴
8168𓀴“Na,” said my uncle, “it’s the mere truth. I take nae manner of interest
8169𓀴in the lad, and I’ll pay nae ransome, and ye can make a kirk and a mill
8170𓀴of him for what I care.”
8171𓀴
8172𓀴“Hoot, sir,” says Alan. “Blood’s thicker than water, in the deil’s name!
8173𓀴Ye cannae desert your brother’s son for the fair shame of it; and if
8174𓀴ye did, and it came to be kennt, ye wouldnae be very popular in your
8175𓀴country-side, or I’m the more deceived.”
8176𓀴
8177𓀴“I’m no just very popular the way it is,” returned Ebenezer; “and I
8178𓀴dinnae see how it would come to be kennt. No by me, onyway; nor yet by
8179𓀴you or your friends. So that’s idle talk, my buckie,” says he.
8180𓀴
8181𓀴“Then it’ll have to be David that tells it,” said Alan.
8182𓀴
8183𓀴“How that?” says my uncle, sharply.
8184𓀴
8185𓀴“Ou, just this way,” says Alan. “My friends would doubtless keep your
8186𓀴nephew as long as there was any likelihood of siller to be made of it,
8187𓀴but if there was nane, I am clearly of opinion they would let him gang
8188𓀴where he pleased, and be damned to him!”
8189𓀴
8190𓀴“Ay, but I’m no very caring about that either,” said my uncle. “I
8191𓀴wouldnae be muckle made up with that.”
8192𓀴
8193𓀴“I was thinking that,” said Alan.
8194𓀴
8195𓀴“And what for why?” asked Ebenezer.
8196𓀴
8197𓀴“Why, Mr. Balfour,” replied Alan, “by all that I could hear, there were
8198𓀴two ways of it: either ye liked David and would pay to get him back; or
8199𓀴else ye had very good reasons for not wanting him, and would pay for us
8200𓀴to keep him. It seems it’s not the first; well then, it’s the second;
8201𓀴and blythe am I to ken it, for it should be a pretty penny in my pocket
8202𓀴and the pockets of my friends.”
8203𓀴
8204𓀴“I dinnae follow ye there,” said my uncle.
8205𓀴
8206𓀴“No?” said Alan. “Well, see here: you dinnae want the lad back; well,
8207𓀴what do ye want done with him, and how much will ye pay?”
8208𓀴
8209𓀴My uncle made no answer, but shifted uneasily on his seat.
8210𓀴
8211𓀴“Come, sir,” cried Alan. “I would have you to ken that I am a gentleman;
8212𓀴I bear a king’s name; I am nae rider to kick my shanks at your hall
8213𓀴door. Either give me an answer in civility, and that out of hand; or by
8214𓀴the top of Glencoe, I will ram three feet of iron through your vitals.”
8215𓀴
8216𓀴“Eh, man,” cried my uncle, scrambling to his feet, “give me a meenit!
8217𓀴What’s like wrong with ye? I’m just a plain man and nae dancing master;
8218𓀴and I’m tryin to be as ceevil as it’s morally possible. As for that wild
8219𓀴talk, it’s fair disrepitable. Vitals, says you! And where would I be
8220𓀴with my blunderbush?” he snarled.
8221𓀴
8222𓀴“Powder and your auld hands are but as the snail to the swallow against
8223𓀴the bright steel in the hands of Alan,” said the other. “Before your
8224𓀴jottering finger could find the trigger, the hilt would dirl on your
8225𓀴breast-bane.”
8226𓀴
8227𓀴“Eh, man, whae’s denying it?” said my uncle. “Pit it as ye please, hae’t
8228𓀴your ain way; I’ll do naething to cross ye. Just tell me what like ye’ll
8229𓀴be wanting, and ye’ll see that we’ll can agree fine.”
8230𓀴
8231𓀴“Troth, sir,” said Alan, “I ask for nothing but plain dealing. In two
8232𓀴words: do ye want the lad killed or kept?”
8233𓀴
8234𓀴“O, sirs!” cried Ebenezer. “O, sirs, me! that’s no kind of language!”
8235𓀴
8236𓀴“Killed or kept!” repeated Alan.
8237𓀴
8238𓀴“O, keepit, keepit!” wailed my uncle. “We’ll have nae bloodshed, if you
8239𓀴please.”
8240𓀴
8241𓀴“Well,” says Alan, “as ye please; that’ll be the dearer.”
8242𓀴
8243𓀴“The dearer?” cries Ebenezer. “Would ye fyle your hands wi’ crime?”
8244𓀴
8245𓀴“Hoot!” said Alan, “they’re baith crime, whatever! And the killing’s
8246𓀴easier, and quicker, and surer. Keeping the lad’ll be a fashious[35] job, a
8247𓀴fashious, kittle business.”
8248𓀴
8249𓀴     [35] Troublesome.
8250𓀴
8251𓀴“I’ll have him keepit, though,” returned my uncle. “I never had naething
8252𓀴to do with onything morally wrong; and I’m no gaun to begin to pleasure
8253𓀴a wild Hielandman.”
8254𓀴
8255𓀴“Ye’re unco scrupulous,” sneered Alan.
8256𓀴
8257𓀴“I’m a man o’ principle,” said Ebenezer, simply; “and if I have to pay
8258𓀴for it, I’ll have to pay for it. And besides,” says he, “ye forget the
8259𓀴lad’s my brother’s son.”
8260𓀴
8261𓀴“Well, well,” said Alan, “and now about the price. It’s no very easy for
8262𓀴me to set a name upon it; I would first have to ken some small matters.
8263𓀴I would have to ken, for instance, what ye gave Hoseason at the first
8264𓀴off-go?”
8265𓀴
8266𓀴“Hoseason!” cries my uncle, struck aback. “What for?”
8267𓀴
8268𓀴“For kidnapping David,” says Alan.
8269𓀴
8270𓀴“It’s a lee, it’s a black lee!” cried my uncle. “He was never kidnapped.
8271𓀴He leed in his throat that tauld ye that. Kidnapped? He never was!”
8272𓀴
8273𓀴“That’s no fault of mine nor yet of yours,” said Alan; “nor yet of
8274𓀴Hoseason’s, if he’s a man that can be trusted.”
8275𓀴
8276𓀴“What do ye mean?” cried Ebenezer. “Did Hoseason tell ye?”
8277𓀴
8278𓀴“Why, ye donnered auld runt, how else would I ken?” cried Alan.
8279𓀴“Hoseason and me are partners; we gang shares; so ye can see for
8280𓀴yoursel’ what good ye can do leeing. And I must plainly say ye drove a
8281𓀴fool’s bargain when ye let a man like the sailor-man so far forward in
8282𓀴your private matters. But that’s past praying for; and ye must lie on
8283𓀴your bed the way ye made it. And the point in hand is just this: what
8284𓀴did ye pay him?”
8285𓀴
8286𓀴“Has he tauld ye himsel’?” asked my uncle.
8287𓀴
8288𓀴“That’s my concern,” said Alan.
8289𓀴
8290𓀴“Weel,” said my uncle, “I dinnae care what he said, he leed, and the
8291𓀴solemn God’s truth is this, that I gave him twenty pound. But I’ll be
8292𓀴perfec’ly honest with ye: forby that, he was to have the selling of the
8293𓀴lad in Caroliny, whilk would be as muckle mair, but no from my pocket,
8294𓀴ye see.”
8295𓀴
8296𓀴“Thank you, Mr. Thomson. That will do excellently well,” said the
8297𓀴lawyer, stepping forward; and then mighty civilly, “Good-evening, Mr.
8298𓀴Balfour,” said he.
8299𓀴
8300𓀴And, “Good-evening, Uncle Ebenezer,” said I.
8301𓀴
8302𓀴And, “It’s a braw nicht, Mr. Balfour,” added Torrance.
8303𓀴
8304𓀴Never a word said my uncle, neither black nor white; but just sat where
8305𓀴he was on the top door-step and stared upon us like a man turned to
8306𓀴stone. Alan filched away his blunderbuss; and the lawyer, taking him
8307𓀴by the arm, plucked him up from the doorstep, led him into the kitchen,
8308𓀴whither we all followed, and set him down in a chair beside the hearth,
8309𓀴where the fire was out and only a rush-light burning.
8310𓀴
8311𓀴There we all looked upon him for a while, exulting greatly in our
8312𓀴success, but yet with a sort of pity for the man’s shame.
8313𓀴
8314𓀴“Come, come, Mr. Ebenezer,” said the lawyer, “you must not be
8315𓀴down-hearted, for I promise you we shall make easy terms. In the
8316𓀴meanwhile give us the cellar key, and Torrance shall draw us a bottle
8317𓀴of your father’s wine in honour of the event.” Then, turning to me and
8318𓀴taking me by the hand, “Mr. David,” says he, “I wish you all joy in your
8319𓀴good fortune, which I believe to be deserved.” And then to Alan, with
8320𓀴a spice of drollery, “Mr. Thomson, I pay you my compliment; it was
8321𓀴most artfully conducted; but in one point you somewhat outran my
8322𓀴comprehension. Do I understand your name to be James? or Charles? or is
8323𓀴it George, perhaps?”
8324𓀴
8325𓀴“And why should it be any of the three, sir?” quoth Alan, drawing
8326𓀴himself up, like one who smelt an offence.
8327𓀴
8328𓀴“Only, sir, that you mentioned a king’s name,” replied Rankeillor; “and
8329𓀴as there has never yet been a King Thomson, or his fame at least has
8330𓀴never come my way, I judged you must refer to that you had in baptism.”
8331𓀴
8332𓀴This was just the stab that Alan would feel keenest, and I am free to
8333𓀴confess he took it very ill. Not a word would he answer, but stepped off
8334𓀴to the far end of the kitchen, and sat down and sulked; and it was not
8335𓀴till I stepped after him, and gave him my hand, and thanked him by title
8336𓀴as the chief spring of my success, that he began to smile a bit, and was
8337𓀴at last prevailed upon to join our party.
8338𓀴
8339𓀴By that time we had the fire lighted, and a bottle of wine uncorked; a
8340𓀴good supper came out of the basket, to which Torrance and I and Alan
8341𓀴set ourselves down; while the lawyer and my uncle passed into the next
8342𓀴chamber to consult. They stayed there closeted about an hour; at the end
8343𓀴of which period they had come to a good understanding, and my uncle and
8344𓀴I set our hands to the agreement in a formal manner. By the terms
8345𓀴of this, my uncle bound himself to satisfy Rankeillor as to his
8346𓀴intromissions, and to pay me two clear thirds of the yearly income of
8347𓀴Shaws.
8348𓀴
8349𓀴So the beggar in the ballad had come home; and when I lay down that
8350𓀴night on the kitchen chests, I was a man of means and had a name in the
8351𓀴country. Alan and Torrance and Rankeillor slept and snored on their hard
8352𓀴beds; but for me who had lain out under heaven and upon dirt and stones,
8353𓀴so many days and nights, and often with an empty belly, and in fear
8354𓀴of death, this good change in my case unmanned me more than any of the
8355𓀴former evil ones; and I lay till dawn, looking at the fire on the roof
8356𓀴and planning the future.
8357𓀴
8358𓀴
8359𓀴
8360𓀴
8361𓀴CHAPTER XXX
8362𓀴
8363𓀴GOOD-BYE
8364𓀴
8365𓀴
8366𓀴So far as I was concerned myself, I had come to port; but I had still
8367𓀴Alan, to whom I was so much beholden, on my hands; and I felt besides a
8368𓀴heavy charge in the matter of the murder and James of the Glens. On both
8369𓀴these heads I unbosomed to Rankeillor the next morning, walking to and
8370𓀴fro about six of the clock before the house of Shaws, and with nothing
8371𓀴in view but the fields and woods that had been my ancestors’ and were
8372𓀴now mine. Even as I spoke on these grave subjects, my eye would take a
8373𓀴glad bit of a run over the prospect, and my heart jump with pride.
8374𓀴
8375𓀴About my clear duty to my friend, the lawyer had no doubt. I must help
8376𓀴him out of the county at whatever risk; but in the case of James, he was
8377𓀴of a different mind.
8378𓀴
8379𓀴“Mr. Thomson,” says he, “is one thing, Mr. Thomson’s kinsman quite
8380𓀴another. I know little of the facts, but I gather that a great noble
8381𓀴(whom we will call, if you like, the D. of A.)[36] has some concern and
8382𓀴is even supposed to feel some animosity in the matter. The D. of A. is
8383𓀴doubtless an excellent nobleman; but, Mr. David, _timeo qui nocuere
8384𓀴deos_. If you interfere to balk his vengeance, you should remember
8385𓀴there is one way to shut your testimony out; and that is to put you in
8386𓀴the dock. There, you would be in the same pickle as Mr. Thomson’s
8387𓀴kinsman. You will object that you are innocent; well, but so is he. And
8388𓀴to be tried for your life before a Highland jury, on a Highland quarrel
8389𓀴and with a Highland Judge upon the bench, would be a brief transition
8390𓀴to the gallows.”
8391𓀴
8392𓀴     [36] The Duke of Argyle.
8393𓀴
8394𓀴Now I had made all these reasonings before and found no very good reply
8395𓀴to them; so I put on all the simplicity I could. “In that case, sir,”
8396𓀴 said I, “I would just have to be hanged--would I not?”
8397𓀴
8398𓀴“My dear boy,” cries he, “go in God’s name, and do what you think is
8399𓀴right. It is a poor thought that at my time of life I should be advising
8400𓀴you to choose the safe and shameful; and I take it back with an apology.
8401𓀴Go and do your duty; and be hanged, if you must, like a gentleman. There
8402𓀴are worse things in the world than to be hanged.”
8403𓀴
8404𓀴“Not many, sir,” said I, smiling.
8405𓀴
8406𓀴“Why, yes, sir,” he cried, “very many. And it would be ten times better
8407𓀴for your uncle (to go no farther afield) if he were dangling decently
8408𓀴upon a gibbet.”
8409𓀴
8410𓀴Thereupon he turned into the house (still in a great fervour of mind,
8411𓀴so that I saw I had pleased him heartily) and there he wrote me two
8412𓀴letters, making his comments on them as he wrote.
8413𓀴
8414𓀴“This,” says he, “is to my bankers, the British Linen Company, placing a
8415𓀴credit to your name. Consult Mr. Thomson, he will know of ways; and
8416𓀴you, with this credit, can supply the means. I trust you will be a good
8417𓀴husband of your money; but in the affair of a friend like Mr. Thomson,
8418𓀴I would be even prodigal. Then for his kinsman, there is no better way
8419𓀴than that you should seek the Advocate, tell him your tale, and offer
8420𓀴testimony; whether he may take it or not, is quite another matter, and
8421𓀴will turn on the D. of A. Now, that you may reach the Lord Advocate well
8422𓀴recommended, I give you here a letter to a namesake of your own, the
8423𓀴learned Mr. Balfour of Pilrig, a man whom I esteem. It will look better
8424𓀴that you should be presented by one of your own name; and the laird of
8425𓀴Pilrig is much looked up to in the Faculty and stands well with Lord
8426𓀴Advocate Grant. I would not trouble him, if I were you, with any
8427𓀴particulars; and (do you know?) I think it would be needless to refer to
8428𓀴Mr. Thomson. Form yourself upon the laird, he is a good model; when you
8429𓀴deal with the Advocate, be discreet; and in all these matters, may the
8430𓀴Lord guide you, Mr. David!”
8431𓀴
8432𓀴Thereupon he took his farewell, and set out with Torrance for the Ferry,
8433𓀴while Alan and I turned our faces for the city of Edinburgh. As we went
8434𓀴by the footpath and beside the gateposts and the unfinished lodge, we
8435𓀴kept looking back at the house of my fathers. It stood there, bare and
8436𓀴great and smokeless, like a place not lived in; only in one of the top
8437𓀴windows, there was the peak of a nightcap bobbing up and down and back
8438𓀴and forward, like the head of a rabbit from a burrow. I had little
8439𓀴welcome when I came, and less kindness while I stayed; but at least I
8440𓀴was watched as I went away.
8441𓀴
8442𓀴Alan and I went slowly forward upon our way, having little heart either
8443𓀴to walk or speak. The same thought was uppermost in both, that we were
8444𓀴near the time of our parting; and remembrance of all the bygone days
8445𓀴sate upon us sorely. We talked indeed of what should be done; and it
8446𓀴was resolved that Alan should keep to the county, biding now here, now
8447𓀴there, but coming once in the day to a particular place where I might be
8448𓀴able to communicate with him, either in my own person or by messenger.
8449𓀴In the meanwhile, I was to seek out a lawyer, who was an Appin Stewart,
8450𓀴and a man therefore to be wholly trusted; and it should be his part to
8451𓀴find a ship and to arrange for Alan’s safe embarkation. No sooner was
8452𓀴this business done, than the words seemed to leave us; and though I
8453𓀴would seek to jest with Alan under the name of Mr. Thomson, and he with
8454𓀴me on my new clothes and my estate, you could feel very well that we
8455𓀴were nearer tears than laughter.
8456𓀴
8457𓀴We came the by-way over the hill of Corstorphine; and when we got
8458𓀴near to the place called Rest-and-be-Thankful, and looked down on
8459𓀴Corstorphine bogs and over to the city and the castle on the hill, we
8460𓀴both stopped, for we both knew without a word said that we had come to
8461𓀴where our ways parted. Here he repeated to me once again what had been
8462𓀴agreed upon between us: the address of the lawyer, the daily hour at
8463𓀴which Alan might be found, and the signals that were to be made by any
8464𓀴that came seeking him. Then I gave what money I had (a guinea or two of
8465𓀴Rankeillor’s) so that he should not starve in the meanwhile; and then we
8466𓀴stood a space, and looked over at Edinburgh in silence.
8467𓀴
8468𓀴“Well, good-bye,” said Alan, and held out his left hand.
8469𓀴
8470𓀴“Good-bye,” said I, and gave the hand a little grasp, and went off down
8471𓀴hill.
8472𓀴
8473𓀴Neither one of us looked the other in the face, nor so long as he was in
8474𓀴my view did I take one back glance at the friend I was leaving. But as
8475𓀴I went on my way to the city, I felt so lost and lonesome, that I could
8476𓀴have found it in my heart to sit down by the dyke, and cry and weep like
8477𓀴any baby.
8478𓀴
8479𓀴It was coming near noon when I passed in by the West Kirk and the
8480𓀴Grassmarket into the streets of the capital. The huge height of the
8481𓀴buildings, running up to ten and fifteen storeys, the narrow arched
8482𓀴entries that continually vomited passengers, the wares of the merchants
8483𓀴in their windows, the hubbub and endless stir, the foul smells and the
8484𓀴fine clothes, and a hundred other particulars too small to mention,
8485𓀴struck me into a kind of stupor of surprise, so that I let the crowd
8486𓀴carry me to and fro; and yet all the time what I was thinking of was
8487𓀴Alan at Rest-and-be-Thankful; and all the time (although you would think
8488𓀴I would not choose but be delighted with these braws and novelties)
8489𓀴there was a cold gnawing in my inside like a remorse for something
8490𓀴wrong.
8491𓀴
8492𓀴The hand of Providence brought me in my drifting to the very doors of
8493𓀴the British Linen Company’s bank.
8494𓀴
8495𓀴
8496𓀴
